I
THE LEMON WOOD
The night was drawing to a close. All was asleep in beautiful, happy Osaka. Only the strident cry of the sentries, calling out to each other on the ramparts, occasionally pierced the silence, which was no longer disturbed by anything except the distant rumour of the sea in the gulf.
Above the great dark mass formed by the palaces and gardens of the siogoun
[1] , a star was slowly fading. The morning twilight shivered in the air. The tops of the woods began to cut their waves more clearly against the bluish sky.
Soon a pale glow touched the tallest trees, then crept through the branches and foliage and filtered down to the ground.
Then, in the prince's gardens, alleys cluttered with flowering brambles took on a hazy perspective; the grass regained its emerald hue; a clump of peonies saw the brilliance of their sumptuous flowers return, and a white staircase was half revealed from the mist in the distance of an avenue.
At last, suddenly, the sky turned red; arrows of light shot through the bushes, causing drops of water to sparkle on the leaves. A pheasant shot down; a crane shook its snowy wings heavily and, with a long cry, slowly flew away into the light, while the earth smoked like a cassolette and the birds, in full voice, cheered the rising sun.
As soon as the divine star had risen from the horizon, the vibrations of a gong were heard. It was struck in a monotonous, hauntingly melancholy rhythm: four loud strokes, four soft strokes, four loud strokes, and so on and so on. It was to greet the day and announce the morning prayers.
A sudden burst of young, sonorous laughter overcame this pious noise for a moment, and two men appeared, dark against the clear sky, at the top of the white staircase.
They paused for a moment on the highest step, admiring the charming thicket of scrub, ferns and flowering shrubs that formed the banisters of the staircase.
Then they descended slowly through the whimsical shadows cast by the branches on the steps.
When they reached the foot of the stairs, they stepped aside quickly to avoid knocking over a tortoise that was walking on the last step: the tortoise's shell had been gilded, but the gilding had faded slightly in the dampness of the grass.
The two men made their way along the avenue.
The youngest of the walkers was barely twenty years old, but you would have thought he was more if you had seen the proud expression on his face and the assurance in his eyes; however, when he laughed, he seemed like a child; but he laughed little and a sort of haughty sadness clouded his charming forehead.
His costume was very simple, a grey crepe dress, a blue satin coat with no embroidery, and an open fan in his hand.
His companion's toilette, on the other hand, was extremely elaborate. The dress was made of soft white silk, tinged faintly with blue, as if it had retained a moonlight sheen; it fell in fine pleats to the feet and was cinched at the waist by a black velvet belt. The man wearing it was twenty-four years old; he was perfectly handsome; a strange charm emanated from the warm pallor of his face, from his mockingly gentle eyes and, above all, from the contemptuous nonchalance of his whole person; he rested his hand on the rich hilt of one of his two sabres, the points of which raised the folds of his black velvet coat, thrown over his shoulders with the sleeves hanging down.
The two walkers were bare-headed, their hair twisted into a rope and knotted at the top of their heads.
- Where are you taking me, gracious master?" suddenly exclaimed the elder of the two young men.
- You've asked me that question three times now, Ivakura.
- But you haven't answered me, glory of my eyes!
- Well, I have a surprise for you. Close your eyes and give me your hand.
Ivakoura obeyed, and his companion led him a few steps across the grass.
- Now look," he said.
Ivakoura opened his eyes and let out a faint cry of astonishment.
In front of him was a wood of lemon trees in full bloom.
Every tree and shrub looked as if they were covered in frost; on the tallest stems, the dawning day cast shades of pink and gold. All the branches bent under their fragrant load, the flowering bunches tumbled to the ground, on which a few too heavy twigs dragged.
Tender foliage appeared here and there in sprigs, amidst this white blossom emanating a delicious freshness.
You see," said the younger man with a smile, "I wanted to share with you, my favourite, the pleasure of seeing this marvellous blossoming before anyone else. Yesterday, when I came, the wood was like a pearl bush; today, all the flowers are open.
- When I look at this wood, I think of a saying by the poet of peach blossom, Ivakoura: "It snowed on this tree butterfly wings that, as they crossed the morning sky, were tinted pink".
- Ah," exclaimed the younger man, sighing, "I'd like to immerse myself in these flowers as if in a bath and be intoxicated to death by their violent perfume!
After admiring the flowers, Ivakoura looked a little disappointed.
- Even more beautiful flowers were about to bloom in my dream," he said, stifling a yawn. Master, why did you get me up so early?
- Come now, Prince of Nagato," said the young man, placing his hand on his companion's shoulder, "I didn't get you up; you didn't go to bed last night!
- What are you saying?" cried Ivakoura; "what makes you think that?
- Your pallor, friend, and your weary eyes.
- Am I not always like this?
- The gown you're wearing would still be too sumptuous at cock-crowing time [2]; and look! the sun is just rising, it's rabbit time
[3] .
- There is no hour too early to honour a master like you.
- Is it also to honour me, unfaithful subject, that you appear before me armed? These two swords, forgotten on your belt, condemn you; you had just returned to the palace when I summoned you.
The guilty party lowered his head, giving up his defence.
- But what's wrong with your arm?" suddenly exclaimed the younger man when he saw a thin white band protruding from Ivakoura's sleeve.
Ivakoura hid his arm behind his back and showed his other hand.
- I've got nothing," he said.
But his companion grabbed the arm he was hiding. The Prince of Nagato let out a cry of pain.
- You're hurt, aren't you! One day they'll come and tell me that Nagato was killed in a futile quarrel. What have you done now, you reckless incorrigible?
- When the Regent Hiéyas is in your presence, you will know only too well," said the Prince; "you will learn great things, O illustrious friend, about your unworthy favourite. It seems to me that I can already hear the terrible voice of this man to whom nothing is hidden: Fide-Yori, ruler of Japan, son of the great Taïko-Sama, whose memory I venerate, serious disorders have disturbed Osaka this night!...
The Prince of Nagato sounded so much like Hiéyas that the young Siogoun could not help but smile.
- And what are these disturbances? - Broken doors, beatings, uproar, scandals. - Do we know who is responsible for these misdeeds? - The one who leads the others is the only culprit and I know who that culprit is. - But who is it? - Who, if not the one you find in every adventure, in every night battle; who, if not the Prince of Nagato, the terror of honest families, the terror of peaceful people? And as you forgive me, O too merciful one, Hiéyas will reproach you for your weakness, sounding it loud and clear, so that this weakness may harm the siogoun and benefit the regent.
- But what if I were finally incensed by your conduct, Nagato," said the Siogun, "what if I sent you to spend a year in your province?
- I would go, master, without a murmur.
- Yes, and who would love me here?" said Fidé-Yori sadly. I see great devotion around me, but not an affection like yours, but perhaps I am being unfair," he added, "you are the only one I love, and it is no doubt because of that that I seem to be loved only by you.
Nagato looked at the prince with a look of gratitude.
- You feel forgiven by me, don't you?" said Fide-Yori, smiling, "but try to avoid the Regent's reproaches; you know how painful they are for me. Go and greet him, it's nearly time for him to rise; we'll meet again at the council.
- So you'll have to smile at that ugly face," grumbled Nagato.
But he had his leave. He saluted the siogun and sulked away.
Fidé-Yori continued to stroll along the avenue, but soon returned to the lemon grove. He stopped in front of it to admire it once more, and plucked a thin branch laden with flowers. But then the foliage began to rustle as if in a strong wind; a sudden movement stirred the branches and, between the blossoms, a young girl appeared.
The prince stepped back quickly and almost screamed; he thought he was the victim of a vision.
Who are you?" he exclaimed, "perhaps the genie of this wood?
- Oh no," said the young girl in a trembling voice, "but I'm a very daring woman.
She came out of the wood, amid a shower of white petals, and knelt on the grass, holding out her hands to the king.
Fidé-Yori lowered his head and looked at her curiously.
She was exquisitely beautiful: small, graceful, as if crushed beneath the fullness of her robes. It was as if their silky weight had brought her to her knees. Her large, pure eyes, like those of a child, were fearful and pleading, her cheeks, velvety like the wings of butterflies, flushed a little, and her little mouth, half-open in admiration, let her milk-white teeth shine.
- Forgive me," she said, "forgive me for being in your presence without your will.
- I forgive you, poor trembling bird," said Fidé-Yori, "for if I had known you and if I had known what you wanted, I would have wanted to see you. What do you want from me? Is it in my power to make you happy?
- O master!" cried the young girl enthusiastically, "with one word you can make me more radiant than TenSio-Daï-Tsin, the daughter of the sun.
- And what word is that?
- Swear to me that you won't go to the festival of the Sea Genie tomorrow.
- Why this oath?" said the siogun, astonished by this strange plea.
- Because," said the young girl, shivering under the king's feet, "suddenly a bridge will collapse and by evening Japan will no longer have a master.
- You must have discovered a conspiracy," said Fidé-Yori, smiling.
Faced with this smile of disbelief, the young girl paled and her eyes filled with tears.
- O pure disc of light," she cried, "he doesn't believe me! All that I have done so far is nothing. Here is the terrible obstacle and I hadn't thought of it. You listen to the voice of the cricket that announces heat, you listen to the frog that croaks a promise of rain; but a young girl who cries out to you: Beware! I have seen the trap, death is in your path, you don't listen to her and you walk straight into the trap. But that's impossible, you've got to believe me. Do you want me to kill myself at your feet? My death would be a token of my sincerity. Besides, even if I was wrong, what does it matter to you? You can always skip the party. Listen, I've come a long way, from a remote province; alone, under the heavy anguish of my secret, I've outwitted the most subtle spies, I've conquered my fears and overcome my weakness. My father thinks I'm on a pilgrimage to Kioto, and you see: I'm in your city, within the walls of your palaces.
Yet the sentries are vigilant, the moats wide, the walls high. Look, my hands are bleeding, I'm burning with fever. Just now I thought I would never be able to speak, my weakened heart trembling with the joy of your presence and the joy of saving you.
But now I'm dizzy, my blood is frozen: you don't believe me!
- I believe you and I swear to obey you," said the king, moved by this desperate accent; "I will not go to the festival of the Genie of the Sea.
The young girl let out a cry of joy and looked gratefully at the sun rising above the trees.
- But tell me how you discovered this plot," continued the siogun, "and who the perpetrators are.
Oh, don't order me to tell you. This whole edifice of infamy that I am bringing down is crumbling on myself.
So be it, young lady, keep your secret; but tell me at least where this great devotion comes from and why my life is so precious to you?
The young girl slowly raised her eyes to the king, then lowered them and blushed, but answered nothing. A vague emotion troubled the prince's heart. He remained silent and let himself be overwhelmed by this gentle impression. He would have liked to remain like this for a long time, in silence, amidst the birdsong and the perfumes, close to this kneeling child.
- Teach me who you are, you who saved me from death," he said at last, "and show me the reward worthy of your courage.
- My name is Omiti," said the young girl, "I can tell you nothing more. Give me the flower in your hand, that's all I want from you.
Fidé-Yori handed her the lemon branch; Omiti seized it and fled through the wood.
The siogun remained motionless for a long time in the same place, worried, looking at the grass trodden by Omiti's light weight.
II
NAGATO'S WOUND
The Prince of Nagato had returned to his palace.
He was asleep, stretched out on a pile of fine mats. All around him was almost complete darkness, for the blinds had been lowered and large screens put up in front of the windows.
A few walls of black lacquer glimmered in the shadows, however, and vaguely reflected, like cloudy mirrors, the prince's pale head lying back on the cushions.
Nagato had been unable to see Hiéyas: the regent was absorbed in a very urgent matter, he had been told. The young prince was delighted at this circumstance and hurried off to rest in the few hours he had to himself before the council.
In the rooms next to the one where he was sleeping, the servants came and went silently, preparing the master's toilet. They walked carefully so as not to creak the wooden floor and chatted amongst themselves in low voices.
- Our poor master has no sense," said an elderly woman, shaking drops of perfume onto a ceremonial coat. Always partying, going out at night, never resting; he'll kill himself.
- Oh no, pleasure doesn't kill you," said an insolent-looking young boy in brightly coloured clothes.
- What do you know, aphid?" said the maid. Doesn't it seem that he spends his life revelling like a lord? Don't talk so brazenly about things you don't know!
- Perhaps I know them better than you do," said the child with a grimace, "you who are not yet married, despite your great age and beauty.
The maid threw the contents of her bottle in the boy's face, but he hid behind the silver disc of a mirror that he was rubbing to make it clear, and the perfume scattered on the floor. The valet put his head forward when the danger had passed.
- Do you want me for a husband?" he said, "you'll give me some of your years, and between us we'll make a young couple!
The maid, in her anger, let out a burst of voice.
- Will you keep quiet?" said another servant, threatening her with his fist.
- But it's impossible to listen to this young rascal without getting irritated and blushing!
- Blush all you like," said the child, "it doesn't make any noise.
- Shut up, Loo!" said the servant.
Loo shrugged his shoulders and pouted, then went back to rubbing the mirror nonchalantly.
Just then, a man entered the room:
- I wish to speak to Ivakoura, Prince of Nagato," he said loudly.
All the servants waved their hands and arms to silence the newcomer. Loo rushed over to him and put the cloth he used to rub the mirror over his mouth, but the man pushed him away violently.
- What is the meaning of this? Are you fools? I want to speak to the lord you serve, the most illustrious daimio who reigns over the province of Nagato. Warn him and stop making faces.
- He's asleep," a servant said quietly.
- We can't wake him up," said another.
- He's terribly tired," said Loo, putting a finger to his mouth.
- Despite his tiredness, he'll be happy that I've come," said the stranger.
- We have orders not to wake him up until a few moments before council time," said the servant.
- I'm not the one who would risk waking him from his sleep," said Loo, pushing his mouth towards his ear.
- Nor I," said the old woman.
- I'll go myself, if you like," said the messenger; "besides, the hour of the council is near: I've just seen the Prince of Arima heading towards the Hall of the Thousand Nats.
- The Prince of Arima!" exclaimed Loo, "He's always late!
- Alas!" said the maid, "will we have time to dress the master?
Loo slid a partition into its groove and opened a narrow passageway; he then gently entered Nagato's room.
It was cool in the room, and a smell of camphor and musk filled the air.
- Master! Master!" said Loo in a low voice, "it's time, and there's a messenger here.
- A messenger!" exclaimed Nagato, rising on one elbow.
- He's dressed like a samurai
[1]: there are swords at his belt.
- Tell him to come in quickly," said the prince with a tremor in his voice.
Loo waved to the messenger, who bowed at the threshold of the room.
- Come here!" said Nagato.
But the messenger couldn't find his way in this dark room, so Loo folded a sheet of a screen that was blocking the light. A strip of light entered the room; it illuminated the delicate texture of the mat covering the floor and made a silver stork, with its undulating neck and open wings, shine on the wall.
The messenger approached the prince and handed him a thin roll of paper, wrapped in a piece of silk, then left the room backwards.
Nagato quickly unrolled the paper and read the following:
"You have come, illustrious one, I know, but why this madness and why this mystery? I cannot understand your actions. I have been severely reprimanded by my sovereign because of you. You know, I was crossing the gardens to follow her to her palace when I suddenly saw you leaning against a tree. I couldn't hold back a scream, and at this she turned towards me and followed the direction of my gaze.
Ah," she said, "it's the sight of Nagato that's making you cry out like that. Couldn't you at least hold them back and hide the spectacle of your shamelessness from me?
"Then she turned back to you several times. The wrath in her eyes frightened me. I dare not appear before her tomorrow, and I am sending you this message to beg you not to repeat these strange apparitions which have such disastrous consequences for me.
"Alas, don't you know that I love you, and must I tell you that I will be your wife whenever you want me to be? But you enjoy worshipping me like a goddess from the pagoda of the Thirty-Three Thousand Three Hundred and Thirty-Three
[2] . If you hadn't risked your life several times just to catch a glimpse of me, I'd think you were playing me.
I implore you, don't expose me to such reprimands again, and don't forget that I'm ready to acknowledge you as my lord, and that living close to you is my dearest desire."
Nagato smiled and slowly closed the scroll; he fixed his gaze on the light strip that the window threw on the floor and dreamed deeply.
The young Loo was very disappointed; he had tried to read behind his master, but the scroll was written in Chinese characters and his science was at fault; he knew the kata-kana fairly well and even had some knowledge of the hira-kana, but he was unfortunately ignorant of Chinese writing. To hide his disappointment, he approached a window and, lifting a corner of the blind, looked out.
- Ah!" he said, "the Prince of Satsouma and the Prince of Aki are arriving at the same time; the people in their retinue are looking at each other sideways. Ah! Satsouma goes in front. Oh, oh, here's the Regent crossing the avenue; he's looking this way and he laughs to see that the Prince of Nagato's retinue is still outside his door; he'd laugh a lot more if he knew where my master's toilet was.
- Let him laugh, Loo, and come here," said the prince, who had untied a brush and a roll of paper from his belt and was hastily writing a few words. Run to the king and give him this paper.
Loo ran off at full speed, jostling and knocking over anyone in his path.
- And now," said Ivakoura, "dress me quickly!
The servants hurried off, and the prince was soon dressed in the large, shaggy trousers that make the wearer look like he's walking on his knees, and the stiff ceremonial cloak, made even heavier by the insignia embroidered on the sleeves. Nagato's insignia consisted of a black line above three balls, forming a pyramid.
The young man, usually so careful with his finery, paid no attention to the work of the servants, not even glancing at the mirror, so well polished by Loo, when the high pointed cap, tied with golden ribbons, was placed on his head.
As soon as he had finished dressing, he left the palace, but his preoccupation was so great that instead of getting into the norimono, which was waiting for him in the midst of his escort, he walked away, dragging his huge trousers across the sand and exposing himself to the sun's rays. The procession, appalled by this outrage against etiquette, followed him in inexpressible disorder, while the spies, charged with monitoring the prince's actions, hastened to report to their various masters on this extraordinary event.
The ramparts of the Osaka residence, wide and high walls flanked by a semicircular bastion, form an immense square enclosing several palaces and vast gardens. To the south and west, the fortress backs onto the city; to the north, the river that flows through Osaka widens and forms a colossal moat at the foot of the rampart; to the east, a narrower river borders it. On the median of the walls, you can see a row of hundred-year-old cedars, with their dark greenery projecting their flat, horizontal branches over the battlements. Inside, a second wall, preceded by a moat, encloses the parks and palaces reserved for the princes and their families.
Between this wall and the ramparts are the dwellings of the civil servants and soldiers. A third wall surrounds the siogun's palace itself, which stands on a hill. This building was built in a simple and noble architectural style. Square towers with several roofs tower above it here and there. Marble staircases, bordered by a light lacquered balustrade and flanked at the base by two bronze monsters or two large earthenware vases, lead up to the outside galleries; the terrace in front of the palace is covered with gravel and white sand that reflects the sun's rays.
At the centre of the building stands a square tower, wide, very high and magnificently decorated. It supports seven roofs, the corners of which curve towards the sky; on the highest roof are two monstrous golden fish, which shine brightly and can be seen from all parts of the city.
In the part of the palace next to this tower is the Salle des Mille-Nattes, where the council meets.
The lords arrive from all sides; they climb the ramps of the hill and head towards the central portico of the palace, which opens onto a long gallery leading directly to the Hall of the Thousand Nats.
This vast, very high room is completely devoid of furniture. Mobile partitions, sliding in grooves, intersect it and form compartments of various sizes when they are brought together. But the partitions are always set wide apart to produce a pleasing effect of perspective. In one compartment, the panels are covered in black lacquer with gold flowers, in another in red lacquer or Jeseri wood, the veins of which naturally form pleasing patterns. Here, the wall, painted by an illustrious artist, has its reverse side stretched out in white satin embroidered with heavy flowers; elsewhere, against a background of dull gold, a peach tree, covered in pink flowers, stretches out its gnarled branches, or simply, on dark wood, an uneven pattern of white, red and black dots flickers in the eyes. The mats covering the floor are the colour of snow and fringed with silver.
The lords, with their wide trousers reaching beyond their feet, seem to be kneeling down, and the cloth ruffles the mats with a continuous noise, like the distant murmur of a waterfall. Those present remain religiously silent.
The hattamotos, people of recent nobility instituted by the regent, crouched in the furthest corners, while the samurai of ancient nobility, owners of fiefs and vassals of the princes, passed by these newly ennobled people, glancing at them with contempt, and moved closer to the large blind that had been lowered to veil the platform reserved for the siogoun. The Lords of the Land, sovereign princes in their own provinces, form a large circle in front of the throne, leaving a clear space for the thirteen members of the council.
The councillors soon arrive; they greet each other and exchange a few words in a low voice, then take their places.
On the left, with their profiles to the lowered blinds, line up the senior councillors. There are five of them, but only four are present. The closest to the throne is the Prince of Satsouma, a venerable old man with a long, kind face. Next to him lies the absent man's mat. Then comes the Prince of Sataké, nibbling his lips as he carefully arranges the folds of his toilet. He is young, dark-skinned; his eyes, very black, are extraordinarily lively. Next to him sits the Prince of Ouésougui, a slightly fat and nonchalant man. The last is the Prince of Isida, short of stature and ugly of face.
The eight lower councillors, crouching in front of the throne, are the princes of Arima, Figo, Vakasa, Aki, Tosa, Issé and Couroda.
There is a movement towards the entrance and all the foreheads bow to the ground. The regent enters the room. He moves forward quickly, unencumbered like the princes by the folds of his trousers, and sits cross-legged on a pile of mats to the right of the throne.
At the time, Hieyas was an old man. His waist arched slightly; he was broad-shouldered, however, and muscular. His half-shaven head showed a broad forehead, dented by prominent superciliary arches. His thin mouth, with its cruel, wilful expression, lowered its deeply sunken corners; his cheekbones were extremely prominent, and his slanting eyes, level with his head, glared sharply and frankly.
As he entered, he cast an evil glance, accompanied by a half-smile, towards the seat left empty by the Prince of Nagato.
But when the blind was raised, the siogun appeared, leaning with one hand on the young adviser's shoulder.
The regent frowned.
All those present prostrated themselves, resting their foreheads on the ground. When they stood up again, the Prince of Nagato was in his place like the others.
Fidé-Yori sat down and signalled to Hiéyas that he could speak.
The regent then read out a number of unimportant reports: magistrate appointments, troop movements on the border, and the change of residence of a governor after his reign had expired.
Hiéyas briefly and volubly explained the reasons for his actions. The councillors looked over the manuscripts with their eyes and, having no objections to make, nodded in agreement. But soon the regent folded up all the papers and handed them to a secretary standing next to him; then he spoke again after coughing.
- I have called this extraordinary assembly today," he said, "in order to inform it of the fears I have for the tranquillity of the kingdom on learning that the strict surveillance ordered against the monks of Europe and the Japanese who have embraced foreign doctrine is being singularly relaxed, and that they are resuming their activities, which are dangerous for public safety. I have therefore come to ask that the law ordering the extermination of all Christians be reinstated.
A strange hubbub arose in the assembly, a mixture of approval, surprise, cries of horror and anger.
- Do you want to see a return to the bloody and hideous scenes whose horror is in everyone's memories?" exclaimed the Prince of Sataké with his usual vivacity.
- It's strange to say that poor people who preach nothing but virtue and harmony can disturb the peace of a country," said Nagato.
- The daimio speaks well," said the Prince of Satsouma; "it is impossible for the monks of Europe to have any influence on the tranquillity of the kingdom. So there's no point in worrying them.
But Hiéyas spoke directly to Fidé-Yori.
- Master," he said, "since they don't want to share my concerns, I must tell you that a terrible rumour is beginning to circulate among the nobles and the people...
He was silent for a moment to give more solemnity to his words.
-... It is said that he who is still under my tutelage, that the future ruler of Japan, our gracious lord Fide-Yori, has embraced the Christian faith.
A great silence followed these words. Those present exchanged glances which clearly showed that they were aware of this rumour, which was perhaps well-founded.
Fidé-Yori spoke up.
- Is it necessary to take revenge on innocent people for a slander spread by ill-intentioned people?
I order that the Christians should not be troubled in any way.
My father, I regret to say, thought he had to pursue his anger and exterminate these unfortunate people, but I swear, as long as I live, not a single drop of their blood will be shed.
Hiéyas was stunned by the young Siogoun's resolute accent. For the first time he had spoken like a master and given orders. He bowed in submission and said nothing. Fidé-Yori had reached his majority, and if he had not yet been proclaimed siogun, it was because Hiéyas was in no hurry to deposit his powers.
The latter therefore did not want to enter into an open battle with his ward; he temporarily abandoned the question and moved on to other matters.
- I have been told," he said, "that a lord was attacked and wounded last night on the road to Kioto. I don't yet know the name of this lord; but perhaps the Prince of Nagato, who was in Kioto last night, has heard of this adventure?
- Ah, you know I was in Kioto," murmured the prince, "so I understand why there were murderers on my route.
- How could Nagato have been in Osaka and Kioto at the same time?" said the Prince of Sataké. "This morning there is nothing but talk of the water festival he gave there last night, which ended so joyously with a battle between the lords and sailors of the shores.
- I even caught a scratch in the melee," said Nagato with a smile.
- The prince crosses roads in a few hours that others would take a day to cover," says Hiéyas, "that's all.
The only thing is, he doesn't spare his horses: every time he goes back to the palace, his mount falls over and dies. The Prince of Nagato turned pale and looked for the sword missing from his belt.
- I didn't think your concern extended to the beasts of the kingdom," he said with outrageous irony. I thank you in the name of my dead horses.
The siogun, full of concern, cast pleading glances at Nagato. But it seemed that on this particular day, the regent's patience was tried and tested. He smiled and said nothing.
However, Fide-Yori could see that anger was rumbling in his friend's soul and, fearing some new outburst, he ended the session by withdrawing.
Almost immediately a palace guard came to warn the Prince of Nagato that the Siogun was asking for him. The prince greeted several lords in a friendly manner, bowed to the others and left without turning his head towards Hiéyas.
When he reached the siogun's flats, he heard a woman's voice, irritated and moaning at the same time.
It was about him.
- I have heard it all," said the voice: "your refusal to accede to the wishes of the regent, whom you allowed to be insulted before your very eyes by the prince of Nagato, whose insolence is truly incomparable; and the marvellous patience of Hiéyas, who did not take up the insult out of consideration for you, out of pity for the man you believe to be your friend, in your ignorance of men.
Nagato recognised that the speaker was the siogun's mother, the beautiful and imperious Yodogimi.
- Mother," said the siogun, "look after embroidery and ornaments; that's a woman's domain.
Nagato entered quickly, so as not to pry any longer.
Yodogimi turned round and blushed slightly as she saw the prince bowing deeply to her.
- I need to talk to you," said the siogun.
- I'll be off then," said Yodogimi bitterly, "and back to my embroidery.
She crossed the room slowly, rustling her long silky dresses, and left, giving Nagato a strange look that was both provocative and hateful.
- Did you hear my mother?" said Fidé-Yori.
- Yes," said Nagato.
- Everyone wants to take me away from you, friend; what could be their motive?
- Your mother is blinded by some calumny," said the prince; "the others see in me a clear-sighted enemy who knows how to foil the plots hatched against you.
- I wanted to talk to you about a plot.
- Against your life?
- That's exactly it. It was revealed to me in a peculiar way, and I find it hard to believe. However, I can't help worrying. Tomorrow, at the celebration of the Genius of the Sea, a bridge is due to collapse beneath my feet.
- How horrible!" exclaimed Nagato. At least don't go to that festival.
- If I don't go," said Fidé-Yori, "I'll never know the truth, because the plot won't come to light. But if I go to the party," he continued, smiling, "if the conspiracy really exists, the truth will be a bit hard to see.
- Of course," said Nagato, "but you have to get beyond the doubt, you have to find a way. Have you decided on the route you're going to take?
- Hiéyas gave it to me.
Fide-Yori took a roll of paper from the shelf. They read
"Quai du Yodo-Gava, Place du Marché-aux-Poissons, Route des Sycomores, Plage de la Mer. Return via Bamboo Hill and the Swallow Bridge...".
- The wretches!" exclaimed Ivakoura, "it's the suspension bridge over the valley!
- The place would indeed be well chosen," said the siogun.
- There's no doubt that it's this bridge; those that cross the innumerable canals of the city wouldn't expose you to death by collapsing under your feet, but at most to an unpleasant bath.
- It's true," says Fidé-Yori, "and from the Swallow Bridge you would be thrown onto the rocks.
- Do you have full confidence in my friendship for you?" said the Prince of Nagato after thinking for a moment.
- Do you doubt it, Ivakoura?" said the siogun.
- Well, don't be afraid, pretend you don't know anything, let yourself be led and walk straight to the bridge. I've found a way to save you while discovering the truth.
- I rely on you, my friend, in complete safety.
- So let me go, I'm running out of time to carry out my plan.
- Go, Prince, I entrust my life to you without trembling," said the Siogun.
Nagato left quickly after greeting the king, who responded with a friendly gesture.
III
THE FESTIVAL OF THE GENIUS OF THE SEA
The next day, from dawn, the streets of Osaka were full of movement and joy. People were preparing for the festival and looking forward to the pleasure to come. Shopkeepers', craftsmen's and common people's houses, wide open to the street, showed off their simple interiors, furnished only by a few colourful screens.
Voices and laughter could be heard, and from time to time a mischievous child would escape from the arms of his mother, who was busy dressing him in her best clothes, and come running and stamping with joy up the wooden steps leading down from the house to the pavement.
It was then with cries of feigned anger that he would be called back from inside, the father's voice would be heard and the child would go back into his mother's hands, quivering with impatience.
Sometimes one of them would cry out:
-Mother! Mother! Here comes the procession!
- You're joking," said the mother, "the priests haven't even finished dressing.
But nevertheless, she advanced towards the façade and, perched over the light balustrade, looked out into the street.
Naked couriers, minus a piece of cloth tied around their loins, run past with a bamboo stalk over their shoulder, bending at the end under the weight of a bundle of letters. They are heading for the siogoun's residence.
In front of the barber's shops, the crowd gathers; the barbers can't shave all the chins and style all the heads that come in. Those waiting their turn chat cheerfully outside the door. Some are already dressed in their brightly coloured, embroidered festive clothes.
Others are more careful, naked to the waist, preferring to finish their grooming once their hair is done. Vegetable and fish merchants walk around, loudly boasting about their wares, which they carry in two tubs suspended from a wooden crossbeam on their shoulder.
Everywhere the houses are adorned with banners, embroidered cloth, covered with Chinese inscriptions in gold on black or purple backgrounds; lanterns and flowering branches are hung.
As the morning progressed, the streets filled with more and more cheerful tumult; the norimono bearers, dressed in light tunics, tight around the waist, wearing large hats like shields, shouted to make room for each other. Samurai pass by on horseback, preceded by a runner who, head bowed, arms outstretched, splits the crowd. Groups stop to chat, sheltered under vast parasols, forming motionless islands amid the tumultuous swell of strollers. A doctor hurried in, fanning himself with gravity, followed by his two assistants carrying the medicine chest.
- Illustrious master, aren't you going to the festival?
- Sick people don't care about festivals," he said with a sigh, "and as there are none for them, there are none for us.
On the banks of the Yodogava, the bustle is even greater; the river literally disappears under thousands of boats; the masts raised, the sails still bent but ready to open like wings, the tents of the cabins covered with silk and satin fabrics, the prows adorned with banners whose gold fringes dip into the water, shine in the sun and stain the azure of the river with multicoloured shivers.
Bands of young women in shining gowns make their way down the white, stepped banks.
They head for elegant camphor-wood boats, embellished with carvings and copper ornaments, and fill them with flowers that waft warm perfumes into the air.
From the top of the Kiobassi, that beautiful bridge that looks like a stretched arch, they unfurl pieces of gauze, crepe or light silk in the freshest colours and covered with inscriptions. A light breeze gently stirs these beautiful fabrics as the boats come and go, pushing them aside as they pass. In the distance, the high tower of the residence and the two monstrous golden fish that adorn its peak shine brightly. At the entrance to the city, to the right and left of the river, the two superb bastions facing the sea have on each tower, at each corner of the walls, the white national standard with a red disc, emblem of the sun as it rises in the morning vapours. A few pagodas, high above the trees, raise their roofs one above the other against the radiant sky, their edges raised in the Chinese fashion.
It is the pagoda of Yébis, the genius of the sea, that attracts particular attention on this day; not that its towers are higher and its sacred doors more numerous than those of the neighbouring temples, but from its gardens the religious procession, so impatiently awaited by the crowd, is due to depart.
At last, in the distance, the drum resounds. We listen to the sacred rhythm, well known to all: a few violent blows, spaced out, then a hurried roll, softening and fading, then sudden blows again.
A huge cheer goes up from the crowd, who immediately line up along the houses on either side of the streets the procession is to follow.
The Kashiras, the guardians of the neighbourhoods, quickly stretched out ropes and attached them to stakes to prevent the crowd from overflowing onto the central thoroughfare. The procession set off; indeed, it crossed the Torié, the sacred portico, which rises in front of the Yébis pagoda, and soon it was denying before the impatient crowd.
Sixteen archers first came forward, one behind the other, in two widely spaced rows. They were dressed in armour made of strips of black horn joined by dots of red wool. Two swords are strapped to their belts; poisoned arrows extend beyond their shoulders and they hold in their hands a large bow of black and gold lacquer.
Behind them comes a troop of servants, wearing silk tassels at the end of long poles. Then the Tartar musicians appear, announcing themselves with a cheerful din. The metallic vibrations of the gong resound from moment to moment, the drums, beaten to excess, the shuddering cymbals, the low-pitched tones of the sea conches, the high-pitched notes of the flutes and the air-shattering blare of the trumpets, creating such an intensity of noise that the nearest spectators blink and are as if blinded.
After the musicians, a gigantic lobster appears on a high platform, ridden by a monk. Standards of all colours, long and narrow, bearing the town's coat of arms, are held by young boys and sway around the enormous crustacean. Then fifty lancers, wearing round lacquered hats, lean their spears, adorned with red tassels, on their shoulders. Two servants then lead a superbly caparisoned horse, whose mane, raised above the collar, is plaited and arranged like a rich trimming. Banner-bearers come forward after this horse; the banners are blue and covered with gold lettering. Then came two large Korean tigers, their mouths open and their eyes bloody.
A few children in the crowd shouted in fright, but the tigers were made of cardboard, and men hidden in each of their paws made them move. A giant cylindrical drum comes next, carried by two monks; a third walks alongside and frequently strikes the drum with his closed fist.
Finally, there are seven young women, splendidly adorned and greeted by a joyful hubbub. They are the most beautiful and illustrious courtesans in the city. They come forward, one after the other, majestically, full of pride, accompanied by a maid and followed by a servant who holds a large silk parasol over them. The people, who know them well, refer to them by name or nickname.
- Here's the woman with the silver teal!
Two of these birds are embroidered on the wide-sleeved cloak she wears over her many dresses, the collars of which are crossed, one below the other, on her chest; the cloak is of green satin, the embroidery of white silk, mixed with silver; the beautiful woman's headdress is pierced by enormous tortoise-shell pins, which form a semi-circle of rays around her face.
- This is the seaweed woman!
These beautiful grasses, with their silken roots embedded in the embroidery of the coat, float out of the fabric and flutter in the wind.
Then come the beautiful girl with the golden dolphin, the beautiful girl with the almond blossom, the beautiful girl with the swan, the peacock and the blue monkey. Their heads are bristling with blonde pins and their faces, skilfully disguised, appear young and charming under the soft shade of the parasol.
Behind the courtesans come men carrying willow branches; then a whole army of priests, carrying on stretchers or under pretty pavilions with gilded roofs, the accessories, ornaments and furniture of the temple, which are being purified as the procession moves along.
Finally appears the shrine of Yébis, the god of the sea, the tireless fisherman, who spends whole days, wrapped in a net, line in hand, standing on a rock half-emerging from the water. It is carried by fifty monks, naked to the waist, and looks like a square house. Its four-sided roof is clad in silver and azure, bordered by a fringe of pearls and topped by a large bird with open wings.
The god Yébis is invisible inside the hermetically sealed shrine.
On a stretcher is carried the magnificent fish consecrated to Yébis, the akame, or red woman, the favourite, moreover, of all those who love good food. Thirty horsemen armed with pikes complete the procession.
The procession makes its way through the town, followed by the crowds moving behind it; it reaches the suburbs and, after a fairly long march, reaches the seashore.
At the same time, thousands of boats arrived at the mouth of the Yodogava, which gently pushed them towards the sea. The sails open, the oars bite the water, the banners flutter in the wind, while the sun casts thousands of sparks on the azure of the stirring waves.
Fidé-Yori also arrived on the beach, along the path that runs alongside the river; he stopped his horse and stood motionless in the middle of his retinue, which was fairly small in number, as the regent had not wanted to overwhelm the religious procession with royal luxury.
As for Hiéyas, he was carried in a norimono like the mother and wife of the siogoun. He said he was ill.
Fifty soldiers, a few standard bearers and two runners formed the entire escort.
The arrival of the young prince divided the attention of the crowd, and Yébis' procession was no longer the only one to attract attention. The royal headdress, a sort of oblong golden cap, placed on Fidé-Yori's head, made him recognisable from afar.
Soon the religious procession slowly marches past the siogoun. Then the priests carrying the shrine leave the line and approach the sea.
Then the fishermen and boatmen from the shore suddenly come running, shouting, jumping and leaping, and pounce on those carrying Yébis. They simulate a battle, shouting louder and louder. The priests pretended to defend themselves, but soon the chàsse passed from their shoulders to those of the sturdy sailors. With howls of joy, the sailors entered the sea and waved their beloved god over the clear waves for a long time, while the orchestras, carried by the junks criss-crossing the sea, blasted out their joyful melodies. Finally, the sailors return to shore to the cheers of the crowd, which soon dissipates and hurries back to the city, where there is much more to do: open-air shows, sales of all kinds, theatrical performances, banquets and sake celebrations.
Fidé-Yori leaves the beach in his turn, preceded by the two runners and followed by his retinue. We enter a small, cool and charming valley, and take the path that leads to the top of the hill on a gentle slope. This path is completely deserted. In fact, since the day before, people have been forbidden to enter.
Fidé-Yori thought about the plot, about the bridge that would collapse and plunge him into an abyss. He had been thinking about it anxiously all night; but under this bright sun, in the midst of this peaceful nature, he could no longer believe in human wickedness.
However, the route he chose to return to the palace was peculiar.
"We'll take this route to avoid the crowds," said Hiéyas; but all he had to do was forbid the people to take another route, and the king could have returned to the castle without making this bizarre diversions.
Fidé-Yori looked around for Nagato. He couldn't find him.
He had asked for him twenty times since the morning. The prince was nowhere to be found.
A painful anguish overcomes the young Siogun. He suddenly wonders why his procession is so small, why there are only two runners ahead of him. He looks behind him and it seems to him that the norimono bearers are slowing down.
They reached the crest of the hill, and soon the Swallow Bridge appeared at the end of the path. On seeing it, Fidé-
Yori, with an involuntary movement, held back her horse; a precipitous beat agitated her heart. This frail bridge is boldly thrown from one hill to the other over the very deep valley. The river, swift as a torrent, leaps over the rocks with a continuous thud. However, the bridge, as usual, seems to rest firmly on the flat rocks jutting out beneath it.
The runners move forward at a steady pace. If the plot exists, they know nothing about it. The young king doesn't dare stop; he thinks he can still hear Nagato's words:
"Walk to the bridge without fear".
But Omiti's pleading voice also vibrates in his ear, and he remembers the oath he took. Nagato's silence above all frightens him. How many things could have stood in the way of the Prince's plan! Surrounded by clever spies watching his every move, he may have been kidnapped and prevented from corresponding with the king. All these thoughts tumultuously filled Fidé-Yori's mind; the last supposition made him turn pale; then, by one of those quirks of thought, frequent in extreme situations, he suddenly remembered a song he had sung as a child to familiarise himself with the main sounds of the Japanese language.
He recites it mechanically:
"Colour and fragrance vanish. What is there in this permanent world? The past day has sunk into the abyss of nothingness. It was like the reflection of a dream. - Its absence caused not the slightest disturbance."
- This is what I learned as a child," said the king to himself, "and today I recoil and hesitate before the possibility of dying.
Ashamed of his weakness, he gave the reins back to his horse.
But then a great noise was heard on the other side of the bridge and, suddenly turning the corner of the road, horses, their manes scattered and their eyes bloody, appeared, dragging after them a cart loaded with tree trunks; they rushed towards the bridge and their furious hooves sounded, with a redoubling of noise, on the wooden floor.
At the sight of these horses coming towards her, the whole of Fidé-Yori's retinue shrieked in horror; the porters abandoned the norimonos, the women got out terrified, and gathering their ample dresses, fled in all haste. The runners, who had already set foot on the bridge, turned round and Fidé-Yori instinctively threw herself aside.
But all of a sudden, like a rope that has been stretched too far that breaks, the bridge burst with a loud crash; first it bent in the middle, then it suddenly raised its two sections, sending a shower of debris everywhere. The carriage and the cart plunged into the river, the foamy water gushing up the hill. For a few moments, a horse hung by its harness, struggling above the abyss, but the ties broke and it fell. The tumultuous river began to push the horses, floating tree trunks and debris from the bridge into the sea.
- O Omiti!" cried the king, motionless with fear, "you did not deceive me! So this is the fate that was in store for me. Without your devotion, sweet young lady, my broken body would be rolling from rock to rock by now.
- Well master, you know what you wanted to know. What do you think of my carriage?" suddenly exclaimed a voice close to the king.
The king turned round; he was alone, all his servants had deserted him, but he saw a head emerge from the valley; he recognised Nagato, who was climbing rapidly up the rugged hill, and was soon close to the king.
- Ah, my friend, my brother!" said Fidé-Yori, who could not hold back his tears. How could I have inspired so much hatred? Who is the wretch who is oppressed by my life and wants to drive me out of the world?
- You want to know who this villain is, you want to know the name of the culprit?
- Do you know, friend? Tell me.
- Hieyas!
IV
THE SISTER OF THE SUN
It was the hottest hour of the day. All the rooms in the palace of Kioto are plunged into a cool darkness, thanks to the blinds drawn and the screens deployed in front of the windows.
Kioto is the capital, the sacred city, the residence of a god exiled on earth, the direct descendant of the celestial founders of Japan, the absolute sovereign, the pontiff of all the religions practised in the kingdom of the rising sun, the mikado at last. The siogun is only the first among the mikado's subjects; but the mikado, overwhelmed by his own majesty, blinded by his superhuman splendour, leaves earthly affairs to the siogun, who reigns in his place, while he absorbs himself in a solitary sense of his own sublimity.
In the middle of the palace grounds, in one of the pavilions reserved for the lords of the court, a woman is lying on the floor covered with fine braids. She raises herself on one elbow and plunges her tiny fingers into the black waves of her hair. Not far from her, a maid crouches on the floor, playing with a pretty dog of a precious breed that looks like two tussocks of black and white silk. A gotto, a thirteen-stringed musical instrument, a writing case, a roll of paper, a fan and a box full of sweets are scattered across the floor, unmasked by any furniture. The walls are lined with cedar wood, either cut to size or covered in brilliant paintings enhanced with gold and silver; half-drawn panels form openings through which you can see other rooms and, further on, still others.
- Mistress, you're sad," says the next woman. Would you like me to pluck the strings of the gotto and sing you a song to soothe you?
The mistress shook her head.
- What's the matter? Fatkoura doesn't like music any more?
Has she forgotten that she owes it to her to see the light of day?
When the Sun Goddess, angered by the gods, withdrew to a cave, it was by letting her hear the divine music for the first time that she was brought back to heaven!
Fatkoura sighed and said nothing.
- Would you like me to grind some ink for you? For a long time now, your paper has remained as intact as the snow on Mount Fousi. If you have a problem, throw it into the worm mould and you'll be rid of it.
- No, Tika, you can't be delivered from love, it's a very burning evil that bites you day and night and never puts you to sleep.
- Unhappy love perhaps, but you are loved, mistress! said Tika, coming closer.
- I don't know what snake hidden deep in my heart is telling me that I'm not.
- How!" said a surprised Tika, "hasn't he, by a thousand follies, revealed his deep passion? Hasn't he come these last few days, risking his life, because the wrath of the Kisaki could be fatal to him, to catch a glimpse of you for a moment?
- Yes, and he ran off without saying a word to me, Tika! added Fatkoura, nervously grabbing the girl's wrists. He didn't even look at me.
- That's impossible!" said Tika, "didn't he tell you that he loved you?
- He told me so, and I believed him, so much I wanted to believe him; but now I don't believe him any more.
- Why not?
- Because if he loved me, he would have married me long ago and taken me to his States.
- But his affection for his master keeps him at the Osaka court!
- That's what he says, but is that how love speaks? What would I not sacrifice for him! ... alas! I thirst for his presence!
His face, so haughty, yet so gentle, passes before my eyes; I want to stare at it, but it slips away. Ah, a few happy months spent close to him, and then I would kill myself, falling asleep in my love, and past happiness would be a soft shroud for me.
Fatkoura burst into tears and threw her face into her hands.
Tika tried to console her, wrapped her arms around her and spoke a thousand sweet words, but she was unable to soothe her.
Suddenly, a noise was heard at the back of the flat.
The little dog began to bark in a high-pitched tone.
Tika got up quickly and leapt out of the room to prevent any servants from entering and seeing her mistress's excitement.
- It's him! It's him!" she cried; "he's here, he wants to see you.
- Don't be silly, Tika," said Fatkoura, rising to her feet.
- Here's his ticket.
And she held out a piece of paper to Fatkoura, who read it at a glance: "Ivakoura Terumoto Mori, Prince of Nagato, requests the honour of being admitted to your presence.
- My mirror!" she cried in panic. I look horrible like this, with swollen eyes, messy hair and a dress without embroidery. Alas! instead of moaning, I should have foreseen his coming and taken care of my grooming since dawn!
Tika had brought the polished metal mirror, as round as the full moon, and the box of make-up and perfumes.
Fatkoura took a brush and lengthened her eyes, but her hand was trembling; she pressed too hard, then, wanting to repair the damage, she only succeeded in smearing her whole cheek with black. She clenched her fists in rage and gritted her teeth. Tika came to her rescue and removed the traces of her clumsiness: she put a little green blush on her lower lip, which turned pink on contact with the skin. To replace the carefully plucked eyebrows, she made two large black patches high on her forehead, designed to make the oval of her face appear more elongated. She spread a little pink powder on her cheekbones, then hurriedly removed all the toiletries and threw a magnificent kirimon over her mistress's shoulders.
Then she ran out of the room.
Fatkoura stood trembling beside the fallen gotto, holding her ornate cloak in one hand and gazing eagerly towards the entrance to the room.
Finally, Nagato appeared. He stepped forward, resting one hand on the golden hilt of one of his two swords, and bowed with noble grace:
- Forgive me," he said, "beautiful Fatkoura, for arriving here like a storm in the sky unheralded by a few precursory clouds.
- You are to me like the sun when it rises over the sea," says Fatkoura, "and you are always expected. Here, I was busy crying because of you. Look, my eyes are still red.
- Your eyes are like the evening star and the morning star," said the prince. But why do they drown their rays in tears? Have I caused you some pain?
- You are here, and I have forgotten the cause of my sorrow," said Fatkoura, smiling; "perhaps I was crying because you were far from me.
- I wish I were still here," exclaimed Nagato, with such an accent of truth that the young woman felt her fears vanish and a flash of happiness light up her face.
Perhaps, however, she had misunderstood the meaning of the prince's words.
- Come and rest on these mats next to me," she said, "and Tika will serve us tea and sweets.
Couldn't I first send the Kisaki a secret request of the utmost importance? said Nagato. I took the pretext of this precious missive to get away from Osaka," he added, seeing a shadow on Fatkoura's forehead.
- The sovereign has been strict with me since your last appearance; I wouldn't dare go near her or send any of my servants to her palace.
- However, this document must be in her hands as soon as possible," said Nagato, with an imperceptible frown.
- What should I do?" said Fatkoura, not unaware of this slight sign of discontent. Would you like to follow me to one of my illustrious friends, the noble Iza-Farou-No-Kami? She is in favour at the moment, perhaps she can be of use to us.
- Let's go to her without further ado," said the prince.
- Let's go," said Fatkoura with a sigh.
The young woman called Tika, who was standing in the next room, and motioned to her to pull open a panel that opened onto a gallery running along the outside of the pavilion.
- You're going out, mistress", said Tika, "should we tell your suite?
- We're going out incognito, Tika, for a walk in the orchard; in reality," she added, putting a finger to her lips, "we're on our way to see the noble Iza-Farou.
The next woman bowed her head in agreement.
Fatkoura bravely set foot on the gallery, but she drew back quickly with a shout.
- It's a furnace! she cried.
Nagato picked up the fan he'd left on the ground.
- Courage," he said, "I'll cool the air next to your face.
Tika took a parasol and opened it over her mistress's forehead, while Nagato waved the large fan. They set off, sheltered at first by the overhanging roof. Fatkoura walked first, touching the exposed cedar balustrade with her fingertips from time to time and squealing as she touched it. The pretty, silky-haired dog, who had felt obliged to join the strollers, followed at a distance, no doubt grumbling some observations about the insanity of a walk at such an hour.
They turned the corner of the house and found themselves on the front landing of a wide staircase leading down to the garden, between two banisters adorned with brass balls; a third banister, placed in the centre of the staircase, separated it in two.
Despite the unbearable heat and the bright light reflecting off the ground, Fatkoura and the Prince of Nagato pretended to be strolling along with no other aim than to pick a few flowers and admire the charming views that opened up at every step. Although the gardens seemed deserted, they knew that the spy's eye never closed. They hurried along a shady path and soon reached a group of sumptuous pavilions, scattered among the trees and linked by covered galleries.
- This is it," said Fatkoura, who, far from looking towards the houses she was pointing at, had bent down towards a small pond full of water so pure it was almost invisible.
- Look at this pretty fish," she said, raising her voice deliberately, "it looks like it's been carved from a piece of amber. And this one, which looks like a ruby powdered with gold; it seems to be suspended in mid-air, so transparent is the water. Look, its fins are like black gauze and its eyes like balls of fire. Decidedly, in all the palace, Iza-Farou has the most beautiful fish.
- I can't believe it! Fatkoura!" cried a woman's voice from inside a pavilion, "are you out at this time of night? Is
Is it because you're a widow that you take so little care of your complexion and are going to let it be devoured by the sun?
A blind was half raised and Iza-Farou stuck out her pretty head bristling with blonde pins.
- Ah!" she said, "the lord of Nagato! You won't pass my house without doing me the honour of entering," she added.
she added.
- We'll be delighted to enter, and we thank chance for bringing us this way," said Fatkoura.
They climbed the stairs to the pavilion and made their way through the flowers that filled the gallery.
Iza-Farou came to meet them.
- What do you have to say to me?" she asked her friend in a low voice, as she greeted the prince graciously.
- I need you," said Fatkoura; "you know I'm in disgrace.
- But can I assure the sovereign that you will never again commit the offence that has so angered her?" said Iza-Farou, casting a mischievous glance at Nagato.
- Fatkoura is not responsible for the actions of a madman like me.
- Prince! I think she's proud to be the cause of what you call madness, and many women are jealous of her.
- Don't mock me," said Nagato, "I've been punished enough for bringing the wrath of the sovereign down on the noble Fatkoura.
- But that's not the point," exclaimed Fatkoura. The lord of Nagato has an important message that he would like to send secretly to the Kisaki; he first approached me; but as I cannot approach the queen at the moment, I thought of your kind friendship.
- Give me this message," said Iza-Farou, turning to the prince; in a few moments it will be in the hands of our illustrious mistress.
- You can see how grateful I am," said Nagato, taking from his chest a white satin envelope containing the missive.
- Wait for me here, I'll be back soon.
Iza-Farou took the letter and ushered her guests into a cool, dark room, where she left them alone.
- These pavilions communicate with the palace of the Kisaki," said Fatkoura, "so my noble friend can visit the sovereign without being seen. May the gods grant that the messenger brings a favourable reply and that I may see the cloud that obscures your forehead disappear.
The prince did indeed appear absorbed and worried; he nibbled on the tip of his fan as he went back and forth in the room.
Fatkoura followed him with her eyes and, in spite of herself, her heart sank; she felt the return of the terrible anguish that had brought tears to her eyes a few moments before, and which the presence of her beloved had suddenly calmed.
presence had suddenly calmed.
- He doesn't love me," she murmured in despair; "when his eyes turn towards me, they frighten me with their icy, almost contemptuous expression.
Nagato seemed to have forgotten the presence of the young woman; he was leaning against a half-drawn panel, and seemed to be savouring a dream that was both sweet and poignant. Iza-Farou returned; she seemed to be hurrying and soon appeared at the bend in the gallery; two young boys, magnificently dressed, followed her.
- These are the words of the divine Kisaki," she said when she was near Nagato: "Let the supplicant himself come and ask for what he wants.
At these words Nagato turned so pale that lza-Farou, frightened and thinking he was going to faint, rushed towards him to prevent him from falling.
- Prince," she cried, "get over yourself; such a favour is indeed capable of causing great emotion, but aren't you used to all the honours?
- It's impossible," Nagato murmured in a voice that was barely distinct, "I can't appear before her.
- How," said Iza-Farou, "do you mean to disobey her order?
- I'm not in court costume," said the prince.
- She is dispensing with the ceremonial this time, as the reception is secret. Don't keep her waiting any longer.
- Let's go, take me with you," suddenly exclaimed Nagato, who seemed to be taming his emotion.
- These two pages will guide you," said Iza-Farou.
Nagato moved off quickly, preceded by the Kisaki's two servants, but he could still hear a muffled cry escaping from Fatkoura's lips.
After walking for some time, passing unseen through the galleries and halls of the palace, Nagato came to a large curtain of white satin embroidered with gold, whose wide, shiny folds, silver in the light, lead-coloured in the half-light, lay thickly on the floor.
The pages pushed aside the drapery, the prince stepped forward and the shivering waves of satin fell behind him.
The walls of the room he entered glowed dully in the half-light, with flashes of gold, the whiteness of pearls and crimson reflections; an exquisite perfume wafted through the air. At the far end of the room, under the curtains draped with gold cords, the radiant sovereign was seated, surrounded by the silky undulations of her red dresses, the three golden blades, the insignia of omnipotence, rising from her forehead. The prince kissed her with an involuntary glance, then, lowering his eyes as if he had been looking at the midday sun, he walked to the middle of the room and threw himself on his knees, then slowly sank face down on the floor.
- Ivakoura," said the Kisaki, after a long silence, "what you are asking of me is serious: I want some explanations from you before I send your request to the sublime master of the world, the son of the gods, my husband.
The prince half rose to his feet and tried to speak, but he could not; he felt as if his chest would burst with the beating of his heart; the word died on his lips and he remained, his eyes downcast, pale as a dying man.
- Is it because you think I'm angry with you that you're so frightened?" said the queen, after looking at the prince with surprise for a moment. I can forgive you, for your crime is a minor one. You love one of my daughters, that's all.
- No, I don't love her!" exclaimed Nagato, who looked up at his sovereign as if he had lost his mind.
- What do I care!" said the Kisaki in a brief voice.
For a second their eyes met, but Nagato closed his guilty eyes and, shuddering at his audacity, awaited his punishment.
But, after a silence, the Kisaki resumed in a calm voice:
- Your letter reveals a terrible secret to me, and if what you suppose is true, the peace of the kingdom may be deeply disturbed.
- This is why, divine sister of the sun, I have had the audacity to ask for your almighty intercession," said the prince, unable to completely control the tremors in his voice. If you grant my prayer, if I obtain what I ask, great misfortunes can be prevented.
- As you know, Ivakoura, the celestial Mikado is in favour of Hiéyas; will he believe in this crime of which you accuse his protégé, and will you publicly support this accusation, which has been secret until now?
- I will support it in front of Hiéyas himself," said Nagato firmly; "he is the author of the odious plot that almost cost my young master his life.
- This statement will put your life in danger. Have you thought about that?
- My life is nothing," said the prince. Besides, the mere fact of my devotion to Fide-Yori is enough to earn me the hatred of the regent. A few days ago, on leaving Kioto, I was almost murdered by people posted by him.
- What prince! Is that really possible? said the Kisaki.
- I only mention this unimportant fact," Nagato continued, "to show you that crime is familiar to this man and that he wants to get rid of those who stand in the way of his ambition.
- But how did you escape the murderers?" asked the queen, who seemed to be taking a keen interest in this adventure.
- The sharp blade of my sword and the strength of my arm saved my life. But is it possible that you stopped your sublime thought on such a futile incident?
- Were there many assassins?
- Ten or twelve perhaps, I killed a few of them, then launched my horse, which soon put enough distance between them and me.
- What!" said the dreamy Kisaki, "this man who has the confidence of my divine husband is so treacherous and ferocious. I share your fears, Ivakoura, and sad forebodings overwhelm me, but will I be able to persuade the mikado that your predictions are not in vain? I will at least try, for the good of my people and the salvation of the kingdom. Go, Prince, be at the reception this evening; I will have seen the master of the world.
After prostrating himself, the prince got up and, with his forehead bowed to the ground, walked backwards, reaching the satin curtain.
Once again, in spite of his will, he looked up at the sovereign who was watching him. But the curtain fell back and the adorable vision disappeared.
The pages led Ivakoura to one of the palaces reserved for sovereign princes visiting Kioto. Happy to be alone, he stretched out on some cushions and, still moved, plunged into a delightful reverie.
- Ah," he murmured, "what strange joy envelops me! I'm drunk. Perhaps it was breathing the air around him? Ah, terrible madness!
terrible madness, hopeless desire that makes me suffer so gently, how much will you not increase as a result of this unexpected meeting! I used to flee from Osaka, distraught, like a diver running out of air, and I would come here to contemplate the palaces that hide her from view, or sometimes to catch a glimpse of her from afar, leaning against a gallery or crossing a garden path among her wives, and then I would take with me a supply of happiness. But now I have breathed in the perfume that emanates from her, her voice has caressed my ear, I have heard my name vibrate on her lips. Will I be able to be content with what once filled my life? I am lost, my existence shattered by this impossible love, and yet I am happy. Soon I will see her again, no longer constrained by a political audience, but able to dazzle myself with her beauty. Will I have the strength to hide my confusion and my criminal love? Yes, divine sovereign, before you alone my proud soul has been able to prostrate itself and my dream rises towards you like mist towards the sun. Goddess, I adore you with awe and respect; but, alas! I also love you with a mad tenderness as if you were only a woman!
V
THE SKY RIDERS
Night has come. The heat of the day has been replaced by a delightful coolness and the flowers in the flowerbeds, wet with dew, give off their fragrance.
The galleries before the palace halls, where the evening's entertainment takes place, are lit up and crowded with guests, who breathe in the delightful evening air. The Prince of Nagato climbed the Great Staircase, lined on either side by a lively banister of pretty pages, each holding a golden rod at the end of which a round lantern was swinging. The prince crosses the galleries slowly, because of the crowds; he bows when he meets a high dignitary of the court, greets the princes who are his equals with a friendly phrase and approaches the throne room.
The throne room glows with the light of lanterns and lamps. A joyful hubbub fills it and the neighbouring flats, which can be seen through the wide gap in the panels.
The ladies-in-waiting are whispering to each other, their voices mingling with the slight rustle of their dresses, whose abundant folds they are arranging. Seated to the right and left of the royal dais, these princesses form groups; each group has its own hierarchical rank and special colours. In one group the women are dressed in light blue gowns with silver trim, in another in green, lilac or pale yellow gowns.
At the top of the dais, covered with soft carpets, the Kisaki shines amidst the streams of satin, gauze and silver brocade that make up her ample red or white dresses, with gems streaming through them. The three vertical blades on top of her tiara look like three rays of gold above her forehead.
Some of the princesses have climbed the steps to the throne and, kneeling on the highest step, talk cheerfully with the sovereign; she sometimes lets out a light laugh that will scandalise some silent old prince, a faithful guardian of the rules of etiquette. But the sovereign is so young, not yet twenty, that it is easy to forgive her for not always feeling the weight of the crown on her forehead, and when she laughs, joy bursts forth from all sides like the songs of birds at the first rays of the sun.
- Praise the high gods!" said a princess in a low voice to her companions, "the worry that saddened our sovereign has finally dissipated: she is happier this evening than ever.
- Is she in a merciful mood! said another. Fatkoura is back in grace. She climbs the steps to the throne. The Kisaki has sent for her.
Fatkoura was indeed standing on the top step of the royal dais; but the gloomy expression of her features, the fixed, bewildered look in her eyes contrasted sharply with the cheerful, serene expression on everyone's face.
She thanked the Kisaki for returning her favours, but she did so in a voice so mournful and strangely troubled that the young queen shuddered and looked up at her former favourite.
- Are you ill?" she said, surprised by the change in the young woman's features.
- The joy of being forgiven, perhaps," stammered Fatkoura.
- I'll excuse you from staying for the party, if you're suffering.
- Thank you," said Fatkoura, who, after bowing deeply, walked away and lost herself in the crowd.
The sounds of a hidden orchestra soon burst forth and the entertainment began.
A curtain rises on the wall facing the throne, revealing a charming landscape.
Mount Fousi rises in the background, its snow-capped peak visible above a collar of clouds; the deep blue sea, dotted with a few white sails, unfurls at the foot of the mountains; a path undulates in the foreground, between trees and flowering groves.
A young man comes forward, bows his head and looks tired and sad. The orchestra falls silent. The young man raises his voice; he tells how misfortune has pursued him; his mother died of grief when she saw the fields, cultivated by her husband, becoming more and more barren; he followed his mother's coffin weeping, then worked himself to death to support his old father; But the father died in his turn, leaving the son so destitute that he did not have the money to have him buried; so he sold himself into slavery and used the price of his freedom to pay his father his last respects; now he is going to his master's house to fulfil the terms of his contract. He is about to set off when a beautiful woman appears on the road. The young man gazed at her in admiration.
- I want to ask you for a favour," said the woman; "I am alone and abandoned, accept me as your wife, I will be devoted and faithful to you.
- Alas!" said the young man, "I own nothing, and even my body does not belong to me. I have sold myself to a master to whom I am going.
I'm skilled in the art of weaving silk," said the stranger; "take me to your master, I'll be useful.
- I consent with all my heart," said the young man, "but how is it that a woman as beautiful as you are wants to take a poor man like me as her husband?
- Beauty is nothing compared to the qualities of the heart," said the woman.
The second part shows the two spouses working in their master's gardens: the man cultivating flowers, the woman embroidering a wonderful fabric she has woven. The master is walking around, watching the slaves; he approaches the young woman and looks at her work.
- Oh, the splendid fabric!" he exclaimed, "it's priceless.
- I wanted to give it to you in exchange for our freedom.
The master agreed to the deal and let them go.
The husband threw himself at his wife's feet and thanked her effusively for having freed him from slavery. But the woman is transformed: she becomes so brilliant that the young man, dazzled, can no longer look at her.
- Your courage at work and your filial piety have touched me, and seeing you unhappy, I have come down from heaven to help you. Everything you undertake from now on will succeed if you never leave the path of virtue.
Having said this, the divine weaver ascended to heaven and resumed her place in the house of the silkworms.
[1] .
The orchestra plays a dance tune. The curtain lowers and soon rises again. It reveals the garden of a pagoda with its bamboo groves and light buildings with vast roofs supported by a tangle of beams of all colours.
Pantomime scenes follow one another without being linked. Religious or warlike legends are staged; fabulous heroes and symbolic characters appear in the costumes of ancient times, some wearing the egg-shaped mitre, others the tunic with long open sleeves, others the ancient helmet without crest, with its gold ornaments protecting the nape of the neck, or a fantastic headdress, wide and high, in the shape of a golden pyramid and all trimmed with fringes and bells; These figures hold branches, swords, mirrors and all sorts of emblematic objects. But often the meaning of the symbol is forgotten, no one understands it any more; it has passed through the ages without losing any of its appearance, but it is like a locked chest whose key has been lost.
Here is the hero who killed a terrible dragon, who lived for many years in the very palace of the Mikados.
Here is Zangou, the royal warrior, who conquered Korea; Yatzizoné the invincible, whose shield is his fan; the illustrious prince who became blind from mourning the death of his beloved, all passing by representing the main event of their lives.
Then the stage empties and, after a prelude from the orchestra, young, charming dancers appear, dressed in resplendent costumes, with bird or butterfly wings on their shoulders and long antennae on their foreheads, trembling gently above their gold crowns, which are cut to size; They perform a slow, limp dance, full of undulations and swaying motions, and when they have finished, they form groups on either side of the stage, while comic dancers with false noses and extravagant costumes make their entrance and end the show with a frenzied dance full of blows and falls. 
Since the performance began, the Prince of Nagato had been leaning against a wall near the theatre and, half-hidden in the folds of a drapery, while all eyes were fixed on the stage, he gazed madly at the sovereign, smiling and radiant.
The queen seemed to feel the weight of this ardent and tenacious gaze upon her, for once she turned her head and locked eyes with the prince.
He did not lower his eyelids, an all-powerful charm prevented him from doing so: that gaze, coming down on him like a ray of sunlight, burned him. For a moment he thought he was mad; it seemed to him that the Kisaki was imperceptibly smiling at him. She immediately lowered her eyes and looked attentively at the flowers embroidered on her sleeve, then, raising her head, she seemed to be following the performance attentively.
When the curtain came down for the last time, amidst the hubbub of conversation, which resumed after a long silence, when agitation succeeded immobility, a woman stopped in front of Nagato.
- I know your secret, Prince," she told him in a low but threatening voice.
- What do you mean?" exclaimed Nagato. I don't understand you, Fatkoura.
- You understand me very well," continued Fatkoura, looking at him fixedly, "and you are right to turn pale, for your life is in my hands.
- My life," murmured the prince, "I will bless him who delivers me from it.
