The young woman had moved away, but there was a great movement on the queen's side, all the ladies-in-waiting had risen and silence was restored in the audience.
The Kisaki was descending the steps of her throne. She walked slowly into the hall, dragging streams of satin behind her.
The princesses, in groups according to their rank, followed her at a distance, stopping when she did. All those present bowed deeply as she passed; she said a few words to an illustrious daimio or a woman of high birth, then continued on her way, arriving in front of the Prince of Nagato.
- Ivakoura," she said, pulling from her breast a sealed letter wrapped in a piece of green satin, "give this paper to the mother of the siogun on my behalf. And she added lower down: This is what you asked for. The Mikado orders that you should not use this document until you are certain that Hiéyas wants to perjure himself.
- Your orders will be faithfully carried out," said Nagato, who took the letter trembling. This very night I shall return to Osaka.
- May your journey be a happy one!" said the Kisaki in a voice that was strangely gentle.
Then she passed on, and for a moment the prince heard the whisper of her dresses brushing against the carpets.
An hour later, Nagato left the dairi and set off.
As he rode through the town, he had to keep his horse in step so as not to knock over the joyful crowds that crowded the streets.
Huge lanterns made of glass, paper, gauze or silk were shining on all sides; their multicoloured glow cast strange reflections on the faces of the strollers who, as they moved along, appeared pink, blue, lilac or green. The horse was a little frightened by the deafening noise that reigned in Kioto. It was the bursts of laughter from the women, stopped in front of a puppet theatre; the tambourine whirring incessantly and accompanying the prodigious tricks of a troupe of tightrope walkers; the shouts of an argument that degenerated into a battle, the silver bells struck by fate, answering a sorcerer who was telling the future to an attentive circle ; the high-pitched chants of the priests of Odjigongem, performing a sacred dance in the garden of a pagoda; then the clamour of a whole army of beggars, some mounted on stilts, others dressed in historical costumes or wearing for a hat a vase in which a flowering shrub is blooming. 
There, begging brothers, dressed in red and their heads completely shaven, puff out their cheeks and blow silver whistles that pierce the tumult and pierce the ears; priestesses from the national cult pass by singing and waving a white paper pin, a symbol of purity; A dozen or so young bonzes, playing all sorts of instruments, strain their ears and try to hear each other, so as not to lose the measure of the melody they are playing, despite the general uproar, while further on a tortoise charmer strikes a tom-tom with precipitous blows and blind people, seated at the entrance to a temple, take turns banging on bells, bristling with bronze pustules. 
From time to time, lords of the mikado's court would break through the crowd; they would go incognito to the theatre, or to one of the teahouses that remained open all night, where, freed from the rigours of etiquette, they could drink and enjoy themselves at their leisure.
Nagato, too, was travelling incognito and alone; he didn't even have a runner to keep the crowds at bay. Nevertheless, he managed to get out of the city without harming anyone. He then gave the reins back to his impatient horse, which soon galloped along a magnificent avenue of sycamore trees, lined with pagodas, temples and chapels that the prince could see spinning to right and left, each one throwing a shred of prayer or sacred chant into his ears. Once Ivakoura turned round and looked back for a long time; through the branches he had caught sight of the tomb of Taïko-Sama, the father of Fidé-Yori; he thought that the ashes of this great man must have shuddered with joy as the man who was going to bring salvation to his son passed by them. He passed the suburbs and climbed a small hill.
He took one last look at the city so dear to his heart. It was shrouded in a luminous mist, red amid the blue glow that the moon cast over the surrounding mountains. On the slopes, between the trees, a few pagoda roofs shone like mirrors. The golden chrysanthemum over the gate of the Daïri had caught a ray and seemed like a star hanging over the city. But everything disappeared behind the folds of the ground; the last rumour of Kioto died away.
The prince heaved a sigh, then, urging his horse on, he dashed like an arrow across the countryside.
He passed several villages along the way and, after an hour, reached Yodo. He crossed the town without slowing his pace and passed a castle, its high towers bathed in moonlight and the water in its moat glistening.
This castle belonged to Yodogimi, the mother of the siogun; it was inhabited at the time by a favourite of this princess, General Harounaga.
- I have little confidence in the worth of the handsome warrior who sleeps behind these ramparts," murmured the prince, glancing at the silent castle.
A moment later he was galloping through a rice field.
On all sides the moon was reflected in pools of water from which the thin ears of rice rose. The rice field resembled a vast pond; fine white mists floated here and there in sheets, close to the ground, and a few large black buffalo, lying half in the water, slept peacefully.
Nagato slowed the pace of his horse, which was panting; soon he threw the bridle over its neck and, lowering his head, plunged back into his tyrannical reverie. The horse began to walk, and the prince, absorbed, let it do as it pleased.
Nagato saw again the shining halls of the palace and the sovereign coming towards him; he thought he could still hear the rustle of the fabrics around her.
- Ah!" he suddenly exclaimed, "that letter that touched her breast, it's pressing against my heart now and burning me.
He pulled the letter from his chest.
- Alas, I shall have to part with this priceless relic," he murmured.
Suddenly he pressed his lips to it. The touch of the soft fabric and the familiar scent that emanated from it sent a burning shiver through the prince's veins. He closed his eyes, overcome by a delicious languor.
A worried neigh from his horse drew him out of his ecstasy.
He plunged the royal missive back into his bosom and looked around. About fifty paces ahead, a group of trees cast their shadows across the road. Nagato thought he saw something stirring in the shadows. He grabbed the pike attached to his saddle and urged his horse, which was stumbling and moving forward only reluctantly.
Soon the prince had no more doubts: armed men were waiting for him as he passed.
- What, again!" he exclaimed. The Regent really wants to get rid of me.
- This time he won't miss you," replied one of the assassins, throwing his horse towards the prince.
- You haven't got me yet," said Nagato as his horse swerved.
His opponent, carried away by the momentum, passed close to him without hitting him.
- What a fool I am," murmured the prince, "to expose this precious message to the vagaries of my fortune.
The naked swords shone around him; the attackers were so numerous that they could not all approach the one they were attacking at the same time.
Nagato was the most skilful marksman in the whole kingdom, full of strength, composure and daring. Twirling his pike, he broke several swords around him, the splinters of which fell amid a shower of blood; then, by making sudden jumps with his horse, he escaped for a moment from the blows intended for him.
- I may be able to defend myself for a few minutes yet, he thought
but I am obviously lost.
A buffalo woke up and let out a long, sad roar, then all that could be heard was the clanking of iron and the trampling of horses.
But suddenly, a voice burst through the night.
- Courage, Prince!" it shouted, "we're coming to help you!
Nagato was covered in blood, but he was still fighting. The voice gave him new strength as it paralysed the assassins, who exchanged worried glances.
A hurried gallop sounded and, before they could recognise each other, a large group of horsemen swooped down on the prince's attackers.
Nagato, exhausted, withdrew a little to one side and looked with surprise, without really understanding what was happening, at these defenders who had arrived at such an opportune moment.
These men looked charming in the moonlight, which illuminated the rich embroidery on their robes and shot blue flashes from their light helmets with their cut-out ornaments.
The prince recognised the costume of the Riders of Heaven, the honour guard of the Mikado.
Soon, all that remained of the assassins posted by the regent were the dead. The victors wiped their weapons clean, and the leader of the troop approached Nagato.
- Are you badly hurt, Prince?" he asked.
- I don't know," replied Nagato: in the heat of battle, I didn't feel a thing.
- But your face is covered in blood.
- That's true," said the prince, putting his hand to his cheek.
- Do you want to get off your horse?
- No, I'm afraid I wouldn't be able to get back on. But let's not talk about me any more; let me thank you for your miraculous intervention, which saved my life, and ask you how you came to be on this road at this time.
- I'll tell you that later," said the rider, "but not before I've dressed this wound that's going to let all your blood escape.
Water was fetched from a nearby pond and washed over the prince's face: a fairly deep gash appeared on the forehead near the temple. For the time being, all they could do was wrap a tight bandage around his forehead.
- You have other wounds, don't you?
- I think so, but I feel strong enough to get to Osaka.
- Well, off we go!" said the rider, "we'll talk while we walk.
The little troop set off.
- So you're escorting me?" said Nagato.
- We have orders not to leave you, Prince, but it is a pleasure for us to fulfil this duty.
- Will you do me the honour of telling me your glorious name?
said Nagato, bowing.
- You know me, Nagato, I am Farou-So-Chan, lord of Tsusima.
- The husband of the beautiful Iza-Farou whom I had the glory of seeing this very day! cried Nagato. Forgive me, I should have recognised you from the terrible blows you dealt my attackers, but I was blinded by blood.
- I am proud and happy to have been chosen to assist you, and to prevent any unfortunate consequences that might have resulted from your reckless audacity.
- I have indeed acted with unforgivable lightness," said Nagato; "I had the right to risk my life, but not to expose the precious message I carry.
- Let me tell you, dear prince, that the envelope you carry contains nothing but blank paper.
- Could this be?" exclaimed Nagato, "Could I have been fooled?
In that case, I can't survive this insult.
- Calm down, friend," said the Prince of Tsusima, "and listen to me: after tonight's feast, as soon as she had returned to her flats, the divine Kisaki sent for me.
Farou," she said, "the Prince of Nagato is leaving Kioto tonight; I know that someone is out to get him, and that he may be ambushed.
So instead of the message he thinks he's carrying, I've only given him an empty envelope; the real letter is here," she added, showing me a small cassette. Take fifty men with you and follow the prince at a distance.
If not, meet him at the gate of Osaka and give him this cassette, leaving him unaware that you escorted him. Here you are, Prince; only you have incomparable horses, and we were too late in coming to your aid.
Nagato was deeply disturbed by this revelation; he remembered how kindly the sovereign had wished him a happy journey and could not help seeing a sign of interest in his life in what had happened. And then he thought that he was going to be able to keep this treasure, this letter that she had held over her heart for a whole evening.
The rest of the journey was silent. Nagato was seized by fever, the coolness of the coming dawn was making him shiver, and he was beginning to feel weakened by the loss of his blood.
By the time we reached the gates of Osaka, it was dawn.
Tsusima took from the saddle-tree a small crystal case, fastened with a skilfully tied silk cord.
- Here, Prince," he said, "the precious letter is locked in this box. I bid you farewell. May your wounds heal quickly!
- Goodbye," said Nagato, "thank you again for risking your precious life for mine, which is worth so little.
After waving goodbye to the small troop of horsemen, Nagato rode off under one of the city gates and, mounting his horse, soon reached the palace.
When Loo saw his master arrive, pale as a ghost and covered in blood, he fell to his knees and remained stupid with astonishment.
- Come on," said the prince, "shut your stunned mouth and get up; I'm not dead yet. Call my servants and run for the doctor.
VI
THE BROTHERHOOD OF THE BLIND
A few hours later, courtiers were gathered under the verandah of the palace of Hiéyas; they wanted to be the first to greet the true master and were waiting for him to wake up. Some of them were leaning against the cedar columns supporting the roof, others were standing proudly, one hand on their hip, ruffling the silky folds of their loose-fitting tunics. They listened attentively as one of them told an anecdote, which must have been very interesting, because it was listened to attentively and the listeners sometimes let out a burst of laughter, which was immediately stifled out of respect for the sleep of the illustrious sleeper.
The narrator was the Prince of Toza, and the Prince of Nagato the hero of the adventure he was recounting.
- Yesterday," he said, "the sun was about to set when I heard a noise at the door of my palace; I approached a window and saw my servants arguing with a group of blind men. The blind wanted to get in as fast as they could and were all talking at once, banging their sticks on the paving stones; the servants were shouting at them to get them out, and we couldn't hear each other at all. I was beginning to get irritated by this scene when I saw the Prince of Nagato arrive; my servants immediately bowed to him and, on his orders, let the blind men into the pavilion used to stable the visitors' horses. I went to meet the prince, curious to have the explanation of all this comedy.
- Let's hurry," he said, entering my room and throwing a parcel on the carpet, "let's take off our clothes and put on these costumes.
- What for?" I said, looking at the costumes, which were not to my taste.
- What!" he said, "isn't this the hour when we leave the dull pomp of our rank to become happy and free men again?
- Yes," he said, "but why use our freedom to dress up in these ungracious costumes?
- You'll see, I have a plan," said the prince, who was already undressing; then, coming close to my ear, he added:
- I'm getting married tonight. You'll see what a wedding it is!
- You're going to get married? And in that costume?
I exclaimed, seeing the prince dressed in a shabby suit.
- Come on, let's hurry," he said, "or we won't find the bride.
The prince was already coming down the stairs; I hastened to put on the garment similar to his and, piqued by curiosity, I followed him.
- But," I shouted, "what about all those blind people you had put in the stable?
- We're going to join them.
- In the stable?" I said.
I didn't understand a word of it; but I trust the prince's whimsical imagination, and I resigned myself to waiting to understand. The blind men had come out into the great courtyard of the palace, and I saw that we were dressed like them. These poor people had the most comical faces in the world, with their eyelashless eyelids, their squashed noses, their fat lips and their stupidly cheerful expressions. Nagato put a stick in my hand and shouted:
- Let's go!
The doors were opened. The blind men, holding each other by the flaps of their clothes, began to walk, tapping the ground with their sticks. Nagato, bending at the waist and closing his eyes, followed them. I knew I had to do the same, and I did my best. So here we were, following this band of blind men through the streets. I couldn't stand it. I was seized by a fit of giggles that all my companions soon shared.
- Nagato has definitely lost his mind!" cried Toza's listeners, bursting into laughter.
- It seems to me that Toza wasn't very sensible either!
- The Prince of Nagato wasn't laughing," continued the narrator, "he was very angry. I tried to find out from the blind man closest to me what the prince's plans were, but he didn't know. All I knew was that the guild I belonged to belonged to a brotherhood of blind men whose job it is to go into the homes of the little people and massage the weak and sick. I was beginning to get a vague idea of Nagato's intention. Under this grotesque disguise, he wanted to break into the home of an honest merchant. The idea that we might have someone to massage plunged me once again into such a fit of gaiety that, despite my efforts to keep a straight face in order to please the prince, I was forced to stop and sit down on a bollard to hold my ribs.
Nagato was furious.
- You're going to ruin my wedding," he said.
I set off again, blinking and imitating as closely as I could the gait of my strange colleagues. They struck the ground with their sticks, singing the well-known tune about their skill in the art of massaging, and at this noise people leaned out of their windows and called out to them. We arrived in front of an unremarkable house, and the noise of the sticks intensified. A voice called for two masseurs.
- Come," said Nagato; "it's here. Separating ourselves from the gang, we climbed a few steps and found ourselves inside the house. I saw two women, whom Nagato greeted awkwardly, turning his back to them. I hastily closed my eyes and waved at the wall. I opened one eye again, however, driven by curiosity. There was a young girl and an old woman there, her mother no doubt.
- Take care of us first," she said, "then massage my husband.
She immediately crouched down and uncovered her back. I realised that the old woman had come back to me and that I really should be a masseur. Nagato was full of greetings.
- Ah! ah! ah!" he said, as inferiors do when greeting a man of high rank.
I began to rub the old woman roughly, who was uttering pitiful moans. I made every effort to contain the laughter that once again rose to my throat and choked me. The young girl had modestly uncovered her shoulder, as if we'd had eyes.
- It's there," she said; "I gave myself a blow and the doctor said a few rubs would do me good.
Nagato began to massage the girl with astonishing seriousness, but suddenly he seemed to forget his role as a blind man.
- What beautiful hair you have! Of course, if you were to adopt the hairstyle of noble women, you wouldn't need to use the same tricks to make your hair longer.
The young girl shrieked and turned round; she saw Nagato's wide-open eyes looking at her.
- Mother," she cried, "these are false blind people!
The mother fell to the ground in a daze that robbed her of all her faculties, and she made no effort to get up.
The father ran over in fright.
I, on the other hand, had given free rein to my laughter and was rolling along the walls of the room, unable to stand it any longer. To my great surprise, the Prince of Nagato threw himself at the craftsman's feet.
Forgive us," he said, "your daughter and I wanted to get married together, and as I have no money I decided, as is customary, to kidnap her to avoid wedding expenses.
According to custom, you would have forgiven us after asking a little.
- Me, to marry that man!" said the young girl, "but I don't know him at all.
- Do you think my daughter would want a bandit like you for a husband? Come on! Get out of here as quickly as you can if you don't want to get to know my fists!
The sound of this angry voice began to draw a crowd to the front of the house. Nagato let out a long whistle.
- Will you leave!" cried the man of the people, red with anger.
And, in the midst of the coarsest insults, he raised his fist at Nagato.
- Don't hit the man who is to be your son," said the prince, raising his arm.
- You, my son? You'd rather see the snows of Fousi-Yama covered in flowers.
- I swear you'll be my father-in-law", said the prince, grabbing the man by the arm.
The man struggled, but Nagato carried him out of the house. I then approached the balustrade and saw the crowd gathered in front of the house move aside in front of the runners, who were leading a magnificent procession: music, banners, palanquins, all bearing the prince's arms. The norimonos stopped in front of the house and Nagato stuffed his father-in-law into one of them, locking it. I understood what had to be done, so I grabbed the old woman and put her in another palanquin, while Nagato came back to get the young girl. Two norimonos greet us and the procession sets off, the music blaring merrily. We soon arrived at a charming dwelling in the middle of the prettiest garden I have ever seen.
Everything is lit up, orchestras can be heard hidden in the foliage, and busy servants are running here and there.
What on earth is this lovely palace?" I said to Nagato.
- Oh nothing," he replies scornfully, "it's just a little house I bought for my new wife.
- He's mad, I thought, and he's going to ruin himself, but that's none of my business.
We were shown to a room, where we were dressed in splendid toiletries, and then down to the banqueting hall, where all Nagato's young friends, Sataké, Foungo, Aki and many others, had gathered. They greet us with extravagant cheers. Soon the bride, magnificently adorned, enters followed by her father and mother, who stumble in the folds of their silk garments. The father seems completely calm, the mother bewildered and the young girl so stunned that she holds her pretty mouth wide open. Nagato declares that he takes her as his wife and the wedding takes place. It was the happiest I've ever seen. The feast was most exquisite, and soon everyone was drunk, myself included; but I made myself go back to the palace at about three o'clock to rest, because I wanted to be at the regent's wake this morning.
- This story is the craziest I know," said the Prince of Figo. Only Nagato really knows how to run a joke.
- And did he really get married? asked another lord.
- Most certainly," said the Prince of Toza, "the marriage is valid, despite the despicable rank of the woman.
- Every day the prince invents new follies and throws splendid parties, and it is certain that his immense fortune will eventually run out.
- If he goes broke, that will please the Regent, who doesn't like him very much.
- Yes, but it will upset the siogoun who loves him very much and won't let him run out of money.
- Here!" exclaimed the Prince of Toza, "here's Nagato returning to the palace.
A procession did indeed pass through the gardens. On the banners, on the norimono carried by twenty men, could be seen the prince's arms: a black line surmounted by three pyramid-shaped balls. The procession passed quite close to the verandah housing the lords, and through the doors of the norimono they saw the young prince dozing on the cushions.
- He certainly won't come when the regent rises," said one lord, "he might fall asleep on Hiéyas' shoulder.
- Nagato never comes to greet Hiéyas, he hates him deeply, he's his declared enemy.
- Such an enemy is hardly to be feared, says the Prince of Toza. When he returns from his nocturnal follies, all he can do is sleep.
- I don't know if this is the regent's opinion.
- If he thought otherwise, would he put up with such insults that he would be condemned to hara-kiri?
[1] . If the prince is still alive, he owes it to the gentleness of Hiéyas.
- Or to the tender protection of Fidé-Yori.
- No doubt Hiéyas is magnanimous only for the sake of his master, but if he only had enemies like Nagato, he would count himself lucky.
While the courtiers talked and waited for him to wake up, Hiéyas, who had been up for a long time, was striding around his room, restless and restless, with traces of sleeplessness on his worried face.
A man was standing next to the regent, leaning against a wall; he was watching him come and go; this man was a former stable hand called Faxibo. Grooms had been held in fairly high esteem since the rise to power of Taïko-Sama, who was originally a groom. Faxibo enjoyed the confidence of the regent more than anyone else, who had nothing hidden from him and thought out loud in his presence.
Every so often, Hiéyas would raise the blind of a window and look out.
- Nothing," he would say impatiently, "no news, it's incomprehensible.
- Wait a few more moments," said Faxibo, "those you've just sent on the road to Kioto can't be back yet.
- But the others! there were forty of them and no one is coming back if they've escaped me again this time, it's driving me mad.
- Maybe you're exaggerating the importance of this man," says Faxibo. It's a love affair that's drawn him to Kioto; his head is full of madness.
- You believe that, and I confess that this man frightens me," said the regent forcefully, stopping in front of Faxibo: one claims that he followed him to Kioto, the other swears that he never lost sight of him for a moment and that he never left Osaka; all his friends had supper with him while he was fighting, on his way back from the Miako
[2] with men posted by me. They think he is asleep or busy with his love affairs; if one of my projects is going to be accomplished, his hand comes down on me at the last moment. For a long time the empire would have been ours without him; my supporters are numerous, but his are no less strong, and he has the law on his side. Now, this plan that I had so skilfully devised to rid the country, under the guise of an accident, of a sovereign without talent or energy, this plan that would have seen power fall into my hands, who aborted it? Who was the cursed coachman who launched this infernal carriage onto the bridge? Nagato! Always him. However," added Hiéyas, "someone else, one of my allies, must have betrayed me, because it is impossible for anything to have transpired from this project. Ah, if I knew the name of the traitor, I would at least give myself the pleasure of terrible revenge.
- I've told you what I've been able to find out," says Faxibo.
Fidé-Yori cried out as the building collapsed: "Omiti, you spoke the truth!
- Omiti! What is Omiti? I don't know that name.
The regent had gone into the room adjoining his bedroom, which was separated only by a large blind from the verandah where the lords were waiting for him to rise. From the inside, this blind made it possible to see without being seen. When Hiéyas heard Nagato's name being spoken, he approached quickly and beckoned Faxibo to come close to him. And so they heard the whole of the Prince of Toza's story.
- Yes," murmured Hiéyas, "for a long time I took him for a man of dissolute morals and no political importance, which is why at first I favoured his intimacy with Fidé-Yori; how I repent of this now that I know what he is worth!
- You see, master," said Faxibo, "that the prince, no doubt warned of your plan, has not left Osaka.
- I can tell you that he was at the Miako and didn't leave until well into the night.
- However, the Prince of Toza did not leave until very late.
- One of my spies followed him to Kioto, entering in broad daylight and not leaving until the middle of the night.
- It's incomprehensible," said Faxibo, "here he is, coming back," he added, catching sight of Nagato's retinue.
- Is that him in the litter?" said Hiéyas, trying to see.
- I thought I recognised him," said Faxibo.
- It's impossible, it can't be the Prince of Nagato, unless it's his corpse.
Just then, someone entered the room and bowed to the ground.
- It's my envoy," cried Hiéyas, who hurried back into the first room, "speak quickly; let's see, what have you learnt?" he said to the messenger.
- I have come to the point on the road that you have indicated to me, almighty master", said the envoy. At this point, the road is covered in dead men. I counted forty men and fifteen horses. Some peasants were grouped around the dead; they were feeling them to see if they had a breath of life left in them; others were chasing wounded horses that were running through the rice fields. I asked what had happened and was told they didn't know; however, at sunrise a troop of horsemen from the divine Mikado had been seen riding towards Kioto. As for the men who died on the road red with their blood, they were all wearing dark suits, without any insignia, and their faces were half-hidden by their headgear in the fashion of bandits and assassins.
- Enough!" cried Hiéyas, his eyebrows furrowed. Go away!
The envoy withdrew, or rather fled.
- He escaped me again this time," said Hiéyas. Well, I'll hit him myself; the goal I want to achieve is noble enough for me not to hesitate to use infamous means to overturn the obstacles that stand in my way.
Faxibo," he added, turning to the former groom, "send in those who are waiting. Perhaps their presence will dispel the sad forebodings that have obsessed me all night.
Faxibo raised the blind and the lords came in one by one to greet the master. Hiéyas noticed that there were fewer courtiers than usual, just the princes who were devoted to his cause and a few insouciants who wanted a special favour from the regent.
While chatting with the lords, Hiéyas stepped out onto the verandah and looked out.
It seemed to him that an unusual movement was filling the palace courtyards. Messengers were leaving at every moment and princes were arriving in their norimonos despite the early hour. They were all heading for the Fide-Yori palace.
- What's going on," he said, "what's all this commotion about, what are these messengers carrying orders I don't know anything about?
And full of concern, he dismissed the lords with a gesture.
- You will excuse me, won't you?" he said, "the interests of the country call me.
But before the princes had taken their leave, a soldier entered the room.
- The siogoun Fidé-Yori begs the illustrious Hiéyas to come to his presence at once," he said.
And, without waiting for a reply, he left.
Hiéyas stopped the lords ready to leave.
- Wait for me here," he said. "I don't know what's coming, but I'm worried sick. You are devoted to me and I may need you.
He greeted them with a gesture and left slowly, brow lowered, followed only by Faxibo.
VII
THE PARJURY
When he entered the room where Fidé-Yori was waiting for him, Hiéyas realised that something serious was about to happen.
All the supporters devoted to the son of Taïko-Sama were gathered in this room.
For the first time, Fidé-Yori was wearing the royal warrior costume that only he could wear. The black horn cuirass hugged his waist, and heavy basques made of strips linked by red silk stitches fell down over wide brocade trousers held from the ankle to the knees in velvet gaiters. He had a sabre on his left side and another on his right.
Three gold stars shone on his chest, and he rested his hand on an iron cane.
The young man was seated on a pliant like a warrior in a tent.
On his right was his mother, the beautiful Yodogimi, pale and worried, but splendidly dressed. On his left was the Prince of Mayada, who shared the regency with Hiéyas; but this very old prince, who had been ill for a long time, stayed away from business, but nevertheless kept an eye on Hiéyas and safeguarded Fidé-Yori's interests as far as possible.
On one side the princes: Satsouma, Sataké, Arima, Aki, Issida; on the other the warriors: General Sanada-Sayemon-Yoké-
Moura, in battle dress; other chiefs, Aroufza, Moto-
Tsoumou, Harounaga, Moritska and a very young man, as handsome as a woman and very serious, called Signénari.
All the young prince's friends and all the regent's mortal enemies were gathered together, but Nagato was absent.
Hiéyas glanced proudly at those present.
- Here I am," he said, "in a very firm voice, waiting: what do you want from me?
Only a deep silence answered him. Fidé-Yori looked away from him in horror.
Finally the Prince of Mayada spoke.
- Your mission, like mine, was completed several months ago, Hiéyas, and in your zeal to govern the empire you have not taken any notice of it. The son of Taïko-Sama is now old enough to reign; your reign is therefore over; all that remains is for you to lay your powers at the feet of the master and give him an account of your conduct, just as I will give him an account of my actions while he was under our tutelage.
- You don't think about what you're saying," cried Hiéyas, whose face flushed with anger; "you apparently want to drive the country to ruin?
- I spoke gently," continued Mayada, "don't force me to take a different tone.
- You want an inexperienced child," continued Hiéyas, heedless of the interruption, "to come and take power in his own hands before he has practised the formidable profession of ruling a kingdom; it's as if you put a heavy porcelain vase in the hands of a new-born baby: he'll drop it on the ground and the vase will shatter into a thousand pieces.
- You insult our siogoun!" cried the Prince of Sataké.
- No," said Hieyas, "Fidé-Yori himself will agree with me. I must involve him slowly in my work and show him the possible solutions to the pending questions. Did he ever concern himself with the affairs of the country? His young intelligence was not yet mature, and I was able to spare him the troubles of government. I alone have the great Taiko's instructions and I alone can continue the gigantic work he has undertaken. The work is not yet complete. Therefore, in order to obey this revered leader, I must, despite your advice, retain in my own hands the power entrusted to me by him; only, to show you how much I heed your advice, from today the young Fidé-Yori will take part in the serious occupations whose burden I have borne alone until now. Answer, Fidé-Yori," added Hiéyas; "proclaim to yourself that I have spoken from your heart.
Fidé-Yori slowly turned his very pale face towards Hiéyas and stared at him.
Then, after a moment's silence, he said in a voice that was a little shaky but full of contempt:
- The sound of the Swallow's bridge collapsing before my feet has made me deaf to your voice. Hiéyas paled in the presence of the man he had tried to drive to his death; he was humiliated by his crime. He saw them in the future darkening his name, which he wanted to be glorious, certain that his duty to his country was to keep in his hands the power of which he was more worthy than any other; he felt a kind of anger at being obliged to impose by force what the public interest should have urged him to do. However, determined to fight to the bitter end, he raised his head, bent for a moment under the weight of tumultuous thoughts, and cast his wild, domineering gaze over the audience.
A menacing silence followed the siogoun's words. The Prince of Satsouma finally broke it.
- Hiéyas," he said, "I summon you in the name of my master to deposit the powers vested in you by Taïko-Sama.
- I refuse," said Hiéyas.
A cry of astonishment escaped the lips of all the lords.
The Prince of Mayada rose to his feet; he walked slowly towards Hiéyas and drew from his chest a paper yellowed by time.
- Do you recognise this?" he said, unfolding the document and placing it in front of Hiéyas' eyes. "Is it with your blood that you have drawn your traitorous name alongside my loyal name? Have you forgotten the words of the oath: "The powers you entrust to us, we will return to your child when he comes of age, we swear on the spirits of our ancestors, before the luminous disc of the sun? Taïko fell asleep peacefully after seeing these few red lines; today he will rise from his tomb, perjurer, to curse you.
The old man, trembling with anger, crumpled the oath written in blood between his hands and threw it in Hiéyas' face.
- But do you really think we're going to let you rob our child before our very eyes? Do you think that just because you don't want to give back what you've taken, we won't take you back? The crimes you meditate on have clouded your intelligence, you no longer have any soul or honour, you dare to stand up before your master, before the one you wanted to kill!
- It's not just me whose life he wants to snatch," said Fidé-Yori; "this man, more ferocious than a tiger, last night had my most faithful servant, my dearest friend, the Prince of Nagato, murdered.
A shiver of horror ran through the assembly as a flash of joy passed through Hiéyas' eyes.
- With this formidable adversary out of the way," he thought, "I'll have no trouble getting the better of Fidé-Yori.
As if in response to his thought, Nagato's voice was heard.
- Don't rejoice too quickly, Hiéyas," she said, "I'm still alive and able to serve my young master.
Hiéyas turned round sharply and saw the prince lifting a drapery and entering the room.
Nagato looked like a ghost, his eyes glowing with the fire of fever and appearing larger and blacker than usual. His face was so pale that one could barely make out the thin white bandage, stained with a few drops of blood, that clung to his forehead. A painful shiver shook his limbs and caused a crystal box to tremble in his hand.
General Yoké-Moura ran to him.
- After losing so much blood, and despite the doctors' orders, you get up and walk!
- Bad friend," said Fide-Yori, "will you not stop playing with your life?
- I will become the slave of the doctors to obey your undeserved interest in me," said the prince, "when I have accomplished the mission with which I have been charged.
He watched and waited, often glancing towards the door as if he wanted to flee.
- I must present this box to you on my knees, and you must receive it on your knees," said the prince, "for it contains a message from your lord and ours, from the one who holds his power from heaven, the almighty Mikado.
Nagato prostrated himself and handed the cassette to the siogun, who received it by bending the knee.
Hiéyas was well aware that this cassette contained his definitive doom, and he thought that, as always, it was the prince of Nagato who had triumphed over him.
However, Fidé-Yori had unfolded the Mikado's message and was looking through it. An expression of joy lit up his face.
He looked up at Nagato with wet eyes, thinking in turn that it was always through him that he triumphed.
- Prince of Satsouma," he said shortly, handing the letter to the old lord, "read this divine writing aloud to us.
The Prince of Satsouma read out the following:
"I, the direct descendant of the gods who founded Japan, look down upon your land and see that time has passed since the death of the faithful servant of my dynasty, Taïko- Sama, whom my predecessor, the King of Satsouma, had killed.
Sama, whom my predecessor had appointed as the chief gêneral of the kingdom. The son of this illustrious chief, who rendered great services to the country, was six years old when his father died; but time has passed for him as it has for everyone else, and he is now of an age to succeed his father, which is why I am appointing him in his turn chief general of the kingdom.
"In a few days' time, men from heaven will go and solemnly announce my wishes to him, so that no one will be unaware of them.
"Now, resting on Fide-Yori to govern, I plunge back into the mysterious absorption of my extra-human dream.
dream.
"Done at the Dairi, in the nineteenth year of Nengo-Kai-Tio [] .
"Go-MiTzou-No."
There is nothing to say in reply to this," said Hiéyas, bowing his head, "the sovereign master has ordered, I obey, I lay down the powers that have been entrusted to me and after the insults I have suffered, I know what I have to do. I hope that those who led this affair will not one day repent of having seen it succeed, and that the country will not have to groan under the weight of the calamities that may befall it.
He went out after saying these words, and all the happy lords crowded around the young Siogoun and congratulated him.
- It's my friend, my brother Nagato, who should be congratulated," said Fidé-Yori, "it's he who has done everything.
- It's not all over yet," said Nagato, who looked worried, "we need to sign Hiéyas' death sentence straight away.
- But you heard him, friend? he said he knew what he had to do, he's carrying out the Hara-Kiri right now.
- That's for sure," says the Prince of Satsouma.
- He knows the code of the nobility," said the Prince of Aki.
- Yes, but he despises its customs and won't abide by it," said Nagato. If we don't condemn this man quickly he will escape us, and once free he is capable of daring anything.
The prince of Nagato had unfurled a roll of white paper and was holding out a brush dipped in ink to the siogun.
Fidé-Yori seemed to hesitate.
- To condemn him like this without a trial! he said.
- There's no need for a trial," Nagato said in front of the whole council, "he's just perjured himself and disrespected you, and what's more he's a murderer.
- He's my wife's grandfather," murmured the Siogun.
- You will repudiate your wife," said Nagato. With Hiéyas alive, there's no peace for you, no security for the country.
Fidé-Yori suddenly picked up a paintbrush, wrote down the sentence and signed.
Nagato handed the order to General Sanada-Sayemon-Yoké-
Moura, who left immediately.
He soon returned, his face flushed with anger.
- Too late," he cried, "the Prince of Nagato was only too right: Hiéyas is on the run.
VIII
OVARI CASTLE
On the shores of the Pacific Ocean, atop a rocky hill, stands the fortress of the Princes of Ovari. Its walls, pierced with loopholes, follow the sinuosities of the terrain. They are masked here and there by groups of trees and bushes whose fresh greenery contrasts happily with the torn walls of rust-coloured rock. In the distance, a square tower rises up, its base widening like the foot of a pyramid; a roof with raised edges covers it.
The view from the top of the fortress is admirable. In the distance, the blue waves of the Pacific Ocean unfurl and trace a rigid, darker blue line on the horizon. On the land side, the first undulations of a mountain range dent the ground; moss-covered rocks dot it, and the high hills are in places cultivated right up to their summits. From one mountain to the next, a valley deepens, revealing a village hidden in the shade of a small wood, close to a stream, then at the end new hills close off the valley.
A wide, well-maintained road runs between the landslides and passes at the foot of Ovari castle. This road, known as the Tokaido, was built by Taïko-Sama; it criss-crosses the entire empire, passing through the domains of the Daïmios and is subject only to the jurisdiction of the siogoun.
The prince who reigned over the province of Ovari resided in his castle.
Around the third hour after noon, on the day that Hiéyas fled from Osaka, the sentry on the highest tower of the Ovari palace shouted that he saw a troop of horsemen galloping along the Tokaïdo. The prince was at that moment in one of the courtyards of the castle, crouching on his heels, hands resting on his thighs; he was watching a Hara-Kiri lesson being given by his young son.
The child was sitting on a mat in the middle of the courtyard, holding an unsharpened short sword in both hands and raising his pretty, naive but already serious face towards his instructor, seated opposite him. Women were watching from the top of a gallery, their dresses making cheerful spots on the light colours of the carved woodwork; huge butterflies were embroidered on their dresses, or birds, flowers or multicoloured discs, all their heads bristling with large blond tortoiseshell pins. They cackled to each other with a thousand charming minces.
In the courtyard, leaning against the support of a bronze lantern, a young girl, tightly clad in a sky-blue crepe dress, all the folds of which were thrown forward, fixed her distracted gaze on the little lord; in her hand was a screen with a hummingbird painted on it.
- Hold the sword vigorously," said the instructor, "press the point
the point below the ribs on the left side, making sure that the edge of the blade is turned to the right. Now hold the hilt tightly in your hand and press down with all your might, then quickly, without ceasing to press down, bring the weapon back horizontally towards your right side, in this way you will split your body according to the rules.
The child executed the movement so violently that he tore his clothes.
- Good! good!" exclaimed the Prince of Ovari, slapping his thighs with his open hands.
At the same time, he looked up at the women leaning out of the gallery and nodded to them.
- He'll be brave and intrepid like his father," said one of them.
It was then that the Prince was informed that a group of horsemen had appeared on the royal road.
- No doubt a neighbouring lord is visiting me incognito", said the prince, "or perhaps these horsemen are simply travellers passing through; in any case there was no reason to interrupt the lesson.
The instructor then enumerated for his pupil the events that oblige a man of noble race to cut open his stomach: having incurred the disgrace of the siogoun, or having received a public reprimand from him, having been dishonoured, having avenged an insult by murder, having voluntarily or involuntarily let prisoners entrusted to your guard escape, and a thousand other delicate cases.
- Add to this," said the Prince of Ovari, "having shown disrespect for his father. In my opinion, a son who insults his parents can only atone for this crime by performing Hara-Kiri.
At the same time, he gave the women another look which meant that it was a good idea to instil in children the terror of their father's authority.
At that moment, a great noise of horses stampeding on the flagstones was heard in a neighbouring courtyard, and an imperious voice shouted:
- Raise the drawbridges! Close the gates!
The Prince of Ovari jumped to his feet:
- Who's in charge here?
- It's me!" replied the same voice.
At the same time, a group of men entered the second courtyard.
- The Regent!" exclaimed the Prince of Ovari, bowing.
- Get up, friend," said Hiéyas with a bitter smile, "I no longer have the right to the honours you pay me, I am, for the moment, your equal.
- What's going on?" asked the prince worriedly.
- Dismiss your wives," said Hiéyas.
Ovari made a sign and the women disappeared.
- Take your brother with you, Omiti," he said to the young girl who had gone terribly pale when Hiéyas entered.
- Your daughter's name is Omiti?" exclaimed the latter, whose face suddenly flushed.
Yes, master. Why do you ask?
- Please call her back.
Ovari obeyed. The young girl returned trembling, her eyes downcast.
Hiéyas stared at her with a facial expression that was frightening for anyone who knew the man. The girl raised her head, however, and you could see in her eyes an invincible fearlessness, a kind of renunciation of self and of life.
- It was you who betrayed us," said Hiéyas in a dull voice.
- Yes," she said.
- What does this mean?" exclaimed the Prince of Ovari, jerking back.
- It means that the plot so well hatched behind the walls of this castle, so mysteriously concealed from everyone, she has surprised and revealed.
- Wretch!" exclaimed the prince, raising his fist at his daughter.
- A woman, a child, thwarting a political project! A vile stone that trips you up and sends you tumbling to the ground, that's derisory!
- I'll kill you," shouted Ovari.
- Kill me, I don't care," said the young girl, "I've saved the king. Isn't his life worth mine? I've waited a long time for your vengeance.
- You won't have to wait much longer," said the prince, grabbing her by the throat.
- No, don't kill her," said Hiéyas; "I'll see to her torture.
- All right," said Ovari, "I'll leave her in your hands.
- Good," said Hiéyas, who signalled Faxibo not to let the young girl out of his sight. But let's put the past behind us and look to the future. Are you still devoted to me?
- Can you doubt it, master? And must I not now right the wrong done to you by one of my own without my knowledge?
- Listen, then, a plot has just snatched power from me. I escaped the death that awaited me and fled to my principality of Micava. Your domains lie between Osaka and my province, your fortress overlooks the sea and can block the path of soldiers coming from Osaka, which is why I stopped here to tell you to assemble your troops as quickly as possible and put the country in a state of defence. Close the fortified castle. I will stay here where I am safe from attack, while my faithful companion Ino-
Kamo-No-Kami (Hiéyas pointed to a lord in his retinue, who bowed deeply to the Prince of Ovari, who returned his greeting)
will reach Micava castle, fortify the whole province and give the alarm to all the princes who are my allies.
- I am your slave, master dispose of me.
- So give your soldiers their orders straight away.
The Prince of Ovari left.
Servants ushered their master's guests into cool, airy rooms, where they were served tea, sweets and a light meal.
Ino-Kamo-No-Kami soon took leave of Hiéyas, who gave him his final instructions, and taking with him two of the lords who had accompanied them, he mounted his horse and left the castle.
Hiéyas called Faxibo.
Faxibo was busy devouring a honey cake, while keeping his eyes on Omiti, who was sitting in a corner of the room.
- Could you disguise yourself so well that you wouldn't be recognised?
- To the point where you yourself wouldn't recognise me," said Faxibo.
- Well, tomorrow morning you'll go back to Osaka and make sure you know what's going on at the palace.
Besides, you'll be travelling with a woman.
Hiéyas leaned close to the ear of the former groom and spoke softly.
Faxibo smiled evilly.
- Good! good! he said, tomorrow at daybreak I'll be ready to leave.
IX
THE TEA HOUSE
In one of the suburbs of Osaka, not far from the beach that lets the smooth slope of its white sand slide down to the sea, stretched a vast building whose roofs, of unequal heights, exceeded the level of the surrounding dwellings. The front of this house opened wide onto a crowded street, always crowded and full of uproar.
The first floor had large windows closed by brightly coloured blinds, which were frequently pushed forward by curious young women, whose bursts of laughter could be heard.
Banners and large diamond-shaped lanterns hung from the corners of the roofs, and the ground floor consisted of a wide gallery opening onto the street and protected by a roof. Three large black characters on a gilded panel formed the sign of the establishment, which read as follows: À l'aurore.
Tea and sake.
Towards mid-day the gallery was crowded with customers; they sat with their legs bent on the mat covering the floor, drinking sake or hiding their faces in the cloud of steam rising from the cup of tea they were blowing on. Coquettishly dressed women, carefully adorned, move gracefully between the groups, carrying the hot drink. In the background you can see the steaming ovens and pretty china arranged on red lacquer shelves.
Every now and then, passers-by, canoe-bearers and men laden with loads stop for a moment, ask for a drink and then leave.
Sometimes an argument breaks out in front of the inn and degenerates into a fight, much to the delight of the customers.
Here is a hawker who has just collided with a seller of octopus and shellfish, the basket containing the fish is overturned and the whole catch, soiled with dust, lies on the ground.
Insults rain down from both sides, traffic is interrupted, the crowd gathers or takes sides with one or the other of the adversaries, and soon the two camps are ready to come to blows.
But those present shouted from all sides: The cable! the cable! no fighting; go and get a cable.
A few people ran away, entered several houses and finally found what they were looking for; they returned with a thick rope.
The spectators then line up alongside the houses, leaving the place free for those who want to fight. They grab the rope with both hands, fifteen on one side and fifteen on the other, and start pulling with all their might. The rope stretches, shudders, then remains motionless.
- Courage! stand firm! don't falter! they shouted from all sides.
However, after a long struggle against fatigue, one of the parties suddenly gives up the rope. The victors fell simultaneously, one on top of the other, their legs in the air, amid shouts and laughter from the crowd.
Nevertheless, they are rescued and helped to their feet, and the reconciliation of the two enemy camps is sealed with sake libations.
The inn is overrun, and the maids don't know what to do.
Just then, an old man holding a young girl by the hand manages to stop one of the inn's maids in her tracks and hold her by her sleeve.
- I'd like to speak to the owner," he says.
- You've chosen the right moment," said the maid, bursting into laughter.
With a sudden gesture, she disengaged herself and walked away without listening to the old man.
- I'll wait," he said.
A barrel of sake was smashed and the happy drinkers chatted and laughed loudly.
Suddenly there was silence, the clear sound of a flute and the vibrations of a stringed instrument. This music comes from the flats above.
- Listen! listen!
A few passers-by stopped and listened.
A woman's voice is heard. The words of the song were clearly distinguishable.
"When Iza-Na-Gui had descended to earth, his companion, Iza-Na-Mi, met him in a garden.
"How wonderful to see such a handsome young man!
she exclaimed.
"But the god, displeased, replied:
"It is not fitting that the woman should have spoken first. Come back and meet me.
"They parted and met again.
What a pleasure to meet such a pretty girl!" said Iza-Na-Gui.
"Which of the two spoke first?
The voice fell silent. The accompaniment continued for a few moments.
A discussion began among the drinkers. They were answering the question posed by the singer.
- Some said it was the god who was greeted first.
- No! no! it was the goddess, cried the others. The god's will cancelled the first greeting.
- Did he cancel it? - No doubt! No doubt! They started again as if nothing had happened.
- Which doesn't change the fact that what was was, and that the woman was the first to speak.
The discussion threatened to escalate, but ended with more cups being emptied. Soon the crowd thinned out and the inn became peaceful again.
Then a maid spotted the old man leaning against a pillar, holding the young girl by the hand.
- Would you like some tea or sake?
- I want to speak to the head of the house," said the man. The maid glanced at the old man. He had a large woven rush hat on his head, like the lid of a round basket; his costume was made of brown cotton and was very worn; he was holding a fan in his hand on which was written the route from Yedo to Osaka, the distance from one village to the next, and the number and size of the inns. The maid looked at the young girl. She was poorly dressed. Her dress, a faded blue, was torn and dirty. A piece of white cloth, wrapped around her head, half hid her forehead. She was leaning on a black and pink parasol, the paper of which had been torn away here and there; but this young girl was singularly beautiful and graceful.
- Have you come for a sale?" said the innkeeper.
The old man nodded.
- I'll tell the master.
She left and soon returned. The master followed her.
He was a man of repulsive ugliness: his small, black, squinty eyes could barely be seen between the narrow fissure of his ridiculously slit eyelids; his mouth, set far back from a long, angular nose, bereft of teeth and topped with a few sparse, stiff hairs, gave a pitiful, sly expression to his pox-ridden face.
- Do you want to get rid of that little girl?" he said, rolling one of his eyes, while the other disappeared into the corner of his nose.
- Get rid of my child," cried the old man. I only want to part with her to keep her out of misery.
- Unfortunately, I have more wives than I need, and all of them are at least as pretty as this one.
My house is complete.
- I'll look elsewhere," said the old man, pretending to leave.
- Don't be in such a hurry," said the man, "if your demands aren't exorbitant we can come to an agreement.
- He beckoned her to follow him into the room at the entrance to which he was standing; this room, which opened onto a garden, was deserted.
- What can the little girl do?" said the dreadful squint.
- She can embroider, she can sing and play several instruments; she can even compose a quatrain.
- And what price do you want?
- Four kobangs.
The innkeeper was about to exclaim "No more", but he held back.
- That's what I was going to offer you," he said.
- Well, it's agreed," said the old man; "I'll rent it to you for whatever you want to do with it for twenty years.
The buyer hurried off to fetch a roll of paper and some paintbrushes; he drew up the contract, which the old man signed without hesitation.
The young girl remained like a statue, not glancing at the old man who pretended to wipe away a tear as he pocketed the kobangs.
Before leaving, he leaned towards the innkeeper's ear and said:
- Watch out for her, she'll try to escape.
Then he left the Teahouse of the Dawn, and anyone who, when he turned the corner of the street, had seen him change his steps, rubbing his hands together and passing the most alert, might have suspected the authenticity of his old age and white beard.
X
THE APPOINTMENT
The Prince of Nagato is lying on a black satin mattress, pressing one of his elbows into a cushion and handing over his other arm to a doctor crouched beside him.
The doctor felt his pulse.
At the Prince's bedside, Fidé-Yori, sitting on a pile of braids, fixes his worried gaze on the doctor's crumpled but inscrutable face.
A huge pair of glasses, with round lenses framed in black, gave a strange and comical expression to the serious face of the respectable scientist.
Near the entrance to the room, Loo was kneeling, his forehead bent towards the floor because of the King's presence; he was amusing himself by counting the strands of silver that fringed the carpet.
- The danger is over," says the doctor at last, "the wounds are closed, and yet the fever persists for a reason I cannot explain.
- I'll explain it to you," said the prince, quickly withdrawing his arm, "it's the impatience of being confined to this bed and not being able to run freely in the open air.
- How, friend," said the Siogun, "when I myself have come to share your captivity, are you so impatient to be free?
- You know very well, dear lord, that it is for your service that I am anxious to leave; the departure of the embassy you are sending to Kioto cannot be delayed indefinitely.
- Why did you ask me as a favour to be the head of this embassy?
- Is it not my happiness to serve you?
- That's not your only motive," said Fide-Yori, smiling.
- You're alluding to my supposed love for Fatkoura," thought Nagato, who also smiled.
- If the prince is reasonable, if he puts an end to this over-excitement that's exhausting him, he'll be able to leave in three days," said the doctor.
- Thank you!" exclaimed Nagato, "this is better than all your drugs.
- My drugs are not to be sneezed at," said the doctor, "and you'll still take the one I'm going to send you.
Then he bowed deeply to the king and his noble patient and withdrew.
- Ah!" exclaimed Fide-Yori when he was alone with his friend, "your impatience to leave proves to me that I was not deceived; you are in love, Ivakoura, you are loved, you are happy!
And he let out a long, deep sigh.
The prince looked at him, surprised by this sigh and expecting a confidence, but the young man blushed a little and changed the conversation.
You see," he said, opening a volume he was holding in his lap, "I'm studying the Book of Laws, trying to see if it doesn't need to be refined and softened.
- It contains an article that I advise you to remove," said Nagato.
- Which article is that?
- The one about mutual suicide for love.
- What does it say?" said Fidé-Yori, leafing through the book.
Ah, here it is: "When two lovers swear to die together and slit their stomachs open, their corpses are seized by the law.
If one of them is not mortally wounded, he is treated as the murderer of the other. If both survive their suicide attempt, they are placed in the ranks of the reprobate."
- It's iniquitous," says Nagato, "don't we have the right to escape too much pain by dying?
- There is one religion that says no," murmured Fidé-Yori.
- That of the bonzes of Europe! the one whose doctrine you have embraced, according to public rumour," said Nagato, trying to read his friend's eyes.
- I have studied this doctrine, Ivakoura," said the siogun, "it is touching and pure and the priests who teach it are full of self-denial. While our monks seek only to enrich themselves, these priests despise riches. And then, you see, I cannot forget the terrible scene I once witnessed, nor the sublime courage of the Christians undergoing the horrible tortures my father had them subjected to. When I was a child, I was made to watch them being tortured to teach me, it was said, how to treat these people. It was near Nakasaki, on the hill. That nightmare will always trouble my nights. So many crosses were planted on the slopes that the hill seemed to be covered with a forest of dead trees. Among the victims, whose noses and ears had been cut off, were three young children, I can still see them, disfigured and bloody, who showed a strange fearlessness in the face of death. All the unfortunates were tied to crosses and their bodies were pierced with spears; the blood trickled down, but the victims did not complain; as they died, they prayed to heaven to forgive their executioners. Those present let out horrific cries, and I, frightened, screamed with them and hid my face on the chest of the Prince of Mayada, who held me in his arms; soon, despite the soldiers who pushed them back and struck them with their spears, the spectators of this horrible scene rushed up the hill to fight over some of the relics of these martyrs, which they left naked on the crosses.
As he spoke, the siogun continued to leaf through the book.
- Precisely", he said with a movement of fright, "here is the edict issued by my father ordering the massacre:
"I, Taïko-Sama, have condemned these men to death, because they came to Japan, claiming to be ambassadors, even though they were not; because they stayed on my lands without my permission, and preached the law of the Christians, contrary to my defence. I want them crucified in Nakasaki".
Fidé-Yori tore out this page and some following pages containing laws against Christians.
- I've found what needs to be taken out of this book," he said.
- You do well, master, to protect these gentle and harmless men," said Nagato, "but take care that the rumour that spreads from mouth to mouth and accuses you of being a Christian does not take hold and that your enemies do not use it against you.
- You are right, friend, I will wait until my power is firmly established before declaring my feelings and redeeming as much as I can the blood spilt before my eyes. But I am going to leave you, dear sick man, you are getting tired and the doctor has ordered you to rest. Have patience, your convalescence is coming to an end.
The siogoun left, giving his friend an affectionate look.
As soon as he had left, Loo finally got to his feet; he yawned, stretched and made a thousand faces.
- Come on, Loo," said the prince, "go and run around the gardens for a while, but don't throw stones at the gazelles or frighten my Chinese ducks.
Loo ran off.
When he was alone, the prince drew a letter from under his bedside table, enclosed in a green satin pouch; he placed it on his pillow, leaned his cheek against it and closed his eyes to sleep.
This envelope was the one given to him by Kisaki; he kept it like a treasure, and his only joy was to breathe in its light perfume. But, much to his chagrin, it seemed to him that for some days that scent had been evaporating; perhaps, being used to breathing it in, he no longer smelt it as keenly.
Suddenly, the prince sat up straight, thinking that inside the envelope, this subtle, delicious fragrance had no doubt been better preserved. He broke the seal, which he had not yet touched, thinking that the envelope was empty, but to his great surprise he pulled out a piece of paper covered in writing.
He shrieked and tried to read, but to no avail. A red veil shivered before his eyes and blood whistled in his ears; he was afraid of fainting and rested his head on the pillow. He managed to calm himself, however, and looked again at the writing. It was an elegantly combined quatrain. The prince read it with unspeakable emotion:
"Two flowers bloom on the banks of a stream.
But, alas, the stream separates them.
"In each corolla there is a dewdrop, the shining soul of the flower.
"The sun strikes one of them. It makes it shine. But she thinks: why am I not on the other side?
"One day, these flowers will bend down to die. They will let their luminous souls fall like diamonds. Then the two dewdrops will meet and merge.
- She's giving me a rendezvous," exclaimed the prince, "further on, later, in the next life. So she has guessed my love! So she loves me! O death, could you not make haste? could you not bring forward the heavenly hour of our reunion?
The prince thought he had been granted his wish, for he fell back on the cushions and lost consciousness.
XI
THE WARRIOR QUAILS
In a lovely landscape in the middle of a dense wood, the Kisaki's summer residence raises its pretty golden roofs.
The thick foliage of the prodigiously tall trees seems to recede reluctantly to make way for these brilliant roofs, which project all around the palace and shelter a wide verandah whose floor is covered with carpets and strewn with cushions of silk and satin embroidered with gold.
The view does not extend very far and the dwelling is as if enclosed by the freshly transparent vegetation. Slender emerald-coloured reeds float like streamers, their narrow leaves seeming to want to detach themselves from the stems and erect a silvery, fluffy plume.
Orange bushes blossom near the tall bamboo trees, their fragrant flowers mingling with the red blossoms of the wild cherry trees. Further on, huge camellias climb the trees; at their feet, broad red leaves covered with a fine down spread out next to tall heathers so delicate and light that they look like tufts of green feathers. Above this first layer of greenery, the palms, banana trees, oaks and cedars intertwine their branches to form an inextricable network through which the light filters, tinted with a thousand shades.
A stream glides slowly over a bed of thick moss, its flowing crystal slightly disturbed by a moorhen, with its charming plumage, which brushes against it as it pursues a dragonfly, whose slender body throws out shards of metal.
But more than the surrounding flowers, more than the velvet of the moss and the silvery gleam of the stream, the dress of the women occupying the verandah is brilliant and splendid.
The Kisaki, surrounded by her favourite wives and a few young lords, the noblest of the court, is watching a quail fight.
Because of the heat, the sovereign wears a light dress made of silk gauze the colour of mountain pigeon, a shade of green that only she can wear. Instead of the three golden blades of her crown, she has placed three daisies with silver petals on her hair. Above her left ear, from the head of a long pin stuck in her hair, hangs from the end of a gold chain a large pearl of rare beauty and perfectly round.
The Kisaki, leaning over the balustrade, is attentively watching the fierce battle between two quails that have already been fighting for a long time.
Two young boys, dressed in a similar costume that differs in colour, are crouching on their heels opposite each other, watching the duel of the pretty birds, ready to raise the dead and bring in new combatants.
- How little chance I have of winning," says a lord with a witty face, "I who have dared to bet against my sovereign!
- You're the only one who's had the audacity, Simabara," said the Kisaki, "but if you win, I'm sure that at the next battle, everyone will bet against me.
- He could well win," said the Prince of Tsusima, husband of the beautiful Iza-Farou-No-kami.
- How," cried the Kisaki, "am I so close to losing?
- Look, your champion is weakening.
- Courage, courage, little warrior! said the queen.
The quails, their feathers ruffled and their necks extended, stopped for a moment, looking at each other motionless, then darted forward again.
One of them fell.
- Ah, it's all over," cried the Kisaki, rising to her feet, "she's dead!
Simabara has won.
Young girls brought sweets and treats of all kinds, and tea from the nearby mountains, and the games stopped for a moment.
Then a page approached the Kisaki and told her that a messenger had been here for a few minutes, bringing news from the palace.
- Let him come", said the sovereign.
The messenger came forward and bowed.
- Speak," said the Kisaki.
- Light of the world", said the man, "the embassy of the Siogun has arrived.
- Ah," said the Kisaki quickly. And which princes are in it?
- The princes of Nagato, Satsouma, Ouésougui and Sataké.
- Good," said the Kisaki, gesturing to dismiss the messenger. These lords will be bored waiting for the day of the audience," she continued, addressing the princes gathered around her. "The Mikado, my divine master, is with all his wives and his entire court at the summer palace; the dairi is more or less deserted. Tsusima, go and fetch these princes and bring them here, so they can take part in our games. Have some pavilions prepared for them within the walls of the residence," she added, turning to her wives.
The orders were passed on inside the house, and the Prince of Tsusima, after bowing deeply, left.
The dairi was only half an hour's walk from the summer palace, so it would take an hour to get there and back.
- Prepare for another battle," said the Kisaki.
The birds called out the names of the combatants:
- The Golden Ergot!
- The Lightning Rival!
- The Golden Ergot is an unknown," said the sovereign; "my money's on the Lightning Rival; I think he's invincible.
de-Corail, who had slaughtered many of his opponents.
All those present bet with the queen.
- If that's the way it is," she said, laughing, "I'll bet alone against all of you; I'll join in the fortune of the Golden Ergot.
The fight began: the Lightning Rival dashed forward with the vivacity that had earned him his name. Usually, at the first impact, he would knock his opponent out of the fight, but this time he stepped back, leaving a few feathers in the beak of his antagonist, who had not been hit.
- Good! good!" cried everyone, "the Ergot d'Or is off to a marvellous start?
A few lords crouched on their heels to follow the fight more closely.
The birds came together a second time. But then nothing was seen but a confused ruffle of quivering feathers, then the Lightning Rival fell with a bloodied head, and the Ergot-
proudly placed one of his paws on the body of his vanquished foe.
- Victory!" cried the Kisaki, clapping her small milk-coloured hands together. The Golden Ergot is the king of the day, he's the one who gets the collar of honour.
One of the princesses fetched a black lacquer screen containing a gold ring enriched with rubies and coral, from which hung a small crystal bell.
The winner was brought to the queen, who took the ring between her fingers and placed it around the bird's neck.
Other battles took place, but the Kisaki, who was singularly distracted, hardly paid any attention to them; she was listening to the thousand shivers of the forest, and seemed irritated by the chirping of the stream, which prevented her from distinctly perceiving a very faint and distant noise. Perhaps it was the light clash of sabres passed through a lord's belt, the crunching of the sand in the paths under numerous footsteps, the sudden snap of a fan being bent and unfurled.
An insect or a passing bird would blur this barely perceptible sound.
However, it soon became clearer; everyone heard it. Murmurs of voices mingled with it.
- Here are the ambassadors," said Simabara.
Shortly afterwards, we heard the clanking of the weapons that the princes were stripping off before appearing before the sovereign.
Tsusima stepped forward from inside the house and announced the noble envoys, who in turn appeared and bowed before the Kisaki.
- Stand up," said the young woman, "and learn the laws that govern our little Court of Flowers. Ceremonial etiquette is banished, and I am regarded as an elder sister. Everyone is free and at ease and has no other occupation than imagining new distractions; here the watchword is gaiety.
The lords rose to their feet, were surrounded and questioned about the recent events in Osaka.
The Kisaki cast a quick glance at the Prince of Nagato; she was struck by the look of weakness in the young man's whole person; but she noticed a strange radiance of pride and joy in his eyes.
- He has read the verses I gave him, she thought. Am I mad to have written that!
But she beckoned him to come closer.
- Imprudent," she said to him, "why did you set off so weak, so ill again?
- You have deigned to protect my life, divine queen," said the prince, "could I delay any longer in coming to show you my humble gratitude?
- It is true that my foresight has saved you from death, but has not succeeded in preserving you from terrible wounds," said the queen; "it seems that all your blood has run out of your veins. You are as pale as these jasmine flowers.
She showed him a blossoming branch that she held between her fingers.
- You must have suffered a lot," she added.
- Ah, may I confess to you," cried Nagato, "that for me physical pain is a relief: there's another, more poignant wound, the one I'm dying of, which doesn't give me any rest.
- Why," said the Kisaki, hiding her deep emotion in a smile, "is this how you comply with my wishes? Have
have you not heard that only gaiety reigns here? Speak no more of death or sadness, let your soul relax amidst the fragrance of this beautiful and invigorating nature. If you spend a few days here, you'll see what a charming country life we lead in this retreat. Our simplicity rivals that of our ancestors, the shepherds, who were the first to pitch their tents on this soil. Iza-Farou", she continued, calling out to the princess who was passing in front of the house, "I want to hear some stories, call our companions back and put an end to their political conversation.
Soon all the privileged guests admitted to the sovereign's intimacy were assembled.
We entered the first room of the dwelling. The Kisaki moved to a very low platform, covered with carpets and cushions, and half lay down on it. The women sat on the left, the men on the right, and immediately servants placed a small golden tray on the floor in front of each of them, covered with sweets and warm drinks.
Through all the open panels, the fragrant woodland air wafted into this fairly large room, which was filled with a green half-light, a reflection of the nearby trees.
green half-light, a reflection of the nearby trees. The walls were marvellously decorated: fabulous animals, the foo bird, the unicorn and the sacred tortoise stood out against azure, gold and purple backgrounds, and a screen in turquoise and dead leaf cloisonné zigzagged behind the dais. There was no furniture, nothing but thick carpets, cushions and satin drapes embroidered with birds in gold circles.
- First of all," said the Kisaki, "I'm not going to say a word. I am invincibly nonchalant and lazy. Besides, I want to hear stories, not tell them.
There was a lot of grumbling about this decision.
- It's irrevocable," said the queen, laughing; "you won't even get a few words of flattery once you've finished telling your story.
- Never mind!" exclaimed Simabara, "I'll tell the story of the wolf who changed into a young girl.
- That's it! that's it!" cried the women; the title has our approval.
- An old wolf.
- Ah, is that wolf old?" said a princess with a disdainful pout.
- You know very well that to give shelter to a human soul, an animal has to be old.
- It's true! it's true!" someone shouted, "start!
- An old wolf," continued Simabara, "lived in a cave near a busy road. This wolf had an insatiable appetite, so he often came out of his cave, walked along the side of the road and snatched up a passer-by. But the travellers didn't like this, so they stopped using the road and little by little it became completely deserted. The wolf meditated deeply and looked for a way to put an end to this state of affairs. Suddenly he disappeared and was thought to be dead. A few daring people ventured onto the path and saw a beautiful young girl smiling at them.
- Would you like to follow me and come and rest in a fresh and charming place?
They could not refuse, but as soon as she was far from the road, the young girl turned back into an old wolf and mauled the travellers; then he resumed his graceful form and returned to the side of the road.
Since then, not a single traveller has fallen into the wolf's mouth!
The princes applauded loudly at this story, but the women recoiled.
- This means that we are dangerous traps hidden by flowers," they said.
- The flowers are so beautiful that we'll never see the trap," said the Prince of Tsusima, laughing.
- Come on," said the queen, "Simabara will drink two cups of sake for hurting the women.
Simabara happily emptied the cups.
- In the old days," said Princess Iza-Farou, giving Simabara a mischievous look, "there were many heroes: people talked about Asahina, who grabbed a fully armoured warrior with both hands and threw him away, about Tametomo and his formidable bow, about Yatsitsoné, whose only shield was his open fan, and about how their great adventures were the talk of the town. It was said, among other things, that one day Sousigé, the unrivalled horseman, was returning from a trip when he spotted several of his friends crouching around a checkerboard; he threw his horse over their heads and the horse stood motionless on its hind legs in the centre of the board. The players were stunned and thought the horseman had fallen from the sky. I've never heard of anything like it in these days.
- Well, well!" exclaimed Simabara, "you're suggesting that none of us would be capable of accomplishing such a feat of horsemanship, and that the time for heroes has passed.
- Indeed, that's what I wanted you to understand," said Iza-Farou, bursting out laughing; "shouldn't I have retaliated against your insolent wolf?
- She had every right to avenge us," said the Kisaki, "and she won't be punished in any way.
- Rosebud knows a story, but she doesn't want to tell it," exclaimed a princess who had been whispering with her neighbour for a moment.
Fleur-de-Roseau hid her face behind the wide sleeve of her dress. She was a very young girl, still a little shy.
- Come on, speak up," said the Kisaki, "and don't be afraid, we have nothing in common with the Simabara wolf.
- Well," said Rosebud, suddenly reassured. On the island of Yéso there was a young man and a young girl who loved each other dearly. They had been betrothed to each other from the cradle and had never left each other's side. The girl was fifteen, the young man eighteen. They were thinking of setting a date for their wedding. Unfortunately, the son of a rich man fell in love with the girl and asked her father for her hand in marriage. However much the young couple begged him, his father remained inflexible.
