So the bride went to her lover in despair.
- Listen," she told him, "since they want to separate us in this world, let death reunite us. Let us go to the tomb of your ancestors and kill each other.
They did as she said, they lay down on the tomb and stabbed each other, but the despised lover had followed them. He approached them when he no longer heard them speak. He then saw them lying side by side, motionless, hand in hand.
As he leaned towards them, two white butterflies rose from the grave and flew away, fluttering their wings.
- Ah," cried the jealous man angrily, "it's them, they're escaping me, they're fleeing into the light, they're happy, but I want to chase them across the sky.
Then he grabbed the abandoned dagger and struck himself.
A third butterfly flew up, but the others were already far away: this one never got to join them.
Even today, look above the flowers when spring returns and you'll see the two winged lovers flying past, very close to each other; look again and you'll soon see the jealous man who pursues them without ever reaching them.
- Indeed," says Iza-Farou, "butterflies are always grouped like this: two fluttering close together and a third following at a distance.
I had also noticed this peculiarity without being able to explain it to myself," said the Kisaki.
- The Prince of Satsouma must tell us something," said Rosebud.
- Me!" exclaimed the good old man, a little moved, "but I don't know any stories.
- Yes, yes, you do," cried the women, "you must tell us one.
- So I'm going to tell you about an adventure that happened a short while ago to the Prince of Figo's cook.
This statement provoked general hilarity.
- You'll see," said Satsouma, "you'll see that this cook has spirit. First of all, he is highly skilled in his art, which is nothing to sneeze at, and what's more, he takes great care over the smallest details of his service. A few days ago, however, at a feast I was attending, the servants brought a basin full of rice and uncovered it in front of the Lord of Figo.
What a surprise it was for him to see a black insect in the middle of the white rice, motionless because it had been cooked! The prince turned pale with anger. He sent for the cook and, using his ivory sticks, seized the vile insect and presented it to the valet with a terrible look on his face. There was nothing left for the unfortunate servant but to open his stomach as quickly as possible; but it seems that this operation was not to his liking, for, approaching his master with all the signs of the most lively joy, he took the insect and ate it, pretending to believe that the prince was doing him the honour of giving him a morsel of the meal. The guests laughed at this feat of wit; the Prince of Figo himself could not help smiling, and the cook was saved from death.
- Good! good!" shouted everyone in the audience, "now that's a story that hurts nobody.
- It's Nagato's turn," said Tsusima, "he must know some charming tales.
Nagato shivered as if he had been roused from a deep sleep. He had heard nothing, listened to nothing, absorbed in the delightful contemplation of the goddess he adored.
- Would you like a story?" he said, looking at the princes and princesses as if seeing them for the first time.
He thought for a few seconds.
- Well, here's one," he said: "There was a very small pond, born on a stormy day, it had formed on a bed of moss and violets, pretty bushes in flower surrounded it and leaned towards it the clouds, its parents, had not yet dissipated and already the birds were coming to brush its water with the tips of their wings and delight it with their songs; it was happy and enjoyed life, finding it good. But then the clouds parted, and something wonderful and dazzling appeared over the pond. It was transformed into a magnificent jewel box, but the clouds returned and the vision disappeared. What sadness and regret! The pond was no longer enchanted by the caresses of the birds; it despised the reflections of the flowers on its banks; everything seemed ugly and dark. At last, the sky became serene again, and this time for a long time. The luminous wonder reappeared; the pond was once again filled with warmth, splendour and joy, but it felt as if it were dying under the ever-burning golden arrows. And yet, if a light branch cast its shadow over him; if a fine mist rose and served as a shield! how he cursed them for delaying his delicious annihilation by one minute! On the third day, there was not a drop of water left - the pond had been drunk by the sun.
This tale plunged the princesses into a sweet reverie. The men declared that Nagato had created a new way of storytelling, and that this improvisation could have been put into verse.
The queen, who understood that the prince had spoken for her alone, glanced at him, almost in spite of herself, with a look of melancholy sweetness.
The day was drawing to a close. Two princesses came to kneel before the Kisaki, to take her orders for the next day's entertainment.
- Tomorrow," she said, after thinking for a moment, "we'll have a country lunch and a poetic battle in the Western Orchard.
They soon parted company and the ambassadors were led to the pavilions reserved for them, which were buried in greenery and flowers.
XII
THE WESTERN ORCHARD
When the Prince of Nagato awoke the next morning, he felt a sense of well-being and joy that he had not known for a long time. Enjoying that moment of nonchalant reverie that is like the dawn of awakening, he let his gaze wander over the leaping shadows of the leaves that the sun threw against the closed blinds from outside. Thousands of birds were chirping and twittering, and you would have thought it was the light itself that was singing in this sparkle of clear voices.
The prince thought about the happy day ahead.
It was an oasis in the arid, burning desert of his love; he pushed aside the thought of the next departure with its trail of sadness, and gave himself over entirely to the sweetness of the present; he was happy and at peace.
The night before, his mind full of memories and his heart full of emotion, he had realised that sleep would stubbornly elude him. So he had prepared a drink to combat insomnia.
He knew he was handsome, he had been told so a hundred times and women's eyes told him so every day. Hadn't this grace of body and face, this charm that emanated from his person, helped to attract the sovereign's benevolent attention? They therefore deserved to be spared the effects of fatigue and fever.
As soon as he had called the servants, the prince had a mirror brought to him and looked at himself in a hurry.
But the first glance reassured him.
His pallor had returned to the warm hues that the illness had taken from him, the blood had returned to his lips and yet his eyes still retained something of their feverish sparkle.
He paid childish attention to the details of his grooming, choosing the sweetest perfumes, the softest clothes, the light, vaguely bluish shades that were his favourites.
When he finally emerged from his pavilion, the guests had already gathered in front of the Kisaki's palace. His arrival caused a sensation; the men raved about his toilette, the women didn't dare speak, but their silence was most flattering, and could be translated as follows: this one is worthy of being loved, even by a queen, because this perfectly beautiful body is the temple of the most delicate mind, of the noblest heart in the whole empire.
Princess Iza-Farou-No-Kami approached Nagato:
- You haven't asked me about Fatkoura, Prince," she said.
The prince hadn't thought about Fatkoura at all, and hadn't even noticed her absence.
- She was ill yesterday," continued the princess, "but the news of your arrival has restored her health. She has been sad for some time, so perhaps your return will console her.
You'll see her in a little while, she's near the Kisaki, it's her week's duty. Well, aren't you going to say anything?
The prince did not know what to say; indeed, the name of Fatkoura aroused in him remorse and annoyance; he reproached himself for having inspired love in this woman, or rather for having seemed to respond to the love he sensed in her. He had used this false passion as a screen between the curious eyes and the sun of his true love. But he no longer felt strong enough to play the part of the infatuated lover, and instead of the compassion and friendship he tried to feel for his unfortunate victim, Fatkoura inspired only profound indifference.
The arrival of the Kisaki relieved him of the need to reply to Iza-Farou. The queen advanced under the verandah, greeting her guests with a gracious smile, and they all dropped to one knee.
As they had to climb a mountain and pass through narrow paths, the Kisaki had put on a dress that was not as loose as the one she usually wore. This glaucous dress was made of crepe, slightly wrinkled like the surface of a lake shivering in the wind; a wide belt of gold cloth tightened around the waist and formed a huge knot around the loins. A branch of blooming chrysanthemum was embroidered on one end of the sash. The queen wore large, finely worked blonde pins in her hair, and above her forehead a small round mirror surrounded by a string of pearls.
Soon, a magnificent chariot, drawn by two black buffaloes, pulled up in front of the palace. This chariot, topped with a roof and covered in gilding, resembled a pavilion. It was closed by blinds that the Kisaki had raised.
The princesses and lords took their places in norimonos carried by a large number of richly dressed men, and they set off joyfully.
It was a beautiful day, with a light breeze cooling the air, so the heat wouldn't bother us.
First we crossed the gardens of the residence. The carriage pushes aside the whimsical branches that flutter over the paths, making the butterflies fly and the flowers fall. Then you reach the wall surrounding the summer palace and pass through the high gate surmounted by the mikado bird, the Foo-Houan, the mythological animal that took part in the creation of the world. You then walk along the outside of the walls, taking a path lined with tall trees that leads to the mountains. It is here that the entire court descends to continue the journey on foot. Groups are formed, the servants open the parasols, and we cheerfully begin to climb the mountain. The Kisaki is the first to walk, as light and joyful as a young girl; she runs for a while, picking wild flowers from the bushes; then, when she has too much, she throws them on the path; conversations begin, laughter resounds, everyone walks as they please; Some of the lords take off their lacquered hats, which resemble round shields, and hang them from their belts, then fix their open fans under their hair, which has been twisted into a rope, so that it forms a canopy over their foreheads. 
From time to time, a gap in the bushes gives a glimpse of the city, which seems to expand as you climb higher; but you don't stop to contemplate it, as your first stop should be on the terrace of the temple of Kiomidz, i.e. the temple of Pure Water, from where the view is admirable.
On one side, this temple rests on prodigiously high wooden pillars that reach all the way down to the foot of the mountain; on the other, it backs onto a sheer rock face and houses a thousand-armed deity under its broad roof covered with blue porcelain panels.
On the pebble-covered terrace in front of the temple, folding chairs have been laid out for the noble strollers to rest and enjoy the beauty of the view.
They soon arrive and settle in.
Kioto stretches out before them, with its countless houses, low but elegant, surrounding the immense park of the Daïri
[1] , a lake of greenery from which rises here and there like an island, a broad and magnificent roof. You can follow with your eyes the clear line drawn by the walls around the park.
To the south of the town, a river, the Idogava, gleams in the sunlight.
The rich, well-cultivated plain stretches beyond. Another river, the Wild Goose, flows through the centre of the town, near the Nisio-Nosiro fortress, with its high ramparts and square tower topped by a roof raised at the edges.
Behind the town lies a semicircle of high hills covered with vegetation and temples of all kinds that climb the slopes and half disappear into the foliage and flowers. The lords show each other the temple of Iasacca or of the Eight Escarpments, the To-Tsé tower, with its five light roofs, the chapel of Guihon, which contains only a round metal mirror, and which is surrounded by a large number of pretty houses in which tea and sake are drunk; then down towards the plain, on the road leading to Fusimi, the colossal pagoda of the Daïbouds, very high, very magnificent, and which contains within its gardens the temple of the Thirty-Three Thousand Three Hundred and Thirty-Three Gods, a very long and narrow edifice. 
Strollers rave about the beauty of the site, delighted to lose themselves in the intricate network formed by the city streets, full of brilliant crowds, the enclosures and courtyards, which from above look like open boxes; With a single movement of their eyes, they cross Kioto; near the river, they see a large open space surrounded by a palisade, the manoeuvring ground of the horsemen of heaven, some of whom gallop within its confines, the embroidery on their clothes, their spears and helmets flashing with lightning. 
The deep green mountains bite into the vivid azure of the sky with their jagged edges, some of the more distant peaks have violet hues, and the atmosphere is so pure that you can clearly make out the little town of Yodo, linked to Kioto by the long ribbon of road that crosses the golden fields.
The Kisaki stands up.
- Let's not stop here for too long. Let's go higher up and drink the water from the Otooua waterfall, which, as the monks say, imparts prudence and wisdom.
- Isn't there a fountain whose water has the virtue of making you foolish and careless?" said Simabara; "that's the one I'd rather dip my lips in.
- I don't see what you'd gain from that," said a princess, laughing; "if the fountain you're talking about exists, you've certainly tasted its water.
If there were one that would make you forget your life and give you the illusion of a dream without waking up," said the Prince of Nagato, "I would get drunk from it.
- If I were you, I'd settle for the one that makes you cautious," said Fatkoura, who had not yet exchanged a word with Nagato.
This bitter, ironic voice made the prince flinch painfully. He said nothing in reply and hurried to join the queen, who was climbing a stone staircase carved into the mountain escarpment.
This staircase, lined with shrubs whose intertwined branches form a network of greenery above it, leads to the Otooua waterfall. You can already hear the sound of water rising from the rock through three cracks and falling from quite a height into a small pond.
The Kisaki is the first to arrive; she kneels on the grass and dips her hands in the pure water.
A young monk ran up holding a golden cup, but the sovereign waved him away and, putting her lips forward, sucked in the sip of water contained with great difficulty in the palm of her hand, then stood up and shook her fingers, a few drops falling onto her dress.
- Now," she laughs, "Buddha himself has no more wisdom than I do.
- You're laughing," said Simabara; "as for me, I believe in the virtue of this water, which is why I won't drink it.
We take a very rough path. The mere sight of it causes the women to cry out in alarm. Some of them declare that they will never venture down such a path, but the lords go first and hold out their closed fans to the most fearful, and we reach the top of the mountain. But then the cries of terror increase. There's a small torrent running ahead, skipping over the rocks, and you have to cross it by striding from rock to rock, at the risk of dipping your foot in the water if you're clumsy.
The Kisaki asked Nagato for the support of her shoulder and crossed. Some of the women followed, then turned round to laugh at those who didn't dare.
A young princess has stopped in the middle of the water, standing on a rock, clutching the voluminous folds of her dresses, laughing, though a little annoyed, she doesn't want to go forwards or backwards.
She only decided to take the wrong step when she was threatened with being left alone in the middle of the torrent.
It's only a few steps to the western orchard, surrounded by a hedge of tea bushes. The queen pushes open a louvered door and enters the enclosure.
It's the most enchanting place you'll ever see. Spring at this height is a little late, and while in the valley the fruit trees have already dropped all their flowers, here they are in full bloom. The plum trees covered in tiny white stars, the apricot trees, the apple trees, the peach trees with their pink blossoms and the cherry trees covered in crimson blossoms bend and twist over the undulating ground covered in thick turf, projecting their dark branches in all directions, their harshness contrasting with the fragility of their open petals.
In the centre of the orchard, a large carpet has been laid out on the grass, and a red satin drapery supported by golden poles flutters above. The food is laid out on this carpet in precious china.
The guests happily squat down in front of the trays laden with delicacies; the walk has given everyone an appetite. The women sit in two groups to the right and left of the Kisaki; the men sit opposite her at a respectful distance.
The most open merriment soon reigned among the noble gathering: laughter sprang from all lips; they chatted loudly and no one listened to the melodies played by an orchestra, hidden by a screen made of reed fibres.
Fatkoura alone keeps a sombre face and remains silent. Princess Iza-Farou looks at her stealthily with growing surprise, and from time to time at the Prince of Nagato, who seems absorbed in a gentle reverie, but never turns her eyes towards Fatkoura.
- What's going on?" murmured the princess, "He certainly doesn't love her any more, and I thought the wedding was so soon!
When the meal was over, the Kisaki got up: "Now," she said, "let's get to work, each of us inspired by nature to compose a quatrain in Chinese characters.
The group disperses under the orchard trees; each one isolates himself and reflects, some stopped in front of a branch in flower, others walking slowly, their gaze fixed on the ground or raising their head towards what we see of the sky through the constellations of white or pink flowers. A few indolent women stretch out on the lawn and close their eyes.
The fresh, cheerful colours of the costumes burst gaily against the greenery, adding yet another charm to the landscape.
Soon all the poets are recalled. The time allotted for devising the quatrain has passed. Everyone gathers and sits down on the lawn. Servants bring in a large bronze basin with sculpted dragons writhing on its sides, surrounded by fantastic branches. The basin is full of white fans, illustrated only with a faint sketch at one corner.
It's a clump of irises, a few slender reeds, a hut by a lake towards which a disbudded willow is leaning, a bird clutching a branch of an almond tree in blossom between its claws.
Each contestant takes one of these fans on which to write the verse piece. Brushes and diluted ink are also brought along. Soon the black characters line up in four vertical rows on the white of the fans; the poems are finished. Each poet reads his out loud.
Princess Iza-Farou begins: THE FIRST FLOWERS
"How fleeting is the moment in life,
"When we have only joys, hopes and no regrets!
"In spring, what is the most delicious moment?
"When not a single flower has yet wilted".
This poem was greeted with great approval.
When silence has been restored, Simabara speaks: THE LOVE OF NATURE
"I look up and see a flock of wild geese.
"One of them, who had been in the lead earlier, let herself be overtaken by her companions.
"There she is, flying behind the others. Why does she linger
Why does she linger like that?
"It's because from the heights of the sky she's contemplating the beauty of a viewpoint.
- Well done," cried the listeners.
Some of the princes repeated the last verse, shaking their heads with satisfaction.
Several more quatrains were read, and then the Kisaki recited her own: THE SNOW
"The sky is pure, the bees shiver above the flowerbeds.
"A warm breeze runs through the trees.
"It makes the plum blossoms fall abundantly.
"How pleasant it is to snow in spring!
- You are the master of us all," we exclaim enthusiastically.
What are our verses compared to yours!
- Our great poet Tsourai-Iouki never wrote a more perfect poem than this," says the Prince of Nagato.
- I was inspired by this poet," says the Kisaki, smiling with pleasure. But it's your turn to read, Ivakoura," she added, looking up at the Prince.
The Prince of Nagato unfolded his fan and read: THE WILLOW
"The thing you love most, that you love better than anyone could love it,
"It belongs to another.
"So the willow that takes root in your garden.
"It bends, pushed by the wind, and embellishes the neighbouring enclosure with its branches.
- The illustrious Tikangué could be your brother, says the Kisaki; there is no quatrain in his works superior to this one. I want to keep the fan that your hand illustrated, so please leave it to me.
Nagato approached the queen and, kneeling down, handed her the fan.
Fatkoura suddenly recited the quatrain she had been improvising:
"The pheasant runs through the fields, attracting attention with its golden plumage.
"It cries out as it searches for its food.
"Then he returns to his mate.
"And, out of love for her, he unwittingly reveals his place of retreat to men.
The queen frowned and paled slightly. A wave of anger swept through her heart, for she realised that Fatkoura, with this improvisation, was directing an outrageous slander against the Prince of Nagato and herself; she was insulting the sovereign with the fearlessness of a soul that has lost everything and is using despair as a shield against vengeance.
The Kisaki, feeling powerless to punish, was seized by a vague terror and tamed her anger. To understand the hurtful intent of Fatkoura's words, was it not to confess a guilty preoccupation, an interest unworthy of her majesty in the love that her beauty had aroused in the heart of one of her subjects?
She complimented Fatkoura in a very quiet voice on the elegance of her poem, and then had a page present her with the competition prize. It was a charming collection of poems, no bigger than your finger, as it was the fashion at the time for books to be as small as possible.
A few hours later, as the Prince of Nagato leaned on the edge of a terrace, gazing down from the mountain at the setting sun spreading crimson fragrances across the sky, the Kisaki woman approached him.
He looked up at her, thinking she wanted to speak to him, but she was silent; her gaze lost on the horizon and saddened, she maintained a solemn attitude.
The reflections of the West prevented her pallor from being seen. She was dominating a painful emotion and wanted to hold back a tear that shivered between her eyelashes and blurred her vision.
Nagato felt a sort of dread; he knew she was going to tell him something terrible, and he wanted to stop her from speaking.
- Queen," he said softly, as if to ward off danger, "the sky looks like a big rose leaf.
- It's the last petal of a day that's fading," said the Kisaki, "of a day that's fading into the past, but which our spirit will remember as a day of joy and peace, perhaps the last.
She turned away to hide the tears that were welling up in her eyes in spite of herself.
The prince's heart was clenched by inexpressible anguish; he was like the victim who sees the knife at his throat, and he dared not speak for fear of hastening the sacrifice.
Suddenly the Kisaki turned towards him
- Prince," she said, "I had this to say to you: you must marry Fatkoura.
Nagato looked at the queen with fear, he saw her eyes wet with tears, but full of a calm and irrevocable resolution.
Slowly he lowered his head.
- I will obey," he murmured.
And as she hurried away he hid his face in his hands and let out the sobs that were choking him.
XIII
THE THIRTY-THREE DINNERS OF THE MIKADO
The sublime Son of the Gods is bored. He is sitting cross-legged on a carpeted platform, between streams of gold brocade that descend from the ceiling and are gathered in large folds on either side.
A string of flats stretched out before the sovereign's eyes.
He thinks how majestic he looks, then yawns.
The hundred and ninth mikado, Go-Mitzou-No, although young, is excessively overweight, no doubt due to the almost constant immobility he maintains. His face is pallid, never touched by a ray of sunlight; several chins fold over his neck, the folds of his crimson robes crowd around him, the high golden blade rises from his forehead, and to his right are the insignia of omnipotence: the sword, the mirror, the iron tablet.
The Mikado finds life monotonous. All the actions of his life are regulated in advance and must be carried out according to meticulous laws. If he leaves the palace grounds, he is locked into a magnificent vehicle pulled by buffalo, but he suffocates in this tightly closed box and prefers to remain on his throne.
If he wanted to admire the flowers in the flowerbeds, he had to go to the gardens accompanied by a large retinue, and the annals of the kingdom record this event. Most of his time must be spent in meditation, but on the whole he meditates little, his mind is numb. When he does think, he is astonished by the strangeness of the ideas that buzz confusedly in his brain. Some of his thoughts are criminal, others buffoonish. These cheer him up, but he dares not laugh, knowing that he is being watched. He then tries to bring his mind back to heavenly things, but this tires him, and he returns to his fanciful dreams. Sometimes he is seized by an invincible desire to move about, to frolic, to jump; this is difficult to reconcile with the silent immobility that the descendant of the gods must maintain. One day, however, or rather one night, he mysteriously fulfilled his desire; he slipped out of his bed and, while all around him slept, he took a step of his own. No one has ever known this, or so he thinks. As he never sees anything but the bent backs of his subjects, he can truly believe that he is a superior species and that ordinary men walk on all fours. However, he finds that he is sometimes treated like a child. His bow and arrows have been taken away from him because one day, while several delegates from the Siogun were prostrating themselves at the foot of his throne, he shot an arrow at the noblest among them. Despite the irritation that sometimes boils within him, he does not dare to revolt; his inaction, the perpetual company of women who alone can serve him, have softened his courage, he feels at the mercy of his ministers, he fears being assassinated.
Sometimes, however, an immense pride invades him, he feels divine blood coursing through his veins, he understands that the earth is not worthy to be trodden by his feet, that men have no right to contemplate his face, he thinks of making the veils that separate him from the world even thicker. Then, the next moment, he imagines that perfect happiness would be to be able to run freely over the mountains, to work in the open air, to be the last of men; he is then seized by a vague despair, he moans, he complains. But we persuade him that his sadness is nothing more than nostalgia for heaven, his true homeland.
At this moment the mikado is ready to receive the envoys from Fide-Yori. They have come to express the latter's gratitude to the supreme sovereign who has conferred on him the title of siogun.
A blind is lowered in front of the throne, and then the princes are ushered in, rushing forward with their foreheads to the ground and their arms outstretched.
After a long wait, the blind was raised.
A profound silence reigns in the hall, and the princes remain face down on the floor, without moving.
The mikado looked down on them from his throne, musing to himself about the way the folds of their clothes had turned; about a sash that had been turned inside out and of which he could see the back; he thought that the insignia of Satsouma, a cross enclosed in a circle, looked like a skylight blocked by two bamboo slats.
Then he said to himself: What would they think if I suddenly started shouting in fury? I'd love to see them sit up straight with stunned faces.
After a few minutes, the blind was lowered again; the princes retreated backwards.
Not a word was spoken.
After the audience, the Mikado left the dais and was stripped of his cumbersome parade robes. Dressed in a simpler costume, he headed for the rooms where he ate his meals.
Go-Mitzou-No considers dinner time to be the most pleasant moment of the day; he prolongs this moment as much as he can. The mikado loves good food; he has a preference for certain dishes. When it came to his preferences, a terrible difficulty had arisen. The Son of the Gods could not reasonably focus his sublime mind on the details of cooking and indicate the dishes he wished to eat; nor could he submit to the whims of his cooks or ministers. After much thought, the Mikado found a way to reconcile everything; he ordered that thirty-three different dinners be prepared for him every day and that they be served in thirty-three different rooms. All he had to do was go through these rooms and choose the meal he liked.
Sometimes, after eating one dinner, he would go to another room and eat a second.
When he passed through the door of the first of the thirty-three rooms, twelve very noble and beautiful women greeted him. They alone had the right to care for him.
In the presence of the master, their hair must be untied and spread in the folds of their flowing dresses.
Soon the mikado sat down on a carpet in front of the dinner of his choice, and began to eat, but then the Kisaki entered unannounced. She too, in order to appear before the supreme lord, had to have freed her hair from all bonds; her beautiful black hair was therefore untied, waving all the way to the floor.
The mikado looked up at her in surprise; he hurried to swallow the morsel in his mouth.
- My beloved companion," he said, "I didn't expect to see you.
- My divine lord," she said, "I have come to tell you that I am shortly going to lose one of my wives; the beautiful Fatkoura is getting married.
- Very well, very well," said the Mikado, "and to whom?
- The Prince of Nagato.
- Ah! ah! I consent to the marriage.
- And which princess will you appoint to replace the one who is leaving me?
- I will appoint whomever you designate.
- Thank you, master," said the Kisaki, "I will leave your divine presence and ask you to forgive me for having dared to interrupt your meal.
- Oh, that's all right," said Go-Mitzou-No, who hastened to make up for lost time as soon as his wife had left.
XIV
THE FLIGHT HUNT
A few days after the reception of the ambassadors, at about the tenth hour of the morning, the hour of the snake, a young horseman was running at full speed on the road which leads from Osaka to Kioto.
At this time of day, the road is very crowded; beasts of burden, peddlers, men and women of the people pass each other all along the way. Farmers take the produce from their fields to the surrounding towns; they go to Fusini, Yodo, Firacca; goods of all kinds are transported from Osaka to Kioto: rice, salted fish, metals, precious wood, while Kioto sends the city siogun, tea, silk, bronze vases and lacquered objects.
The young rider took no notice of the congestion, handing the reins back to his horse and urging it on with his voice; besides, the road was always clear ahead of him, people hurriedly moved aside at the sound of his furious gallop and passers-by threw themselves to the sides of the road, which was lined here and there with houses built of beech wood.
The rider passed so quickly that, despite their efforts, the onlookers could not make out his face.
- It's a warrior," said someone, "I saw his weapons gleaming.
- It wasn't very hard to see," said another, "every movement he makes throws off flashes of light.
- He's a warrior of a high rank, I saw the golden straps of his command whip.
- Is he a general?
- Ask the passing swallow to go and see if the copper horns shine on his helmet. She's the only one who can catch up with this rider.
When he reached Kioto, the young warrior didn't slow down, he galloped through the town and entered the palace, asking for the envoys of the Siogun.
- They're at the summer residence," he was told, "or rather they're not. They are accompanying our divine Kisaki on the hunt; they left at sunrise.
- Which way is the hunt?
- On the shores of Lake Biva, at the foot of the mountains," replied the valet; "but my lord, would you like to join the illustrious hunters?
- Give me a horse," said the young man coldly, without answering the question.
He dismounted at the same time, and the servant led the exhausted horse away. Soon, two grooms brought in another horse, fully harnessed and full of enthusiasm.
The warrior got back in the saddle and set off again.
Lake Biva lies behind the chain of hills that envelops Kioto. To get there, you had to follow several valleys and take many detours. The young man could not always keep his horse at a gallop, because of the slopes to be climbed and descended. Sometimes, instead of following the winding path, he ran over the thick grass of the valleys to shorten the route. After an hour, he reached the shore of the lake, but then he didn't know which way to turn.
The lake, as blue as a sapphire, stretched as far as the eye could see; to right and left, small clumps of woodland, brown rocks, vast spaces covered in moss and heather, following one another indefinitely.
There was no trace of the hunt, no clue as to which direction to follow.
The young warrior did not seem to be moved by this circumstance; he made his horse climb an eminence and looked around. He then saw, in the middle of a bamboo grove, the roof of a small temple half-buried under the foliage.
He ran to the temple and, without dismounting, rudely struck the calling bell.
The noise woke up the temple guardian, an old monk with a bald head and a long, thin face.
He ran over and rubbed his eyes.
- Do you know which way the royal hunt is going?
- This morning I heard barking, neighing and laughter", said the monk, "but I didn't see anything. The hunters didn't come this way.
- That's because they've turned right", said the warrior, throwing a silver coin into the alms trunk covered with a bamboo trellis.
He galloped off again.
He ran for a long time, sometimes stopping to listen.
Finally, he heard some confused barking, even though the shore was deserted before his eyes. He stopped and looked around.
The barking was coming from the mountains; the galloping of horses could also be heard.
Suddenly, without transition, the noise was loud and violent. Black hounds emerged from a narrow gorge between the hills, soon followed by horsemen.
The whole hunt passed in front of the young man. He recognised the Kisaki by the veil of red gauze floating around her. Some of the princesses had a hooded falcon on their left fist; the lords, leaning forward, ready to throw arrows, held a large black lacquer bow.
As all the hunters had their heads raised and were looking high into the sky at a hawk chasing a buzzard, they passed by without spotting the young warrior. He galloped along beside them.
The dogs flushed out a pheasant, which came screaming out of a bush.
Another hawk was released.
As he ran, the warrior looked for the Prince of Nagato among the lords and approached him.
- Stop, Ivakoura," he shouted, "Fidé-Yori sent me to you.
The prince turned his head and shuddered. He stopped his horse. They stayed behind.
- Signénari! Nagato as he recognised the young chief.
What's happened?
- I bring grave and sad news," said Signénari. Civil war threatens us. Hiéyas has raised armies and is occupying half of Japan. With surprising speed, he has assembled considerable forces, far superior to ours.
The danger is imminent, which is why the master wants to gather all his servants around him.
- Alas! alas!" exclaimed Nagato, "the future frightens me, the country is going to be flooded with the blood of its own children. What does General Yoké-Moura say?
- Yoké-Moura is full of energy and confidence, and is calling a meeting of the war council. But another misfortune has struck us: the Prince of Mayada has ceased to live.
- He's dead, that dear old man," said Nagato, bowing his head, "the only one who never bowed to the invading power of Hiéyas! Had he been Fide-Yori's father, he wouldn't have loved him more than he did. It was he who, when Taïko died, brought him as a child to the Hall of the Thousand Nats and presented him to the princes, who swore loyalty to him. How many have betrayed him since that day! How many will betray him again! Poor Mayada, only you could command a little respect from Hieyas; now he fears nothing.
- He will fear us, I swear it," exclaimed Signénari with a heroic flash in his eyes.
- You are right, forgive me this moment of weakness," said the prince, raising his head. "I am so crushed by grief that this new sadness overwhelmed me for a minute.
The hunters had noticed the absence of the Prince of Nagato. Believing it to be an accident, the alarm was raised and the whole court fell back.
The prince was soon seen chatting with Signénari. They were joined, surrounded and questioned. The dogs barked, some horses reared; the falconers called back the birds that refused to obey and continued to pursue their prey.
- What's happened?
- It's a messenger.
- Is he bringing news from Osaka?
- Bad news!
Kagato led Signénari in front of the Kisaki.
The queen was riding a white horse covered in a network of pearls and adorned on the forehead with a silk tassel.
- This is the bravest of your soldiers," said Nagato, pointing to Signénari. He comes from Osaka.
Signenari bowed deeply, then resumed his grave and reserved attitude.
- Speak," said the Kisaki.
- Divine sovereign, it is with sorrow that I come to disturb your pleasures," said Signénari, "but I must inform you that the peace of your kingdom is threatened. He is preparing to attack Osaka in order to usurp the power entrusted to your servant Fide-Yori by the celestial Mikado.
- Is this possible?" exclaimed the Kisaki. Hiéyas would dare to commit such a crime! Is this man so soulless that, to satisfy his insatiable ambition, he would not hesitate to arm brother against brother and spill the blood of the sons of Japan on Japanese soil? Are you sure of what you are saying?
- The news reached Osaka last night by several messengers hastily sent by the princes, who are hastening to fortify their provinces. The daimio of Arima arrived at dawn this morning and confirmed the assertions of the messengers. Scouts were immediately sent to various points, and the siogun ordered me to call back his ambassadors as soon as possible to hold a council.
- Let's return to the palace," said the Kisaki.
The princesses whispered amongst themselves as they watched the young warrior.
- He's so charming!
- He looks like a woman!
- Yes, but what energy in his eyes!
- What coldness too! His quiet gravity is worrying and frightening.
- He must be terrible in battle.
- His heart must be as steely as his sword, so let's not look at him so much.
- Yes," said another, "a lot of women lose their minds over him; I've been told that he's so annoyed by the love letters and poems that are constantly being slipped into his sleeves, that he wears them slit open; that way the tender missives fall to the ground.
Nagato rode close to the queen.
- These events are going to delay your wedding, Ivakoura!
she said with a certain joyful emotion.
- Yes, Queen," said the prince, "and the hazards of war are so great that it may never happen. However, since Fatkoura is publicly my fiancée, I want her to go and live in my castle at Hagui, near my father, while we wait for the wedding; if I die, she will bear my name and be sovereign of the province of Nagato.
- You will be right to do so," said the Kisaki, "but death will spare you. I will make a wish that you remain safe and sound.
Nagato looked at her reproachfully. He didn't dare speak, but that look said everything he was thinking, it meant: "You know very well that death would be sweeter for me than the union you forced upon me.
The Kisaki, moved, turned her head away and put her horse down.
We returned to the Dairi.
When the mikado heard the news of the probable war, he seemed distressed, but he was also pleased: he did not like the regent, nor did he like the siogoun; although he was their sovereign lord, he had a vague feeling that they dominated him, he knew he was being watched by both of them, and he feared them. He was therefore happy to think that they were going to do each other the harm he wished them both.
The same day the envoys of Fide-Yori left Kioto and returned to Osaka.
XV
THE USURPER
In just two months, as Signénari had said, Hiéyas had become formidable; he had under his command an army that public rumour put at five hundred thousand men. The provinces of Sagami, Mikava and Sourouga, which belonged to him, had provided a considerable number of soldiers.
The lord of Ovari, the most devoted of Hiéyas' supporters, made all the able-bodied men in his principality take up arms, so that not a single labourer was left on his land. The prince of Toza had entrenched himself in a formidable manner on the large island of Sikof, situated to the south of the kingdom, opposite the bay of Osaka; from there he threatened the capital of Siogun.
Most of the sovereign lords of Japan, confident in the fortunes of Hiéyas, lent him their support and kept their armies at his disposal.
Hiéyas settled in Yédo, which was only a small town at the time, but whose strategic position had seduced him. Situated about halfway along the length of the large island of Nipon, at the end of a bay that cut deeply into the land, surrounded by high mountains, it was easy to fortify and, once fortified, impregnable. What's more, its position in the centre of Japan meant that, given the island's narrow width, it could cut off overland communications between the large island of Yéso, the northern part of Nipon and its southern part, in which Kioto, Osaka and the principalities of Fidé-Yori's supporters were located. In this way, half of Japan was isolated and forced to remain neutral or to take sides with Hiéyas.
The former regent had displayed unparalleled activity.
Despite his advanced age and failing health, he had travelled to all the places where he felt his influence was needed. Among the princes who were hostile to him, he pretended to still possess the power he no longer had and demanded the number of soldiers they were obliged to provide to the government in time of war. Then he would hasten to send these troops to distant points. If his enemies learned the truth, they would be unable to harm him.
But once he had carried out these bold plans and was in a position to begin the great struggle he wanted to undertake to usurp power, Hiéyas suddenly felt so weak, overwhelmed by fever and suffering, that he thought he was going to die. He hurriedly summoned his son, who was then staying at Mikava castle.
Fidé-Tadda, son of Hiéyas, was then forty-five years old.
He was a man of no great personal value, but patient and submissive to intelligences superior to his own. He professed boundless admiration for his father.
He rushed to his father's side, bringing with him his youngest daughter, a charming child of fifteen.
Hiéyas lived in a fortified castle that he had been building for many years in Yédo and which was not yet completely finished. From the room in which he was lying on thick cushions, he could see through the wide window opening the admirable Fouzi-Yama, whose snow-covered summit was billowing a light white smoke.
- Is that your daughter?" said Hiéyas, when Fidé-Tadda was beside him with the child.
- Yes, illustrious father, she's the younger sister of the siogoun's wife.
- Of the siogoun," repeated Hiéyas, shaking his head and sniggering.
She's very pretty, the little girl," he continued, after considering the young girl for a few moments, who was blushing and lowering her long black eyelashes to her cheeks.
Then he motioned for the child to be taken away.
- I may be dying, my son," he said when he was alone with Fide-Tadda, "that's why I sent for you; I want to give you my last instructions, to tell you what you should do when I'm gone.
Hearing her father speak like this, Fidé-Tadda could not hold back her tears.
- Wait! wait!" cried Hiéyas, smiling, "don't cry yet, I'm not dead and you'll see that my mind is not clouded as old Mayada would have you believe.
Listen to me and remember my words.
- Every word that comes from your mouth is to me as a fine pearl would be to a miser.
- I'll be brief," said Hiéyas, "I'm tired of talking. First of all, my son, you should know that the predecessor of Go-Mitzou-No, the present mikado, once honoured me with the title of siogoun. This was after the death of Taïko. I did not make a show of this title so as not to offend the friends of Fidé-Yori. I let the princes and the people get into the habit of calling me the regent. I didn't care what name was used to designate power, as long as the power was in my hands. But today, the title of siogun is of the utmost importance to me, because it is hereditary, and I can abdicate in your favour. You were talking earlier about siogoun. I am the siogun. It is true that Fidé-Yori has received the same title, and I did not remind his insolent advisors that this title belongs to me. I acted prudently. If I had been in their hands, they would have murdered me. But now I am waging this war, you should know, as the sole representative of regular power. I have embroidered on my banners the three chrysanthemum leaves that form the insignia given to me by the former Mikado; and it is in the name of his heir that I lead my armies. I act without his will, it's true; but as soon as I am victorious, he will approve of my actions.
Hiéyas was silent for a moment and took a sip of tea.
- But," he soon resumed, "death may surprise me, it threatens me, and my work must be finished after me.
That is why I abdicate today in your favour. You will remain at Mikava Castle, safe from the hazards of war, watching over your daughter, who may serve one of my projects, until the day when victory proclaims you the master of Japan; then you will establish your residence at Yedo, the best situated city in the kingdom.
Taïko-Sama, who was a man of genius, even though he was the son of a peasant, conceived the plan, as soon as he came to power, of making the sixty-one small kingdoms of which Japan is composed into a single kingdom with the Siogun as its head. One man's life was not long enough to see this project come to fruition. Nevertheless, Taïko undertook it with vigour, while carefully concealing his intentions. I alone was the confidant of his thoughts, and to this day I have not revealed them to anyone. When Taïko threw the princes into this war against China, which appeared to many to be an act of madness, it was to weaken the lords by an expensive war and to keep them away from their principalities for some time. While he led them into battle, I carried out his orders. I had the Tokaido built, the wide road that brazenly crosses the regions formerly under the sole control of the princes; I had the wives and children of the absent lords brought to Osaka, on the pretext of protecting them from all danger if, by misfortune, the Chinese army invaded the country. When the princes returned, the women were refused permission to leave. They were forced to take up permanent residence in Osaka, where they remain today, precious hostages who answer to the loyalty of the lords of the land. As Taïko was also a great man of war, victory crowned his risky venture and consolidated his power.
For a long time now, the Mikado had paid little attention to the affairs of the kingdom. Taïko thought it good that he should concern himself even less; he made his power illusory... Listen," continued Hiéyas, lowering his voice, "this power must be weakened still further; the mikado must have nothing more than the title of sovereign; heap honours on him, divide the kingdom and make him a king.
accuse him of honours, make him more and more divine, so that he raises his eyes to heaven and turns them away from earth for good.
Taiko has been interrupted by death in the accomplishment of his work, which has barely begun; the princes are still powerful and rich. Continue this work after me, divide up the kingdoms, sow discord between the lords; if two friends have principalities close to each other, forbid them to reside in their domains at the same time if they are enemies; instead, let them draw closer together. War will break out between them and one at least will be weakened. Always keep their wives in Yedo. Make ruinous luxury fashionable; the women will help you in this. Deplete the husbands' coffers, so that they are forced to sell their land. If, however, one of them is rich enough to be able to meet all these expenses, pay him a visit.
visit him, and may he be forced to spend his last scrap of gold in order to receive such an honour with dignity. Take care to keep Japan strictly closed to foreigners: the princes could form fearsome alliances with them. Therefore, do not let a ship from a distant land enter our ports. Seek out the Christians and slaughter them mercilessly: they are capable of fomenting insubordination and revolt. Do you understand me, my son? You must strive to make Japan a kingdom under a single master. But this goal will be difficult and long to achieve, and a man's life is short, which is why, when time has turned your hair white, you will call your son as I have called you today and pass on my words to him. I've finished.
- Father," said Fide-Tadda, kneeling before Hiéyas, "I swear to you that I will carry out your wishes to the letter.
- Good, my child, but send for the doctor," said Hiéyas, who was breathing heavily, suffocated by his long speech.
The doctor was ushered in.
- Illustrious scholar," said Hieyas, staring at him, "am I very ill?
- No, master," said the doctor with some hesitation.
- I order you to tell only the truth. Am I very ill?
- Yes," said the doctor.
- In danger of death?
- Not yet, but the life of fatigue that you lead may hasten your end.
- Will I be able to see the outcome of the war I am waging, supposing it lasts six moons?
- Oh yes!" said the doctor, "you could even prolong the war longer.
- Well, I'm rich," laughed Hieyas, "I don't need to hurry, I'm going to take a few days off.
XVI
THE FISHERMEN OF OSAKA BAY
An extraordinary commotion reigned in the castle of Fidé-
Yori castle. At any moment, military leaders covered in heavy armour pass through the gate of the first wall: the tread of their horses can be heard echoing under the deep vault.
They hastened to the third wall and entered the Siogun's palace.
Fidé-Yori, in a room next to the Hall of the Thousand Nats, was holding a council in the midst of the chiefs of his army and the princes most devoted to him.
The young Siogun's brow is worried; he does not hide his concern, which is shared by most of the warriors. However, some of them, full of ardour and faith, lifted the courage of their master.
- Our situation is not hopeless," says General Sanada-
Sayémon-Yoké-Moura, the most skilful warrior in the kingdom, but we must look at it coldly. Hiéyas has only one advantage over us: while we were not thinking of war, he has gathered armies; he is in a position to start the fight, we are not ready; but, in a few days, this inferiority will no longer exist; our troops will be on their feet and the game will become equal. For the time being, therefore, we must keep the enemy busy with insignificant skirmishes and keep them away from here, while we gather our forces around Osaka.
- For my part, I'm in favour of attacking Hiéyas immediately and not allowing him to take the offensive," said General Harounaga, a soldier of no great merit, but who had been quickly elevated by the active protection of Yodogimi, Siogoun's mother.
- Are you thinking about it?" exclaimed the young Signénari, "it would mean having our army massacred in a few hours by an army three times our size. We need to occupy the fortresses and protect ourselves from surprise until all our forces are united. If by then Hiéyas has not yet attacked us, it will be time to take the offensive.
- I stand by my proposal," said Harounaga. I have an idea that the army of Hiéyas is far from being as large as people imagine; how, in the space of one moon, could he have become so formidable?
- We can't act on suppositions," says Yoké-Moura, "and we're not in a position to attack; first and foremost, we need to build up our army.
- How many soldiers do we have at the moment? asked Fidé-Yori.
- Signénari, who has just been honoured with the rank of general despite his great youth, has twenty thousand men under his command; Harounaga has as many; Moto-Tsoumou and Massa-Nori each command ten thousand soldiers; Moritzka has fifteen thousand, and Yama-Kava five thousand. I am at the head of thirty thousand men. That makes a total of one hundred and ten thousand soldiers.
- How are we going to increase this army?" said the Siogun.
- You don't think, Master," said Yoké-Moura, "that the princes have not yet sent the troops that they are obliged to supply you with in time of war, and that these troops will at least triple the size of your army.
- You must not forget, however," exclaimed the Prince of Aki, "that certain provinces are directly threatened by Hiéyas or his allies and that these provinces will be forced to keep their soldiers or risk being immediately invaded.
- The provinces most at risk," said Signénari, glancing at a map, "are Satsouma, Nagato and Aki, because of their proximity to the principalities of Figo and Toza.
- What!" cried Fidé-Yori, "the Prince of Figo and the Prince of Toza have abandoned me?
- Alas, friend," said Nagato, "you didn't know; I had warned you of their treachery a long time ago, but your pure soul cannot believe in crimes.
- If this is so," said the Siogun, "the princes must guard their soldiers and go and lead them. You will have to leave me, Ivakoura.
- I'll send someone in my place," said the Prince of Nagato. I've decided to stay here. But let's not worry about that, let's hurry up and send our soldiers to their posts, let's not waste time with empty words.
- I agree with Yoké-Moura," said the Siogun, "that he should keep the enemy away from Osaka, while we finish gathering our forces.
- General Moritzka will leave immediately with his fifteen thousand men," said Yoké-Moura, "he will go to the province of Issé and inform the prince who governs this country of the defence plan. He should leave him five thousand men with orders to watch the movements of the lord of Ovari, his neighbour, and blockade his fortress, if possible. Then Moritzka will cross Japan in its width, and, leaving on the borders of the rebellious provinces the number of men he deems necessary, he will reach the principality of Vakasa and establish himself there. With the armies raised by the princes of this region, we will have around forty thousand men on the border.
Yama-Kava and his five thousand soldiers will camp on the shores of Lake Biva, behind Kioto, and the horsemen of the sky will join them and settle on the heights. Harounaga will lead his army to Yamasiro and cover Osaka from the north; Signénari will occupy the island of Avadsi, south of Osaka, and hold the treacherous lords of Toza and Figo at bay, whose attack would be most formidable at the moment. The rest of the army will remain in the vicinity of the city ready to be sent to the most threatened points.
- There is nothing to be taken back from the plan you have just outlined to us," said the Siogoun; "let it be done according to your orders and let us make haste.
The generals came one by one to kneel before the siogun, then left the room.
- Princes," said the Siogun to the lords who had remained near him, "return to your states. Those whose estates are threatened should keep their soldiers; the others should immediately send me all the men they can spare.
The princes came in turn to bow to the master: Satsouma, Ouésougui, Arima, Aki, Vakasa, then they left.
Fidé-Yori remained alone with Nagato.
- Ivakoura," he said, looking him in the eye. What do you think of this war?
- I think it will be murderous; but justice is on our side; even defeated we will be noble and glorious, and Hiéyas, even if victorious, will be covered in opprobrium. We have youth, ardour and strength. Hope is marching ahead of us.
- Thank you, friend, for encouraging me with your confidence, for my heart is full of worry.
- I'm leaving you, Master," said the Prince of Nagato. I'm going to organise my army.
- What do you mean by that?
- Do you think I'm going to sit here idle, useless? Do you think I'm going to watch the others kill each other and not get involved? I don't have soldiers but I will have them.
- Don't at least call up those from your province, don't let them invade your States.
- I'm not thinking about that," said the prince. I will not recall these soldiers, not because I want to keep my principality, but because my father lives in Hagui castle and my fiancée has just moved there to be with him: it is their precious lives that I want to keep safe behind the living rampart of my loyal army. Not one man will leave the province of Nagato.
- Well, where will you take this army you speak of? said the Siogun.
- That's a secret," said the prince; "when this army has performed some brilliant action, I will present it to you.
- I can't guess your plans," said Fidé-Yori, "but I'm sure that you will do nothing but noble and heroic deeds. Go, friend.
The Prince of Nagato returned to his palace, where he found a score of samurai, his vassals, gathered to do his bidding.
- Get your servants together and prepare your luggage; I'll let you know my wishes before sunset.
Nagato went back to his flats, but as he approached, a strange noise struck his ear.
- What's going on in my house?" he murmured.
He hurried into the room next to his bedroom.
He then realised that it was young Loo who was causing all the noise. He was armed with a chipped sabre and was circling a screen depicting life-size warriors. Loo was stamping his feet, uttering strange cries, insulting the motionless warriors and mercilessly thrusting his sword through them.
- What are you doing here?" cried the prince, half angry, half laughing.
Loo, at the sight of his master, threw down his weapon and rushed to his knees.
- What's the meaning of this?" continued Nagato.
Why are you tearing this piece of furniture to pieces?
- I was practising warfare," said Loo, in a voice he was trying hard to sound maudlin. This," he added, pointing to the screen, "is Ovari's castle with its soldiers; I was the army of the Siogun.
The prince bit his lips to keep from laughing.
- Are you brave, Loo?
- Ah, yes," said the child, "and if my sword cut, I wouldn't fear anyone.
- I think that if these warriors, instead of being made of silk and satin, were made of flesh and bone, you'd run like hell.
- Not at all!" exclaimed Loo, sitting back on his heels. I'm very mean and I've often got into fights; once I bit the ear off a warder because he wouldn't let me through, claiming it was too late; while he was calling for help, holding his ear, I jumped over the fence. Another day, I was chasing a stork that I had injured by throwing a stone at it. I grabbed its neck and bit it so hard that it ran away screaming. But I was angry with the dog because the stork had gone.
The prince reflected as he listened to Loo's stories; he remembered having heard about these adventures; they had been told to him and he had been advised not to keep this young servant at home.
- Would you like to go to war with me?" he said suddenly.
- Ah, my master," cried Loo, clasping his hands together, "I beg you, take me with you; I'm more flexible than a snake, more agile than a cat, I know how to slip in everywhere, you'll see that I won't be useless; besides, the first time I'm scared, you'll cut my head off.
- It's agreed," said the prince with a smile, "go and put on a very simple dark suit and be ready to accompany me. I shall need you this evening.
Nagato entered his room, while Loo, drunk with joy, frolicked away.
The prince was about to strike a bell to summon his servants, when he thought he heard a faint knocking under the floorboards.
Nagato went to close the open panels around the room, then returned to the spot on the floor where the noise was being heard; he lifted the mat and looked for a wooden knot on which he pressed his finger: part of the floor then parted, revealing a staircase leading down into the darkness. A man climbed the last few steps and entered the room.
At first glance, this man looked like Nagato; he was like a rough sketch of the perfect statue created by the prince.
- What have you been up to, my poor Sado? I'd forgotten all about you.
- I'm happily married," said Sado.
- Oh, I remember: the story of the princes disguised as blind men and the kidnapping of a whole family! You've got spirit.
This adventure kept the idle busy for a long time. But what do you
do you want from me? Are you short of money?
- Master, I've come to tell you that I'm ashamed of the life I lead.
- How can you have forgotten our agreements?
- No, my lord, I have forgotten nothing - I was a criminal, I was about to be beheaded when you pardoned me, because your illustrious father exclaimed when he saw me: This man looks like you, Ivakoura.
- I forgave you too," said the prince, "because, in my eyes, your crime was minor: you had avenged an insult by killing your enemy, that's all. But what were my conditions in pardoning you?
- To obey you blindly, to be devoted to you to the death.
That is what I have come to remind you of today.
- To what end?
- To the death..." repeated Sado, emphasising each syllable.
- Well, you're still alive, you're not released from your oath.
- Master," said Sado in a serious voice, "I am of noble origin, my ancestors were vassals of your ancestors, and until the day when anger made me commit a crime, not a stain had tarnished the lustre of our name. You saved me from death, and instead of making me expiate my fault by a hard life, which would have raised me in my eyes, you made my existence a continual feast. In your name I have performed a thousand follies, I have indulged in senseless luxury, I have enjoyed life, fortune and honours as if I were an almighty prince.
- Well, you were doing me a favour by doing my bidding, that's all. Your resemblance to me served to deceive my enemies and panic their spies.
- You have driven away your enemies today," continued Sado, "and my role as a young fool is over; but think, my lord, how useful I can be to you in the war that is beginning. Thanks to skilfully prepared make-up, I am able to make my face a fairly exact image of yours, I have become accustomed to imitating your voice and your walk, many of your friends know only me, and to them I am the true Prince of Nagato. What an advantage in battle to be two-faced! I'll draw the enemy in on one side, while you act on the other. They'll think you're here, but you'll be somewhere else. I accomplished my mission well when it involved being crazy and spilling gold, and I'll accomplish it even better when it involves being brave and spilling my blood for you.
- Your noble origin reveals itself to me in your words," said the prince, "and I esteem you enough to accept the offer you are making me; I know your skill in matters of war, it will be invaluable to us. But you should know that in this battle the dangers will be great.
- My life belongs to you, don't forget that, master, and if by chance I should one day die for you, the stain on my name will be removed.
- Well," said the prince quickly, "you are going to leave for my States, the neighbouring lords are seriously threatening them; you will lead my troops and defend the territory. But my supposed presence in my kingdom may attract many enemies. Hieyas hates me personally.
Whatever happens, you must uphold the honour of my name; remember that, to everyone, you are the Prince of Nagato.
- By imitating you, I have taken something of your soul," said Sado, "I swear to you that I will be worthy of you.
- I trust you," said the Prince, "I know how intelligently you have managed to fulfil the strange role I have entrusted to you, and all the adventures you have led in my name have ended in my honour. That is why I am giving you my full powers today. You will leave here, taking with you a large retinue, and it is I who will take the underground path; will you tell me what the exits are?
- There are two, master," said Sado: "one leads to an uninhabited fisherman's house on the banks of the Yodogava; the other to my wife's house. For, as I told you, I married a charming young girl whom I loved.
- What will become of her if you die?
- I place her under your protection, my lord.
- Make arrangements for her today," said the prince. I too can be killed and not return; my coffers are at your discretion.
- Thank you, magnanimous prince," said Sado, kneeling for a moment at Nagato's feet. Do you have anything else to recommend?
- Send the letter I'm about to write to the Siogun.
The prince took a sheet of bamboo fibril paper with a flowering vine on it and wrote quickly:
"Master, if anyone tells you that I have changed my mind and left for my States, beware of believing it, but let it be said.
"IVAKOURA".
He handed the note to Sado.
- Now," he said, "hide behind this screen for a moment, so that no one sees us together; when I am gone, you will do as I say.
- May happiness be your companion!" said Sado, hiding behind the screen.
- Thank you for your wish," said the prince, who sighed.
He went to pull out a sign and called Loo.
The young servant came running. He was dressed like a craftsman's child, but he had passed his sword to his belt.
He helped his master put on an unadorned suit, then the prince opened a chest and wrapped a considerable sum of money in his belt.
- Let's go, now," he said as he approached the underground passage.
Loo looked at the open trapdoor without showing the slightest sign of surprise. A lighted lantern was placed on the last step; he took the lantern and began to descend; the prince followed him and closed the trapdoor. They then descended fifty steps and found themselves in a small crossroads from which two narrow corridors led off.
The place smelt of damp earth and was bitterly cold.
- Which way are we going, Master?" asked Loo, looking at the two diverging roads.
The prince took his bearings for a moment.
- Let's turn right," he said.
They entered the narrow gallery, supported at intervals by wide black wooden beams, and walked for about half an hour until they reached the foot of a staircase which they climbed. This staircase led to the only room in a fisherman's house.
- We've arrived," said Nagato, glancing around.
The room was deserted and almost empty, a few blackened nets forming a kind of drapery on the walls; in one corner a small boat lay on its side.
- It's not nice here," said Loo disdainfully.
The door was closed on the inside by an iron bar, so Nagato lifted it and slid the panel into its groove.
The sun had gone down and night was rising fast, but the sky was still purple and bloody over the river. A few large boats could be seen moored near the banks, other boats were coming in from the sea, the sailors were lowering their woven reed sails, the sound of the ring sliding down the mast could be heard; a few fishermen climbed the steep staircases and dragged their wet nets back to their homes.
The large square-shaped lanterns on the fronts of the tea houses were already being lit; joyful clamour was beginning to escape from their gardens and open rooms.
The prince, followed by Loo, made his way towards the noisiest of these establishments, but to his great surprise, when he entered the gallery, which was already full of people, he was greeted by enthusiastic cheers.
- It's my brave Sado who's made me so popular," he thought.
he thought.
- The lord! The lord!
- Bring on the sake! Let's fan the barrels! The daimio wants us to get drunk!
- We will be! we will be! so drunk that we can't tell the moon from the sun!
- But we need lots and lots of sake! Then we can sing the ancient song of Daïnogon-Ootomo.
They all sang along to the song:
"Is there anything in the world more precious than sake?
"If I were not a man, I would like to be a barrel.
However, a sailor, naked to the waist and with a broad, unattractive face, approached the prince.
- We'll drink later," he said. The last time we saw each other, you split my cheek with a punch; I want to give you a rib or two and then we'll be friends.
- Do you know who you're talking to?" shouted Loo furiously, rushing towards the man of the people.
Loo pushed him away, but the child grabbed his arm and bit him until he bled.
The sailor cried out in pain.
- It's a wolf, that one! he shouted.
And he ran at Loo with his fists raised, but the prince grabbed him by the wrists.
- Leave this child alone," he said, "let's fight if you want. What's your name?
- Don't you know my name?
- I've forgotten it.
- It's all right for a prince to forget the name of an ordinary sailor," cried everyone, "his name is Raïden, like the god of storms.
- Well then! Raiden," said Nagato, "let's fight, since you hold a grudge against me.
- Let me go first," said Raiden, who was making a vain effort to free himself.
The prince let go. Then the sailor, clenching his fists, watched his adversary for a moment, then rushed at him; but Nagato, with one sudden and violent gesture, sent him rolling with his legs in the air amidst a great clatter of broken china, among the cups and flasks laid out on the floor.
All present burst into laughter.
If the prince doesn't pay, you'll have to sell a lot of fish to make up for it.
- I'll pay," said the prince, "but speak up, Raïden, do you want to continue the fight?
- No, thank you," said Raiden, "I've fallen into boiling tea, and it's cooking me; besides, you're even stronger this evening than usual, I'd be beaten.
- Sake! Sake! now that the quarrel is over, said those present. Tell me, Prince, how are we going to enjoy ourselves this evening?
- Let's drink first," said the prince, "today is hardly the time for merriment; sad news is circulating in the castle, anxiety is in everyone's heart, for civil war is about to break out; the follies we used to do are no longer in season, any more than flowers and foliage are when the first gust of winter blows.
Sake had been brought in. There was a great silence; all eyes were fixed on the prince.
- I've come to talk to you, who have sometimes been my companions in pleasure," he continued. You love to fight, you are brave, you are strong; would you like to be my companions again and fight under my orders, with the enemies of Fidé-Yori?
- We certainly would!" cried some of the sailors.
- But what would become of our wives and children?
- Who would feed them in our absence?
- You know very well that gold flows from my fingers like water from a fountain. I won't make you leave your job and risk your life without paying you handsomely. How much does a sinner earn in a day?
- That depends: on a bad day, when the sea is merciless, you don't earn even an itzibou; good catches sometimes earn up to half a kobang.
- Well, I'll pay you half a kobang a day for as long as the war lasts.
- Our blood isn't worth that!
- I won't back down," said the prince.
- But just think," cried Raïden, "there are so many of us, and if you hire us all at that price, the total will be considerable!
- I know how to count," said the prince, smiling, "I need two hundred men, that'll be a hundred kobangs a day, three thousand kobangs a month, thirty-six thousand kobangs a year.
Raiden's eyes widened.
- Where will you find so much money?
- You have no idea of the fortunes of princes," said Nagato, astonished by this strange debate; "I'll hardly notice this expense, so don't have any scruples.
- Fine! fine! if that's how it is, we accept," cried the sailors.
- For that price you can have us cut into fifty pieces," said Raïden, who had not yet recovered from his amazement.
- You will be running great risks," said the prince, "and you will have to be devoted and fearless.
- Those who fight with the sea no longer fear men," said a sailor; "we are used to danger.
- Listen," said Nagato, "you will choose fifty of the best and strongest of your boats; you will change nothing to their peaceful appearance; you will leave them equipped with their fishing gear and keep them ready to put to sea at the first signal.
- Agreed," said Raïden.
- I will provide you with weapons," continued the prince, "but you must hide them carefully; you must look like fishermen and not warriors.
- Very well! We understand," exclaimed Raïden, who was standing with his arms folded, listening attentively to the prince.
- I have nothing else to command you at the moment," said Nagato, "but keep our agreements secret.
- We won't even tell the seagulls that fly over the sea.
- The prince opened his belt and poured a heap of gold onto the floor.
- The pledge begins this very day for those present here," he said, "and I am going to give each of you a hundred kobangs. You will choose from among your companions the number of men necessary to complete my small troop; hire the bravest and the most discreet.
- Sailors don't talk much," says Raïden.
- Fishermen especially, noise frightens the fish.
- Come on, Loo," he said, "the prince has come to count the money.
Loo approached and began to arrange the small gold blades in piles.
Each man came forward in turn and said his name, which Nagato was writing on a long sheet of paper.
The prince gazed with pleasure at the naïve and fearless faces of these men who had just sold their lives to him; he told himself that rarely at court had he met the loyal gaze that he saw shining there in all the eyes.
Most of these men were bare-chested, showing off their strong muscles and laughing with pleasure as they took the money.
Soon the prince left the teahouse and made his way up the banks of the River Neuve. For a long time afterwards, he heard the laughter and voices of the sailors drinking sake and singing at the top of their voices the song of Daïnogon-Ootomo Loo, who had heard it for the first time, tried to remember it, and hummed it as he walked behind the prince:
"If I were not a man, I should like to be a keg!"
XVII
THE ISLAND OF THE DRAGONFLY
The beautiful Yodogimi is shedding tears. She is standing, leaning against a black lacquer panel, one arm raised in pain, her fingers lightly clasped on the smooth, shiny wall, her head tilted back, slightly tilted towards her shoulder: she is crying without forgetting that she is beautiful.
Yodogimi is nearly forty: who would believe it to see her looking so charming? Her very large eyes are still full of sparkle, her lips are fresh, her complexion is pure and the single twist of her hair rolls down to the ground like a black snake.
The princess, as is her custom, is magnificently adorned; a prize belt clasps her slender waist and the embroidery on her dress is of marvellous workmanship.
A few steps away from her, General Harounaga, her lover, is standing in full war costume, whip with golden straps in hand; he is looking intently at the floor and would like to bring a tear to the edge of his eye, but he cannot.
From time to time, he lets out a deep sigh.
- Alas! alas!" cries Yodogimi, "you are going to leave, forget me, perhaps die!
- I can die," said the general, "but I cannot forget you.
- To die! Have you no heart, then, that you dare speak to me of your death? Men are cruel; they swear to be devoted to you, and then for nothing they abandon you.
- It's not my fault there's a war on, I have to leave for Yamasiro with my soldiers.
- What if I ordered you to stay?
- I would disobey you, Princess.
- You shamelessly admit it. So I forbid you to leave!
- So be it," said the general, "I don't know how to resist your wishes; but this very evening I'm going to slit my stomach.
- Because you're bored of staying with me?
- No, because I will be dishonoured, and one should not survive dishonour.
- Ah, I'm mad," said the widow of Taïko-Sama, wiping her eyes; "I'm talking like a child, I advise you to be a coward.
Go on, spare no blood; if you die, I shall die too.
How handsome you look in battle dress! she added, looking at him with complacency. So you dress up like that for the enemy?
- It's customary," said Harounaga, "besides, arrows bounce off these horn scales and sabre blows can't damage them.
- Don't talk like that, I feel like I'm in the middle of battle," cried Yodogimi. I can see the arrows flying and hear the clash of iron. What will become of me during these long days of worry?
- Yamasiro is not far from Osaka," said the general, "I will send you news from the camp often.
- Yes, won't you? Send a messenger every day.
- Let him bring me a word from you every day. Farewell, most beautiful princess.
- Farewell, intrepid warrior. May heaven grant that we may meet again soon!
Harounaga rode away, and as he crossed the palace courtyard, Yodogimi leaned out of the window to see him again.
The page holding the warrior's horse told the general, as he helped him into the saddle, that the most worrying news was circulating in the castle. The vanguard of the enemy army had been seen at Soumiossi, that is to say a few leagues from Osaka; the troops of the siogun had therefore not succeeded in blocking the whole width of the island of Nippon, as had been planned.
Harounaga hurried to join his army corps, which was waiting for him outside the castle walls, ready to leave.
Several horsemen galloped out to meet him. The siogun had just arrived at the camp and was asking for Harounaga.
- Don't go to Yamasiro," he told him as soon as he saw him, "go to Soumiossi and try to crush the rebels, if it's true that they are already established there.
- I'm on my way, master," said Harounaga, "and I swear to be victorious.
A few moments later, he left Osaka with his army.
At the same time, several fishing boats, taking advantage of the tide, left the port and, pushed by a strong breeze, made their way out to sea.
It was Nagato's flotilla.
The prince was one of the first to learn of the appearance of Hiéyas' soldiers at Soumiossi. He immediately decided to set sail and sail to the threatened area.
Each boat was manned by four men; the one Nagato was in had an extra man: Loo. Loo had caught a few fish and was watching them writhing and dying with naive cruelty. Raïden was at the helm.
The prince, lying at the bottom of the boat, gazed vaguely above him at the big brown sail, creaking as it swelled, and the tangle of ropes; he was dreaming. The same dream, always, filled his soul; it was like the sea that eternally reflects the sky. Every event, every action painfully worried the prince, saddened him; they were clouds veiling his love, preventing him from absorbing himself entirely in it.
