However, his noble character urged him to devote himself to his lord, to shed his blood for him, to save him, if possible; but, in spite of himself, he often forgot the war, Hiéyas, the intrigues, the crimes, just as silence, when it returns, forgets the clamour that had disturbed it for a moment. He would then think of a look falling on him, an inflection of his voice, a fold of veil lifted by the wind and brushing against his lips; he would rediscover the shiver that this light contact had sent through his blood. He thought for a moment that perhaps
Perhaps she too was thinking of him, and he pursued this wandering thought through space.
The waves gently rocked him and encouraged him in these wild reveries; the wind blew, the swollen sail resembled an enormous crescent; the water, quickly pushed back, lapped at the bow.
- It's not to distance myself from her," he murmured, "that I've embarked on this singular adventure. I am counting on chance to provide me with opportunities to serve my prince, because if I were asked to explain my plan of campaign I would be very embarrassed. I have no other aim than to move to the most perilous points, fight furiously and then move away without making myself known. According to General Yoké-Moura, however, a small independent cohort, appearing in the middle of a battle, can sometimes tip the scales of victory and render great services... I remember this at the right moment to justify my conduct," added the prince, smiling.
The fifty boats making up the flotilla were scattered across the sea; Loo said they looked like a swarm of butterflies about to drown.
Towards the middle of the day, we approached the coast. Soumiossi was only a short distance away and Nagato wanted to go ashore to try and gather more information about the enemy army.
A small cove sheltered the boats that landed, most of which stayed out to sea, but only twenty men went ashore with the prince. They reached a road that ran a hundred paces from the sea and set off in search of a village. They walked for some time, but suddenly those who had been ahead and had already turned a bend in the road hurried back.
- A daimio! A daimio! they shouted.
- Well, what does it matter," said the Prince of Nagato.
- If we block the road, they'll either walk over us or cut off our heads," said Raïden.
- Go and see, Loo," said the prince, "what name is written on the post by the side of the road; if the lord whose passage it announces is less noble than I, we will throw the post to the ground, and, although I have no retinue, the prince will make way for me.
Loo, after looking around for a moment, ran towards one of the posts that the lords have planted on the roads they have to travel, to indicate the day of their passage.
The child soon returned with a stunned look on his face.
- It's you, master, who'll be passing this way," he said.
- How?" said the prince.
- It's written on the board," said Loo, "The almighty Ivakura-Teroumoto-Mori, Prince of Nagato, will pass through this region on the tenth day of the fifth moon.
- Silence, Loo," said the prince, "don't be surprised by anything and be discreet... it's Sado who's going to my States," he added to himself.
- Already, in a light cloud of dust, the forerunners of the procession turned the corner of the road.
They were valets, scribes and cooks carrying all sorts of utensils.
The sailors knelt at the side of the road; the prince hid behind a hedge of rosehips.
The first group passed, followed first by a score of horses laden with boxes and packages wrapped in red leather, then by a large number of men carrying pikes, banners, swords, bows, quivers and parasols.
A crowd of servants then came forward; each man carried on his shoulder a varnished chest containing clothes or some object for the prince's use.
Then appeared, in succession, officers holding luxury weapons and the princely lances adorned with cock feathers or leather straps; grooms driving richly harnessed horses; a samurai, followed by two valets, holding on his arms the hat under which the prince shelters himself from the sun when he sets foot on the ground; another lord carrying a parasol in a black velvet sheath; behind them, the servants and luggage of these lords paraded silently.
Then appeared twenty-eight pages in round hats, preceding the prince's litter. These pages moved in a peculiar way; at each step they threw one of their feet backwards, raising it as high as possible, and at the same time threw one hand forward, as if they wanted to swim.
At last, the lord's norimono approached, carried by eight men who took small steps forward, supporting in the palm of their hand the single stretcher passing like a bow over the palanquin, and whose other outstretched hand seemed to want to impose silence and express respectful fear.
The walls of the norimono were covered in black lacquer spiked with gold nails, bearing the insignia of the sovereign of Nagato: three balls surmounted by a bar. The interior of this large chest was lined with brilliant silk fabrics, and on a mattress covered with a velvet carpet, the prince was lying down, leafing through a book.
The norimono passed and the procession ended with a crowd of squires, pages and banner-bearers marching in perfect order and in the deepest silence.
- The truth is," said Raïden, who stood up and rubbed his dust-stained knees, "this is all very nice, but I'd rather be just a sailor and walk as I please without all this cumbersome paraphernalia.
- Be quiet," said another, "you'll upset the lord.
- He undoubtedly shares my opinion," said Raïden, "since, as a prince, he became a sailor.
They headed for the next village, and before they had questioned anyone, they had more than enough information about what they wanted to know. Several neighbouring villages had immigrated to this one. The streets were packed with people, carts and cattle. A tremendous hubbub arose from this crowd of men and animals. The frightened buffaloes bellowed and crushed each other; the pigs, which were tripped over, uttered high-pitched howls; the women moaned, the children cried; and the story of events, repeated over and over, ran from group to group.
- They've taken Dragonfly Island!
- Opposite Soumiossi, you can see them from the coast. The islanders were unable to flee.
- They came on three war junks, three beautiful junks, gilded in places, with very high masts and banners floating on all sides.
- Is it the Mongols?" asked a few old men, who had vague memories of ancient wars and foreign invasions.
- No, it's the regent who wants the Siogun to die.
- How many soldiers have landed on the island?
asked Kaïden, who had slipped through the crowd.
- We don't know; but there were a lot of them; the junks were all full of them.
- About fifteen hundred men," said Raïden.
- It's the vanguard of the Hiéyas army," said Prince de Nagato in a low voice; "if Fidé-Yori's troops don't arrive soon, Osaka will be in the greatest danger. Let's take to the sea again," he added, "I have a plan which, although madly audacious, may succeed.
Before leaving the village, Nagato ordered Raïden to buy a large number of carpenter's tools. They then made their way to the beach and reembarked.
Towards evening, the small fleet arrived in sight of Soumiossi and took shelter behind a promontory that completely masked them.
The place was admirable. Enormous trees, their uncovered roots clinging to the earth and rocks like the talons of birds of prey, leaned out to sea; bushes and shrubs crumbled towards them with clumps of their superb flowers; the waves were all strewn with flying petals, which sailed away, gathering in islets or long garlands. On some of the sharp rocks the waves leapt, throwing up a white foam; seagulls flew in, looking like foam made into birds. The water was a uniform shade of blue satin, glazed with silver, of incomparable softness and brilliance; and the sky retained from the vanished sun an expansion of fluid gold that still dazzled. In the distance, the island of Dragonfly, green and fresh, cut its insect-like contours. The coast of Soumiossi, all ruddy, stretched out with its jagged cliffs, and at the top of the promontory a small pagoda raised its pointed roof, paved with porcelain, and whose angles seemed to be raised by the four chains attached to a golden spire.
The prince thought of another sunset, the one he had seen from the top of the mountain, near Kioto, with the queen at his side; he closed his eyes and saw her again, so beautiful, so noble in her silent admission of her grief, her eyelashes shining with tears, turning her pure gaze towards him and ordering him to marry his rival. The smallest details of her words and gestures, the little mirror above her forehead casting rays like a star, were engraved in his mind with astonishing clarity.
- It was a painful moment, he said to himself, and yet it seems to me, from the memory, that it was full of charm. She was there at least, I could see her, I could hear her, the sound of her voice was a balm to the cruelty of her words, but now what a pain to live, time is like a boundless sea, where no rock, no ship's mast allows the exhausted wing to rest for a moment!
Three very light canoes were put out to sea, barely protruding above the water. As soon as night had fallen, Nagato chose eight of the most intrepid men, keeping Raïden and another sailor called Nata with him. They climbed into the canoes, three men in each boat.
- If you hear gunfire, come and join us," said the Prince of Nagato to those who remained.
And the three canoes sailed away without a sound.
Those on board were armed with swords and daggers, and they were carrying tools bought in the village, as well as several rifles. These weapons, of foreign invention, were often damaged or imperfect, and most of the time either failed to fire or exploded in the hands of the soldier, so they were usually equally feared by those who used them and those they threatened. The prince had succeeded in obtaining fifty new, well-made rifles, which was a great strength for his small army; however, the sailors looked at these foreign devices out of the corner of their eye with a certain contempt.
The boats glided through the shadows, steering straight for Dragonfly Island. The sound of the oars, handled with care, mingled with the thousand muffled clamourings of the sea. A light breeze picked up and whistled in our ears.
The closer we got to the island, the quieter we tried to move forward.
Fires could already be seen between the trees; we weren't far off, because the ear could clearly hear the regular steps of a round passing close to the shore.
The prince ordered them to sail around the island and look for the war junks.
They were at anchor a short distance from the shore, with the island of Dragonfly between them and the coast of Soumiossi.
Soon they appeared to those in the canoes, their large hulls and tall masts outlined in black against the less intense darkness of the sky; placed almost flush with the water as they were, these junks seemed gigantic to them. On each of them a lantern shone at the foot of the mast, masked from moment to moment by a sentry who came and went on deck.
- Those sentries will see us," said Raïden in a low voice.
- No," replied the prince, "the lantern lights up the place where they are and prevents them from making out anything in the darkness where we are. Let us approach now, and may our mad venture end in glory!
The three boats moved away from each other, and each of them, without making more noise than a gull gliding on the water, went alongside one of the ships.
The canoe carrying the prince had approached the largest of the junks, which was positioned between the other two.
The shadows were gathering even thicker under the bulging sides of the ship, the black water lapping, causing the light boat to bang against the giant hull; but the noise mingled with the incessant shock of the water and the continual fall of wave after wave onto the shores of the island.
- Let's stay here," said the prince in a voice that was barely audible, "no matter how much we lean over the top of the ship, no one will be able to see us.
- That's true," said Raïden, "but we won't be able to do anything here, the boat isn't stable enough; if we could reach the bow of the ship, we'd be more at ease.
- Come on," said the prince.
The three of them knelt in the boat, pressed their hands against the junk and moved forward rapidly; sometimes an involuntary bump, which seemed to make a terrible noise, made them stop, then they set off again. They reached the bow of the ship.
At that moment the sentry shouted:
- Oho!
He was answered from the other junks:
- Oho!
- Oho!
Then all fell silent.
- Let's get to work," said Nagato.
It was simply a question of sinking these large vessels by making a wound below the waterline wide enough to allow the water to flood them.
- What the reef accomplishes with the greatest ease, we might be able to do with a little effort, the prince thought.
The tools that had been used to build the ship's hull could usefully be employed to demolish a fragment of it. All we had to do was make an opening wide enough to stick our fist through, or lift a plank, and the water, which is only too happy to enter and slip through, would be happy to do so.
Raïden, leaning out of the dinghy, felt the slimy, water-covered walls of the ship and looked under the sticky foam, tar and paint for the heads of the nails fixed into the wood.
The prince and the sailor Nata were trying to keep the canoe more or less still.
Raïdcn took a tool from his belt and, with great effort, popped a few nails.
- This ship is solidly built," he said, "the nails are as long as sabres, and what's more, they're rusty and stick in the wood like big teeth in a young jaw.
- Do you think you'll be able to get the job done?
- I hope so," says Raïden. It's impossible for a lord like you to have gone to all this trouble for nothing. It's just that I'm not comfortable in this position, with my head down, and forced to pull the nails at an angle: I have to get into the water.
- Have you thought about that?" says Nata, "the sea is very deep here.
- There's a rope in the dinghy, isn't there?
- Yes," says Nata.
- Well, tie both ends to the seat.
Nata hurried to obey, and Raïden passed the rope under his arms.
- This way I'll be suspended in the water," he said.
And he let himself slip silently out of the boat.
For more than an hour, he worked in the dark, without saying a word, and as his hands worked below the water, he made no noise. We could hear the monotonous footsteps of the sentry and the crash of the waves against the ship.
- Pass me the sake," said Raïden at last, "I'm cold.
- It's my turn to work," said Nata. Get back in the boat.
It's finished," said Raïden, "the nails have been taken out all around a plank as long as our boat and as wide as Nata is from shoulder to shoulder.
- So you've succeeded completely? says the prince.
- Not yet, the hardest part is still to come: the plank is interlocked with its two neighbours and there's nothing I can do to pull it out.
- Try to slide your tool into the crack.
- I've been trying for a while, but to no avail," says Raiden; "the board would have to be pushed from the inside.
- That's impractical," said Nagato.
Raiden raised his head; he was looking at the hull of the ship.
- Isn't there a porthole above us?
- I can't see anything," said the prince.
- You're not used to seeing in the dark on stormy nights like we are," said Nata, "but I can see the porthole perfectly well.
- You'd have to go in there and push the plank out," said Raïden.
- You're crazy, none of us can fit through that narrow opening.
- If little Loo were here," murmured Raïden, "he'd get in!
At that moment, the prince felt something stirring in his legs, and a small shape rose from the bottom of the boat.
- Loo knew he would be needed," she said.
- Are you there?" said the prince.
- We're saved, then," said Raïden.
- Quick," said Loo, "hoist me up to the skylight.
- Listen," said Raïden in a low voice, "once you're in, you'll feel the wall and count five planks as you go down, right below the opening, the sixth plank being the one you'll find.
If you pushed it all the way in, the water would swallow you up.
- Good!" said the child.
Nata leaned against the junk.
- You're not afraid, Loo," said the prince.
Loo, without speaking, nodded. He was already on Nata's shoulders and clinging with both hands to the edge of the opening. Soon he pushed his torso in, then his legs, and disappeared.
- It must be even darker in there than it is here," said Nata, who was pressing her ear against the junk.
They waited. Time seemed long. The same anxiety kept them immobile.
Finally, a creak was heard. Raïden felt the plank oscillate. A second jolt made it protrude from its grooves.
- Enough! enough! or you're lost!" said Raïden, without daring to raise his voice.
But the child heard nothing; he continued to strike with his closed fists with all his strength. Soon the plank came loose and floated above the waves.
At the same time, with the sound of a torrent, the water rushed into the ship.
- And the child! the child!" cried the prince in anguish.
Raïden plunged his arms desperately into the gaping, black, tumultuous opening.
- Nothing!" he said through gritted teeth. He's been swept away by the force of the water.
At that moment, cries were heard from one of the neighbouring junks; lights were running across the deck; in the darkness, they seemed to move in the air.
- Our friends may need us," said Nata.
- We can't abandon this poor child," said the prince, "as long as there is hope of saving him; we won't move from here.
Suddenly, Raïden let out a cry of joy: he had just felt a small hand clinging to the edge of the hole in the ship.
He soon pulled the child to him and threw him into the boat.
Loo did not move; he had fainted. Raïden, dripping with water, climbed back into the boat.
- Here's one who won't be long," said Nata, rowing the junk away from the light boat.
- Let's go and see the others," said Nagato, "it may not be over yet.
The cries redoubled and the alarm was raised on all sides. On the shores of the island there were also lights and the sound of hastily gathered weapons.
- We're sinking! We're sinking! shouted the crew of the junks.
Several men threw themselves into the sea. They breathed heavily as they swam hastily towards the shores of the island.
The junks were sinking steeply; you could hear the bubbling of the water invading them; the enemy was there and you couldn't see them. The more the lights were multiplied, the darker the sea seemed.
The Prince of Nagato leaned out of the boat and tried to peer into the darkness. Suddenly, a violent shock made the boat jump, and for a few moments it shook in a disordered manner.
- We can't see anything either," said a voice. "Forgive us, Prince, for hitting you like this.
- Ah, it's you," said Nagato, "have you succeeded?
- We'd still be at work if our mission wasn't finished. Like an army of rats we gnawed away at the wood and made a big hole in the junk.
- Good! good," said the prince, "you really are invaluable helpers.
- Let's take to the open sea," said Raïden, "they still have boats, they might come after us.
- And our companions?
- They'll be all right, you can be sure of that. Perhaps they are already far away.
The soldiers were throwing arrows at random, and some of them could be heard falling into the water like rain around the canoes.
- They're so clumsy they could hit us without meaning to," said Nata, laughing.
- Out to sea!" cried Raïden, rowing vigorously.
The darkness had lessened for a moment, and a pale whiteness spread across the sky like a drop of milk in a cup of water. From the edge of the horizon, the glow emanated more brightly, though dimly, barely illuminating. It was the dawn of the full moon. Soon, like the tip of a sword reaching beyond the horizon, the star cast a steely glint. Immediately a trail of alternating light and dark ran across the sea to the shore, blue sparks sparkled on the crests of the waves; then the moon appeared like the arch of a bridge, and finally it rose up in its entirety, like a mirror of metal.
We were beyond the reach of the soldiers, Nata had taken the oars; Raiden was rubbing sake into Loo's head, which was resting on the prince's lap.
- At least the poor child isn't dead!" said Nagato, placing his hand on Loo's heart.
- No, see: his little chest is rising painfully, he's breathing, only he's cold; we've got to get him out of his wet clothes.
Nata took off her tunic and wrapped the child in it.
- You remember, Prince, how he bit me when I wanted to fight you? All I want is for him to bite me again.
The sailor tried to loosen Loo's clenched teeth and poured a stream of sake into his mouth.
The child swallowed hard, sneezed, coughed and then opened his eyes.
- So I'm not dead?" he said, looking round.
- But it seems not," cried a joyful Raïden. Would you like a drink?
- Oh no!" cried Loo, "I've had enough. Sea water is very bad, I've never tasted it before, I'll have to eat a lot of banana jam to forget what it tastes like.
I'll have to eat a lot of banana jam to forget that taste.
- You're not suffering? said the prince.
- No," said Loo; "has the junk sunk at least?
- By now, all you can see is the tip of the mast," said Nata. You had a lot to do with the success of the venture.
- You can see, Master, that I can be of some use," says Loo proudly.
- Certainly, and you are as brave as the bravest man," said the prince; "but how did you get here?
- That's it! I saw that they didn't want to take me away, so I hid under the bench.
- Will you tell me," cried Raïden, "why you pushed the plank so hard, despite my recommendations?
- It was to be sure that the junk wouldn't escape; and then I heard noises in the ship: we had to hurry.
Besides, I might not have been able to get back up. There were all sorts of beams, ropes and chains banging against me, because I couldn't see any more than if my head had been in a black velvet sack.
- And when this column of water fell on you, what did you think?
- I thought I was dead, but that the junk would sink of course; I heard what sounded like thunder, and I drank! I drank! through my nose, through my mouth, through my ears, and then I didn't feel a thing, I don't remember.
- You were very close to death, my poor Loo," said the prince; "but for your good behaviour, I'll give you a fine, sharp sword, and you can wear it at your belt, like a lord.
Loo looked at his companions, illuminated by the moon, with a look of pride, accompanied by a smile that puffed out his cheeks and made two dimples.
A misty blue glow spread over the sea, and you could see for quite a distance.
- Two junks have disappeared," said Nagato, looking towards the island, "the third is still standing.
- I seem to see boats circling around it, could our friends have been surprised?
Suddenly, the junk tilted to one side, and a small boat broke away and fled.
The longboats, full of soldiers, set off in pursuit, throwing a swarm of arrows towards it.
A few shots were fired from the boat.
- Let's run to their aid!" cried the prince.
Raïden had already turned the canoe round, and the other boat, which was with them, followed close behind.
- They won't be caught," said Raïden, turning his head as he rowed.
Indeed, the light dinghy was bouncing on the waves, while the heavier longboats, overloaded with men, were moving with great difficulty.
- The sinking junk! The sinking junk!" shouted Loo, clapping his hands.
Indeed, the last ship left standing sank slowly, then almost at once disappeared.
- Victory! Victory!" shouted the sailors around the prince.
- Victory!" came the reply from the pursued boat, which was getting closer and closer.
The three boats soon joined up.
- Let us be pursued," said the prince, "and let us not flee too quickly to give them any hope of reaching us.
A few shots were fired and several soldiers fell, who were immediately thrown into the sea to lighten the boats.
An arrow hit Raïden in the shoulder, but it had no strength left; it barely stung him and fell into the canoe.
- It was well aimed," said Raïden.
The moon was in the middle of the sky, but this polished mirror was fading, as if under the mist of a breath; it soon took on a shade of pink vermeil, then became cottony, and was no more than a white cloud. The blue and silver of the sky was invaded by a pale shade of amethyst that flowed rapidly from the horizon, and purple shivers ran across the sea.
It was daytime.
Behind the promontory, the prince's flotilla had heard the shots, which for them were a signal, and they immediately left the shore and unfurled their sails, which took on the lovely colour of peach blossom in the first rays of the sun.
As soon as the boats were within earshot, the Prince of Nagato, standing in the dinghy, shouted at the top of his voice:
- Surround those boats, cut off their retreat, take them prisoner!
Loo was overjoyed.
- After sinking the big boats, we're going to confiscate the small ones," he said.
The soldiers realised the danger, turned and began to flee. But how could they fight speed with their oars against those big sails inflated by the morning breeze?
The longboats were soon overtaken and overtaken.
The soldiers were lost. If they steered straight at them and with a single blow, one of these large boats could sink them in a second. They hurried to throw their weapons into the water as a sign of submission.
The men were hoisted aboard and then the boats collapsed and sank.
- Go and find your enormous mother at the bottom of the ocean!
shouted Loo as he watched them sink.
The three boats rejoined the flotilla. The prince and the sailors climbed back onto the big boats.
Loo then told those who had remained how the enemy junks had been sunk, how he had drowned in a hole and then risen to carry a sword like a lord.
We counted the prisoners who, resigned and with heads down, awaited their fate. There were fifty of them.
- The daring plan we formed has succeeded better than we could have hoped," said the prince; "I'm still amazed that it has come to fruition, but since Marisiten, the genius of battles, the god with six arms and three faces, has been so favourable to us, let's not rest yet: we must now surround the island of the Dragonfly and isolate it from the rest of the world, until such time as the army of the Siogun comes to relieve us.
- Good! good!" shouted the sailors, thrilled by their recent victory.
- How many soldiers are there on the island?" the prince asked one of the prisoners.
The soldier hesitated; he looked left and right, as if seeking advice. Suddenly, he decided to speak.
- Why should I hide it? There are two thousand of them.
- Then we won't have taken fifty prisoners, but two thousand!
Nagato's words were greeted with tremendous cheers. We set off. Soon the sake was flowing, and the sailors began to sing a war song, which they each sang to their heart's content, producing a deafening and joyous uproar.
The deepest consternation reigned on the island; people didn't want to believe what was happening: the junks, so strong and beautiful, suddenly sinking into the sea; the longboats full of soldiers not returning. All the sentries had seen was a frail dinghy, manned by three men who brazenly clung to the ship, banging their arms against its hull and fanning it, then fleeing, taunting them.
So there were no more ships; they even lacked the longboats and had no way of leaving the island. They had established themselves as if in a fortress, surrounded by a huge moat.
Protected by their war junks, it was indeed an excellent position. But now the fortress had become a prison for them; if help did not arrive soon, they would be lost. The chief who commanded these two thousand men
- ordered the two best boats to be chosen from among the wretched boats belonging to the islanders. When this order had been carried out, he put five men in each boat.
- You're going to leave in a hurry," he told them, "you're going to join the main army, and you're going to tell the general how distressed we are.
The boats set off, but when they were a short distance away, they saw a circle of large immobile sails closing the way.
The boats turned back.
We were blocked.
Sandaï had the supplies gathered. They took the cattle and the harvests of the inhabitants. There was enough to live on for eight days; what's more, fish could be caught.
- We must build large rafts and try to reach land at night without being seen," said the chief.
We set to work, felling trees and stripping them of their small branches, and so it went on for the rest of the day. They also worked at night, but the next morning they saw a luminous swarm on the Soumiossi coast.
It was General Harounaga's army.
This fine warrior was rather embarrassed. He did not know what to do in the face of an enemy separated from him by the sea.
The fleet of war was setting sail from Osaka; it was not yet ready to leave; if he had to wait for it to attack, the enemy could escape.
Harounaga had his troops camped by the sea, then his tent was pitched and he shut himself in to meditate.
In the meantime, the soldiers fired a few arrows towards the island as a salute; they fell into the water, as the island was out of range.
However, towards the middle of the day, a skilfully launched arrow fell in front of Harounaga's tent and hit the sand with a shudder.
A piece of paper was attached to the feathers of the arrow, which was plucked from the ground and carried to the general.
Harounaga unfolded the paper and read the following:
"Prepare to attack. The enemy is in your power. I have taken away his means of escape. I will provide you with the means to reach him.
This note was not signed.
The general left his tent and looked out to sea.
A fishing boat passed slowly between the island of Dragonfly and the coast of Soumiossi.
- Who could this note be from?" said Harounaga to himself. Are they making fun of me? Is this the vulgar boat they're offering me to transport my whole army?
But as he looked, other boats appeared on the sea; they came closer, they multiplied.
Harounaga counted them.
Good! good!" he said, the proposal became acceptable.
Get up, soldiers!" he shouted, "take up arms, here comes a fleet!
As soon as the movement of the troops was noticed, the boats moved towards the shore. The one carrying the Prince of Nagato was the first to reach the shore.
The prince recognised the general.
- Ah! it's that stupid Harounaga," he murmured.
Loo jumped to the ground. He had a magnificent sword at his belt.
- Twenty men per boat!" he shouted. There are forty of them, which will make eight hundred men for each crossing.
The general stepped forward.
- The Prince of Nagato!" he exclaimed.
- I'm here incognito," said the prince, "and all the glory of this adventure will be yours.
- A sovereign who exposes himself to the hazards of battle!
Harounaga exclaimed in surprise.
- I wage war as I please, without being subject to anyone, and I find a certain pleasure in these new emotions.
- You, who only liked feasts! parties!
- I like war better these days," said the prince, smiling.
Shots and confused clamour could be heard in the distance.
- What is this?" said the general.
- It's a false attack directed at the other side of the island, to encourage your soldiers to disembark.
- You could be a general as well as me," said Harounaga.
The prince smiled contemptuously, hiding it behind his fan.
The boats loaded with men left the coast, and the general climbed into the boat carrying the prince.
Loo had picked up a sort of trumpet and was blowing into it, leaning forward with all his might.
The soldiers of Hiéyas were waiting, massed on the shore, ready to oppose the landing with all their might; arrows began to fly on both sides.
The Prince of Nagato brought forward a boat full of men armed with rifles. They fired almost continuously at their enemies, who had no firearms.
On the shore, a furious hand-to-hand struggle ensued. Legs were thrust into the water; sabre blows sent foam flying. Sometimes two opponents would drag each other down, roll and disappear. Several corpses and a large number of arrows floated on the waves.
People clung to the boats, pushing them violently out to sea; a powerful stroke of the oar brought them back. Then we would hang from one side to capsize them. Hands clinging to the ledges were struck with sabres, blood spurted out and then, like torn shreds, dragged across the water.
As soon as a boat was empty, they would rush out to find more soldiers. Soon the partisans of the usurper were overwhelmed. They surrendered.
The dead and wounded were numerous. They were laid on the sand, bandaged and encouraged with gentle, fraternal words. Were they not brothers? Indeed, they wore the same uniform, they spoke the same language; some wept as they recognised friends in the enemy ranks. The defeated sat on the ground in an attitude of despondency, folding their hands on their knees and bowing their heads.
Swords and bows were gathered up in heaps and returned to the victors.
The Prince of Nagato and the General advanced into the interior of the island. Harounaga let the whip with its golden straps hang from his wrist, the scales of his armour clinked and rustled; he rested one hand on his hip.
- Bring in the leader of the rebels," said the prince.
Sandaï stepped forward.
He still had on the black patent leather mask that fits over the helmet and is worn in battle; he removed it and showed his saddened face.
Nagato's presence singularly disturbed this chief, who had once sought and obtained his protection from Fidé-
Yori. He had later attached himself to the regent out of ambition.
Now he was betraying his first lord.
Nagato's calm, contemptuous gaze brought home to him the infamy of his conduct; he understood that he could no longer hold his head high under the double humiliation of defeat and dishonour.
What's more, the prince seemed to him to be clothed in a special majesty.
In the midst of these armoured warriors, sheltering their foreheads under sturdy helmets, Ivakoura was bareheaded, dressed in a black silk robe with a golden ripple running through it.
above each arm a stiff breastplate, forming an epaulette and making his shoulders appear very broad. This made him look more formidable than anyone else.
The prince played nonchalantly with his iron fan.
He didn't seem to remember that he had ever known Sandaï.
- Rebel," he told him without raising his voice, "I'm not asking you if you want to renounce your crime and go back to being the servant of your true master: I know that pride outlives honour in man, and you would refuse.
- Prince," said Sandaï, "before the battle, your voice could have reminded me of my duties and thrown me at your feet; but, after defeat, a leader cannot renounce his deeds and serve his victor.
That's why I don't agree to submit.
- Well, I'm going to send you back to the master of your choice," said Nagato. You will go alone, without a page or a squire, to join Hiéyas and tell him this: General Harounaga defeated us, but it was the Prince of Nagato who sank the junks that could bring us back.
- Illustrious Daimio," said Sandaï without anger, "I am a general and not a messenger. I may have been guilty, but I am no coward; I know how to endure deserved insults without revolt, but I cannot survive them. Send another messenger to Hieyas, and let him add the news of my death to the news he will bring.
There was a great silence among the soldiers. They understood the general's intention and no one wanted to oppose his execution.
Sandai sat down on the ground; he drew his sabre and his dagger; then, after saluting the prince, he stretched out his stomach; thrust the dagger across his throat and, with one clean stroke, struck on the back of the blade, severed the artery; his head fell on his chest, amidst a stream of blood.
- This action relieves you in my eyes," said Nagato, who was perhaps
perhaps even heard by the dying man.
- Let this warrior be buried on the island, with all the pomp and ceremony his rank requires," said Harounaga.
Sandaï's body was raised.
- Now," said the prince, "I'm going to take a little rest. I'm beginning to remember that I spent the whole night running on the sea, and that my eyes didn't close for a second. The victory is as complete as possible; all that remains for you, Harounaga, is to establish communication between Soumiossi and the island you have conquered; you can do this with the help of rafts forming a sort of bridge. Send messengers to Fide-Yori, occupy the island and the coast, watch the sea and await further orders from Osaka.
- Thank you for this invaluable advice," said the general; "you are the real victor, will you allow me to let our beloved lord know?
lord?
- No," said the prince, "just let Hiéyas know, because I want my name to sound like a threat to him.
The Prince of Nagato left.
The night came, quiet and warm, then it passed and daylight returned.
Then General Harounaga came out of his tent and asked if the prince was awake. He was getting used to taking his orders and advice, so he didn't have to think: he had a thousand things to ask him.
We approached the tent that had been set up for Nagato; it was ajar; we looked inside, but the prince was not there.
- Perhaps he's gone back to his boat," said Harounaga.
We ran to the shore. The sea was empty, the Prince of Nagato's flotilla had disappeared.
XVIII
THE PRINCIPALITY OF NAGATO
Fatkoura had left with her entire household, all her baggage and a guard of honour given to her by the queen because of the dangers of the road. She had gone to Hagui, to her fiancé's castle.
The young woman felt a kind of cruel joy in the midst of the despair of her disappointed love.
- We are three unhappy people now," she said.
She had agreed to marry the prince out of a desire for revenge. Besides, could she refuse? What's more, everyone in the palace knew Fatkoura's feelings for the Prince of Nagato: she had let them be seen boldly in the pride of her joy, when she thought she was loved.
She left the court in a hurry, tired of showing a smiling mask to her friends, whose congratulations overwhelmed her.
During the journey, she looked at nothing of the charming country she was passing through, her gaze constantly fixed on the carpet of her norimono, deepening her pain.
Sometimes she called Tika in.
The young maid would crouch down opposite her and look at her with worried compassion. She would try to distract her from her painful dream.
- Look, mistress," she said, "look at the pretty absinthe-coloured river flowing between these velvety hills. Every shade of green is here: the pale willow, the dark cypress, the silver-glazed birch, the emerald-clear grass; each gives its own note. You see, to make it green too, the moss has invaded this water mill, whose image the river repeats; and over there these reeds that look like swords, and these ducks that flap their wings on the water and flee with outstretched necks, they are green like the whole landscape.
Fatkoura wasn't listening.
- He'll come back to you," said Tika, giving up trying to distract her mistress from her stubborn grief, "when you're his wife he'll love you again: you're so beautiful!
- He has never loved me and I don't want him to love me," said Fatkoura, "because I hate him.
Tika sighed.
- The only joy I have is knowing that he is suffering and that she too, the one who crushes me with her unparalleled power and beauty, is bitten by pain. They love each other and they can't admit it. I am yet another obstacle between them: if the mikado had died, she would have married him.
- A kisaki! marrying a prince," cried Tika.
- Are you forgetting," said Fatkoura, "that Nagato's grandfather was the first after the mikado? The Ivakoura insignia still say so, as they are made up of two Chinese characters meaning 'The first rank'. When I loved the prince, even the son of the gods could not have driven him from my heart.
- You love him more than ever," murmured Tika.
Sometimes Fatkoura felt sorry for herself, remembering the time when the happiness of being loved filled her soul, and she wept profusely.
But these tears didn't make her feel any better.
- I'm mad!" she said; "I'd like to cry on his shoulder; I'd like to pour my sorrow into that cruel, cold heart!
Then her anger returned.
At last she reached the town of Hagui, on the shores of the Sea of Japan. She passed through the magnificent gateway of the ancient fortress of the Princes of Nagato.
In the first courtyard, Ivakoura's father met her and greeted her in a friendly manner.
- Princess of Nagato," he said, "welcome home.
This lord was sixty years old, upright and strong; in the nobility of his features the young woman found something of Ivakoura's face. For several years the old prince had abdicated his power in favour of his eldest son; he was busy educating his youngest son, a child of thirteen, who was standing beside him at the moment, and on whose head he was resting his hand.
Fatkoura had to smile again and look happy.
She hid her mouth behind the sleeve of her dress, with that modest and affectionate movement familiar to Japanese women, then knelt for a moment before the lord.
He treated her paternally, installed her in the flats of honour, gave parties for her and organised hunts. He showed her his estates and gave her splendid gifts.
Fatkoura felt a strange sensation in this environment where everything spoke to her of her fiancé. She saw the room where he had been born, and was shown the toys that had been broken by his hands as a child, and his first clothes, which bore the memory of a form that was already graceful.
She was told a thousand charming stories of his adored childhood, then the heroic deeds of the adolescent, the young man, his literary successes, the nobility of his soul, his kindness, his devotion. The lord never dried up; the father's love tortured and fuelled the woman's painful love.
Then a kind of resignation came over her. By dint of hiding her pain, she buried it deep within herself and toned it down; she tried to forget that she was not loved; she found consolation in the strength of the feeling she felt.
- I love, she said to herself, that is enough; I will be content to see him, to hear him, to bear his name; I will be patient; perhaps time will heal him, he will then have pity on my long resignation, he will remember all that I have suffered for him; his heart will soften, he will love me; I will end my life, happy, close to him; I will be the mother of his children.
Soon there were rumours of war. Worry invaded everyone's hearts, the life of the absent man was in danger.
- Where is he now?" said Fatkoura.
- He's at the most dangerous post, I'm sure! replied the old lord.
He said this with pride, raising his head, but his voice trembled and tears came to his eyes.
The news became clearer. The princes of Figo and Toza were threatening Osaka; they were also threatening the province of Nagato.
Ivakoura's father set up an army and sent troops to the borders.
- We have an ally, the Prince of Aki," he said, "and besides, they won't attack us, they're not after us.
He was wrong. The soldiers he had sent had not yet reached the borders of the kingdom when the Prince of Toza was already landing on the coast of the Inland Sea.
Full of concern, the prince sent a deputation to the lord of Aki, his neighbour. The latter declared that he wished to remain neutral in this war.
- He's a traitor! an infamous one!" cried old Nagato, when his envoys brought him this reply; "well, we'll defend ourselves alone, without the hope of triumphing, it's true, but with the certainty of not diminishing the lustre of our ancient glory.
When he was alone with Fatkoura, the lord let his dejection show.
- I vow," he told her, "that my son will remain with the Siogoun and not return here. If he were here, he would be killed, and who would avenge us?
Riders entered the castle. The lord turned pale when he saw them. They wore Nagato's insignia on their shields.
- You bring news of my son?" he said in an uncertain voice.
- Illustrious lord, the Prince of Nagato is in good health," said a samurai. He is at the moment on the edge of his kingdom, busy gathering the army around him; he is going to march against the Prince of Figo.
- Aki is a traitor; does my son know that? said the lord.
- He knows, master. The prince has crossed the province ruled by this infamous man; he thought it was friendly, but he was attacked treacherously. Thanks to his unrivalled bravery, he dispersed the attackers, but half his baggage was lost.
- What orders did he tell you to pass on to us?
- Here, my lord; the Prince of Nagato asks you to make an extraordinary collection of troops and to send them to meet the Prince of Toza, who is advancing towards Chozan; then to double the defenders of the fortress, to stockpile supplies there and to lock yourself in; he also asks you to give me command of the troops you will send against Toza.
These orders were carried out with haste.
Events followed in quick succession. More messengers arrived. The Prince of Nagato had fought a battle to the north of the kingdom, in the territory of Souvo; the Lord of Souvo, a vassal of the Prince of Aki, had encouraged the landing of Figo's soldiers; but Ivakoura had driven these soldiers into the inland sea; many had drowned, the others had returned to the ships at anchor. Meanwhile, the small army of the lord of Souvo had attacked the prince from behind, trying to separate him from the province of Nagato, but this army had been completely defeated and the prince was able to return to his kingdom.
Now Figo, backed by new forces, had reappeared on the shores of Nagato, and Ivakoura was preparing to repel a second attack.
But while the Prince of Nagato was triumphing in the north of his domains, the Prince of Toza was invading them from the south.
The province of Nagato, the extreme tip of the island of Nipon, is bounded on three sides by the sea: to the south-east is the inland sea, separated from the Pacific Ocean by the island of Sikof and the island of Kiou-Siou.
Siou, to the west the Korean Strait, to the north the Sea of Japan, and to the east a chain of mountains separates it from the principalities of Souvo and Aki.
The prince of Toza had come from the island of Sikof via the Boungo canal, and had crossed the inland sea as far as Chozan; he wanted to cross the entire width of the province and march on Hagui, the capital, situated on the other side, on the shores of the Sea of Japan.
Toza met the hastily-raised troops sent by the old lord of Nagato, but these ill-trained troops bent before the invader's well-disciplined army; they retreated and fled back to Hagui.
Preparations were made for a siege.
The fortress stood some distance from the town on an elevation surrounded by a moat; from the top of its towers you could see the fields and the sea.
Soon Toza's army covered the plain.
The old lord watched from the top of the fortress.
- My daughter," he said to Fatkoura, "why didn't you stay in Kioto?
- My father," replied the young woman, "to be here in my husband's castle at a time when he is under threat is both my duty and my pleasure.
All her anger was gone, all she had left was love, she trembled for the life of her beloved, terrible anguish tortured her, and the arrival of a messenger did nothing to calm her.
- Since that man left her," she said to herself, "he could have died twenty times.
But the castle was blocked and the messengers stopped arriving.
The town put up stiff resistance and was taken on the fifth day; then the siege of the fortified castle began.
The Prince of Toza himself supervised the work of his soldiers.
First they built a long wooden roof covered with iron plates, then they raised it on very high posts and secured it. This made a sort of shed which they placed in the ditch. They then brought earth, stones and brush and threw them into the water. The arrows thrown at them bounced off the roof. Huge blocks of rock were pushed from the top of the eminence to crush this dangerous shelter; but as they rolled down the slope, their force was dampened and most of them fell into the ditch. They strengthened the work of the besiegers, who quietly filled in part of the ditch under the shield they had built for themselves.
They stopped throwing missiles from the top of the walls.
The soldiers tried to get out; they went down the path that wound like a ribbon around the hill, and approached the moat. To reach the place where the enemies were working, they had to leave the path, sheltered by a double row of cypress trees, and walk on the slippery grass that covered the steep slope of the hill.
The soldiers tried to do this, but they felt uncomfortable shooting, as they offered themselves up as targets for their opponents' blows. The wounded rolled and fell into the ditch.
They gave up and retreated into the walls. The attackers completed their work without being troubled; they made a fairly wide causeway that joined the foot of the hill and over which the army could pass.
The assault began.
The castle put up heroic resistance; it refused to capitulate.
On its crumbling walls, the besieged still defended themselves. The castle was invaded. The victors opened the gates, the bridges were lowered and the Prince of Toza entered Nagato Castle to the sound of triumphal music.
In the first courtyard he entered, the sight that struck his eyes made an unpleasant impression.
There had been no time to bury the dead; they had been gathered together in this courtyard. Sitting on the ground, leaning against the wall, there were a hundred of them; with their green faces, their mouths and eyes wide open, their arms hanging down: they were terrible.
The Prince of Toza imagined that they were watching him and forbidding him to enter. Being superstitious, he was on the verge of turning back.
However, he quickly overcame this weakness, entered a room in the palace and ordered the lord, his wives, his children and his entire household to be brought before him.
They soon appeared.
There were old women there, some accompanying their very old and trembling fathers, some young girls and children. The lord came forward, holding his son by the hand, Fatkoura walking beside him.
- If you want to destroy the women," said old Nagato, looking at Toza with contempt, "make haste to say so, so that I can curse you and bring all the afflictions upon you.
- I don't care whether these women live or die," cried Toza, "you yourself, having abdicated, are nothing, and I will spare your old age. I'm looking for a hostage among you who is valuable enough to be able to vouch for the submission of the Prince of Nagato, because after victory I can't settle in his lands, the war is calling me elsewhere. Who will I take," he continued, "the son or the father? The child is still very young and worthless; for want of anything better, I'll take the father.
- Then take me with him!" cried the child.
Fatkoura suddenly stepped forward.
Since you think the father is too old and the brother too young," she cried, "take the sovereign's wife prisoner, if you think she's worth missing.
- Of course I'll take you, because you must be passionately loved," said Toza, struck by Fatkoura's beauty.
- My daughter," murmured old Nagato, "why did you betray yourself? Why didn't you let me go?
- Is she really Ivakoura's wife?" asked the victor, troubled by a doubt.
- Every word that comes out of my mouth is the word of truth," said Nagato, "this woman is the wife of my aïs, since promises have been exchanged, only the war has delayed the wedding.
- Well, Ivakoura will come to fetch his fiancée from the castle of the princes of Toza, and the ransom he will have to pay to get her back will be proportionate to the value of the treasure I'm taking with me.
- What have you done? What have you done?" said the old prince, sighing. How dare I tell my son that his wife is a prisoner?
- Rejoice, on the contrary," said the Prince of Toza, "for you can see how magnanimous I am. I leave you alive, you and your son, and all those of your house, I allow you to raise the crumbling walls of your castle, and I am content with this one captive.
- I'm ready to follow you," said Fatkoura, happy to be sacred to the salvation of all.
- You will be treated by me as a sovereign should be.
That same evening, Fatkoura left Nagato's castle.
She tried in vain to hold back her tears as she passed through the gate in her norimono carried by the victor's people.
- I'll never go back to that house! she cried.
Tika was crying too.
When we were a short distance away, Fatkoura had the palanquin bearers stop and, leaning out of the window, she took one last look at Hagui's fortress, silhouetted against the red sky on the crest of the hill.
- Farewell! Farewell!" she cried, the last refuge of my tenacious hope. Behind your walls, castle of the beloved, I could still dream of a late and distant happiness, but it's over, I'm doomed to despair; the last light that shone for me is extinguished with the fading day.
We set off again and the castle disappeared. The Prince of Toza left half his army in Nagato's territory. Messengers told him that Figo had not been able to break through the enemy lines, but that on hearing the news of the siege of Hagui, Ivakoura had suddenly moved away to help the fortress. He had left during the night without a sound; in the morning the plain was deserted. Figo was going to pursue him, but victory would be certain if we could block the enemy's path and crush him between two armies.
Toza gave orders to the leaders of the troops he was leaving behind, then hurried to Chozan, where his ships were waiting. This lord did not want to leave his states undefended any longer: he feared the proximity of the Prince of Awa, whom he believed to be devoted to Fidé-Yori.
When the junks had left the coast and sailed inland towards the Boungo canal, the prince came to greet his prisoner. He had installed her in a superb tent at the stern of the most beautiful ship, the one he himself was riding. Fatkoura was seated on a bench covered with a rich carpet; her gaze was fixed on the shores of Nagato, disappearing into the light-flooded distance.
- Do you have a desire, beautiful princess?
Would you like to hear the sound of the flute or the biva?
- All my desires remain on this earth that I am leaving," she said.
I take only one with me, that of death.
- I respect your pain," said the prince, who withdrew. But he didn't go far; he strolled along the bridge and, as if in spite of himself, often returned to the tent where Fatkoura was staying. Tika watched him out of the corner of her eye.
He had changed out of his military costume and was dressed with a certain amount of finesse. The Prince of Toza was thirty years old, a little fat and short, with a bearded complexion that made the whiteness of his teeth stand out. His eyes, veiled by the fold of his eyelids raised towards his temples, had a certain softness about them.
Tika thought the prince was not without charm, and she half-smiled every time he let out a sigh or cast a furtive glance at Fatkoura, who was watching the ship's wake.
- She's beautiful, isn't she?" she said softly; "you think the Prince of Nagato is very happy to have such a fiancée, and you'd like to take her away from him. I guessed you right away.
As soon as you saw her at Hagui Castle, you couldn't take your eyes off her, and you took her away in all haste; you were afraid that the fiancé would arrive in time to snatch her away from you. But you are wasting your efforts, she will never love you... It's not that I don't wish for you," continued Tika, continuing her monologue, "but if she could recover and become Princess of Toza, I would be truly delighted. The Prince of Nagato, too, would gladly consent to this marriage, but you have no idea.
The Prince of Toza was also examining the young woman.
- Yes, yes, I understand," murmured Tika. You're looking at the rung that might help you get to her.
Soon the young girl got up and, as if to breathe more easily, walked out onto the deck; she leaned against the parapet and looked out over the sea.
Below, however, she was watching the Prince's movements.
- Oh, you'll come to me," she said, "I'm sure of it.
Let's see how you start the conversation?
The prince approached, slowly and hesitantly.
Tika looked off into the distance.
- The air is fresher here, isn't it, young lady?" said the prince at last, stopping in front of her.
- That's rather banal, thought Tika, who replied by inclining her head.
- Why doesn't your mistress go for a little walk?
Why doesn't she let the gentle breeze cool her brow?
- The wind that blows from the land of exile is hotter than a flame," said Tika in a solemn voice.
- Is it so terrible to live in one castle rather than another?" said the prince; "Fatkoura will be treated like a sovereign. I swear to you that I want her captivity to be sweeter than the freedom of another. Tell me what she likes.
- Hasn't she told you herself that she has no taste for anything? She used to love finery, parties and music; above all she loved to hear her fiancé's footsteps on the outside gallery.
- So she loves Nagato very much?
- He deserves to be loved, he's the most perfect lord there is.
- There are some who are well worth it," said Toza.
- You'd think!" exclaimed Tika incredulously, "I've never heard him say that.
- He loves her madly, doesn't he?
- How could anyone not love her!
- She's beautiful, it's true," said the prince, glancing at Fatkoura.
- You think she's beautiful, now that her eyes are drowned in tears, now that she disdains make-up and finery! If you had seen her when she was happy!
- I'll do everything I can to put a smile back on her face," says Toza.
- There's only one way to do that.
- And that is? You tell me.
- It's to return her to her husband.
- You're making fun of me," exclaimed the prince, frowning.
- Do you think I'm deceiving you and that this wouldn't be the best way to make my mistress happy? I know you won't use it, so you'll never see her smile.
- Well, she'll stay sad," said Toza, "I'll keep her close to me.
- Alas!" sighed Tika.
- Shut up!" exclaimed the prince, stamping his foot, "why do you say: alas! what does it matter to you whether you serve her here or there, don't you see that she has charmed me and that I am unhappy?
The prince walked away after saying these words, while Tika pretended to be deeply stunned.
- I didn't think you'd be so quick to confide in me," she murmured when he had gone, "I had guessed you, but you don't yet suspect that I want to protect your love.
Tika returned to sit at her mistress's feet.
- You're leaving me alone to talk to our jailer," said Fatkoura.
- It was he who came to talk to me, mistress," said Tika, "and in the space of a few minutes he taught me some very strange things.
- What did he tell you?
- Do I have to tell you? You won't be angry?
- I don't know; just say it.
- Well, you're the jailer, he's the prisoner.
- What are you trying to say?
- That the Prince of Toza loves Fatkoura and that, if she knows how, she'll do whatever she wants with him.
- What does it matter to my contempt whether he loves me or hates me?" said Fatkoura, turning her head away.
- He's not so contemptible," said Tika, "he's a very powerful and illustrious prince.
- You talk like that about our mortal enemy, Tika? said Fatkoura, looking at her sternly.
- Don't scold me," said Tika caressingly, "I can't help hating him less since I know that your grace has subjugated him and that, in the space of a few hours, you have invaded his heart.
- Yes, you think that another, on the contrary, turns his eyes away from me, and you are grateful to this one for repairing the insult done to me! said Fatkoura, hiding her forehead in her hand.
As the sea was beautiful and the journey easy, instead of crossing the land, they skirted the coast of the island of Sikof, rounded the Cape of Toza and, after sailing northwards for a few hours in the Pacific Ocean, the junks entered the port of Kotsi. The town was abuzz with banners, streamers and lanterns, and the streets were strewn with blossoming branches. The sovereign, at the head of his victorious troops, was making a triumphal return.
When they had passed the town and the castle walls, the prince himself led Fatkoura to the pavilion he had intended for her. This was the palace of the Queen of Toza, who had died a few years earlier.
- I am very sorry that the joyful clamour that greeted me has struck your ear," said the prince to his prisoner; "I could not object to my people expressing their satisfaction, but I was suffering because of you.
- I heard nothing, my thoughts were elsewhere," replied Fatkoura.
The prince did not visit the young woman for several days. His budding love made him shy, and he was surprised by this new feeling for him.
One morning he went for a walk alone in the part of the park occupied by Fatkoura.
Tika was watching him; she said nothing to her mistress and let herself be seen on the gallery. The prince beckoned her to come close to him, and she obeyed.
- Is she always so sad?
- She always is.
- She hates me, doesn't she?
- I don't know," said Tika.
- I let slip the other day in front of you a confession that I should have kept quiet," said the prince.
- I'm in the habit of keeping nothing from her, my lord.
- Ah!" asked the prince sharply, "what did she say when she learned of my love for her?
- She said nothing; she hid her face in her hands.
The prince sighed.
- I want to see her at all costs! For three days I've been depriving myself of her presence and I'm overwhelmed with boredom, I've forgotten too much that I'm the master.
- I'll tell him you're coming," said Tika, who rushed back into the house.
A moment later Toza appeared before Fatkoura. He found her even more beautiful than the last time he had seen her; sadness ennobled her beauty; her complexion, free of make-up, showed her feverish pallor, and her eyes had a resigned and proud expression that was most touching.
The prince was moved before her and remained silent. She greeted him by raising the sleeve of her dress to the height of her mouth.
It was she who spoke first.
- If you have any pity in your soul," she said in a voice shaking with tears, "don't leave me in this terrible uncertainty, give me news of my husband!
- I fear to sadden you even more by telling you news that is good for me and bad for you, since you are my enemy.
- I implore you!" exclaimed Fatkoura in horror.
- Well then, the army of the Prince of Figo, assisted by my soldiers, has triumphed over the Prince of Nagato, who defended himself heroically, I admit; at the moment he must be a prisoner; the latest news tells me that with barely a hundred men, Nagato has entrenched himself in a small wood, my troops have surrounded him and he cannot escape.
Fatkoura bowed her head in dismay. She couldn't believe it, she couldn't imagine him unhappy. In her eyes he always triumphed, he was the first, the most beautiful, the most noble: how could he be a prisoner, anyway, when he could escape captivity through death?
She looked up at the Lord of Toza, doubting his words.
- You are hiding the truth from me," she said with a frightening intensity of gaze, "you want to prepare me for the fatal blow of his death?
- I spoke frankly," said Toza; "he will be taken alive.
But I want to give you some advice: forget this man," he added, irritated by Fatkoura's pain.
- Me, forget him!" she cried, clasping her hands together.
- I have to, it's all over for him. Do you think I'm going to set him free, the one that Hiéyas hates so much that he's going to be the first in the kingdom, the man who will free him from this adversary, the one who has humiliated us all with his luxury, his wit, his beauty; the one you love at last, and who is my rival, because I love you?
- You love me!" exclaimed Fatkoura in horror.
- Yes," sighed the prince, "and I had come to say sweet words to you; but you have led me to speak of things I did not want to say. I know that my love will be hateful to you at first, but you must get used to it; it is not offensive to you. I am free and I offer to be your wife. Just think, the Prince of Nagato no longer exists.
Toza withdrew, so as not to hear Fatkoura's reply. He was irritated with her and displeased with himself.
- I was brutal, he thought, I didn't say the right thing, but jealousy suddenly bit me in the heart; it's a very violent torture that I've never experienced before.
He wandered around the gardens for the rest of the day, bullying anyone who came near him.
- She'll never want to love me," he said to himself, "I have no way of taming her heart; but if the Prince of Nagato falls into my hands, I'll take my revenge on him.
Fatkoura couldn't stay still either; she went from one room to another in her flats, wringing her hands and weeping silently. She didn't dare ask any more questions, but every hour that passed increased her anxiety.
One night, she heard an unusual noise in the castle. Bridges were being lowered and the clash of armour sounded.
She got up and ran to a window; she saw lights shining through the trees.
- Get up, Tika," she said, waking the young girl; try to slip in unseen and hear what's being said; try to find out what's going on in the castle.
Tika dressed quickly and left the pavilion silently.
Her mistress followed her with her eyes, but she soon disappeared into the darkness.
When she returned, she was very pale and pressed her hand to her heart.
- The Prince of Nagato has just entered the palace," she said, "I saw him pass between the soldiers, he's in chains and they've stripped him of his weapons.
At these words, Fatkoura let out a loud scream and fell to the floor.
- Could she be dead?" exclaimed a frightened Tika, kneeling down beside her mistress.
Her heart was beating rapidly, but her eyes were closed and she was cold and motionless.
- What to do? What to do?" said Tika, who did not dare to call out, her mistress having forbidden her to let any of the servants placed at her disposal by the Prince of Toza go near her.
She fainted for a long time. When Fatkoura opened her eyes again, it was daylight. She looked at Tika for a moment in surprise, but her memory quickly returned. She stood up abruptly.
- We've got to save him, Tika," she cried with feverish excitement, "we've got to get him out of this castle.
- Has she gone mad?" said Tika.
- Let's get out," continued Fatkoura; "let's find out which part of the palace he's locked up in.
- The sun hasn't yet drunk the morning vapours, and people will be suspicious of us if they see us walking around so early, especially as you haven't left your flat once since we've been here.
- Anyway, you'll say that the fever drove me out of bed.
Let's go.
Fatkoura went down into the garden and started walking ahead of her. The grass was all wet, the trees and bushes were bathed in a pink atmosphere that blended with the sky, and the highest roofs of the castle's great tower were already receiving the sun's rays and glistening with dew.
Tika followed her mistress. They arrived at the palisade surrounding their private enclosure, the gate closed only at the latch, the prisoners were free in the well-guarded fortress.
The soldiers who had brought the Prince of Nagato were camped out in the alleys of the park; most of them were sleeping on their stomachs, their heads in their arms; others, crouched around a dying fire, were eating rice from large bowls wrapped in straw.
- Tika," said Fatkoura, looking at these men and the weapons shining beside them, "a sword is a faithful companion that opens the door to the next life and allows you to escape dishonour. The victor took the dagger I carried with me. Try to steal the sword from one of these soldiers.
- Mistress!" said Tika, casting a frightened glance at the young woman.
- Obey," said Fatkoura.
- So, let's get away from those who are awake, and stay back - the sound of your dresses could give us away.
Tika sank down between the clumps of flowers, then stretched out on the grass and lay down as far as she could towards a soldier lying at the edge of the path. He was sleeping on his back, his nose in the air, his sabre lying beside him.
The young girl touched the weapon with her fingertips, her nails against the scabbard making a small noise; Tika's heart was beating very fast.
The soldier didn't stir.
She moved forward a little more and grabbed the sword by the middle, then backed away slowly, sliding on the grass.
- I've got it, mistress!" she said casually, turning back to Fatkoura.
- I'll feel calmer with this defender beside me.
Fatkoura hid the sword against her chest and then started walking again, at random, getting lost.
Suddenly she found herself a few steps from the palace inhabited by the Prince of Toza; people were coming and going and she could hear the sound of voices.
She listened carefully.
She caught a few words and understood that the prince was being congratulated on the capture he had just made. The inferiors spoke in half voices, respectfully, and Fatkoura could not hear very well, but the Prince of Toza spoke loudly, and then she heard only too well.
- I thank you," he said, "for taking part in the joy caused me by this event; Nagato is the most bitter enemy of our great Hiéyas, so it is a glory for me to free him from this hated adversary: I have condemned him to the ultimate torture, he will be executed tomorrow, at midday, within the walls of the fortress, and his head will be taken to Hiéyas on my behalf.
Fatkoura had the strength not to scream. She went to join Tika, she knew enough. Her pallor was frightening, but she was calm, she pressed the sword against her flesh, it hurt, but it calmed her.
- I beg you, go back inside, mistress," said Tika; "if you were caught, they'd take us into their confidence and lock us up.
- You're right," said Fatkoura, "but I absolutely must know which part of the palace Nagato was taken to. They want to kill him, they're condemning him to an ignominious death. If I can't save him, I'll at least give him something to die for nobly.
- I can go unnoticed," said Tika, "I can talk to the servants without arousing suspicion, and I'll find out what you want to know.
Fatkoura returned to the palace and, overwhelmed, let herself fall onto the cushions, almost without a thought.
Tika was absent for a long time; when she returned, her mistress was still in the same place, motionless.
- Well, Tika?" she said as soon as she saw the young girl.
- I know where it is, mistress, I was shown from a distance the pavilion that houses it, I'll take you there.
- Come on," said Fatkoura, rising to her feet.
- Are you thinking about it?" exclaimed Tika; it's still broad daylight and we have to wait for nightfall.
- That's right," said Fatkoura, "let's wait.
She fell back down again.
Until evening she remained motionless, speechless, her gaze fixed on the same spot on the floor.
When it was quite dark, she got up.
- Let's go," she said.
Tika said nothing and went ahead. They passed through the gardens again, skirting other houses and courtyards; the young girl oriented herself by occasionally looking up at the great tower, on which a lantern shone.
- See that pavilion with the two roofs? You can see them against the sky. That's the place.
- The window is lit," said Fatkoura, "he's there; is that really possible? defeated, a prisoner, ready to die.
They moved on.
- Are there any soldiers?" asked Fatkoura in a low voice.
- I don't know," said Tika, "I can't see anyone.
- If I can't talk to him, I'll throw the sword through that open window in front of him.
They continued walking, down a small slope.
Suddenly Fatkoura felt a strong arm embrace her and pull her back.
- One more step and you'd fall into a deep ditch that's right there on the ground," said a voice.
Fatkoura recognised the Prince of Toza.
- It's all over," she murmured.
He was still holding her and she made every effort to free herself from his embrace, but she was unable to do so.
- This is how you thank me for saving your life," he said.
Fortunately, I was warned about the walk you were going to take this evening, and I followed you to keep you out of danger. Do you think, then, that your every word and your every move have not been faithfully reported to me? Do you think that I was unaware of your insane plan to free your fiancé or to provide him with a means of escaping my vengeance?
- Let me go, infamous Fatkoura moaned as she struggled.
- No," said the prince, "you will remain on my heart. The touch of your supple waist enchants me. I am determined to love you in spite of yourself.
But I want to make one last attempt to win your love. Grant me this and I will allow you to take the sword you stole from one of my soldiers to Nagato.
- This proposal is worthy of you," said Fatkoura scornfully.
- Are you refusing?
- The Princess of Nagato will not dishonour her name.
- Then you'll have to give me back this weapon," said the prince, who himself took it from Fatkoura's chest, "you could escape me by dying, which would be a bitter grief for me.
Think about the proposal I have just made to you, and you have until tomorrow to make up your mind. Until the time of the execution, which you will attend, it will be in your power to give your husband a gentler death.
The prince led the young woman back to his palace, then left her.
She was so overwhelmed by fear and despair that it seemed as if she no longer existed.
Her sleep was full of nightmares, but all the terror that fever dreams can give rise to was less horrible than reality. When she awoke, her first thought was to clench her heart and bathe her forehead in a cold sweat.
The Prince of Toza asked her what she had decided, and what kind of death the Lord of Nagato should be prepared for.
- Tell Toza," replied the princess proudly, "that he must stop insulting me by pretending to believe that I am capable of tarnishing the name of Nagato by committing an infamous deed.
He was then told that the execution would take place in front of his windows, just as the sun began to sink towards the west.
- Perhaps this odious lord imagines", said Fatkoura when she was alone again with Tika, "that I will survive the death of someone dearer to me than myself. He believes that the blow that will strike him before my very eyes will not kill me. He doesn't know what a woman's heart is.
Tika, appalled, said nothing. Sitting at her mistress's feet, she let her tears flow.
People came and went in front of the house, many footsteps making the sand crunch.
Fatkoura went over to the window and peered through the blind.
Poles were being planted all around the bare square that stretched out in front of the palace. Men on ladders struck the ends of the poles with mallets to drive them into the ground. Then black lacquer boxes with silver corners were brought in and white silk draperies were taken from them and hung on the posts, so as to enclose the square on all three sides in a fabric wall. Several mats were spread out on the ground, with a white one in the middle, bordered by a red fringe; the condemned man was to sit on this mat. A folding chair was placed under Fatkoura's window for the Prince of Toza, who wanted to watch the execution.
The unfortunate young woman walked feverishly around her room, moving away from the window and then returning to it against her will. Her teeth were chattering; a sort of horrible impatience was agitating her: she was terrified of waiting.
Soldiers arrived in the square, followed by samurai, vassals of the Prince of Toza.
They gathered in groups and, resting their hands on their swords, chatted in hushed tones, loudly criticising their lord's conduct.
- To refuse Hara-Kiri to one of the noblest of Japan's sovereigns, I can't understand that decision," said one.
- It's never been done before," said another, "even by simple samurai like us.
- He wants to send the head of the Prince of Nagato to Hiéyas.
- Once the prince had taken justice into his own hands, we could cut the head off the corpse in secret, without dishonouring the memory of the condemned nobleman.
- The lord of Toza undoubtedly has a motive for hatred against Nagato.
- No matter! Hatred is no excuse for injustice.
When the time came for the execution, the blinds were raised in Fatkoura's palace.
The distraught young woman fled to the back of the flat; she hid her head in the folds of a satin drapery to be blind and deaf, to muffle the burst of her sobs.
But suddenly she stood up and wiped her eyes.
- Come, Tika," she cried, "this is not how the wife of Ivakoura should behave; I will know how to lock up my pain within myself, help me to get to that window.
When she appeared leaning on Tika, a great silence fell over those present, a silence full of respect and compassion.
The Prince of Toza arrived at the same time and raised his eyes to hers, but she looked at him with such hatred and contempt that he lowered his head.
He came and sat down on the folding table and motioned for the prisoner to be brought forward.
He soon came forward, nonchalantly, with a disdainful smile on his lips; he had been released from his chains and was playing with his fan.
Two executioners walked behind him, bare-legged, dressed in black tunics tightened by a belt with a long sabre running through it.
He put his foot on the white mat that would, a few minutes later, be reddened by his blood, then raised his head.
Fatkoura gasped.
The man before her was not the Prince of Nagato.
The gaze of the infatuated woman, which had so often and for so long rested on the face of the beloved, could not be mistaken, even by a resemblance that deceived everyone.
She didn't hesitate for a minute. She found neither the radiance of his eyes, nor the melancholy of his smile, nor the pride of his brow, of the man who filled her heart.
- I knew he couldn't be defeated and humiliated," she said to herself, overcome by a wild joy that she could hardly conceal.
The sentence was read to the prisoner.
It condemned him to have his hands and then his head cut off.
- The infamy you have just told me dishonours you," cried the condemned man. My hands have only ever committed noble deeds and do not deserve to be detached from the arms that guided them. But invent the torments that please you, torture me as you please, I remain the prince and you lower yourself to the rank of executioner. I fought with all my might against the enemies of our rightful lord; you betrayed him for another who betrayed him, and you came underhandedly, without any reason for war between us, to attack my kingdom. You wanted my head in order to get a good price for it from Hiéyas; the dishonour is yours. I don't care about your ridiculous sentence!
- Who is this man who speaks with such courage?
Fatkoura asked himself.
The samurai approved of the prisoner's words, but let the Prince of Toza see their displeasure.
- Don't deny him the death of nobles," they said, "he has done nothing to deserve such rigour.
Toza was furious.
- I don't think my revenge is enough," he said through clenched teeth, "I'd like to find something even more terrible.
- But you can't think of anything," laughed the condemned man, "you've always lacked imagination. Do you remember when you used to follow me to the parties, to the joyous adventures I used to organise? You never knew how to invent anything, but your mind of the next day remembered our minds of the day before.
- Enough!" cried Toza, "I'll rip off your flesh with pliers and pour boiling pitch into your wounds.
