- All you've found there is an improvement on the moxas invented by doctors: look further, it's not enough.
- I can't explain this man's heroic behaviour," thought Fatkoura; "he knows he's being mistaken for someone else and he's playing a role that's going to lead him to a terrible death.
She wanted to shout the truth, to say that this man was not the Prince of Nagato; but she thought she would not be believed; besides, since he was silent himself, he must have had serious reasons for acting like this.
- I swear that you will be avenged in a resounding manner," she cried.
It is the wife of the Prince of Nagato who makes this oath and she will keep it.
- Thank you, divine princess," said the condemned man, "you, the only regret left in the world I am leaving; tell my master that I died cheerfully for him, seeing in the ill-satisfied rage of my executioner proof of our superiority and future glory.
- You shall speak no more," exclaimed the Prince of Toza, gesturing to the executioner.
Sado's head was severed with a single blow.
A stream of blood flooded the white mat. The body fell.
Fatkoura could not hold back a cry of horror.
The samurai turned their heads away, frowning. They walked away after silently saluting the Prince of Toza.
Full of anger and shame, he shut himself away in his palace.
That same evening, a messenger carrying a bloody head, wrapped in red silk and enclosed in a straw sack, left Toza castle.
XIX
A BURIAL
The news of General Harounaga's victory at Soumiossi had quickly reached Osaka. Yodogimi herself had come with impetuous joy to announce it to Fidé-Yori. She made no secret of her pride in her lover's triumph. Soon, however, peasants arriving from Soumiossi recounted in detail the events of the battle. The name of the Prince of Nagato replaced that of Harounaga everywhere.
Yodogimi forbade the repetition of such slander under severe penalties; she lost her temper and tired her son with wild recriminations. Fidé-Yori let her have her say, praising Harounaga loudly and thanking his faithful friend, whose devotion was unquenchable.
Unfortunately, other events, sad ones this time, came to erase the joy caused by the first victory: Hiéyas did not make any of the movements that had been planned, he did not attack Osaka from the south; General Signénari was therefore inactive on the island of Avadsi and yet no one dared to call him back. Nor did the usurper try to break the lines which barred the island of Nipon: his army, divided into small detachments, came by sea, landed at various points on the coast near Osaka, then, at night, surprised and captured a position.
Hachisuka, general of Hiéyas, captured a village near the capital in this way. The news spread throughout Osaka, causing widespread alarm. The siogun's soldiers had been massacred. At the time of the attack, their leader, Oussouda, was absent, drinking sake in a nearby teahouse.
General Sanada-Sayemon-Yoké-Moura wanted to attack the victors immediately and try to dislodge them from the conquered position. Fidé-Yori begged him not to.
- Your army is not large enough to lay siege to a village," he told him, "and if by misfortune you were defeated, the town would be defenceless. Call back the troops you sent to Yamasiro, and until we arrive let's content ourselves with defending Osaka.
Yoké-Moura reluctantly obeyed. He had clever spies keep an eye on the enemy.
Soon Yamasiro's troops returned. A battle was imminent. However, this time it was Yoké-Moura who refused to leave the city and give battle.
He would not even leave the fortress; he could be seen wandering around day and night, restless, worried, seemingly looking for something. At night especially, accompanied only by his son Daïské, who was only sixteen, he wandered relentlessly along the first wall.
The sentries, who saw him pass and pass again, with his son carrying a lantern, understood nothing of his behaviour and believed that the general had gone mad.
Every now and then Yoké-Moura rushed to his knees and pressed his ear to the ground.
Daïské held his breath.
Once, the general stood up quickly, all moved.
- Is it my blood ringing in my ears? he said; I thought I heard something. Listen, my son, and see if I'm not mistaken.
The child knelt down and put his ear to the ground.
- Father," he said, "I can clearly hear distant, muffled, regular knocks.
The general listened again.
- Yes, yes," he said, "I can hear them very well too; they're the blows of a pickaxe against the earth, it's there! We've got them, we're saved from a terrible danger!
- What is it, Father?" asked Daïské.
- What it is! The soldiers of Hiéyas are busy building an underground passage from their camp, passing under the town and the moat, and ending up here.
- Is this possible?" exclaimed Daïské.
- Luckily, a spy warned me about the work they were doing, but no one knew where the tunnel would end up. If I had left the castle, as Fide-Yori wanted, we would have been lost.
Yori wanted, we were lost.
- It was time to find out where they had chosen to invade the fortress," said Daïské, who was still listening.
- They still have a day's work ahead of them," said Yoké-Moura.
Now that I know where they are, I'll keep an eye on them. But follow
But follow me, my son; I want to entrust to you alone the delicate mission that must now be carried out.
The General returned to the pavilion he lived in at the castle.
He wrote a long letter to the man in command of the troops who had returned from Yamasiro; his name was Aroufza and he was Harounaga's brother. He gave this leader all the necessary instructions for the next day's battle.
When he had finished, he called a peasant who was waiting in the next room.
- This man knows where the underground begins", Yoké-Moura told his son, "when the time comes, he will lead the army there. You will leave with him; try not to be seen by anyone; you will take this letter to Aroufza, and tell him that he must carry out my orders scrupulously and allow himself to be guided by this man. Be careful, be skilful, my son; it is easy to reach Arufza's camp; but remember that we must reach it without being seen, so as not to arouse the spies that Hiéyas undoubtedly has among us. As soon as you arrive, send a messenger to me.
- I'll leave straight away and take advantage of the darkness," said Daïské. "In a few hours, Father, you'll have news from me.
The young man left with the spy.
As soon as daylight came, Yoké-Moura went to the siogoun to greet him.
Fidé-Yori received him coldly; he was unhappy with the general and could not explain his inaction.
- Yoké-Moura," he told him, "my confidence in your great valour and your devotion to me alone prevent me from ordering you to begin the attack immediately. Here are three great days lost. So what are you doing? Why are you lingering like this?
- I couldn't start until I'd found something I was looking for," said Yoké-Moura.
- What does this mean?" exclaimed the Siogun, seized by a terrible anxiety.
He in turn wondered if the general had lost his mind; he looked at him, the warrior's face expressed a joyful tranquillity.
- I have indeed been told," continued Fidé-Yori, "that for some time you have been wandering night and day like a madman.
- I'm resting now," said the general, "I've found what I was looking for.
The siogun bowed his head.
- Decidedly, he thought, he is mad.
But Yoké-Moura answered his question.
- Wait until tomorrow to judge me," he said, "and don't worry, master, if you hear any noise tonight.
After saying these words, he left and went to give orders to his soldiers.
He ordered two thousand men out of the town, who went to camp on a small hill in view of the enemy.
- People were preparing to attack, Osaka was saying.
The people invaded the hills, the pagoda towers and all the high places.
Fide-Yori himself climbed, with a few courtiers, to the top floor of the great Tower of the Golden Fish, in the centre of the fortress.
From there he could see Aroufza's soldiers on the plain, around eight thousand men, and further away, indicated by a few glimmers of weapons and armour, the enemies camped near a small wood. On the seaward side, in the bay, the fleet of war was just setting sail; closer by, the streets of the town, cut by innumerable canals like azure ribbons, were filled with anxious crowds. Work had been suspended; everyone was waiting.
The troops stayed put.
Fidé-Yori was getting tired of watching: a dull irritation was beginning to grow inside him. He sent for Yoké-Moura.
- The general is nowhere to be found," he was told. "His army is under arms, ready to come out at the first signal, but so far only two thousand men have left the fortress.
Finally, towards evening, the enemy made a move. They advanced towards the town. Immediately, the soldiers placed on the hill by Yoké-Moura rushed down. A few shots rang out and the battle began. The enemy was outnumbered. At the first impact, they drove back the men of the siogoun.
- Why doesn't Aroufza move?" said the siogoun; "is it treason? I really don't understand what's going on.
Many hurried footsteps were heard in the tower, and suddenly Yoké-Moura came out onto the platform.
He was holding a large bale of rice straw in his arms. The men following him were carrying brush.
The general briskly pushed aside the courtiers and even the siogoun.
He formed a huge pyre and set it alight.
The flame soon rose up, clear and brilliant; its glow illuminated the tower and made it impossible to see across the plain, which the twilight was invading.
Yoké-Moura, leaning over the balustrade, shielded his eyes with his hands and tried to pierce the gloom with his gaze.
- Good," he said.
And he went back down quickly, without answering the many questions that were put to him.
He took up position some distance from the point where the underground passage was to end. It was finished, because since the middle of the day the digging had stopped; only a thin layer of earth had been left, which was to be drilled at the last moment.
When night fell, the general listened carefully and heard the hum of footsteps; the enemy had entered the underground. That's when the flame was lit on the tower. At this signal, Aroufza was to move off and attack the enemy at the other end of the underground passage.
Night had fallen. Yoké-Moura and his soldiers waited in the deepest silence.
At last, small knocks began to be heard. The knocking was done carefully, to produce as little noise as possible.
The general and his men stood motionless in the shadows, listening intently. They could hear the earth falling in clods, then it cracked and the sound of breathing could be heard.
Soon a man's torso emerged from the opening, standing out against a shadow that was deeper than the darkness. He went out, another followed.
No one moved.
They moved forward cautiously, looking all round, and about fifty of them were let out; then suddenly, with ferocious cries, they were rushed on. They tried to retreat towards the underground.
- We've been betrayed!" they shouted to their companions.
Do not advance, flee.
- Yes, traitors, your deeds have been discovered," said Yoké-Moura, "and you have dug your own grave.
All those who had emerged from the underground were massacred. The cries of the dying filled the palace.
People ran with lights. Fide-Yori himself came between two hedges of torch-bearing servants.
- This is what I was looking for, master," said the general, showing him the gaping hole. Now do you think I did the right thing in not leaving the fortress?
The Siogun was dumb with surprise at the danger he had run into.
- No one will ever get out of there alive!
- But I think they'll escape through the other exit," said Fidé-Yori.
- You were surprised just now by Aroufza's immobility on the plain: he was waiting for the best part of the enemy army to enter this path before closing the gate on them.
- Then they're lost!" said the siogun. Forgive me, bravest of my warriors, for having doubted you for a moment, but why didn't you warn me of what was going to happen?
- Master," said the general, "spies are everywhere: there are spies in the fortress, in your palace, in my room. One surprised word and they were warned. At the slightest noise, the bird they wanted to catch flies away.
No one ventured further underground.
- They think they can escape," said Yoké-Moura; "they'll come back when they realise their retreat is cut off.
Soon, in fact, cries of distress were heard. They were so heart-rending that Fidé-Yori shuddered.
- The unfortunates," he murmured.
Their situation was horrible indeed: in this narrow corridor, where two men could barely advance abreast, where breathing was difficult, these distraught soldiers, mad with fear, were pushing each other, crushing each other in the darkness, desperate for light, even the light of night, which would have seemed brilliant next to this sinister shadow.
A terrible push pushed some of the men out of the underground passage and they fell under the soldiers' swords.
In the midst of the cries, these words could be heard confusedly:
- We surrender.
- Open up and let us out.
- No," said Yoké-Moura, "no mercy for traitors like you. I told you, you dug your own grave.
The general had stones and earth brought in to fill the opening.
- Don't do this, I implore you," said Fidé-Yori, pale with emotion, "these cries are tearing my heart apart; they are asking to surrender; let's take them prisoner, that's enough.
- You don't have to beg me, master," said Yoké-Moura; "your words are my orders. Whoa, you lot," he added, "stop shouting, you're being pardoned; you can get out.
The shouting redoubled.
It was impossible to get out. The terrifying thrust had suffocated many of the men, and the corpses blocked the opening; they formed a compact rampart, swelling from moment to moment, impassable. All had to perish; their groans made the ground tremble; they crushed each other, biting each other, their sabres digging into their ribs; their breastplates shattered with their bones; they died in the midst of an intense shadow, suffocated in a sepulchre that was too narrow.
They tried in vain to clear the entrance from outside.
- What a horrible thing, war!" cried Fidé-Yori, who fled in shock.
Soon the cries became less frequent, and then there was complete silence.
- It's over, they're all dead," said Yoké-Moura, "all that remains is to close the grave.
Five thousand men had perished in this underground passage several leagues long.
XX
THE MESSAGERS
Hiéyas himself had advanced, with fifty thousand men, a few leagues from Soumiossi. He had come by sea, passing very far from the coast so as not to be seen by the soldiers of Massa-Nori, camped on the banks of the province of Issé.
Hiéyas was quickly aware of all the defence plans put in place by the Fidé-Yori generals, and he did his utmost to thwart all the predictions of his adversaries. He let them blockade Nipon Island and, setting sail, he advanced behind their lines and landed between Osaka and Kioto. His aim was to lay siege to Osaka as quickly as possible, the capture of which would put an end to the war.
Although he was ill, he had come this far to be at the very heart of the struggle, his weakened nerves unable to withstand the feverish wait for news.
It was he who had devised the idea of digging an underground passage under the town and the moats to penetrate the fortress; he knew that it could not be impregnated by force, and this bold attempt could succeed. The loss of the two thousand soldiers taken on Dragonfly Island had upset him, but the success of General Hachisuka in capturing a village close to Osaka consoled him. He waited impatiently for the outcome of the adventure, sitting in his tent, looking out over the ocean, which was shaking the war junks. The sea was very rough; a stormy wind blew in from the open sea and raised high waves whose sharp tops foamed.
It was not good weather for small boats, for fishermen's boats.
The Prince of Nagato's fleet was at sea.
It had set off from Soumiossi with the intention of getting closer to the point occupied by the enemy, to see if they were in force and if Hiéyas had really advanced that far. Nagato didn't want to believe it.
But the wind picked up and suddenly became furious.
- Let's head for land, and quickly," cried Raïden, looking at the horizon from which slate-coloured clouds rose like mountains.
- Do you think we can't hold out to sea?" asked the prince.
- If we're still here in an hour, we'll never see land again.
- Luckily, the gust is blowing in from the open sea," said Nata, "and we'll be pushed straight to the coast.
- Come on," said Nagato, "especially as the boat's speed doesn't suit me very well. Will it last like this?
- Certainly," said Raïden, "the sail will support us a little, but we'll be dancing.
- The wind will blow me away," said Loo, who was piling up bundles of ropes and chains to make himself heavier.
We hoisted the sail and began to flee; the boat leapt up, then seemed to sink, leaning to the right or left, the sail touching the water. You could no longer see the horizon on either side, but only a succession of hills and valleys that distorted and reformed, sometimes a wave jumped into the boat and fell with a sharp noise, as if a bunch of stones had been thrown.
Loo was stunned by the breath of the wind, which never drew breath and sent a shower of foam in his face; he found on his lips the salty taste that had so displeased him when he had almost drowned.
- Pass me the bailer," said Nata, "the boat is full of water.
Loo looked for a moment.
- I can't find it," said Loo, "I can't see a thing, the wind is blowing my eyelashes into my eyes.
The prince picked up the bailer himself and gave it to the sailor.
- Are we still far from land?" he asked.
Raïden climbed onto a bench seat holding onto the mast and looked out over the waves.
- No master," he said, "we're making fast speed. In a few minutes we'll be there.
- And the other boats," said Loo, "you can't see them any more.
- I can see them," said Raïden. Some are very close to land, others are further away than we are.
- Where are we going to land?" asked the prince, "in enemy territory perhaps, because at the moment Japan looks like a chessboard; the white squares are in Fide-Yori, the red squares in Hiéyas.
- As long as we're not thrown onto any rocks, everything will be fine," says Nata, "the usurper won't worry about poor sailors like us.
- I'm not a sailor," said Loo, pointing to his sabre, "I'm a lord.
The sky darkened, a dull rumble rolled around the horizon.
- This is my boss speaking," said Raïden. Steer left, Nata," he added, "we're heading straight for a rocky bank. More! More! Careful, Prince! Hold on tight, Loo, this is it, this is the hardest part.
Indeed, the storm was raging and, close to the shore, the waves were leaping up and down with a sort of madness. They came in a furious gallop, their foamy crest driven forward; then they poured in like cataracts. Others would turn back, leaving a broad sheet of white foam on the sand.
The sail was lowered quickly; the mast was lowered: all we had to do was let the sea push us along. But it seemed impossible not to be torn to pieces by these formidable waves, which struck the boat with redoubled blows, breaking against it and leaping over it every now and then.
Fortunately, the bottom was fast approaching.
Raïden suddenly jumped into the waves. He had a foothold. He pushed the boat backwards with all his strength.
Nata also got out and pulled the boat by a chain. Soon its keel sank deep into the sand.
We hurried ashore.
- It's terrible, the sea!" said the Prince of Nagato when he reached the shore, "how it howls! how it sobs! What despair, what terror, makes her panic so? Does it not seem as if she is fleeing in pursuit of a formidable enemy? It's really a miracle that we managed to escape her!
- Unfortunately, you can't always escape her," says Raïden, "she devours a lot of sailors. How many of my companions have lain beneath its waves! I often think of them, in the storm, I think I hear them and I tell myself that it is with the voices of the shipwrecked that the sea laments and cries.
All the boats had reached the shore one after the other without serious accident; some were half broken, however, by the impact against the shore.
- Where are we?" said the prince, "let's try to find out.
The boats were pulled as far as possible out of the reach of the sea and we left the flat white beach that stretched as far as the eye could see.
Above the low cliff, formed by the accumulation of sand, we found a large, half-cultivated plain, which seemed to have been abandoned. A few huts stood out and we walked towards them.
We called out, but no one answered.
- The noise of the wind has deafened the inhabitants," said Loo.
He began banging his fists and feet against the doors.
The huts were empty.
- It seems that we are in the game of Hiéyas on the chessboard that you were talking about earlier," said Raïden, "the peasants are not fleeing from the troops of the siogoun.
- If we're close to the enemy, so much the better," said the prince, "since it's the enemy we've been looking for.
- How dark it is," exclaimed Loo, "it looks like night.
- It's the storm," said Nata, "these huts are the perfect place for us to take shelter.
Indeed, the rain soon began to fall in torrents, and the few trees scattered across the plain bent to the ground with all their branches driven to one side. Thunder rumbled.
The sailors invaded the deserted huts; they were tired, they lay down and fell asleep.
Meanwhile, the prince, leaning against a doorframe, watched outside as the rain, as hard as the stalks of corn, dug into the ground; sometimes the wind broke it up and blew it away as dust.
In reality, Ivakoura didn't see what he was looking at; what he saw was Kioto's palace, the verandah amidst the flowers, the queen slowly descending the steps, searching for him with her eyes, half-smiling at him. He was beginning to feel the unbearable pain of this long separation. He told himself that perhaps
perhaps he would die without seeing her again.
Two men appeared on the plain. Battered by the storm, they hurried along the path.
Nagato instinctively hid in the doorway and watched the men.
They were dressed like peasants, but the wind, which blew their clothes haphazardly, showed that they were armed with swords.
They marched straight towards the huts.
The prince woke up Raïden and Nata and showed them these armed peasants who were still approaching, blinded by the rain.
- You see," he said, "in wartime, fishermen are not what they seem, and neither are the peasants.
- These people have replaced their spades with sabres," said Raïden. Are they our enemies or our allies?
- We'll find out," said Nagato, "because we're going to take them prisoner.
The two men came forward with their heads down to protect their faces from the rain; they thought the huts were deserted and ran towards them to find shelter.
- Come in and dry off," shouted Raïden when they were close, "the rain is bouncing off your skull like a waterfall off a rock.
Hearing this voice, the new arrivals leapt back and fled.
We soon caught up with them.
- What does this mean?" said Raïden, "why are you fleeing so quickly?
Do you have something to hide?
- You'll show us," said Nata with her good, silly laugh.
All the sailors were awake and gathered in the same hut.
The two men were brought before the prince. On their heads they wore hats like mushrooms that hid half their faces, and on their shoulders they wore coarse coats of unbraided straw that made them look like thatched roofs. They were dripping.
- Who are you?" asked Nagato.
They stared at the prince with a dumbfounded expression? one of them stammered something unintelligible.
- Speak more clearly," said Nagato. Who are you?
Then they both shouted together:
- Peasants.
Loo, sitting on the ground with his chin in his hand, looked at them. He burst out laughing.
- Peasants!" he said, "more like monkeys; your feigned naivety does a poor job of hiding your malice.
- Why did you try to flee?" said the prince.
- I was afraid," said one, stamping his feet and scratching his head.
- I was scared," repeated the other.
- You're not peasants," said the prince.
why are you hiding two sabres in your belt?
- It's because... there's war everywhere, it's good to be armed.
- There's war everywhere," repeated the other.
- Come on!" cried Raïden, "tell the truth; we're friends of Hiéyas; if you're one of us, you have nothing to fear.
One of the men gave Raïden a quick glance.
- Strip them of their weapons and search them," said the prince to the sailor.
- By all the Kamis, you have beautiful swords!" cried Raïden; "they must have cost you a lot of money. You must be rich peasants.
- We took them from dead soldiers.
- So you're thieves!" exclaimed Loo.
What's all this?" said the sailor, grabbing a piece of paper carefully hidden under the clothes of one of the strangers.
- Since we can't escape, we might as well admit the truth; we're messengers," said one of the men, losing his stupid look. This is a letter written by the general Hachisuka to Hiéyas.
- Very well," said Raiden, passing the letter to Nagato.
- If you really are servants of the same master as us," said the other messenger, "don't keep us any longer, let us carry out our mission.
- When the rain stops," said Loo.
The prince opened the small paper bag, sealed at one end with rice glue, and took out the letter. It read as follows:
"General Hachisuka rushes forehead to the ground before the illustrious and all-powerful Minamoto Hiéyas.
"Happy days are followed by unhappy ones, and it is a disaster that I am ashamed and pained to announce to you today. The subterranean affair, so well imagined by your great mind, has been carried out. With enormous effort, thousands of soldiers working night and day, came to the end of the work; we were sure of victory. But Marisiten, the genius of battles, was cruel to us. By some treachery, Yoké-Moura was warned, and I hardly dare tell you that five thousand heroes died on the narrow road we had dug, without the enemy losing a single man.
We have regained the position in the village that we had lost for a moment. So nothing is compromised yet, and I intend to announce to you soon a dazzling revenge.
"Written under the walls of Osaka, on the fifth day of the seventh moon, in the first year of the Fide-Tadda siogun".
- This is happy news, my friends," said the prince, who had read the letter aloud, "and I want to take it to Hiéyas myself. I'd be curious to enter his camp and slip into his tent.
- So you are not friends of Hiéyas, as you said? said one of the messengers.
- No, we're not his friends," said Nagato, "but what do you care, since I'm carrying the message for you?
- It's true, all in all, I don't mind, especially as bringing bad news is always poorly received.
- Where is the camp of Hiéyas?
- Half an hour from here.
- On which side?
- To the left, on the edge of the plain, in a wood.
- Is Hiéyas there in person?
- He's there now.
- Is there a password to enter the camp?
- There is," said the messenger hesitantly.
- Do you know it?
- Of course, but I mustn't say it.
- Then Hiéyas won't get the message.
- That's true, isn't it? Are you determined to keep us here?
- Absolutely," says Nagato, "and we won't harm you if you tell the truth, and we'll kill you if you deceive us.
- Well, the password is Mikava.
- The name of the province where Hiéyas is prince," said Nagato.
- Precisely. What's more, you have to show the sentries three chrysanthemum leaves engraved on an iron blade.
The speaker pulled a small iron plate from his belt and handed it to the prince.
- Is that all," asked Nagato, "you told the truth?
- I swear it. Besides, our lives are in your hands and will depend on our sincerity.
- So give us only your hats and straw cloaks.
The messengers obeyed, then went to lie down in a corner.
- You will accompany me, Raïden," said the prince.
The sailor, proud to have been chosen, swelled with pride.
- And me?" said Loo, pouting.
- You'll stay with Nata," said the prince. Later, perhaps tonight, I'll need you all.
Loo withdrew disappointed.
They waited until evening, and then the Prince and Raïden, disguised in their turn as peasants, headed for the camp of Hiéyas.
The sailors watched their leader leave with concern.
- May your venture succeed! they shouted at him.
- May Marisiten protect you!
The rain had stopped, but the wind was still blowing, passing over the fallen grass with a silky whistle; in the still-clear sky, thick clouds spun rapidly, covering and uncovering the thin crescent moon. At the end of the plain, the wood could be seen silhouetted against the horizon.
- Don't you have any instructions to give me, Master?" asked Raïden when they got close to the wood.
- Observe and remember what you have seen," said the prince, "I want to know if the enemy camp cannot be attacked from any point; in that case I will call Harounaga, who is still in Soumiossi, and we will try to beat Hiéyas. In any case, we'll try to surprise some of his plans.
The sentries had already signalled the arrivals. They shouted:
- Who's there?
- Messengers!" replied Raïden.
- Where are they from?
- From Osaka; General Hachisuka sent them.
- Do they know the password?
Mikava!" shouted the sailor.
A soldier approached with a lantern. Then the prince pulled from his belt the iron plate on which the chrysanthemum leaves were engraved.
- Come," said the soldier, "the master is impatiently waiting for you.
They entered the wood.
A few lanterns hung from the trees, sheltered from the wind by two shields, and they walked on straw that had been dragged out of the tents by the comings and goings.
From far and wide, a soldier holding a high spear, his quiver on his back, would appear standing motionless; behind the trees, under the half-open tents, other soldiers could be seen drinking or sleeping. Beyond, the gaze was lost in a deep darkness.
The tent of Hiéyas was set up in the centre of a clearing that had been carved out like a room and surrounded by a red drapery fixed to stakes; a large banner fluttered on the tent, tormented by the wind; two archers stood on either side of the opening made in the first wall of cloth.
The messengers were ushered in.
Hiéyas was seated on a folding chair. He seemed weighed down by age, slumped over, his head bowed on his chest, his lower lip hanging down, his eyes watery and weary. To see this attitude and this dazed expression, one could not believe in the powerful genius, the tenacious will locked up in this debilitated and ugly envelope. However, the spirit kept watch, lucid and ardent, exhausting the body, overwhelming it with fatigue and heroic contempt.
- Any news from Osaka? he said, quickly.
The letter was handed to him, which he hastily unfolded.
The wind penetrated to the tent, stirring the flames of the lanterns hanging from the central pole. The forest rustled violently and we could hear the sea crashing against the shores.
Hiéyas gave no indication of the feelings he was experiencing as he read General Hachisuka's letter. He beckoned to some of the chiefs in his tent to approach him, and handed them the letter.
Then he turned to the messengers.
- Did Hachisuka give you a verbal message in addition to this letter?
Raiden was about to answer when several men entered the tent.
- Master!" shouted a soldier, "here are other messengers arriving at the same time from different points.
- Good! good!" said Hiéyas, "let them come closer.
One of the newcomers came forward and knelt down. He was carrying something under his cloak.
- Illustrious lord," he said in a firm, triumphant voice, "I have come from the castle of Toza. I bring you, on behalf of my master, the head of the Prince of Nagato.
This time, Hiéyas could not conceal his emotion; his lips twitched and he held out his trembling hands with senile impatience.
Raïden jerked when he heard the messenger, but the prince gestured to him to be silent.
- I'm curious to see that face," murmured the sailor.
The man had uncovered a bag of plaited straw, closed at the end by a rope, and was untying it.
Hiéyas motioned for a lantern to be unhooked and brought close to him.
- Is it really true? is it really true?" he said, "I can't believe it.
The envoy pulled the head out of the bag; it was wrapped in a piece of red silk that seemed to have been dyed with blood.
The cloth was removed, and Hiéyas took the head in his hands and rested it on his knees. A man standing next to him was shining the lantern on it.
The head was so pale that it looked like marble; the deep black hair, knotted at the top of the skull, gleamed with flashes of blue, the eyebrows were slightly contracted, the eyes closed, a mocking smile tightened the discoloured lips.
- If the Prince wasn't standing right next to me, I'd swear this head had been cut off on his shoulders," thought a stunned Raïden.
Nagato, painfully moved, had grabbed the sailor's hand in a nervous movement.
- My poor Sado!" he murmured, "devoted unto death, as you said!
Hiéyas, his forehead bent, gazed hungrily at the head in his lap.
- It's him! it's him!" he said, "he's vanquished at last, he's dead, the one who insulted me so often and who always escaped my vengeance! Yes, there you are, motionless and frightening, you whom the women followed with sighing eyes, whom the men envied and tried to imitate. You are even paler than usual, and despite the scornful expression that your features still retain, you will no longer scorn anyone, your gaze will no longer clash with mine like a sword against a sword, you will no longer stand in my way. You were a noble heart and a great mind, I admit, but unfortunately you failed to understand how unselfish and useful to the country my plans were. You devoted yourself to a lost cause, and I had to break you.
- Truly!" murmured Raïden.
The messenger recounted how the prince had been captured and executed.
- They disarmed him!" cried Hiéyas, "they didn't allow him to kill himself.
- No, my lord, he was beheaded alive, and from the moment his head fell off he never stopped insulting his conqueror.
- Toza is a zealous servant," said Hiéyas with a hint of irony.
- He is a villain," murmured the Prince of Nagato, "and he will atone for his crime harshly. I will avenge you, brave Sado.
- How cold death is!" said Hiéyas, whose hands froze at the touch of the pale flesh, and who handed Sado's head to one of the chiefs standing next to him. Toza can ask me whatever he likes," he added, addressing the envoy, "I won't refuse him anything; but there was another messenger, what does he have to say?
The second messenger came forward and bowed.
- Your soldiers have taken Fusimi and are about to begin their attack on Kioto.
On hearing these words, the Prince of Nagato, who was still holding Raiden's hand, squeezed it with such force that Raiden almost screamed.
- Kioto's attack! What does that mean?" said the prince in fright.
- If it's like that," said Hiéyas, rubbing his hands together, "the war will soon be over. Once the Mikado is in our power, Osaka will fall of its own accord.
- We have to get out of here," said the prince in Raïden's ear.
- Hiéyas is just dismissing the messengers," said Raïden.
As the sheet closing the tent was lifted, a red glow lit up the wood.
- What is it?" asked Hiéyas.
Several chiefs came out of the tent and enquired. A great flame was rising on the seaward side; the wind was activating it and bringing the sound of crackling, fizzing wood.
- What could be burning on this beach?
- There's no village on this side.
Information was coming in.
It's boats," someone said.
- Our boats!" sighed Raïden, "well, that's nice!
- We don't know where they came from, but suddenly we saw them washed up on the beach.
- Are there many of them?
- About fifty of them. We walked towards them; they were deserted.
These large, well-equipped boats looked suspicious.
- We remembered Soumiossi.
- So we set fire to them. Now they're blazing merrily away.
- What a misfortune! What a misfortune!" said Raïden, "our beautiful boats! What are we going to do?
- Silence," said the prince; "let's try to get out of here.
- It may not be as easy as getting in.
They realised that they were free in the camp, no one was paying any attention to them, so they set off in search of a way out.
- They're attacking Kioto and I'm here!" said the prince in extraordinary agitation; "our flotilla is destroyed, I'd need two hundred horses; where can I get them?
- There's no shortage of them here," said Raïden, "but how can we get hold of them?
- We'll come back with our companions," said the prince, "look how these horses are tied.
- Simply by the bridle to the trunks of the trees.
- They are placed behind the tents in groups of five or six, as far as I can see in the dark?
- Yes, master.
- We'll have to take them.
- We'll do what you tell us," said Raïden, without objecting that it might be impossible.
They had arrived at the edge of the wood, at the point where they had entered the camp. The sentries were being relieved and the one who had brought them in recognised them.
- Are you leaving already?
- Yes," said Raïden, "we're taking orders with us.
- Bon voyage!" said the soldier.
And he motioned to the man replacing him to let them through.
- Well, we're almost out of here," said Raïden when they reached the plain.
The prince walked quickly. Soon they were back in the huts. All the sailors were on their feet and in despair.
They ran to meet the prince.
- Master! Master!" they cried, "our boats are burnt.
What will become of us?
- It was that infamous Hiéyas who did this," cried Loo; "but I will take revenge on him.
- Do you have your weapons?" asked Nagato.
- Of course we have our swords and guns.
- Well, now you must show me that your courage is worthy of the confidence I have in it. We have to do something heroic that may cost us our lives. We are going to penetrate the camp of Hiéyas, jump on his horses and flee towards Kioto. If we're not dead, we'll be in the sacred city before sunrise.
- Very well!" said Loo, "let's enter the camp of Hiéyas, I've got my idea.
- We're ready to follow you," said the sailors, "our lives are yours.
- The darkness will hide us from the eyes of our enemies, and the sound of the wind stirring the leaves will prevent the sound of our footsteps from being heard. Maybe we'll get through. The only thing that worries me is that we won't have time to steal the head of the brave servant who died for me, so that we can bury him with the respect he deserves.
- What head?" Loo asked Raïden quietly.
- I'll tell you what I know," whispered the sailor.
- Let's split up," said the prince, "one by one we have a better chance of going unnoticed; if we have to meet again, it will be on the other side of the wood. May the Kamis protect us!
The sailors dispersed. The darkness was deep and they suddenly disappeared.
Loo stayed by Raïden's side, questioning him about everything he had seen in the camp. When he'd had enough, the child escaped and ran ahead.
He had a plan, two in fact, since he knew about the adventure with the severed head: he wanted to steal the head and then take revenge for the burning of the boats. For him, entering the camp without being seen was just a game: he had the silent walk of a cat; he knew how to leap, slide and lie flat on his stomach without stirring a blade of grass; he wouldn't have aroused a watchdog.
The lights of the camp guided him; he ran straight towards the edge of the wood; he wanted to be the first to enter.
He almost reached the sentry without seeing her; he threw himself flat on his stomach: she didn't see him.
- I've got it," he said, slipping into a thicket; "the hardest part is over.
The wind was still blowing and great flashes of blue filled the night from time to time.
- Ah god of storms!" said Loo as he ran on all fours under your leaves, "you're behaving badly; bang your gongs as much as you like, but turn off your lantern. As for you, Futen, genius of the wind, blow! blow! even harder!
With the exception of the sentries, the whole camp was asleep; in the intermittent wind, one could hear regular breathing and a few snores. Loo was heading, according to Raïden's indications, towards Hiéyas's tent. He reached it and recognised the red draperies that formed a wall around the tent. Two archers stood in front of the entrance. Lanterns hung from poles above them, and they leaned against the poles with their backs.
- Yes! yes! look out to sea for the last glimmers of our burning boats," said Loo, "that will prevent you from seeing me go by.
He crawled under the drapery, flattening himself against the ground; but to reach the tent, he still had a fairly wide, bare and lit space to cross.
He hesitated for a moment and glanced at the archers.
They have their backs to me," he said, "I think they're sleeping standing up.
He stood up and, in three leaps, reached the edge of the canvas; then he sank underneath.
A blue lantern lit up the inside of the tent. Hiéyas, lying on a silk mattress, his upper body supported by a large number of cushions, was sleeping soundly; sweat was beading on his forehead and he was breathing heavily.
Loo looked up at the former regent and made a face at him, then looked around the tent.
On a mat, not far from the master, a servant was sleeping.
A writing case and a few rare porcelain cups lay on a very low, black wooden stepladder; in one corner, a complete suit of armour, collapsed in on itself, gave the impression of a man cut into pieces. A large red lacquer chest, on which the three chrysanthemum leaves, the insignia of Hiéyas, protruded in gold, caught the light and gleamed.
The straw bag containing Sado's head was leaning against the chest. Hiéyas had wanted to keep it so that he could show it to all his soldiers the next day.
Loo guessed that the severed head must be locked in this bag; he crawled over to it and opened it; but at that moment Hiéyas woke up. He gave several groans of pain, wiped his forehead and took a few sips of a drink prepared for him.
The child had hidden behind the chest and was holding his breath. Soon the old man fell back onto the cushions and dozed off again.
Loo then pulled his head out of the bag and carried him away.
No sooner had he left the tent than cries of alarm rang out from all sides. The trampling of horses and the clash of weapons could be heard over the constant rustling of the trees in the wind.
Hiéyas woke a second time and stood up, breathless from the start. He pulled aside the sheet that closed the tent.
A flash of lightning dazzled him, then he saw nothing but deep darkness. But soon, in the light of a new flash, longer and brighter than the first, he saw, with horrible surprise, the man he had thought dead, the man whose lifeless head he had held in his hands a few moments before, the Prince of Nagato, sword in hand, riding a horse that seemed to Hieyas to make no sound.
His weakened nerves, his mind overexcited by fever and still numb from sleep, did not allow him to react against a superstitious fear; his fortitude deserted him; he let out a frightening cry:
- A ghost! a ghost!" he cried, spreading fear throughout the entire camp.
the whole camp. Then he fell roughly to the ground unconscious.
They thought he was dead.
Some of the chiefs also recognised the Prince of Nagato and, no less frightened than Hiéyas, completed the confusion in the army.
The cry "A ghost!" went from mouth to mouth. The soldiers, who had left at the sound of the alarm, rushed back to their tents.
Someone heroic had the idea of approaching the bag to see if the severed head was still there. When he saw that it had disappeared, this unbeliever began to shout out the news; confusion was at its height; all these men, so brave in the face of real danger, shuddered in the face of the supernatural; they threw themselves flat on their stomachs, loudly invoking the Kamis or Buddha, according to their beliefs.
The Prince of Nagato and his men were very surprised by the welcome they received, but they took advantage of it and crossed the wood without being bothered.
When they reached the other side of the forest, they waited for each other, then counted each other; not one was missing, they were all on horseback.
- The kamis are really protecting us," said the sailors; "who would have thought that the adventure would end like this?
- And that we would be taken for ghosts!
We were about to set off.
- And Loo!" suddenly exclaimed Raïden, "where is he?
- It's true," said the prince, "he's the only one who hasn't returned.
- He was the first to leave," said Raïden. They waited a few moments.
- Unfortunately," said the prince, "the duty that calls me does not allow any delay, we must leave; it is with sorrow that I abandon this devoted child.
Abandoning Loo! the joy of all, the one who reminded fathers of their children, this little mocking hero, a little cruel, who feared nothing and laughed at everything! We set off, all sighing with heavy hearts.
- Perhaps he got lost in the darkness," said Raïden, turning round frequently.
We had been walking for ten minutes when those behind us thought they heard a hurried gallop. They stopped and listened. A horse was indeed coming; the sound of footsteps was soon mingled with bursts of laughter: it was Loo.
- Raïden shouted, come and pick me up, I'm going to fall, I can't take it any more, I've laughed too much!
Raïden hurried to meet the child.
- Well, here you are," he said, "why did you stay behind?
You scared us.
- It's because I had a lot of things to do," said Loo; "you finished before me.
- What did you do?
- Take this for me first," said Loo, handing Raïden the severed head; it's as heavy as if it were made of stone.
- How did you manage to steal it?
- Yes," said Loo, who kept looking behind him, "and they think it's gone off on its own, so they're all mad at the moment.
We set off again at full gallop to join the prince and those who had accompanied him.
- Has the child returned?" asked Nagato.
- Yes, Master, and he's brought the head of the man who looked like you," exclaimed Raiden with a sort of paternal pride.
- That's not all I did," said Loo, who was still looking back. "Look at those pink lights over there, doesn't it look like the sun is coming up?
- Indeed, the sky is lit up," said the prince, "it looks like the reflection of a fire.
That's just it," said Loo, clapping his hands, "the forest is burning.
- You've started the fire!" cried Raïden.
- Didn't I swear that I would avenge our beautiful boats that are lying on the beach, reduced to ashes?
- How did you do it? Tell us about it," said the sailor.
- Ah!" exclaimed Loo, "I'll tell you: as soon as I stole the head of the victim, I heard cries from all sides. So I looked for a horse to be ready to flee. However, I had no idea of leaving yet. When I was on the mount of my choice, I broke off a resinous branch and lit it on a lantern, which I unhooked and then threw into the straw of the bedding. This straw caught fire immediately, and the wind blowing on my torch activated it. I moved away, setting fire to everything. To my great surprise, the soldiers, instead of jumping on me and wringing my neck, threw themselves on their knees when they saw me, stretched out their hands towards me and begged me to spare them; some took me for Tatsi-Maki, the dragon of the Typhoons, others for Marisiten, and they thought they saw in my horse the boar on which the god of battles stands.
I was writhing with laughter, and the more I laughed, the more frightened they became; so I rode through the forest, taking my ease, lighting a banner here, a dead tree there or a bundle of fodder.
- I would never have believed that an army of brave men could be so terrified by a child!" cried Raïden, laughing heartily.
- If you had seen them," said Loo, "how they mumbled, how they trembled. And there was good reason for it: people were saying on all sides that a ghost had stretched out his sword-wielding arm towards Hiéyas, who had immediately fallen to his death.
- Yes," said Nata, "we've been mistaken for a legion of ghosts.
The glow of the burning forest filled the sky to the zenith. The prince turned his head and looked.
- Loo," he said, "I have to praise myself every day for taking you with me; you have the fearlessness of a hero and, beneath your frail exterior, the heart of a lion. The two deeds you have just accomplished deserve a resounding reward: I give you the title of samurai.
On hearing this, Loo was speechless with emotion. He looked at Raïden, who was riding beside him, and then suddenly threw himself into his arms.
On the Prince's orders, some of the men dismounted and, with the tips of their sabres, dug a grave by the side of the road to bury the head of the heroic Sado.
- We'll come and take it back later to give it the honours it deserves," said the prince.
When the grave was closed, stones were heaped on top of it to find it.
- Now," said the prince, "let's make haste; we must be in Kioto before dawn.
The horses were put into a gallop. A few men went ahead, scouting.
The prince also went ahead of the rest of his troop. He wanted to be alone, to hide his emotion and anxiety. He had not been dreaming; the messenger had indeed told Hiéyas that the attack on Kioto was about to begin. Attacking the sacred capital of the Mikados!
to lay hands on the divine person of the Son of the Gods!
Nagato could not believe such a sacrilege; what's more, the idea that the Kisaki was in danger upset him. The thought of her sovereign power being insulted by one of her subjects, frightened by war cries and the sound of battle, perhaps forced to flee, sent him into a frenzy of rage. He was astonished that he hadn't jumped at Hiéyas' throat and strangled him with his hands the moment he'd mentioned Kioto.
- I had pity and respect for his old age, he said to himself, does such a man deserve pity?
However, in the midst of these thoughts of anger and worry, he could not help feeling a deep sense of joy. To be closer to her, to see her again, to hear again that voice his ears were so eager to hear! Was it possible? His heart swelled in his chest, a smile parted his lips, all he could see was her.
- It's fate," he said to himself, "it's prevented me from leaving Kioto; a premonition warned me that she would need me.
What did he intend to do to defend the sacred city against what were undoubtedly considerable forces? He couldn't say.
However, he had no doubt that he would triumph over his adversaries, whoever they might be. There are sovereign wills that tame events, that, in battle, drive the combatants, exalt their courage and make them formidable.
The Prince of Nagato felt within himself one of these irresistible wills. To save her, it seemed to him that he alone would have scattered an army.
XXI
THE KISAKI
Kioto was only five leagues from the camp of Hiéyas, but the Prince of Nagato made a diversion so as not to enter the town from the Fusimi side, occupied by the victors; they reached the banks of Lake Biva and rode along them.
Daylight was beginning to break. It was still dark all over the land, but the sky and water were whitening; a fine mist hung here and there.
The lake is shaped like a musical instrument called a biva; it stretches behind the mountains that surround Kioto and separate it from the town.
circle, enters Kioto from the south.
By order of General Sanada-Sayemon-Yoké-Moura, General Yama-Kava was to camp with his five thousand men on the edge of the lake at the foot of the mountains, but as he advanced, the Prince of Nagato became certain that Yama-Kava had abandoned the position.
Kava had abandoned the position. He found traces of the camp, the ashes of extinguished fires, the holes dug by the tent poles.
- What does this portend?" he said to himself, "If the general has left his post, it must be that danger has called him elsewhere; perhaps the battle has begun, perhaps it's all over, and I'll be too late!
At this thought, the prince, seized by a terrible anguish, threw his horse towards the mountain and pushed it up a rough and inaccessible path. If he succeeded in climbing the hill, he would reach Kioto in a few moments, instead of having to spend several hours making the long diversions along the banks of the lake and the river.
Loo was the first to set off behind his master; all the sailors soon followed, after calling back the vanguard.
The crest of the hill was reached with great difficulty; it was joined by a shallow curve to another higher peak: this was the mountain of Udji, on which the most delicate tea is harvested.
The Western Orchard, where the poetic struggle presided over by the Kisaki had taken place, was located on this mountain. The prince found this enclosure in front of him, so he took his horse through the palisade and crossed the orchard.
The trees were laden with fruit, the heavy branches bending down to the turf.
The prince stopped at the edge of the terrace overlooking the city, just where a few months earlier the queen had approached him and spoken with tears in her eyes.
He glanced quickly at Kioto.
Black smoke was rising from various points. It was also visible in the dairi's compound. The palace and the town had been set on fire. The fortress of Nisio-Nosiro, on the Wild Goose River
River, was under siege; the horsemen of heaven were no doubt defending it. The Mikado must have taken refuge behind its ramparts. Further on, on the other side of the town, a battle was being waged between Yama-Kava's men and the soldiers of Hiéyas. The latter were more or less masters of Kioto. Yama-
Kava still held the eastern part of the town, but the Hieyas banner flew at all other points.
The Prince of Nagato, his eyebrows contracted, stared at the scene unfolding at his feet; he bit his lips to the quick, full of anger, but retained his lucidity of mind and coolly examined the situation.
When a battle takes place in a city, the combatants are inevitably scattered: the design of the streets and their narrow width force them to divide up. Each street and crossroads has its own special, isolated battle, ignoring the phases of neighbouring battles.
The Prince of Nagato immediately understood the advantage offered by this arrangement of the battle. His small troop, nowhere to be found on the plain where its small size would have been exposed, could, with an impetuous dash, produce a happy effect by surprising the enemy from behind, perhaps throwing confusion into his ranks.
The prince made up his mind quickly, shouting to rally his men who, with great difficulty, had managed to join him, then he threw his horse onto the other side of the high hill and shouted:
- Follow me.
The descent was most perilous, but the energy of the men seemed to be communicated to the horses: they reached the bottom of the slope without accident, then rushed, with formidable impetuosity, into the street most crowded with soldiers.
The noise produced by this precipitous gallop on the flagstones was enormous. The soldiers turned round and saw the street full of horsemen and, with that instinctive fear that men on foot feel when faced with men on horseback, they wanted to pull over. To do so, they jostled and tumbled over each other, trying to invade the side streets. The horsemen fired a few shots, which made it even quicker for the pedestrians to flee. In an instant the street was emptied, and the fugitives went to cause alarm in the neighbouring districts.
It was as if they were caught between two armies.
The street Nagato had entered was very long, crossing almost the entire city, and ended in a small square.
On the other side of the square, the roads leading to it were occupied by Yama-Kava soldiers; in the square itself, the opponents had joined forces.
The battle had only just begun. Despite being outnumbered, Fidé-Yori's supporters were not backing down.
At the entrance to the square, the prince stopped; he was master of the street and he had to hold it.
- Twenty men should go and defend the other end of this street," he shouted, "and two men should stand in front of each alleyway that opens onto it; now the soldiers of Yama-Kava should be told that they must try to join us.
Raïden dashed across the square, a hail of arrows enveloped him, his horse fell, the sailor got up, he was wounded, but he was able to reach the other side of the crossroads.
A burst of musket fire brought down many men. An empty space formed in front of the street occupied by the prince, the enemy soldiers gathered around their leaders, they consulted each other; they were of the opinion that they should withdraw into the neighbouring streets and abandon the square.
They made this move, which was almost a retreat.
From then on, nothing was easier for the men of Yama-
Kava's men than to join forces with Nagato's men. The former crossed the square at a run and entered the conquered street. Soon their generator himself appeared, on horseback, masked, armoured in black horn, spear in hand.
- It's the lord of Nagato!" he exclaimed, recognising the prince. I am no longer surprised to have seen the enemies so rudely repulsed. Victory seems to be your captive.
- If it is true that I have chained her, may she never regain her freedom! said the prince. So what's going on here?
he added. What sacrilege, what unprecedented crime, are we witnessing?
- It is indeed incredible," said the general. Hiéyas wants to kidnap the mikado and burn down the city.
- And for what purpose?
- I don't know.
- I think I can guess," said the prince; "once the mikado was in his hands, he would have forced him to proclaim him siogoun, the whole people would have declared for Hiéyas and Fidé-Yori would have been forced to lay down his arms.
- This man has all the audacity!
- Where is the mikado at the moment?" asked the prince.
- In the fortress of Nisio-Nosiro.
- I thought so, and I think I met you in the battle plan.
- It would be an honour for me," said the general.
- I think your army is going to spread out along this street, like a lake that becomes a river, and envelop the enemy. In this way it will separate them from the banks of the Kamon-Gava, and isolate the attackers, who are fairly few in number, it seems to me, from the fortress.
It's towards the fortress that you should retreat, to take shelter behind its walls.
- This was indeed my plan," said the general, "but without your help I would probably not have been able to break through the enemy ranks.
- Well then, now lead your men towards the fortress, while I hold our opponents here for as long as possible.
The general left.
The soldiers of Hiéyas returned; the initial panic had subsided; through all the left-hand alleys, they attacked the street separating them from the river; they were met with rifle shots and volleys of arrows; they retreated, then came back.
- We have to barricade these alleys," said the prince.
- With what?
The hermetically sealed houses seemed dead, their mere appearance, mute and blind, made it clear that to strike would be useless and would awaken no echo in the souls of the terrified inhabitants.
The shutters were torn down, the windows collapsed, the houses were invaded, a sort of looting began, and everything was thrown out: screens, bronze vases, lacquerware, mattresses, lanterns. With astonishing speed, all this accumulated in a jumble at the entrances to the alleyways. A tea merchant was completely robbed, and all the exquisite varieties of the aromatic leaf, wrapped in silk paper, in lead boxes or in precious chests, lay on the ground, open to arrows and bullets: the air was perfumed.
The enemy pressed on, but could not cross the street.
Towards the river, the sound of another battle could be heard.
The prince sent one of his men to that side.
- As soon as Yama-Kava has triumphed, come and tell us.
The battle was becoming terrible: some barricades had been broken through and people were fighting hand to hand in the street, which was full of dust and smoke.
- Courage! courage!" shouted Nagato to his men; "just a moment more!
At last the envoy returned.
- Victory!" he shouted, "Yama-Kava has crossed the river.
Then Nagato's men began to fall back.
Yama-Kava, protected by the horsemen of the sky, who from the top of the towers were showering arrows on the attackers, had entered the fortress with his five thousand men. The mikado was now out of danger: seven thousand men behind the ramparts were worth the enemy general's ten thousand men in the open. The enemy general-
This one, full of anger, badly obeyed, understanding the mistake he had made by engaging his soldiers in the tangle of streets, rushed at the head of his men to raise their courage, force this passage so well defended and reach the banks of the Kamon-Gava.
He found the Prince of Nagato in front of him, both on horseback. They looked at each other for a moment.
- So it is you," cried the prince, "who are the instrument of this crime, so odious as to be unbelievable; it is you who would have the audacity to lay hands on the divine mikado!
In response, the general shot an arrow at Nagato that grazed his sleeve. The prince retaliated with a rifle shot from almost point-blank range. The warrior fell, never to rise again, onto the neck of his horse, without uttering a single cry.
News of his death spread quickly; the soldiers, left without a leader, hesitated.
- It was said that his sacrilegious audacity had brought him misfortune, and that it might well bring misfortune to us too.
The prince, who noticed this hesitation and the confused remorse that was rising in the soldiers' souls, came up with a plan that would make the victory decisive if it produced the effect he was expecting.
He ran to the banks of the Wild Goose River and shouted to the soldiers guarding the fortress:
- Make the mikado appear at the top of the tower.
His thoughts were understood, and they hurried to fetch Go-Mitsou-No.
Mitsou-No and brought him almost by force, more dead than alive, to the highest tower of the castle.
The Sun Goddess seemed to cast all her rays on this divine man whose equal she was; the mikado's red robes shone, the high golden blade of his crown sparkled on his forehead.
- The Son of the gods! The Son of the gods!
The soldiers raised their heads: they saw this dazzling display of purple and gold at the top of the tower, the man they were not supposed to see, the one who was surrounded by a frightening prestige and whom they had just outraged; they believed that the Mikado was about to take flight and leave the earth to punish it for the wickedness of men.
They threw down their weapons and rushed to their knees.
- Grace!" they cried, "don't leave us; what would become of us without you?
what would become of us without you?
- Sublime Lord! Almighty Master! We are wretched, but your goodness is infinite!
- We drag ourselves through the dust, flooding it with our tears of repentance!
Then they recriminated against their leaders.
- They pushed us, they trained us!
- They made us drunk on sake to make us lose our minds.
- The general paid for his crime with his life.
- Damn him!
- May he be carried off by the foxes!
- May the great judge of hell be inexorable to him!
The mikado looked around the city; he saw it full of smoke on all sides. He stretched out his arm and pointed to the burnt spots.
The soldiers below thought they saw an order in this gesture; they stood up and rushed to put out the fires they had started.
The victory was complete. The Prince of Nagato smiled as he saw how the effect produced by the appearance of the Mikado had lived up to his expectations.
But suddenly, just as he was about to set foot on the drawbridge and enter the fortress, panicked servants rushed to the banks of the Kamon-Gava.
- The queen," they shouted, "we're kidnapping the queen!
- What are you saying?" cried the prince, blushing, "the queen is not in the fortress?
- She hasn't had time to take refuge there, she's at the summer residence.
Without listening further, Nagato rushed like a flock in the direction of the palace, followed by what was left of his sailors, about fifty able-bodied men.
But these men lost sight of the prince, they didn't know the way, they got lost.
Nagato soon reached the gate of the summer palace. Some pages were standing on the threshold.
- This way! This way! they shouted to the prince, pointing out the road at the foot of the mountains.
Nagato took this road, lined with tall trees; it undulated in curves; there was little distance before his eyes; he saw nothing. He bloodied the flanks of his horse; it leapt. He threw down his rifle to lighten his load.
After ten minutes of mad running, he caught sight of the rump of a horse in a cloud of dust; the prince was gaining ground; he soon saw a veil floating and a man turning his head uneasily.
- Who is this man who has dared to take her in his arms?" said Nagato to himself, gnashing his teeth.
The kidnapper threw himself into a valley, and the prince soon joined him. Seeing himself lost, the man slid off his horse and fled on foot, abandoning the queen.
The prince thought he recognised the man fleeing as Faxibo, the former groom who had become Hiéyas' confidant.
It was indeed Faxibo. This man, who respected nothing, seeing the battle lost and the mikado out of reach, remembered Kisaki, isolated and defenceless in the summer palace; he understood the value of such a capture and resolved to kidnap the sovereign. He entered the palace pretending to be an envoy from Yama-Kava. He was on horseback when the queen rode out onto the verandah, so he seized her and fled from the palace before the servants had recovered from their surprise.
The prince had no time to pursue Faxibo, as the horse carrying the queen continued to run.
Nagato rushed towards her and took her in his arms - she had fainted.
He carried her to the shade of a tea bush and laid her on the grass, then dropped to one knee, trembling with emotion, distraught, mad. The dizziness of the run he had just made, the fatigue of the battle and of the sleepless night clouded his mind; he imagined he was dreaming; he looked at the woman who relentlessly filled his thoughts, and blessed the illusion that made him believe she was right in front of him.
Lying there in an abandoned, supple pose, very pale, her head turned back, her body wrapped in the fine folds of her lilac crepe dress, which the rapid beating of her heart lifted, she seemed to be asleep. Her sleeve had been raised a little, revealing her arm; her little hand resting on the grass, palm up, looked like a water lily flower.
- What sovereign beauty! the prince thought ecstatically; surely the Sun Goddess is no more resplendent! It seems as if a light shines through the whiteness of her skin, her mouth is reddened by the blood of a flower, her large eyes under their long black lashes look like two swallows drowned in milk. Don't fade away, celestial vision, stay always like this, my gaze riveted on you!
Gradually he regained a sense of reality, and realised that she was suffering and that he had forgotten to help her.
But what could he do? He looked around for a stream or a waterfall, but saw nothing. So he unfurled his fan and waved it gently over the queen's face.
She remained motionless.
The prince took her hand, thinking that perhaps she was cold; but he got up quickly and stepped back a few paces, frightened by the deep confusion caused by the touch of that soft, warm hand. He called out. No one answered. Those who, like him, were pursuing the queen's kidnapper, had continued straight ahead instead of entering the valley.
Nagato returned to the Kisaki; it seemed to him that she had moved; he knelt down again and gazed at her.
She opened her eyes, then closed them again as if dazzled by the light. The prince bent over her.
- Beloved Queen," he whispered, "come back to yourself!
She opened her eyes a second time and saw the prince. Then a lovely smile parted her lips.
A bird was singing above them.
- It's you, Ivakoura," she said in a faint voice, "you are with me at last! You can see that death is merciful and that it has reunited us!
- Alas!" said the prince, "we are still alive.
The Kisaki stood up and leaned on one hand. She looked around her, trying to remember, then looked back at Nagato.
- Didn't a man snatch me from my palace and carry me off?
- A wretch was indeed guilty of a crime worthy of a thousand deaths.
- What did he want with me?
- He wanted to take you prisoner so that he could impose conditions on the Mikado.
- The infamous man!" exclaimed the queen. I can guess the rest," she added.
You pursued my kidnapper and saved me. That doesn't surprise me. When I was in danger, I called on you! Just now, when I lost consciousness, I thought of you. I was calling out to you.
After saying these words, the Kisaki lowered her eyes and turned her head away, as if ashamed of such a confession.
- Oh, I implore you," cried the prince, "do not retract your words, do not repent of having spoken them, leave this divine dew to a plant scorched by an implacable sun.
The Kisaki raised her big eyes to the prince and looked at him for a long time.
- I do not repent," she said, "I love you, and I confess it proudly. My love is as pure as the light of a star, and there is no reason to hide it. I thought a lot while you were away: I was frightened by the feeling that was penetrating me more and more, I thought I was a criminal, I wanted to tame my heart, to silence my thoughts, but what was the point? Can a flower refuse to be born and bloom, can a star refuse to shine, can the night rebel when the day invades it as you have invaded my soul?
- Have I heard you right! It is to me that such words come from such a mouth," cried the prince, "you love me! you, Daughter of the Gods! Let me take you then; let us flee from the kingdom to a far-off land that will be paradise. You are mine because you love me. I have been so unhappy! Now happiness overwhelms me. Come, let us make haste; life is short to lock up such love.
- Prince," said the queen, "the confession I have just made to you, being what I am, should show you the extent to which my love is free from earthly concerns. I do not belong to this world, I am a wife, I am a sovereign, and no sinful action will be committed by me. My soul, without my will, has given itself to you; could I hide it from you? But if I have spoken today, it is because we must not meet again in this world.
- Never to see you again!" exclaimed the prince in horror. Why do you say such a cruel thing? Why, after opening up heaven before my eyes for a moment, do you suddenly throw me into the tortures of hell? Being deprived of your presence will kill me as surely as being deprived of air and light.
Nagato covered his face to hide the tears he couldn't hold back, but the queen gently removed his hands.
- Don't cry," she said. What is life? Little compared to eternity. I'm sure we'll meet again.
- But what if death were to be disappointing," said the prince, "what if life were to end in nothingness, what if everything were over after the last breath?
- That's impossible," she said, smiling, "because my love is infinite.
- Very well," said the prince, "I will kill myself.
- Swear to me you will do nothing of the sort," cried the Kisaki. What do we know
the will of heaven? Perhaps we don't have the right to escape our destiny, and if we don't accept it, we are obliged to return to earth.
- But that's impossible, I can't bear life," said the prince. Don't you understand what I'm suffering? You say you love me and you torture me like this!
- Do you think I don't suffer? I swear to you that I will die of this love without resorting to suicide.
The Prince had thrown himself on the ground, his face in the grass; great sobs were shaking him.
- You make me despair, Ivakoura," cried the queen, "all my fortitude is shattered by your pain. I am only a woman in front of you; my will is no longer sovereign: what must I do to dry your tears?
- Allow me to see you from time to time as I used to," said the prince; "only then will I be able to let death come.
- See each other again after what I have told you.
- I will forget if I must, divine friend; I will remain your humble and submissive subject. Never a look, never a word will betray the pride with which my soul is full.
The queen smiled as she saw happiness once again light up the prince's still moist eyes.
- You have defeated me", she said, "I thought my resolution was irrevocable; may I not be punished for my weakness!
- Punished! Why?" said the prince, "What harm are we doing?
Are not all the lords of the court admitted to your presence? I alone, because I am blind to anything that is not your beauty, would have been exiled. Wasn't that unfair?
- It was wise and prudent," said the queen, who sighed, "but I've given in, so let's not talk about it any more. Let's go back to the palace," she added.
Let's go and let the people know that I've been saved.
- Oh, stay a moment longer," murmured the prince, "we'll never find ourselves like this, in the middle of nature, alone, far from any eyes. It took civil war, crime and sacrilege to bring this about. Tomorrow all the pomp of your rank will envelop you again, and I will only be able to speak to you from afar.
- Who knows what will happen next?" said the queen, "the Mikado took refuge in the fortress, which was immediately surrounded by soldiers, and I was forced to stay in the summer palace.
- But since then they have been completely defeated," said the prince; "the enemy general has been killed and the army has surrendered; the Mikado is free. But let's not talk about that. Who cares about the war! Tell me, how long have you loved me?
- As long as I've known you," said the Kisaki, lowering her eyes.
I didn't suspect anything, when one day jealousy revealed my love for you.
- You, jealous?
- Yes, and madly so; I felt a strange, continuous pain, I couldn't sleep any more, pleasures irritated me, anger swept me away at every moment and I bullied my wives; the one I thought was in love with you, I hated; one evening, I chased her out of my presence because, on seeing you, she had betrayed her love with a scream. I was passing by on my way back to the palace; you were leaning against a tree in my path, and I can still see you, lit by the moon, pale with your burning eyes.
- Didn't you see that they were only looking at you?
- No, and all night long I wept silently.
- Oh, don't drive me mad!" cried the prince.
- You see," she said, "I'm not hiding anything from you, I'm laying my heart bare before you, trusting in your loyalty.
- I am worthy of that trust," said the prince, "my love is as pure as yours.
- A few days later," continued the queen, "you were kneeling before me in the audience hall. Surprised by your confusion, I let myself talk to you about my maid of honour. You cried out that you didn't love her, looking at me with a look that revealed your whole soul. Do you remember how angry and contemptuous I looked? If you only knew the ineffable joy that flooded through me: the gazelle that a tiger clutches between its claws and then suddenly abandons must feel a sensation similar to the one I was experiencing. I realised then that it was me you loved, your look and your emotion had told me so. When I left you, I ran into the gardens and wrote the quatrain that I gave you so lightly.
- It's there on my heart," said the prince, "it never leaves me.
- Do you recognise this?" said the Kisaki, showing the prince a fan tucked into the silver cloth belt that held her dress.
- No," said Nagato, "what is it?
She took the fan and unfolded it.
It was made of white paper powdered with gold; in one corner was a clump of reeds and two storks taking flight; in the other corner were four verses in Chinese characters.
The thing that you love more than anything else," said the queen, reading the verses, "that you love better than anyone else could love it, belongs to someone else; - so the willow that takes root in your garden - bends down, pushed by the wind, and embellishes the neighbouring enclosure with its branches.
- These are the verses I wrote in the Western Orchard!
exclaimed the prince. Have you kept this fan?
- I never wear another one," said the Kisaki.
They both laughed, forgetting their past suffering, enjoying with delight this minute of happiness. She was no longer talking about returning to the palace.
- If you were my brother!" she suddenly exclaimed, "if I could spend my life with you without being slandered, how delightfully the days would pass!
- And you, cruel woman, wanted to banish me from your presence!
- The Queen had ordered it; faced with your tears, the woman could not obey her! But, in your turn, tell me, how did you love me?
- I've loved you for a long time," said the prince; "my love was born long before you even saw me. When my father abdicated in my favour, I came to make my submission to the Mikado. Just as I was leaving the audience, you walked past me on a gallery. I thought I saw Ten-Sio-Daï-Tsin herself, and I was speechless with surprise and admiration. Your eyes were downcast; your long eyelashes cast a shadow across your cheeks. I can still see you when I close my eyes. A white peacock was embroidered on your dress; lotuses adorned your hair; your hand was absent-mindedly waving a fan made of pheasant feathers. It was only a flash: you disappeared, but from then on you were my whole life... I didn't return to the palace until a year later.
- That was when I saw you for the first time," said the queen.
Everyone was talking about you: my wives were not at a loss for words; your eulogy was on everyone's lips. I was curious to see this hero, to whom all virtues were attributed and who was said to have every grace. Hidden behind a blind, I watched you as you crossed the great courtyard of the dairi. I found that the praise was less than the truth, and I went away greatly disturbed.
- For a year I had waited impatiently for the moment when I hoped to see you again, and that year of waiting ended in disappointment. I couldn't help coming back a few days later; this time I was admitted to a party, which you attended. It was at this party that I realised Fatkoura's interest in me and that I formed the guilty plan of hiding the invincible passion that subjugated me behind a feigned love.
- How she must suffer, the unfortunate woman, to love and not be loved!" said the Kisaki, "I pity her with all my heart. Where is she
is she at the moment?
- In my castle at Hagui, near my father; I sent a messenger to him, so that he could bring me accurate news of the events that have taken place. My father must think I'm dead, because as you probably don't know, my kingdom has been ransacked, my fortress taken and my head chopped off; but never mind all that, I'd give my kingdom and the whole world just to see that lovely hollow that forms at the corner of your lips when you smile.
- Ah," said the Queen, "I too would happily give my diadem and all the splendours that surround me, to be your wife and live close to you; but let us not think of what is impossible," she added, "let us remember that our hope goes beyond the limits of this world.
As she said this, she raised her eyes to heaven.
- See, my friend!" she cried, "these clouds illuminated by bloody reflections, the sun is already setting, is it possible?
- Alas!" said the prince, "we must return to mankind.
- Don't be too sad," she murmured, "for we shall meet again.
