The prince got up and looked for his horses. The one he had been riding had fallen exhausted and was dying. The other, very tired, had stopped a few paces away. With a deep sigh, he took the horse by the bridle and began to guide it over the grass.
As the Kisaki and the prince rode away, the bush that had sheltered them shook, and a man who had been hiding there fled.
XXII
THE MIKADO
Kioto thus escaped the danger she had run; the battle was over, the fires were extinguished. The Queen, who had been carried off by criminal hands at a time when the town was in confusion and terror, was brought back by the Prince of Nagato, through a people drunk with joy. The houses, so tightly closed just a few hours before, were wide open; everyone went out into the streets; the inhabitants chatted with the soldiers; sake barrels were rolled outside, collapsed, sung and danced. People thought they were dead, but now they were alive. There was much to be joyful about: cries went up from a street or a square; they bounced from mouth to mouth and soon the whole town was repeating them:
- Glory to the Mikado!
- Death to Hiéyas!
- A curse on his race!
- Blessings to General Yama-Kava!
- Praise to the horsemen of heaven!
- And glory to the Prince of Nagato, to whom we owe our victory!
- And who brings us back our divine Kisaki," said another.
The prince did indeed step forward, guiding the horse carrying the queen. The crowd moved aside, bowing before her in sudden silence, which ceased abruptly as soon as she had passed.
The queen had pulled her veil over her face and was clutching its folds to her chest with one hand; the horse, covered in foam, was whistling as it went. Nagato held it by the bridle, turning occasionally towards the queen, who smiled at him from behind the gauze of her veil, while all their foreheads touched the ground.
They thus reached the fortress of Nisio-Nosiro and crossed its ramparts. The Sky Riders came to receive the Kisaki. Her wives had stayed behind in the summer palace; she was asked if they should go and fetch them.
- Why?" she said; "aren't we going back to the palace?
They didn't dare answer her, as the Mikado, not reassured, refused to leave the fortress and intended to stay there.
The Son of the Gods was exasperated; victory had not calmed his fear or his anger. He, attacked in his palace! Not by Mongols, not by Chinese, by Japanese! His people, in other words his slaves, those who were not worthy to speak his name, had had the unheard-of audacity to take up arms against him. His sacred person had been forced not just to walk, but to run. The mikado, the one whose gaze could reduce a man to ashes, had fled pale with fear; the rigid folds of his satin robes had been disturbed; he had stumbled in the waves of fabric as he ran through the streets. What had become of the sacred majesty and divine prestige of the descendant of the gods in the midst of this adventure?
Go-Mitsou-No, furious, trembling and stunned, was not pacified by the victory. He ordered the massacre of all the soldiers who had submitted.
- They will come back against me," he said, "kill them to the last man.
- We'll kill them later," replied the Minister of the Right Hand, one of the highest dignitaries in the Dairi.
Then the Mikado cried out:
- Bring me Hiéyas, gouge out his eyes, rip out his entrails, cut him to pieces!
- Later," said the Minister of the Left Hand, "Hieyas is now beyond our reach.
- Call together all the warriors, all the princes, all the ministers," cried the Mikado, "I want to tell them my will.
There was nothing to object to. But the surprise was great: the Mikado had a will and was showing the desire to make a speech. Such a thing had not been seen since the general Yoritomo, under the reign of Tsoutsi-Mikado, had repulsed the invasion of the Mongols and received the title of Siogun for this fine deed. Since then, the sioguns had reigned in the name of the Mikados, who had never thought of taking back the sceptre, which they had entrusted to other hands. Was the true master finally waking up from his long torpor? Was he thinking of seizing power again and governing his kingdom himself? The ministers looked at each other, vaguely afraid; some of them secretly favoured Hiéyas, others were loyal to the dynasty of the mikados, but they lacked energy and feared any revolt against those who were the masters of the army.
But since the son of the gods had the whim to command, there was no escaping obedience. The lords and warriors were hastily assembled in the largest room in the castle. The mikado sat cross-legged on a platform surrounded by a small balustrade. The folds of his robes were arranged around him; then the lords sat on the ground, holding a long, narrow screen in front of their faces, to put an obstacle between their gaze and the face of the sovereign.
The Prince of Nagato, Farou-So-Chan, who was chief of the Sky Riders, Simabara, General Yama-Kava, all the ministers and all the lords were present.
Go-Mitsou-No glared at them, his cheeks even paler than usual, then blew loudly as if he wanted to scatter grains of dust.
Finally he spoke, abruptly and a little tearfully.
- So," he said, "I'm no longer the master, I'm no longer the representative of the gods. I'm under siege, I'm being insulted, I'm being taken over! I'm surprised you're still alive. What is the meaning of all this? Is this the way to treat a god? I'm the mikado, the supreme lord, have we forgotten that? I am on earth for the good of mankind, when I could be with my family in heaven. If things go on like this, I'll leave you. Are you not trembling? What are you thinking about? Have you not heeded the signs of anger shown by my heavenly ancestors?
Remember: not long ago, a mountain suddenly rose from the sea off the island of Fatsisio; isn't that terrible? Isn't it a sign of the displeasure that men inspire in the gods? The ground will shake again, and everything will be turned upside down. A few days after this mountain rose from the water, did it not rain hair in the vicinity of Osaka? Is that not a sign of doom? Are you deaf and blind? Do you no longer understand the threats of heaven? Are you hardened to crime? Do you fear nothing, since you do not tremble under the breath of my wrath?
- We are your faithful servants," said the minister of the Right Hand.
- I, Go-Mitsou-No, the one hundred and nineteenth of my race," continued the mikado, "have been insulted, and if the earth has not split into four pieces it is only because my feet are still on its surface, it has been spared because of me. Yes, men, my subjects, came to the dairi, they forced open the doors, they wanted to take me, to make the son of the gods a prisoner! and, to escape them, I had to flee. A mikado fleeing from men! I'm choking with rage. I'll plunge you into darkness, I'll extinguish the sun, I'll turn the seas upside down and I'll blow the earth to smithereens.
- We are your submissive slaves," said the Minister of the Left Hand.
- If you are my slaves, obey me," cried the son of the gods, "I command that all be ended, that the war cease, and that all things return to their usual order.
- Divine Lord! Master of our destinies!" said the Prince of Nagato, "do you allow me to speak in your presence?
- Speak," said the Mikado.
- The monster known as Hiéyas," said the prince, "fears nothing and insults the gods; yet if the order you have just given were served on him in front of the whole of Japan, he would be forced to obey and would consent to peace.
- Explain yourself," said Go-Mitsou-No.
- It is with sorrow that I note," continued the prince, "that despite the many defeats he has suffered, Hieyas is still the strongest, his supporters are increasing day by day, but they would rapidly diminish and soon all would abandon him if he openly resisted a universally known order emanating from the Mikado.
- There's no doubt about it," cried the ministers and lords.
- What should we do?" asked the Mikado, addressing the Prince of Nagato.
- Sublime master", said the prince, "I am of the opinion that a herald should be sent to all the towns and villages to proclaim your will; at the same time, a deputation composed of a large number of men should be sent to Fide-Yori and Hiéyas to tell them that they must stop the war, that this is your will.
- We will follow your advice," said the Mikado, "it is good advice. To thank you for it, I give you the title of Naï-daï-Tsin.
- Lord," cried the prince, "I am not worthy of such an honour.
- Send the envoys away quickly," said the Mikado. No more war, just rest and peace as before. I feel exhausted by all these emotions," he added more quietly, addressing the Prime Minister, "I could well die.
They soon parted.
On leaving the castle, the Prince of Nagato met a messenger looking for him.
- Where are you from?" asked Ivakoura.
- From Nagato.
So the messenger recounted all the events that had taken place in the province: the battles, the capture of Hagui, the capture of Fatkoura by the lord of Toza.
- How!" exclaimed Nagato, "Fatkoura is in the hands of that wretch who has princes beheaded. I cannot delay my revenge a moment longer. I shall leave at once to rescue her and make this infamous man pay dearly for his crimes and his impudence.
The prince enquired about his little troop. He wanted to know what the morning's battle had left him. Of the two hundred sailors, eighty had been killed, fifty were wounded and around sixty were fit to set sail again.
Raïden had been hit in the arm by an arrow, but the bone had not been affected; the sailor had had his arm bandaged and claimed not to feel anything. He begged the prince to take him with him.
- The journey will do me good," he said, "besides there are only sixty of us left, which is not much to take a kingdom, and with such a small number a man more or less is something.
- I need twenty thousand men to march against Toza," said the prince, "I'm going to ask the Siogun for them.
- Have I behaved badly that you want to keep me away from you?" said Raïden.
- No, my good servant," said the prince, smiling, "come if you like, and stop at Osaka if your wound hurts.
- Are we leaving at once?" asked the sailor.
- Are you mad!" exclaimed the Prince, "we've had a hard night and an even harder day, you're wounded and you don't even think of taking a rest! I confess that if you are tireless, I, who am by nature very unchalant, feel exhausted.
- If I'm allowed to sleep, I'll sleep heartily," laughed Raïden, "but if we had to set off again, I'd still have been able to stand up.
- Where's Loo?" asked the prince, "I lost sight of him in the battle.
- He's sleeping in a house on the shore, and so soundly that I could pick him up and carry him off without him noticing. This young samurai earned his sleep well, he had taken the rifle of one of our fallen companions and I was told that he fought like a demon.
- Is he unharmed?
- Luckily, he doesn't have a scratch on him.
- Well, go and join him and get some rest; tomorrow, around midday, we'll be leaving.
The next day, Nagato went to say goodbye to the Kisaki; she had returned to the summer palace, and he saw her among her wives.
- You're already leaving this city, which owes you victory, without taking the time to rest?
- I'm going away with a heavy heart," said the prince, "but an imperious duty calls me; before peace is signed, I must avenge the outrage done to my name, I must save Fatkoura, my fiancée.
- Is Fatkoura in danger?
- She is the prisoner of the Prince of Toza; a messenger brought me this news yesterday.
- Such reasons cannot be denied," said the queen; "hasten to punish this infamous man, and may the god of battles be good to you.
His voice trembled a little as he spoke; he was going to run into danger again, expose his life and perhaps die.
- I think I'm invincible," said Nagato, "an all-powerful goddess
powerful goddess protects me.
The Kisaki tried to smile.
- May you triumph and return promptly," she said.
The prince walked away. Before leaving the room, he looked at her again; a strange anxiety chilled his heart.
- Every time I part from her, it seems to me that I must never see her again," he murmured.
She also looked at him, troubled by the same anguish; she pressed the tip of the fan the prince had given her to her lips.
He withdrew from her presence.
That same evening, he arrived in Osaka and immediately went to see the siogun.
- It's you!" exclaimed Fidé-Yori with joy. I didn't expect to see you again so soon; your presence is a relief in the midst of the troubles that are weighing me down.
- What!" said Ivakoura, "we are victorious. Why are you sad?
- What are you saying, friend? It is true that Yoké-Moura has driven the enemy from the village they occupied near Osaka; but Harounaga has just been completely defeated by retreating to Yamasiro. Our enemy now holds two thirds of the kingdom.
- Never mind! We have won at Soumiossi; we have thrown the camp of Hiéyas into disarray; we have triumphed at Kioto, and the Son of the Gods, coming out of his torpor for a moment, is going to order the two parties to reconcile.
- Hiéyas will refuse.
- He cannot refuse; he cannot openly rebel against the Mikado.
- He who attacked him with such sacrilegious audacity!
- He attacked him to take control and dictate his will. The imprisoned mikado was nothing; the free mikado, seizing power for a moment, is all-powerful.
- Hiéyas will impose unacceptable conditions on me. It is in his interest to continue the war.
- Nevertheless, he will be forced to obey for the time being, and what we need above all is a few months' respite.
- Certainly, we could then muster all our forces. Communications have been cut off, the armies of the princes have not arrived.
- Are Signenari and his twenty thousand men still on the island of Avadsi?
- Always," said the siogun, "and the young general is in despair at having been reduced to inaction.
- I just want to ask you to give him the order to enter the campaign.
- What do you mean by that?
- I have a personal injury to avenge, and I beg you to lend me this army.
- What do you want to avenge, friend?
- Against one of those who betrayed you, the Prince of Toza. He attacked my kingdom, sacked my fortress, kidnapped my fiancée, and deceived by a resemblance, he thought he had me, and refusing him the death of nobles he cut off the head of one of my servants.
- Such things can only be washed away with blood," said Fide-Yori, "I will give you an order for Signenari, and I will place a war junk at your disposal. Do not spare this infamous Toza, this traitor, envious and cowardly, unworthy of the rank he occupies.
- I'll have his towers razed to the ground, his crops burnt and I'll kill him like a pig being slaughtered," said the prince, regretting that he had only one life to pay for all his crimes.
- May you succeed!" said the Siogun. Alas!" he added, "I was looking forward to seeing you again, and you arrive to leave again! What solitude, what emptiness around me! what sadness! My heart is gnawed by a secret sorrow that I can't talk about. One day I'll confide it in you, and that will make me feel better.
The prince looked up at the siogun; he remembered that several times before a confession had reached the king's lips, and that a sort of savagery and modesty had stopped him there. This time too, Fidé-Yori became confused and looked away.
- What is it?" said Nagato to himself.
Then he added loudly:
- I promise I'll never leave you again, once my revenge is complete.
On leaving the Siogun's flat, the Prince of Nagato met Yodogimi.
- Ah, there you are, beautiful victor," she said to him bitterly, "you have come to reap the praise deserved by your fine conduct.
- It is only from your charming lips that praise would be sweet to me," said the prince, bowing with a slightly outraged politeness, "but they have only harsh and contemptuous words for me.
- If we are enemies, it's because you wanted us to be," said Yodogimi.
- I always wanted not to displease you, but my lack of merit betrayed me. You declared war on me, but I didn't accept it and remained your slave.
- A very unassuming slave who draws all the light to himself, allowing no-one else to shine beside him.
- Do I really shine so brightly?" said the prince. And now, in spite of yourself, you're letting slip the praise you've been refusing me.
- Stop jeering!" exclaimed Yodogimi, "I'm happy to tell you that while everyone loves you and cheers you, I hate you.
- She won't forgive me for the defeat of Harounaga," murmured the prince.
Yodogimi walked away, giving Nagato an angry look. In the past, the beautiful princess had secretly loved Ivakoura; the prince had not wanted to see this love; hence the hatred with which Yodogimi pursued him.
Nagato left the palace, and a few hours later he set sail for the island of Avadsi.
XXIII
FATKOURA
The captive of the lord of Toza found the days long and monotonous. She waited for the avenger, certain that he would come to free her, but telling herself that he was a little late. She was obsessed by Toza's growing love for her. After the execution of the man he believed to be Nagato, he had refrained from visiting her; then, realising that Fatkoura's pain was not very violent, and that she seemed resigned, he had conceived some hope and resumed pestering her. At times he was humble, submissive and suppliant; at others he became angry and threatened; sometimes he tried to soften the young woman with tears, but she remained implacable.
- Tears of a tiger," she would say, "who sees his prey escape him.
- You won't escape me!" cried Toza.
Fatkoura was rigorous with Tika; she had realised that the young maid was favouring the prince's love. Tika's plan was to make his mistress a princess of Toza. "Since the Prince of Nagato is dead! she said. Besides, Fatkoura was soon consoled by his loss.
- You're free now," she told her one day, "you can love the Prince of Toza.
- I will never love anyone but Ivakoura," the young woman replied.
- Loving a dead man won't last, Tika thought.
But from that day on, Fatkoura no longer spoke to her, she didn't even allow her to be in her presence. Tika cried against the door; her mistress pretended not to hear her. However, she missed her young companion more than she cared to admit.
This companion in her misfortunes, this confidante of her sadness and sorrows, was necessary to her life. Captivity seemed harder to her since she had exiled her from her side; she missed one thing above all: not being able to talk about her beloved with Tika.
She resolved to forgive her and tell her that the prince was still alive.
One day, she called him.
Repentant Tika knelt down in the middle of the room, hid her face behind her wide sleeves and let her tears flow.
- You won't speak to me about the Prince of Toza again," said Fatkoura.
- Never, mistress," said Tika, "except to curse him.
- Well, I forgive you; speak to me of my beloved as you did before.
- Alas, he is dead," said Tika; "I can only mourn him with you.
- Don't you think I was quick to console myself?
Tika, surprised, looked up at her mistress, who was smiling.
- But it seemed to me..." she stammered. I thought he was wrong to let himself be defeated in front of you.
- If I told you that he has never been defeated, that he is alive...
- He triumphs in your heart, he's alive in your mind, that's what you mean.
- No, he still breathes the earth's atmosphere.
- Alas, that's impossible. Before our very eyes, I still shudder, his pale head has bounced off the ground.
- This man who died in front of us was not Ivakoura.
- Has his mind been disturbed by pain?" said Tika, looking at her mistress with fear.
- Do you think I'm mad?" said Fatkoura, "you'll see when he comes to open the doors of this prison whether I've told the truth.
Tika didn't dare contradict her mistress, and pretended to believe that Nagato was alive.
- Better this strange hallucination than the despair that would have overwhelmed her! she said to herself.
They began again, as before at the Dairi, to talk about the absent man. They remembered the words he had said, the anecdotes he had told. They tried to imitate the inflection of his voice; they reconstructed his every toilet, remembering his features, his smile, his attitudes.
Often they would have long discussions about a detail, a date, a phrase he had said.
In this way, the hours flew by.
Every day the Prince of Toza sent presents to Fatkoura: flowers, rare birds, marvellous fabrics. Every day Fatkoura flapped the birds and threw the fabrics and flowers out of the window. The prince never tired. In the middle of the day, he would visit the prisoner and talk to her about his love.
One day, however, he entered Fatkoura's house with a strange expression on his face.
He waved Tika away with a gesture that brooked no reply; then he walked over to Fatkoura and stared at her.
- Are you determined always to resist me?" he said after a silence.
- Always, and to hate you as much as I despise you.
- Is that your last word? Think again.
- I don't have to think, I hated you as soon as I saw you, and I'll hate you until the day I die.
- Well then!" exclaimed the Prince in a terrible voice, "I will know how to force you to become my wife.
- I defy you," said Fatkoura, who did not lower her eyes to meet the prince's gaze.
- I swear I will defeat you, just as I defeated your fiancé.
Fatkoura smiled mockingly.
- Yes," continued the prince, "you have wearied my patience; my love had made me merciful, timid, fearful even. I begged, I cried, I waited! I gave your pain time to heal. Your refusals inflamed my tenderness, I got carried away, then I humiliated myself. But I'm tired of this long torture; the prayers are over; no more sweetness, no more tears, from now on you'll be the one crying, begging. One last time, will you
do you want to love me?
- The vulture asks no gratitude from the bird it smothers in its claws, but you demand the love of a woman whose husband you have killed.
- I know you'll never love me," says Toza, "but you'll tell me you love me, and you'll try to make me believe it.
- I'm curious to know the means you'll use to make me say such things.
- You will know soon enough," said the prince, who walked away.
From that day on, a series of sufferings began for the prisoner. First she was separated from Tika and locked in her flat; then the windows were shut, letting in only a little daylight from above. In this way Fatkoura was deprived of the view of the gardens and the fresh evening air. She was served food she didn't like. Little by little, all the things she could use disappeared. Each day made her situation worse. None of the servants cared for her any more. Soon she was put in a prison, then lowered into a dungeon where she had to wait all day for a bowl of cooled rice.
- That's how he makes himself loved!
said Fatkoura, sustained by the hope of deliverance.
But one day, suddenly, these rigours ceased and the young woman was taken back to the pavilion she had first occupied.
She saw Tika again, looking very cheerful.
- The province of Toza has been invaded," she exclaimed; "an army is approaching and we shall be delivered.
- You can see that he is coming, my lord, my beloved husband," said Fatkoura.
He is coming to relieve us of our sorrow and avenge the man who died so bravely in his place.
There is only talk of General Signénari, sent by the Siogoun.
- You can be sure that Ivakoura is close to him.
- It's possible," said the young girl.
- It's certain! So I'm going to see him again at last. After so much suffering, happiness is going to return! Do we know anything about the battle?
- The Prince of Toza left in a hurry. His soldiers, who were not expecting this attack and who were resting after their victory, were completely beaten. The Siogoun army is a few leagues from here.
- They will soon be within these walls," said Fatkoura, "and we will once again undergo a siege, but whereas at Hagui we wanted to win, this time we will tremble at the prospect of victory.
A few days passed in feverish anticipation.
Suddenly, the army of the Prince of Toza, driven by a rout, stormed back into the fortress. The gates were closed.
The siege began.
Without giving the besieged time to recognise each other, the attackers launched an assault.
A terrible noise filled the castle. Inside, there was confusion, constant coming and going, calls and shouts. Outside, there was non-stop banging. Tika ran to get the news, came back and left again. On the third day, the soldiers suddenly moved to the same spot. A breach had been made. Cries of discouragement went up from all sides.
- We'd better surrender.
- We can't hold out much longer.
- We are lost.
Towards midday, the Prince of Toza suddenly entered Fatkoura's flat.
She was standing by a window, looking out; joy lit up her face.
She turned round and saw her enemy staring at her with his arms folded. A sort of instinctive fear seized her when she saw him. He was pale, with a sinister expression. In his right hand he held a bloody sword that was dripping on the floor. He calmly put it back in his belt.
- The battle is lost," he sneered, "I am defeated.
- The man you thought you'd dishonoured is at your door, coming to punish your crimes," said Fatkoura.
- Ah, you know that Nagato is not dead," exclaimed the prince, "but that doesn't matter, he is here, it's true, he's coming to free you, but before he takes you back," he added in a thunderous voice, "before he has crossed the crumbling walls of my castle, you understand, you will belong to me.
Fatkoura leapt backwards and retreated to the back of the flat.
- You can guess," continued Toza, "that I have not left the battle for nothing. The winners are hot on my heels. I won't waste time in vain pleas.
He walked towards Fatkoura.
Help me!" she cried in a heart-rending voice, "Help me, Tika!
Nagato! Come to my aid!
Toza put his hand over her mouth.
- What's the point of screaming? he said, no one will come. Resign yourself, you're mine now; you can't escape me.
He had wrapped his arms around her, but suddenly he saw something shining above her eyes. Fatkoura had just grabbed the long dagger from the prince's belt.
- You are mistaken, I'm escaping you this time too," she said. My last thought is of you, Ivakoura.
Toza shrieked; he had seen the dagger disappear down to the hilt into the young woman's chest, then she pulled it out and threw it to the ground.
At that moment, the panel closing the entrance shattered. The Prince of Nagato, sword in hand, rushed into the room.
He pounced on the Lord of Toza.
- Ah, you wretch!" he cried, "you insult your captive, my betrothed! You add this unparalleled crime to all your previous ones! But the hour of vengeance has come, the earth will be delivered from you!
Toza had drawn his sabre; it clashed with Nagato's, but he was trembling; a superstitious fear froze him; he felt he was going to die.
Ivakoura, with irresistible strength, pushed him back to the other side of the flat. He cornered him against a panel.
Toza, his eyes bloodshot, looked at his opponent in horror; he was defending himself badly. Nagato knocked the sword out of his hand.
- Now you're going to die," said Ivakoura; "I'm going to kill you, not like freeing yourself from a loyal enemy, but like crushing a scorpion.
And with a tremendous blow of his sabre, he nailed him to the partition by the throat.
Fatkoura had not fallen. She had remained leaning against the wall, pressing her hand against her wound, blood gushing through her fingers.
The Prince of Nagato abandoned his enemy, who was writhing in terrible agony, and ran towards her, seeing the blood trickling down.
- What's the matter?" he cried.
- I'm dying," said Fatkoura.
She slipped to the ground, and the prince knelt down beside her and supported her on his knees.
- Is anyone here?" he shouted, "bring the doctors.
- I beg you," said Fatkoura, "don't call, there's nothing anyone can do for my wound. It was to spare your name from being insulted: I've hit hard, I can't be saved. Don't send for anyone, let me die near you, since I couldn't live there.
- Unfortunate woman, this is where I have brought you!" exclaimed the prince, "it is for my sake that you are dying after a life of suffering; you, so beautiful, so young, and who were made for happiness. Ah! why did I find myself in your way?
- I was happy for a while," says Fatkoura, "very happy, you seemed to love me, but I paid dearly for those days of joy. What had I done to you, cruel one, to make you desert me like that?
- You guessed it, sweet princess; an all-powerful, invincible love turned me away from you, my will no longer obeyed my reason.
- What can you do against love? I know how it tames you, I who tried in vain to hate you. Yes! you have experienced these acute tortures, these aimless expectations, these feverish dreams, these hopes that don't want to die; you have known these sobs that don't relieve, these tears that burn like a rain of fire. In the grip of an impossible love, you suffered as much as I did. Isn't that awful, and don't you feel some compassion for me?
- To repair the harm I have done you, I would like to give my life.
- Don't you have rest neither night nor day? It's as if you were at the bottom of a precipice lined with steep rocks; you want to climb back up, then you fall back down again. But I am mad," added Fatkoura, "your suffering is nothing compared to mine, you were loved.
The prince shuddered.
- Yes, she loves you, I know it," continued Fatkoura with a faint smile. Do you think that the jealous gaze of the scorned woman has not been able to read her eyes? How their pride was extinguished when they weighed themselves on you, how her voice, in spite of itself, became soft when it was you she was talking to, what joyful anxiety on your arrival, what sadness after your departure! I watched, and every discovery was a sword stroke for me. Rage, hatred and love tore at my heart. No, you didn't suffer as much as I did.
- Don't blame me, Fatkoura," said the prince, "I didn't deserve such love. You are here dying because of me, and I cannot save you. The horrible pain that is gripping me at this moment will avenge you for all the suffering I have caused you.
- I'm happy now," says Fatkoura, "I could have died before you arrived, and now I'm close to you.
- But you won't die," exclaimed the prince. Am I a fool to stand by, stupid with fear, instead of coming to your aid and having your wound dressed! You're young, you'll heal.
- What's the point," says Fatkoura. Will you love me afterwards?
- I'll love you as I love you, with infinite tenderness.
- Like you love your sister," murmured Fatkoura with a bitter smile. Ah, let me die.
- Alas, this blood that is leaking out and taking your life!" cried the prince, mad with grief.
He began to shout violently. They were heard. Soldiers and servants invaded the room. General Signénari came too, still bleeding from the battle. They moved aside as he passed.
- What's happening, Prince?" he cried.
- A doctor, by grace, and right away," said Nagato; "my fiancée has stabbed herself to death to escape the outrages of the infamous Toza; she's dying.
Fatkoura had lost consciousness.
The palace doctor soon arrived. He uncovered the wound and, on seeing her, made a grimace that was hardly reassuring.
- She didn't spare herself," he said.
- Can she be saved?" asked the Prince of Nagato.
The doctor shook his head.
- I don't think so," he said, "the iron went in too deep.
When I have dressed the wound, the blood will no longer flow outwards, but it will flow inwards and suffocate her.
- And if you didn't close the wound?
- The woman would be dead in a few minutes.
The doctor brought his lips close to the wound. When he touched the raw wound, Fatkoura didn't twitch. He shook his head a second time.
- A bad symptom," he murmured.
When the dressing was finished, he placed the neck of a small bottle containing a comforting liqueur between the young woman's lips and made her drink it all.
Fatkoura soon opened her eyes again; she was still leaning on Nagato's lap. Tika was sobbing at her feet.
She cast a disgruntled glance at those who filled the room; with a slow, painful gesture she motioned for them to be removed.
Signenari let them out and left, leaving only the doctor and Tika.
- You disobeyed me, Ivakura," said the dying woman in a voice that was growing weaker.
- I wanted to save you.
- I am lost... What would I have done in this world?
Spasms seized her, she stretched out her arms, blood choking her.
- Air!" she cried.
Tika rushed over and opened all the windows. Then her mistress saw her.
- Goodbye, Tika," she said, "you see he wasn't defeated, he wasn't dead! We will speak of him no more.
The young woman next to her wept, her face in her hands.
Fatkoura raised her gaze to the prince.
- Let me see you," she said, "it's been so long since my eyes have reflected your face! How beautiful you are, my beloved!
beloved! - You see," she continued, addressing the doctor, "he is my husband, he came to rescue me from captivity, but Toza insulted me and I threw myself to my death.
She spoke in an interrupted, muffled voice, growing weaker and weaker. Her eyes widened, a waxen pallor invaded her face.
- Tell your father about me, Ivakura," she continued; "he liked me! I told him I'd never see the castle again. I was almost happy there. I saw the room where you were born, your childhood clothes... Ah, I did love you!
She was panting; beads of sweat were beading on her forehead. She tore the bandage from her wound.
- Ivakoura," she said, "I can't see you any more.
closer... Ah!" she suddenly exclaimed, "to leave while he's here!
- She's dying!" cried the distraught prince.
- She's dead," said the doctor.
Tika screamed in pain. The prince hid his face in his hands.
- All suffering is over for her," said the doctor; "she rests and forgets her torments in the serene tranquillity of the last sleep.
XXIV
THE PEACE TREATY
Hiéyas agreed to put an end to the war, but, as Fidé-Yori had said, his conditions were harsh.
- I demand," he said, "that one of the following three proposals be carried out: that Fide-Yori abandon the fortress and spend seven years in Yamato; that Yodogimi be given to me as a hostage; or that the walls and ditches of Osaka Castle be demolished and filled in.
Only the last proposal was acceptable. This was the opinion of the generals meeting in a council of war. Yoké-Moura, however, considered the destruction of the ramparts deplorable.
- This peace will be short-lived," he said, "and if the war resumes, what will become of us with our castle dismantled?
He was in favour of letting Yodogimi go.
- My mother! Are you thinking about it," exclaimed the siogun. With such a hostage in his hands, we'd be nothing more than slaves to Hiéyas.
- It's true," exclaimed General Harounaga, "we can't think of that.
- Our walls are demolished and we are defenceless. War was better than a peace like that," said Yoké-Moura.
He would gladly have sent Yodogimi; he didn't care much for a woman.
- Hiéyas has specified," someone said, "that the ditches must be filled in so that three-year-olds can climb in and out without difficulty.
- Ten thousand workmen will have to knock down the walls in a hurry," said another.
Yoké-Moura sighed.
- We have to accept that," said the siogoun, "we'll be forced to do it. At the slightest hint of war, we'll raise the walls and dig up the ditches.
- Since you demand it," said Yoké-Moura, "I agree with you; let's demolish the fortress.
- I have charged General Signénari with going to the camp of Hiéyas to exchange peace treaties; he will represent me with dignity and, I am sure, will conduct himself nobly in this delicate matter.
- I will do my utmost to deserve the trust you have placed in me," says Signénari. I await your orders for departure.
- You have barely wiped away the sword that has just chastised the province of Toza," said the Siogun, "if you need a day's rest, take it.
- I'll leave tonight", said Signénari.
The same day, in fact, the young general, accompanied by a large and magnificent escort, left for the usurper's camp.
After the forest fire, in which some of his men had perished, Hiéyas had moved to the neighbouring plain. He did not want to abandon this position so close to Osaka. Reinforcements arrived and he marched against Harounaga, who was still occupying Soumiossi. The general was defeated and his army routed. However, Hiéyas left only a vanguard on the conquered site and returned to his camp. It was there that he received the order from the Mikado to make peace. Hiéyas had then called some of the lords of his council to him: Ovari, Dathé, Todo, Couroda; they were all of the opinion that it was impossible to resist the order of the son of the gods; that we should apparently give in, but create an obstacle to the signing of the treaty.
- Let's make sure that Fide-Yori refuses to sign the peace treaty," said Hiéyas. That way, the wrath of heaven will fall on him.
Much to his surprise, Hiéyas was told that an envoy had arrived from Osaka. Fide-Yori accepted the conditions imposed.
- Who is he sending?" asked Hiéyas.
General Signénari.
The young warrior, whose heroism was well known, was held in high esteem even by his enemies. When he arrived in military costume and rode through the camp, the sovereign princes greeted him.
Signenari did not respond to the salutes.
- What is the meaning of this pride?" asked a lord.
Someone said:
- He represents the siogoun Fidé-Yori, he must not salute.
He was ushered into the master's tent.
Hiéyas was seated at the back on a folding bed, and mats had been laid out on the ground to his right and left. The princes and generals were present.
They tried to seat Signénari next to the princes, but he didn't seem to understand and sat down opposite Hiéyas.
- It's true," said a lord in a low voice, "this warrior, despite his great youth, has already acquired the dignity and prudence of an old man.
Signénari unrolled a piece of paper.
- These are the words of my master, the siogun Fide-Yori, son of the siogun Taïko-Sama," he said. And he read the scroll he held in his hands: "I, Fide-Yori, General-in-Chief of the Mikado armies, agree, in order to put an end to the unjust war declared against me by Hiéyas, which is devastating the kingdom, to accept one of the conditions imposed by my adversary for the conclusion of peace; I will demolish the first wall of the fortress of Osaka and I will fill in the moats, so that all hostility will cease and we will lay down our arms. "I have written this in all sincerity, on the fifteenth day of the second autumn moon, in the nineteenth year of Nengo Kai-Tio, and I sign with my Fide-Yori blood."
- If this is so," said Hiéyas in his weak, trembling voice, "I accept the peace.
He sent for writing materials and dictated to a secretary:
"I, Minamoto Hiéyas, proclaimed siogoun by the predecessor of Go-Mitzou-No, in the name of the siogoun Fidé-Tadda, in whose favour I have abdicated, agree to put an end to the war, on condition that Fidé-Yori has the walls of Osaka castle thrown down and its moats filled in, so that three-year-old children can go down and come back up while playing."
Hiéyas was given a new paintbrush and a long needle with which to prick the tip of his finger in order to sign in his own blood.
He pricked himself weakly and obtained only a pale droplet; he signed nevertheless, and the treaty was passed to Signénari.
- This won't do," said the general, casting his eyes over the paper, "the blood is too pale. Your name is illegible, start again.
- But," said Hiéyas, "I am old, weak and ill; for me, a drop of blood is precious.
Signénari pretended not to hear.
With a sigh, Hiéyas pricked himself again and strengthened his signature; only then did the young general give him the treaty signed by Fidé-Yori.
XXV
CONFIDENCES
A wild joy filled Osaka. This city of pleasure, luxury and perpetual festivities abhorred war, political conflict and bereavement, all of which prevented it from enjoying itself: entertainment being, for the inhabitants, the main aim of life. So that was it! The face stretched out by sadness and worry could be replaced by a face enlarged and full of laughter. At the first news of peace, the whole city began to dance, the sailors on the quays of the Yodo-Gava, the merchants at the threshold of their houses, the servants in the courtyards of the palaces. The wealthy individuals, the civil servants and the nobles were no less pleased, if they were a little more reserved in expressing their joy. The princesses in particular were happy: confined to their palaces, separated from their husbands, they had thought they were growing old in this war. They were waking up from a nightmare. They would still be allowed to be beautiful, to smile, to adorn themselves.
They ran to the great lacquer chests and, amid the scent of musk and precious woods, pulled out the superb dresses they had buried there, to adopt darker clothes. The floor was an admirable heap of satin, silk and crepe in the most tender colours. But the dresses were found to be a little faded and wrinkled, and the manufacturers, tailors and embroiderers were called in.
That very evening, the court announced a party on the water, in which the wealthy inhabitants of Osaka could take part. It was a fever. There was little time to prepare and decorate the boats.
Evening came and the river lit up.
Thousands of boats, garlanded with lanterns, left the banks and glided slowly up and down the river.
The court boats soon arrived. Wider and more beautiful than the other boats, they were lined with silk fabrics that spilled over and dragged across the water, lit by huge round lanterns made of gauze or painted glass, and surrounded by the multicoloured rustle of countless banners. Under the shelter of magnificent tents, stretched out nonchalantly on cushions, amid the many folds of their dresses, graceful women appeared in the soft light.
The embroidery of their kirimon and the large, radiant pins of their headdresses gleamed. Lords stood beside them, telling them a thousand foolish things, which they laughed at, tilting their heads a little. Long, luminous snakes danced on the water.
At the widest point of the river, where the banks are cut into vast tiers over a long stretch, fireworks were set off on rafts, waiting to be lit when the court arrived. A huge crowd, boisterous and full of joy, lined the terraces along the banks and watched the festivities.
The spectators, some standing, others sitting or lying down, all carried lanterns and took part in the illumination. There was no shortage of sake barrels: they tumbled down from the banks, rolling and bouncing amid shouts and laughter. A few fell into the water, and it was quite a comedy to catch them; several sank; nevertheless, everyone was soon drunk.
Fidé-Yori attended the party incognito. He and the Prince of Nagato were riding in a light, dimly-lit boat. Two men standing at the bow were steering it.
Half-reclining on cushions, the two friends silently watched the boats come and go.
The clear voices of female singers of national legends could be heard, accompanied by the biva or the semsin. Orchestras passed by, drowning out the gentle female song with their noisy music. But all of a sudden, the fireworks exploded, rockets shot off in all directions, sprays of fire bloomed and rained down stars. Once they had started, the fireworks never stopped, the pieces being renewed as they went up in smoke. They whistled, sparkled and radiated continuously.
The boat carrying Fidé-Yori came across the one carrying her mother Yodogimi. The princess, fully illuminated, appeared in a resplendent toilette. Her boat was entirely upholstered in gold brocade; the tent, made of purple satin, had tassels of pearls at each corner. General Harounaga, completely drunk, was laughing loudly, sprawled on the cushions.
The siogun turned his head away. The boat passed. Fidé-Yori heard the soldier's laughter for a moment longer.
The Prince of Nagato was dreaming; he was looking at nothing but the reflection of the lights in the water; he thought he saw embers, jewels, flames and molten metal shimmering in the water. He snapped out of his reverie, but finding that the silence was dragging on too long, he looked up at the siogun. Fidé-Yori's face was deeply melancholy, yet the young man searched every passing boat with an avid gaze.
Nagato examined him for a few moments.
- What is he looking for?" he asked himself.
Fide-Yori was obviously looking for someone; he let out a deep sigh whenever his hopes were disappointed.
- Master," said Ivakoura at last, "the whole nation is rejoicing today. I thought that sadness had taken refuge in my heart alone, but I see that you have kept some of it.
- I should indeed appear happy," says Fidé-Yori; "but to you I show myself as I am. You see, friend, a moral wound that won't heal is torturing me. The kingdom is at peace, but my heart is not.
- Do you remember, a few days ago, you promised to confide your grief to me.
- I wanted to do so a long time ago; I don't know what strange modesty held me back; it seemed to me that this feeling, so sweet and so cruel, which I was experiencing for the first time, the one who had inspired it should be the first to know it.
- You are in love, my friend, I suspected it. But why do you suffer for this love?
- The one I love saved my life, I've only seen her once, her name is Omiti; that's all I know about her," said the siogun.
- Poor dear prince!" exclaimed Nagato; "and you couldn't find her?
- Alas!
- Do you know what class she belongs to?
- She's a noble girl," said Fidé-Yori; "her language and her appearance have revealed that to me. But even if she belongs to the ranks of the reprobate, if ever heaven allows me to find her, she will be my wife.
- We'll look for her together," said Nagato.
- I'm looking for her right now, in the middle of this crowd.
Every boat that goes by, laden with women, makes my heart beat faster and faster.
- Do you think she lives in Osaka?" said the Prince of Nagato.
- I hope so and have a premonition of it," said Fidé-Yori.
- Then she is certainly at this party. Which young lady will be staying at home today?
- I thought the same as you, friend," said the siogun; "that's why I'm here.
- Let's see, give me a few words about the girl you love," said Nagato, "so that I can help you in your search.
- She is full of exquisite grace, small, with very large eyes, she looks like a child; her smile is like a dewy flower.
- The portrait lacks a little precision," says Ivakoura, smiling. Never mind, let's have a look; you're here to correct any mistakes I make.
They ordered the boatmen to row quickly and cover the entire stretch of the river that the illuminated boats had crossed. The light boat began to glide like a swallow. It came and went, running from one bank to the other without ever bumping into the other boats. Not a single one escaped the scrutiny of the two friends, but their search was fruitless.
- She's called Omiti, don't you know anything more?" said Nagato.
- I know nothing. But I think the family she belongs to is one of my enemies. When she told me about the plot, she refused to name the people behind it.
- Ah!" exclaimed Nagato suddenly, "look at that young girl over there, isn't she the one you're looking for? I've never seen such beautiful eyes.
Fide-Yori turned round sharply.
- Ah!" he said, "you're joking, she's got thick lips and a squashed nose.
- It's true," said Nagato, "forgive me, she seemed pretty from a distance.
The boat carrying them arrived at the point where the river widened, and from where the fireworks continued to fly into the sky.
It was Fidé-Yori's turn to cry out.
Through a spray of fire he had thought he saw Omiti's face, and he was not mistaken.
- There! there!" he shouted, "get to that boat, hurry!
The oarsmen hurriedly tacked. But they had to make a diversion, as the large rafts carrying the fireworks were blocking the way. When we passed them, we didn't know which boat to go after. Fidé-Yori had only seen the young girl's face, he could no longer see it. He could not see the number of lanterns or the colours of the banners on the boat in which she had passed. Moreover, the place was so crowded with boats of all shapes and sizes that it was almost impossible to move around.
Fidé-Yori was trembling with emotion and worry.
- She's going to escape me," he said; "after waiting so long to find her, only to lose her immediately!
- Did you see which way the boat was going?
- It seemed to be going up the river.
- Well, let's head that way; it couldn't have gone that fast. We're like prisoners here; we'll find her.
Fidé-Yori regained his courage.
- Go back up the river," he said to the boatmen.
The young siogoun leaned over the edge and looked eagerly. A few people recognised him. A large number of court princesses, lords and warlords passed by him. He saw his mother and General Harounaga again, but the face he had been looking for was nowhere to be seen.
- Perhaps we went too fast," he said.
They stepped back, then climbed back up again.
- The party is coming to an end!" suddenly exclaimed Fidé-Yori.
Let's wait for this boat above the central crowding; when it pulls away, it will pass us by.
- Which way should we go?" said Nagato.
- On the upper town side, there are no noble dwellings on the seaward side.
They waited in vain; the boat didn't show up; it had gone down the river and headed towards the suburbs.
Fidé-Yori, discouraged, returned to the palace. The Prince of Nagato tried to console him.
- Are you sure that the woman you saw was the one you were looking for?
- Certainly!" cried Fidé-Yori; "I only saw her face once, but my eyes will never forget her.
- So," said the prince, "instead of being sad, rejoice. You only imagined that she lived in this town; now you are certain that she does. So we're sure to find her. You'll throw another party and she'll still be there.
- You're right, friend," said Fide-Yori, "you'll help me; we'll search the town. We'll find her, and she'll be my wife.
Then life, which has been full of sadness and disappointment for me, will begin to smile on me. From tomorrow, won't we?
We will study the city quarter by quarter and try to wrest its secrets from it. Oh, you've given me back my courage, you've made me almost happy!
Hope lit up the young Siogoun's eyes and a smile parted his lips.
Suddenly a cloud passed over his forehead.
- How selfish and cruel I am! he cried. You, my dearest friend, my devoted brother, have just lost the woman you loved, she died a terrible death, and I insult your grief by telling you of my love and my hope. I dare to be joyful when you are sorry.
- Master," said Nagato, "I am deeply saddened by the loss of the woman who died for me. I had a brotherly affection for her, but my fiancée was not the woman I loved.
- What are you saying," cried Fidé-Yori, "you've taken a huge weight off my heart. So you can still be happy, as happy as I am.
Ivakoura shook his head.
- My love is made up of light and shade," he said. I'll never be completely happy; it's part heavenly joy and part deep suffering; as it is, however, it's my whole life.
- Who do you love?" said Fidé-Yori.
- Oh, master," said the prince, putting his hand over his eyes, "don't ask me.
- It is so sweet to talk about the one you love. You see, since you became my confidant, my pain has been halved.
- I'm condemned to silence.
- Even from me? That's how you love me! I regret having opened my heart to you.
- As soon as I tell you who I love, you'll never speak to me again.
- Is it my mother?
- No," said Nagato, smiling.
- Then who is it? I beg you, tell me!
- The Kisaki.
- You wretch!" cried Fide-Yori.
And, just as the prince had predicted, he didn't add a word.
The next day, work began on demolishing the fortress walls. Ten thousand men tried hard: they resisted. They didn't know how to go about it; the stones were leaning against an earthen embankment, as if they were set into it. At the top, on the platform that formed a vast terrace, cedar trees grew up and spread shade. First, the towers projecting far and wide in front of the walls were attacked; they were thrown into the ditch, then blocks of the rampart were torn out and the work was completed. Only the demolished walls still seemed to be standing; the stones were no longer there, the mountain of earth remained, but the ditch had been filled in.
While this work of destruction was being accomplished, the city continued to rejoice. Fidé-Yori had a huge bell melted down and solemnly dedicated it to the temple of Buddha: From now on, my house will be peaceful.
Public celebrations were held to mark the dedication. Now a splendid performance was announced at Osaka's main theatre. A new drama was to be unveiled: Taïko-ki, the story of Taïko.
This semi-historical work had just been written in honour of Fide-Yori's father. The time was right to perform it, so everything was hastily prepared. But the staging had to be very meticulous, and the day of the performance had not yet been fixed.
It was the talk of the town. The seats were all reserved in advance; they were paid for with five or six kobangs
[1] . The women were going crazy as they prepared their outfits; the tailors and embroiderers were panting. The main actor who was to play Taiko was praised. Everyone knew him; he was famous. He was nicknamed Nariko-Ma, the "Snoring Top".
Fidé-Yori, too, was impatiently awaiting the day of the performance. He hoped Omiti would be there, and that at least she wouldn't be able to escape him. His searches of the town with the Prince of Nagato had produced no results. It was not as easy as they had imagined to enter every house and ask about a young girl. They started with the homes of the nobles. There, it was easier. The siogun would pay a visit, incognito, to the wives of absent lords; he had the fantasy of seeing the families of princesses, and thus reviewed all the noble girls of Osaka.
To enter the homes of wealthy individuals, the two friends were forced to disguise themselves, and they were not always well received.
Their tricks for getting the girls of the house to show themselves to them varied: they claimed to have seen a priceless object fall from a young girl's sleeve, which they wanted to return only to her. Or they claimed to have been sent by a despairing old man who had just lost his only daughter and was looking for a child of the same age who looked a bit like her, so that he could leave her his fortune, which was immense. This latest invention of the Prince of Nagato was quite successful, but the work was hard; they had already spent eight days on the search and had only visited the palaces and one street in Osaka.
- We'll never get to see all the houses in this immense city," said Fidé-Yori, "we're mad.
- We might grow old before we find what we're looking for," replied Nagato. Never mind, let's keep on looking, perhaps we'll come across it in the next house we enter.
Fidé-Yori sighed.
- Let's wait until the day the theatre opens its doors," he said.
Finally, large posters painted on silk fabrics or coloured paper announced the date of the performance.
- We'll see her at the theatre; I'm sure she'll be there," said the Siogoun, clinging to this hope.
XXVI
THE GREAT OSAKA THEATRE
On one of the vast canals that cut the city of Osaka in all directions, the theatre unfurls its wide façade, topped by two roofs. You can get to the show by boat; you can also walk or take a norimono, as a quay, paved with grey flagstones, stretches out in front of the building and separates it from the canal.
Two gigantic blue silk banners, covered in Chinese characters, hang from poles at each corner of the façade, far above the roofs of the building. The main scenes of the plays to be performed are painted with unprecedented richness of colour on large boards with gold backgrounds. We see warriors, princesses, gods and demons in the most exaggerated poses. Often the paint is replaced by a combination of fabrics, laid out in relief in velvet, crepe or satin, which represent the characters' clothes and produce the most brilliant effect. Huge lanterns, round on the ground floor and square on the upper floor, hang from criss-crossing red beams under the roofs. On the crest of the roof, a fantastic animal, dog or lion, strides towards the façade, opening its wide mouth and bristling its tail and mane.
From eight o'clock in the morning, the hour of the dragon, crowds would gather in front of the façade of the Grand-Théâtre. Those who had no hope of getting in at least wanted to enjoy the brilliant spectacle of the arrival of the wealthy individuals and elegant women.
On either side of the main door, reached by a wide staircase, were high platforms on which several delegates of the actors of the troupe came forward, dressed in street clothes, fans in hand. In a pompous style, with delightful gestures and grimaces, they praised to the audience the plays that were about to be performed, the splendour of the costumes and the staging, and the incomparable merit of the actors; then, when this subject was exhausted, they amused the crowd with all sorts of jokes, quips and anecdotes spouted with comic gravity and accompanied by the perpetual movement of the fan, handled in a skilful and graceful manner.
Soon the privileged public, who had been able to reserve seats in advance, arrived from all sides. On the two bridges that curve over the canal to the right and left of the theatre, the norimonos and cangos move forward at the steady pace of the porters, one after the other; other palanquins emerge from all the streets. The black lacquer gleams in the sunlight, and the women's toilettes, hastening to enter, have the fresh radiance of flowers in bloom.
A few young men arrived on horseback; they threw the bridle to the valet who had been running ahead of them, and quickly climbed the stairs to the theatre. Sheltered under large parasols, several families were advancing on foot. On the canal, a crowd of boats besieged the landing stage, the boatmen exchanged insults, and the women put their feet on the ground with frightened cries. They were followed by servants carrying magnificent chests of carved ivory, mother-of-pearl and sandalwood.
The auditorium was soon filled and the doors closed.
Inside, the theatre is shaped like a long square. The floor is divided by low partitions into a series of equal compartments: there are no paths to these sort of lockers, and it is on the crest of these small wooden walls, wide enough for you to put both feet on, that you venture to reach the compartment reserved for you. The journey was not without its perils, and was accompanied by shouts and bursts of laughter. The women, embarrassed in their beautiful clothes, moved forward cautiously, sometimes stumbling, as the men held out their arms to make them jump into the compartments. Some prefer to sit on the partition and let themselves slide. Each locker can hold eight people; they squat on the floor on a mat and, as soon as they are settled, a servant, attached to the theatre, brings them tea, sake, a small brazier and pipes on a lacquer tray.
Above the parterre, two rows of boxes extend along three sides of the auditorium, the fourth side being occupied by the stage. These boxes, richly decorated with gilding and paintings on red or black lacquer backgrounds, are the most sought-after seats in the theatre, especially those on the upper floor. It is here that the most elegant coquettes display the magnificence of their toilette. The view of the auditorium is a feast for the eyes; most of the women are charming with their matte white complexions, their glossy hair and their dark eyes; the shimmer of the silk, the brilliant breaks of the satin, the brilliance of the colours and the embroidery form a joyful and superb whole. Married women are easily recognisable by their teeth blackened by a mixture of iron filings and sake, their eyebrows plucked and the belt of their dress tied in a huge knot on their stomachs. Young girls tie the knot on their loins and leave their teeth their natural whiteness. They also wear their hair differently. Instead of letting their hair hang down in a long twist or gathering it into a single mass at the top of the head, they raise it on the forehead, arrange it like wings on either side of the face and form a complicated, voluminous bun. Here's a woman who has replaced the tortoiseshell pins that usually go through all hairstyles with pins that are no less long, but made of chiselled gold. Her neighbours prefer to adorn their hair only with flowers and silk cords.
The men are no less adorned than the women; crepe, brocade and velvet are not forbidden to them; some wear an embroidered scarf over their shoulder, with one end hanging down from the front; the longer the scarf, the higher the rank of the person wearing it; when greeting a superior, he must bow until the scarf touches the ground, so the longer it is, the less he bows. Several lords, protected by their incognito, their faces hidden in a black crepe bonnet that only let them see their eyes, filled the boxes on the first floor; but one of them, very close to the stage, remained empty; it opened suddenly and a woman appeared.
The audience gasped as they recognised Yodogimi. Was this possible? The mother of the siogoun openly entering a theatre! Had she lost all respect for custom, propriety and herself? The light gauze veil, which clung to the large pins of her headdress and passed over her face, if it indicated an intention to remain incognito, in no way masked the princess: she had been recognised at first glance. However, astonishment soon gave way to admiration. People were grateful that she had not hidden her charming face, which the thin veil made even more beautiful. The fabric of her dress was pale gold, covered with fine pearls and grains of crystal; it looked as if stars were trapped in the folds of the fabric. The princess smiled as she saw how quickly her initial displeasure had been overcome by admiration. She sat down slowly, and when she was seated, a masked warrior could be seen standing behind her.
But there were vague shivers of gongs, a few rolls of flute, a few muffled blows on the tambourines. The musicians were taking up their instruments, so we were about to begin.
The audience turned towards the stage, which was enclosed by a canvas covered in rhombuses, and in the centre of which appeared, on a red disc, a gigantic Chinese character: this was the name of the Snoring Top, the famous actor who had no equal. A wealthy silk merchant had dedicated this painting to him; it was not to be replaced until the day the Snoring Top either surpassed or surpassed him.
It was to be replaced only on the day when the Snoring Top was surpassed or equalled by one of his colleagues.
The curtain billowed, and a man, lifting it slightly, stepped out from underneath. As soon as he appeared, the hubbub that had filled the room ceased abruptly. The man greeted the audience with all sorts of pretences. He was dressed like a rich lord and held a roll of paper in his hands, which he began to unroll.
People waited in deep silence for his words. However, they all knew that no one would be able to make out what he was saying, because that was his mission: to speak without being understood. If anyone discovers anything about the true meaning of what he reads on his scroll, he has missed his goal. However, he must read the text exactly, without skipping a word, without adding anything.
This script contains a summary of the play that is about to be performed, the names of the characters, the actors and the places where the action takes place.
The announcer, by cutting words and phrases, stopping where he shouldn't, joining what should be separated, completely disfigures the text, creating misunderstandings and producing buffoonish phrases that make the audience laugh out loud.
Nevertheless, the audience listens, trying to reconstruct the true meaning. But the announcer is clever: he withdraws before we can find out what it's all about.
When he has disappeared, great music is heard behind the stage and the curtain rises.
The scene is an elegant bedroom, with a large window opening onto a landscape. Rich screens and a bed - a velvet mattress and cushions - furnish the room.
The audience immediately recognised the setting of one of the most popular plays in the repertoire.
It's the third act of The Vampire," murmurs everyone.
A young lord appears on the stage. He is sad, overwhelmed by remorse. He has yielded to the seductions of an enchantress who has pursued him, but he has allowed himself to be overcome only by the power of magic; he loves a young woman he has just married, but he has made himself unworthy of her; he trembles to see her again.
Here she comes, bent over.
- My dear husband!" she says, after a few days of marriage, "is this how you desert me?
The husband protests his fidelity, but pushes her away when she wants to throw herself into his arms. By dint of tears and prayers, the young woman finally moved him, he pressed her to his heart and they both fell onto the bed; Her hair fluttered on her shoulders, her eyes flashed with lightning, she was terribly beautiful, her dance was enchanting; she made mysterious signs, accompanied by strange, interrupted music; the two spouses trembled with all their limbs; she tore the woman from the young man's arms and drew him towards her. His teeth clashed, his knees collided and he fell to the ground unconscious. Then the vampire rushes over him and bites him on the neck; she sucks his blood with delight, only interrupting herself to express her joy by dancing wildly.
Finally, with a long dagger, she splits the young man's chest and eats his heart.
The audience expresses its emotion with a long murmur and the canvas falls.
Only this act of The Vampire was performed; it is the best, the most dramatic. Later, the young lord is resurrected and returned to his wife.
During the intermission, most of the audience leaves the auditorium and invades the teahouse adjoining the theatre. They are served their morning meal, or simply hot drinks and sweets, amid indescribable noise and confusion. Everyone shares their impressions of the merits of the play they have just seen, and of the acting of the actors; they imitate their gestures, their cries, their contortions; some try their hand at somersaults and capersaults, to the great hilarity of those present; others play chess, dice or mourre.
The intermission was long. The young boys who played the women in the first play will appear again in the second, and we have to give them time to rest, take a bath and put on their new costumes. But the time passed pleasantly: people ate, smoked, laughed, and soon returned to the theatre in an uproar.
The appearance of the auditorium had been completely transformed: all the women in the dressing rooms had changed their clothes, and the ones they were wearing were even more luxurious than the first.
All eyes turn to Yodogimi. One wonders what she could have thought up to make her second outfit worthy of the one that had dazzled the spectators just a moment before. This time, too, we are speechless with surprise. Her dress is entirely covered in the feathers of hummingbirds, firebirds and hummingbirds; she casts multicoloured glints of sapphires, rubies, emeralds and glowing embers. A carnage of these living jewels was made to cover the size of this dress: it cost the price of a castle.
The announcer reappeared; he made a speech no less mysterious than the first one, and the canvas was lifted.
This time it depicts a scene from the Onono-Komati-Ki.
Onono-Komati was a beautiful young girl attached to the court of Kioto. Passionate about poetry, she devoted herself to serious study and composed verses; but in her love for perfection, once she had written a poem she would wash it out and start again. Young men fell in love with her beauty and obsessed her with their pursuit. She spurned them and continued her favourite studies, but the spurned lovers did not forgive her her disdain; with perfidious slander they brought her down in disgrace. The inspired young woman left the palace and set off on her adventures. Little by little she became miserable, but her love of poetry did not diminish; she contemplated the beauties of nature and sang them with a rare perfection of style. As she grew older, her hair turned white; she was in the most complete destitution, wandering from village to village, leaning on a stick, a basket in her arm, containing her poems and a little rice; she lived on alms. Children would gather around her when she sat on the threshold of a temple; she would smile gently at them and teach them verses. Sometimes a monk would respectfully ask her permission to copy one of the pieces in her basket. The sweetly inspired woman died; only then did the hatred fall silent and her glory shine forth. She was deified and remembered by all.
After performing a few fragments of the play depicting the life of Onono-Komati, a burlesque interlude was performed.
The scene takes place in a public bath, and according to custom, the men and women, completely naked, bathe together, telling each other all sorts of comic anecdotes and doing a thousand crazy things. One day, a pregnant woman comes along, starts crying out in pain, and finally gives birth to a fat boy. The bathers welcome the birth with a frenzied dance.
At last, the Taïko-Ki began.
The canvas rises over a vast decoration representing an encampment of soldiers. The chief's tent stands taller than the others in the centre.
Envoys arrived, all in a daze, making great gestures with their arms and legs.
- We want to see him right now!
they shouted.
Then the curtains of the tent opened and Taïko appeared.
Nariko-Ma had succeeded in reproducing exactly the attitude and costume of the hero he was representing. The audience expressed its satisfaction. Those who had seen the illustrious Siogun in their youth thought they were seeing him again.
- What do they want with me? says Taïko.
The emissaries no longer dared to speak.
- Well!" said Taïko, frowning and putting his hand on his sabre.
- While you are fighting the enemies of the country, Mitsou-
Fidé, to whom you entrusted your leadership of the kingdom, has seized power.
At this news, Taïko's face went from surprise to worry to fury.
A man, holding a light hanging from the end of a horizontal wooden rod, brought it close to the actor's face, so that the audience would not lose any of the play of his physiognomy.
- Let's go! let's go!" he cried, "my presence alone can restore order in the palace.
He entrusts the command of his troops to one of his generals, and leaves the stage along a path that crosses the parterre and is lost in the folds of a drapery.
The stage swivels round to reveal the interior of a pagoda.
Taiko enters. He asks to rest and spend the night in the pagoda. He is told that Mitsou-Fidé has just arrived with his wife and mother. They are travelling and have stopped here.
Taïko leaps back.
- My enemy so close to me! Should I flee? No, I must disguise myself.
He got himself a razor, shaved his head completely and put on a monk's robe. No sooner had he stapled it on than Mitsou-
Fidé stepped forward and cast a wary glance at Taïko; the latter, to give himself the air of being at ease and unconcerned, began to sing a naive song popular throughout the kingdom:
- From the top of the mountain, I look down into the valley.
"The cucumbers and aubergines, the hope of the harvest, are in flower.
- Come here, monk," says Mitsou-Fidé. My mother is tired from the journey and needs a bath.
- Who'd have thought I'd come here to be a bath boy!
exclaims Taïko, turning round to face the audience with a most successful facial expression. I obey," he adds loudly.
The bathroom is only separated from the bedroom that fills the stage by a frame covered in oiled paper. Taïko prepares the bath while amusing the audience with a thousand strange reactions, accompanied by appropriate grimaces.
Mitsou-Fidé's mother enters the stage and asks if the bath is ready. When the fake monk answers in the affirmative, she disappears behind the frame. But Mitsou-Fidé has just learnt that Taïko is in the pagoda, and arrives furious, shouting where his enemy is.
- He's in the bath at the moment," says a monk.
- He won't escape me.
During this scene, Taïko slips away.
Mitsou-Fidé cut a long stalk of bamboo in the garden, sharpened the tip and hardened it over the fire of a bronze stove. Then, walking towards the frame that enclosed the bathroom, he pierced the paper with this improvised spear and, thinking he had killed his enemy, killed his mother.
- What have I done!" he cried out in horror as he heard a woman's scream.
- You have killed your mother!" said the young wife of Mitsou-Fidé, who entered pale with horror and trembling.
Repent! Repent as she expires," she cried in song.
"This cruel death caused by your hand is heaven's vengeance.
"Did I not tell you: beware of betraying your master? You have usurped power.
"See where ambition has led you. You have killed your mother; at least repent while she is dying".
- Alas! alas! screams the parricide, let us leave these cursed places, let us flee; remorse tears at my heart. For three days I have possessed the power, the punishment is terrible, my mother killed by my hand, I cannot believe it!
He rushes into the bathroom and comes out with all the signs of despair bordering on madness.
The scene rotates once more to show a field. Taïko in war costume, surrounded by soldiers, waits for his enemy who wants to flee. Mitsou-Fidé crosses the stage with a small retinue; he is surrounded by Taïko's men.
After a long speech in which he reproaches his unworthy servant, Taïko has him put in chains and taken prisoner.
The canvas falls away; the play is finished.
The audience was very interested: in certain situations they found analogies with the events that had just troubled the country; they often thought of Hiéyas as Mitsou-Fidé.
Everyone left very satisfied.
Not everyone. Fidé-Yori is dead at heart.
The young girl was not at the performance. Nagato tries in vain to console his friend.
- I'll never see her again! he cried. I had hoped that I would at last be able to live happily ever after, but misfortune has set its sights on me. Well, friend," he added, "I feel like dying. My mother's behaviour, her ruinous and mad luxury, on public display, fill my heart with bitterness. Several times, on hearing the outbursts from the soldier she has the weakness to love, I've been on the verge of jumping into that dressing room, punching him in the face and chasing her away with the anger that such a lapse in decorum deserves. And then my rage fell at the thought of love that invaded me. I hoped she would come, the young girl who is all my hope; I searched the room with an avid gaze.
But she didn't come! All is over, all is sadness in my mind, and this life that she has kept for me, I would like to free myself of it now!
XXVII
OMITI
Winter has come. Scorching days are succeeded by icy ones. The sky, the colour of ashes, seems to have changed roles with the earth, dazzling white in its snowy garment.
Near the outskirts of Osaka, the deserted beach has kept intact the thick cotton wool that has fallen from the sky. The waves, reflecting the dull clouds, look like ink. A few rocks stand here and there, torn snow clinging to the rough edges of their walls. Seagulls, thwarted in their flight by the wind, flap their wings; they look grey and dirty against this whiteness.
The last house in the suburb stretches out along the beach, the high palisade of its garden; it is all covered in snow, and the cross sign hanging from the top of the two posts flanking the door is illegible. The large lanterns on either side of the entrance have been drawn back and secured with a hook; a small awning protects them. The triple roofs of the dwelling appear to be covered with silver thatch.
This building is the tea house of the Rising Sun. This is where Omiti has been suffering her cruel fate for many long days. She suffers, but silently, with a proud resignation that accepts neither consolation nor pity. She has sacrificed herself to save the master of the kingdom, and submits to the consequences of that sacrifice without a murmur. Only she sometimes thinks that it would have been more merciful to kill her. She has no desire to see the king again, even though she loves him all the same. Her love was born of a young girl's reverie. Before she had ever seen Fide-Yori, this young prince, said to be charming and full of gentleness, crossed her dreams, and during the day, while embroidering, she thought of him. When she overheard the horrible plot that threatened the life of the man who filled her thoughts, she thought she would die of fright, but the desire to save him had given her the energy and courage of a hero. In her only meeting with the king, near the lemon grove, she had understood that her heart had not been deceived and that she would never love anyone but him.
But the idea that he might love her had not even occurred to her, her modesty had pushed it aside, and since, sold for the pleasure of all, she had fallen to the lowest rank in society, the thought of appearing before Fide-Yori again made her ashamed.
Rich merchants from the city often brought their wives to the teahouse to spend a few hours in the company of the courtesans. The courtesans would teach them manners, the art of playing the semsin and composing verses. Sometimes the woman of the world, crouched opposite Omiti, listening with half-opened lips to the painful song of the young girl, had been surprised to see tears suddenly welling up in the singer's eyes, but she had thought that this was a seductive ruse, and, going home, had tried to cry by making the strings of her instrument vibrate.
Under its blanket of snow, behind the closed windows, and although it appeared silent from the outside, the teahouse was full of people and bustle.
For several weeks now, it had been invaded daily by a crowd of people from all walks of life, who seemed to gather there for some secret purpose. The master of the establishment was undoubtedly in agreement with these men; he always interfered in their conversation; he even often seemed to direct it and inflame it. They talked about the country's affairs: the misery was terrible; this civil war, occurring at a time when the fields needed man's care the most, had damaged the harvests; several had been completely destroyed by the armies, the others had been bad; a famine threatened the whole part of the kingdom that still belonged to Fidé-Yori. The north, on the other hand, had been preserved and was flourishing. While rice was in short supply around Osaka, it was sold at half the normal price in the northern provinces, but Hiéyas was absolutely opposed to exporting it to the south. The siogoun did not bother to bring it from elsewhere. While the people were starving, the court displayed unparalleled luxury: every day there were receptions, parties and banquets. Yodogimi aroused popular indignation; she drained the treasury. Taxes had been raised and salaries cut. Obviously, this was madness. The court, dragging after it streams of gold and satin, to the sound of dizzying music, was running, dancing, towards an abyss. Everyone was blinded; no one thought about the possible resumption of the war; they got drunk, laughed and sang between the fallen walls of the fortress; no one bothered to put the army back on its feet, to increase it if possible.
