Yoké-Moura had tried in vain to take action; he lacked money; the follies and ruinous finery of Princess Yodogimi absorbed everything. And what was the Siogun doing? Plunged into an inexplicable sadness, he wandered alone in the gardens, taking no interest in anything, abandoning power, so to speak. It was obvious that Hiéyas was only waiting for an opportunity to give the final blow to this crumbling edifice. But why wait? Did not the wisdom of the old man contrast with the short-sightedness of the young man and the folly of his court? It was time to call Hiéyas: his accession would save the people from misery and possible starvation; why allow themselves to be reduced to the last extremity? We had to try to bring about the inevitable outcome as quickly as possible.
Omiti, with growing dread, heard daily speeches similar to this one. It was not always the same men who returned; they went elsewhere to foment revolt and stir up anger. Emissaries from Hiéyas were obviously mixed up with these craftsmen. The usurper saw the value of a movement in his favour in Osaka; he wanted to provoke it. Moreover, the insouciance of the court supported him wonderfully. Omiti understood all this; she twisted her arms and wept in despair.
- No one has the courage to warn him of the danger!
she cried in her sleepless nights.
One day, as she was embroidering in her room, she noticed that those talking in the room below were lowering their voices.
Usually they didn't care if they were heard. Her heart leapt in her chest.
- I absolutely must hear what they're saying," she murmured.
she whispered.
She walked to the edge of the staircase and took hold of the banister, sliding down as lightly as a cloth.
The conversation had started and she caught bits of it.
- Yes, this beach is deserted.
- You enter the inn through the door facing the sea.
- And we'd leave in small groups on the street side.
- The soldiers would have to be disguised as craftsmen.
- Of course, but they should keep their weapons under their clothes.
- The town is already very agitated, we would march en masse to the fortress, and we would demand that the Siogun relinquish power.
- If he refuses, we will invade the palace and seize his person.
Omiti shuddered with horror.
- I absolutely must get out of here and raise the alarm," she murmured.
The conspirators continued:
- We must hurry, tomorrow, at nightfall, the soldiers will be able to disembark.
- Immediately afterwards, a cargo of rice and wheat will arrive.
Omiti went back to her room; she knew enough, her mind was made up. A sort of mystical ardour filled her spirit.
- My mission in this world is to save him, to keep him on the brink of the abyss," she said to herself with exaltation. This is the second time my ear has caught a criminal secret, a plot against the man I loved before I met him. Heaven's will is showing itself in this: this time too, I must warn him of the danger, and my feeble hand will stop the crime from being carried out.
She thought about the means she could use to escape from the inn.
Two other young women shared her room at night, but she could not confide in them, as they did not love her and were all devoted to the master.
On the ground floor, all the doors were locked from the inside with heavy bars; moreover, the valets in charge of the cellar slept downstairs. So there was no need to think about escaping from this side. There was still the window; the first floor was quite high, but that wasn't what worried Omiti. How could she slide the window shutter open without waking the young women? If she did it quietly, the cold air entering the room would rouse them from their sleep. Omiti thought of the window that opened onto the landing of the staircase; but the one in the bedroom looked out onto the street, whereas this one looked out onto the garden, and once in the garden there was still the palisade to cross.
- Anyway," Omiti said to herself, "I'll go down by the window of the staircase.
But how? She didn't have a ladder at her disposal.
With a rope! Where would she get a rope without arousing suspicion? She decided to make one. Her companions had gone for a walk and she had some time to spare. Opening the chests containing her clothes, she pulled out some strong silk dresses and cut them into strips. She then plaited the strips together and tied the braids with strong knots. Then she rolled up the rope and hid it under her mattress.
- Now," she said, "I'm sure I can save him.
The day seemed long to her, and the fever of waiting gave her a nervous tremor, and she chattered her teeth from time to time.
The girls came back with their cheeks all rosy with cold; they tired Omiti by recounting everything they had seen and done; they had gone as far as the banks of the Yodo-Gava to see if it was carrying ice cubes. They thought they could make out a few, but perhaps it was just floating snow; besides, there was snow everywhere, even on the golden fish in the high tower of the fortress, which had become silver fish; the breeze was icy cold; but, to protect themselves from the cold, the men had invented embroidered velvet earflaps...
Omiti was not listening to the endless cackling of the young women. She was pleased to see the lanterns being lit. Night was coming on, but the evening would still be long. She couldn't eat anything at the evening meal, and said she was sick so she didn't have to sing or play the biva.
She went back up to her room. Her companions soon joined her there; the walk had tired them, and they promptly fell asleep.
The noise, the laughter and the songs of the men getting drunk in the rooms below continued for a long time, and finally she heard the familiar crash of the bars falling into the hooks, everyone had left.
She waited another half hour to give the valets time to fall asleep, then without making the slightest noise she got up, took her rope from under the mattress and moved the panel that opened onto the staircase a little.
She listened and heard nothing more than a few snores, but that noise was reassuring. She opened the window and shivered in the night air. She leaned out and looked down; the white snow cast a vague light.
- It's a long way up! she thought, but will my rope be long enough?
She tied it to the windowsill and let it unwind. The rope reached the ground and even dragged a little on the snow.
Omiti wrapped her dress around her legs and knelt down on the windowsill, but as she was about to let go of the frail rope, a sort of instinctive fear overcame her and she hesitated.
- How!" she said, "I tremble for my life when his is in danger!
She let herself go abruptly, holding on to the rope with both hands. A sharp pain almost made her scream: it seemed as if her arms were going to be torn from her shoulders; her hands were scraping as they slid against the rope; she was descending rapidly. But suddenly one of the knots in the silk stretched under her weight and the rope snapped.
She fell into the snow, her body making a hole in the snow that almost swallowed her whole. But the fall had been cushioned and Omiti stood up, feeling no pain apart from a sudden weariness. After shaking off the snow that covered her whole body, she crossed the garden and reached the palisade. Luckily, the gate was only closed by a large round bolt; after some effort, Omiti managed to pull it.
She was on the beach, out of that dreadful house, free at last! The violent wind from the sea, whose monotonous roar she could hear, hit her in the face. She began to run, sinking ankle-deep into the thick layer of snow, kicking it up into the dust.
She was in such a hurry to get away from the inn that instead of turning the corner of the house and taking the street on which the façade opened, she followed the garden fence, which soon disappeared and was replaced by a wall bordering another enclosure.
- I'll enter the city by the next alley that opens onto the beach, Omiti thought.
She reached a sort of crossroads, open on the seaward side, bordered on the other by a semi-circle of miserable huts, which she could make out confusedly beneath their snowy bonnets.
In the centre, hanging from a post, a lighted lantern made a bloody spot that shook. The light was poor. The young girl took a few steps into the crossroads, but suddenly recoiled with a cry of horror; she had just seen a frightening face staring at her from beneath the lantern.
The young girl's scream was answered by the cries of countless ravens, which, suddenly awakened, took to the skies and began to circle incoherently.
Omiti was enveloped in this sinister flight. Immobile with terror, she thought she was the prey of an hallucination, and opened her eyes wide, trying to understand what she was seeing. The face was still staring at her, with snow in its eyebrows and on its hair, its mouth open and its eyes haggard. At first Omiti thought she saw a man leaning against a post, but on closer inspection she saw that the head, which had no body, was hanging by its hair from a nail.
Omiti recognised that she was in the crossroads of capital executions.
The ground was lumpy with hastily dug graves in which the victims were buried. The body of the last victim had been left at the foot of the post; a dog, busy removing the shroud of snow covering the corpse, let out a long howl and ran off carrying a bloody shred.
A large bronze statue of Buddha seated on a lotus appeared, stained with a few white flakes.
Omiti overcame her terror and crossed the crossroads, stretching out her arms to ward off the swarm of frantic crows that swooped down on her. They pursued her with their mournful cries, which mingled with the moans of the sea.
The young girl quickly made her way down a narrow street where there was no light. The snow had been trampled underfoot and she was now walking through icy mud.
The darkness was deep, no longer illuminated by the whiteness of the ground. Omiti walked along the walls, trying to lean against them, but the houses were not evenly spaced; there were gaps and she sometimes lacked the support she needed. Her feet sank into potholes of soft snow that was beginning to melt in places. She would fall, then get up again; the bottom of her dress was soaked. She felt freezing cold.
- Will I get where I'm going? she thought.
Another street came into view, intersecting the first; a few lights shone on it. Omiti turned into this street.
Without realising it, the young girl was passing through the most despicable part of the capital. Thieves, women of ill repute and scoundrels of all kinds haunt and inhabit it. There is also a particular kind of man: the Lonines. These are young men, sometimes of noble birth, driven to the lowest degree of ignominy by debauchery. Driven from their families or stripped of their jobs, but retaining the right to carry two swords, they take refuge among the reprobates, indulge in all sorts of shameful industries, murder on behalf of others, lead gangs and wield great power in the midst of this horde of wretches. A few hours earlier, it would have been impossible for the young girl to cross this district without being assaulted, insulted and dragged by force into one of the bad places that make it up. Fortunately, it was late at night and the streets were deserted.
But there was another obstacle in Omiti's way: the neighbourhood was closed off by a fence, which a man was guarding. How could the gate be opened at such an hour? What pretext could he give the guard, who was suspicious and probably off-putting? Omiti thought about this as she walked.
Soon, at the end of a street, she saw the wooden gate illuminated by several lanterns; she saw the hut made of planks that sheltered the guard.
- If I show the slightest concern, he will not trust me.
She walked straight to the door. The man was no doubt asleep, because the noise she made as she stepped forward did not attract him outside. Omiti measured the barrier with her eyes. It was impossible to get through; a portcullis of criss-crossed iron rods stood above it.
With a flutter of her heart, the young girl went to knock against the boards of the hut.
The guard came out with a lantern. He was well wrapped up in a wadded-up robe, and his head disappeared under the wrappings of a brown woollen cloth; he looked sickly and stupefied by drunkenness.
- What's the matter?" he said hoarsely, raising his lantern to Omiti's face.
- Open the door for me," said the young girl.
The guard burst out laughing.
- You've lost your mind," he cried.
And he turned on his heels.
- Listen," she said, holding him by his dress, "my father is ill and has sent me for the doctor.
- Well, there's no shortage of doctors in the neighbourhood, there's one ten paces from here, there's another in the rue de la Cigale-d'Automne, and a third at the corner of the sentier des Maraudeurs.
- But my father only trusts one who lives in the next district.
- Go home and sleep well," said the man; "you're telling me a lie, but I'm not easy to fool.
He was about to close the entrance to the hut.
- Let me out," cried Omiti in despair, "and I swear you'll be rewarded beyond your expectations.
- Do you have any money?" said the guard, turning round sharply.
Omiti remembered that she had a few kobangs in her belt.
- Yes," she said.
- Why didn't you say so straight away?
He took the big key hanging from his belt and approached the door. Omiti gave him a kobang. It was a large sum for a man who was paid very little and who, moreover, drank up all his pay.
- With a reason like that in your hands, you didn't need to put your father to death! he said as he opened the door.
- Which is the shortest route to the banks of the Yodo-Gava?
- Walk straight ahead. You'll find a new barrier. It opens onto the shore.
- Thank you," she said.
And she walked quickly away. The path was better; the snow had been piled up in large mounds.
- I'm saved now," said the young girl to herself, joyful and oblivious to the fatigue that was weighing her down.
She reached the second barrier. But this time she knew how to get the gate to open. The guard was walking up and down, stamping his feet to keep warm.
- A kobang for you if you open the door for me! she shouted.
The man held out his hand and put the key in the lock. Omiti passed by; she was on the banks of the River Neuve. Now all she had to do was go back up to the castle. There was still a long way to go, but there were no obstacles. She walked bravely, clutching her dress to her chest to keep out the cold.
Night watchmen passed by on the other shore, beating tambourines to announce the last watch of the night. When the young girl reached the castle, a pale, dull day was trying to pierce through the clouds; the snow was back to its brilliant bluish whiteness, seeming to give off light rather than receive it from the dark sky, which seemed to be covered in red smoke.
The castle towered imposingly above her. The tall towers rose into the sky, the broad roofs of the princely pavilions were spread out, and the cedars along the first terrace had heaped up bundles of snow on their still-green branches, fragments of which occasionally broke off and tumbled from branch to branch.
Omiti felt tears welling up in her eyes as she saw the walls destroyed and the ditches filled in.
- My poor beloved prince," she said to herself, "you have given yourself over to your enemy; if the war started again you would be lost; at least you will still escape the odious plot hatched against you!
Everything in the castle was asleep, apart from the numerous sentries who came and went; the fallen walls had been replaced by living ones.
Omiti, as he reached his goal, feared he would not have the strength to take the few remaining steps to reach the fortress gate. Drenched in snow, shattered by fatigue and emotion, the icy chill of the morning made her tremble from head to toe; everything swayed before her eyes, her temples pounded and her ears buzzed. She hurried towards the door.
The sentries crossed their spears in front of her.
- You can't go through," they said.
- Yes, I must go at once and see the king, or you'll be severely punished!
The soldiers shrugged their shoulders.
- Get back, woman! You're drunk or mad, go away!
- I beg you, let me in; call someone, I think I'm going to die; but first I must speak to the king! I must, do you hear? Don't let me die without speaking.
Her voice was so painful and pleading that the soldiers were moved.
- What's the matter with her?" said one, "she's as pale as snow; she could well die as she says.
- What if she has something to reveal?
- Let's take her to the Prince of Nagato; he'll decide whether to listen to her.
- Come in," said one of the soldiers, "we feel sorry for you.
Omiti staggered a few steps, but her strength failed her. She hurriedly grabbed a dried flower from her chest and held it out to the soldiers; then, with a muffled cry, she fell backwards.
The worried and embarrassed soldiers looked at each other.
- If she's dead," said one, "we'll be accused of killing her.
- We'd better throw her in the river.
- Yes, but how can we touch a corpse without becoming impure?
- We'll purify ourselves according to the prescribed laws, and that's better than letting our heads be cut off.
- True enough. Let's make haste. It's a shame," added the soldier, leaning towards Omiti; "but it's her fault too, why did she have to die like that?
Just as they were about to lift her up and carry her to the river, a clear young voice burst out singing a song:
"Is there anything in the world more precious than sake?
"If I were not a man, I would like to be a barrel!
The soldiers jumped to their feet. A young boy came forward, wrapped in a fur-trimmed robe, his head buried in a bonnet tied under his chin. He proudly rested his velvet-gloved hand on the handles of his sabres.
It was Loo returning from a night party, alone and on foot so as not to be denounced by his retinue to the Prince of Nagato, for Loo had had a retinue ever since he became a samurai.
- What's going on? Who is this woman lying motionless on the snow?" he cried, turning a terrible gaze from one soldier to another.
The soldiers fell to their knees.
- Lord," they said, "we are not guilty. She wanted to enter the castle to speak to the Siogun; moved by her prayers, we were going to let her pass and have her taken to the illustrious Prince of Nagato, when all of a sudden she fell dead.
Loo leaned towards the young girl.
- Donkeys! empty brains! milk drinkers! collapsed shoes!" he cried angrily, "can't you see that she's breathing, that she's only fainted! You leave her in the snow instead of helping her. To cure you of your stupidity, I'll have you caned and left dead in the square.
The soldiers were shaking all over.
- Come on," said Loo, "lift her carefully and follow me.
me.
The soldiers obeyed. When they had passed through the gate of the fortress, the young samurai went to knock on the guardhouse a few steps away.
- Renew the sentries," he shouted, "I need them.
I need them.
And he continued on his way. The Prince of Nagato was asleep. Loo didn't hesitate to go and wake him up. He knew that the Siogun was trying to retrace the steps of a young girl he loved. He and his master had followed the king as he searched the city. The woman he had just seen, fainting at the castle gate, closely resembled the portrait drawn by Fidé-Yori.
- Master," he said to the prince, who was still awake and looking at the child with a weary, surprised gaze, "I think I've found what the Siogun is looking for.
- Omiti!" exclaimed Nagato, "where did you find it?
- In the snow! But come quickly, she's cold and motionless, we mustn't let her die.
The prince put on a fur-lined robe and ran to the room where Omiti had been laid.
- This could well be the one we were looking for," he said when he saw her. But first, send for some maids and have them remove the girl's wet and muddy clothes. Call the palace doctor too.
Omiti was wrapped in the softest furs and the flames were rekindled in a large bronze basin. The king soon arrived. From the threshold of the room, through the open panel, he saw the young girl in the midst of this heap of superb fabrics and fleeces. He let out a cry of joy and rushed towards her.
- Omiti!" he cried, "am I dreaming? It's you! After being separated for so long, you've finally been returned to me!
The young girl shuddered at the king's cry. She opened her eyes. The doctor arrived out of breath; he knelt down beside her and took her hand.
- It's nothing," he said, after feeling her pulse carefully, "just a minor fainting spell, no doubt brought on by the cold and fatigue.
Omiti, with her large, surprised eyes, shaded by long, fluttering eyelashes, looked at all the people grouped around her. She could see the king at her feet; standing next to her, the Prince of Nagato, whose handsome face smiled at her; then the grave face of the doctor, made strange by an enormous pair of spectacles. She thought she was in a dream.
- Are you suffering, my sweet beloved?" said Fidé-Yori, taking Omiti's little hand in hers. What's happened to you? Why are you so pale?
She looked at the king and listened to his words without understanding them. Suddenly the memory came back to her and she stood up abruptly.
- I must speak to the siogoun!" she cried to him alone, "at once!
With a gesture, Fidé-Yori dismissed those present; he held back the Prince of Nagato.
- You can speak in front of him, he's my dearest friend," he said.
But calm down; why do you look so frightened?
Omiti tried to gather her thoughts, clouded by fever.
- Here," she said. Through skilful emissaries, Hiéyas is inciting the people of Osaka to revolt, to hate the rightful lord. Soldiers disguised as craftsmen will disembark on the beach near the suburb; they will break into the city and come right up to your dismantled castle to demand that you abdicate your title or take your life if you refuse. You don't doubt my words, do you? Once already you have had proof, alas! that the misfortunes I am announcing are real.
- What!" cried Fide-Yori, her eyes wet with tears, "you have come to me again to save me. You are the good genius of my life.
- Hurry up and give orders, take measures to prevent the crimes that are being prepared," said Omiti, "time is running out: this evening, have you understood? the soldiers of Hiéyas are to treacherously invade your town.
Fidé-Yori turned to the Prince of Nagato.
- Ivakoura," he said, "what do you advise me to do?
- Warn General Yoké-Moura. Tell him to put his men under arms and keep an eye on the beach and the town. Isn't there a place where the leaders of the conspiracy should meet?" he added, addressing Omiti.
- There is," said the young girl, "it's the Rising Sun tea house.
- Well, we need to surround the inn and seize the agitators. Do you want me to carry out your orders, master?
- You will make me happy, friend, by doing so.
- I leave you, my lord," said Nagato, "worry about nothing, and give yourself over to the joy of having found the one you love.
The prince walked away.
- What does he mean?" thought Omiti in surprise, "the one you love", who is he talking about?
She was alone with the king and didn't dare look up. Her heart was beating violently. Fidé-Yori, too, was troubled; he did not speak, but gazed at the charming child trembling before him.
The young girl, blushing, was twirling a small dried-up branch in her fingers.
- What have you got in your hand?" asked the siogun softly; "is it a talisman?
- Don't you recognise the blossoming lemon branch that you gave me when I saw you?" she said. "Just now, when I fainted, I held it out to the sentries; I thought that they would bring it to you and that when you saw it you would remember me. But I found it in my hand.
- How did you keep these flowers?
Omiti looked up at the king with a beautiful gaze that showed his soul, then looked down again.
- Because you gave them to me," she said.
- So you love me?" cried Fidé-Yori.
- Oh, master," said the frightened young girl, "I would never have the audacity to confess the folly of my heart.
- You don't want to confess your love. Well, I love you with all my soul, and I dare to tell you so.
- You love me, you siogun!" she exclaimed with touching amazement.
- Yes, and for a long time, evil one, I've been waiting for you, I've been looking for you, I was plunged in despair, you made me suffer cruelly; but now that you're here, everything is forgotten.
Why did you wait so long? Weren't you thinking about me?
- You were my only thought, blooming like a divine flower in the midst of my painful life; without you I would have died.
- You thought of me while I moaned about your absence, and you didn't come?
- I didn't know that you deigned to keep my memory alive.
Besides, if I had, I wouldn't have come.
- How," cried the siogoun, "that's how you love me; you refuse to live with me, to be my wife!
- Your wife!" murmured Omiti with a sad smile.
- Certainly," said Fidé-Yori, "why this expression of bitterness on your face?
- It's because I'm not worthy to be just one of the maids in your palace, and when you find out what I've become, you'll chase me away in horror.
- What do you mean?" exclaimed the siogun, turning pale.
- Listen," said the young girl in a deaf voice. Hiéyas came to my father's castle; he knew that I had discovered the terrible plot against your life and that I had denounced it. There I lived as women who are slaves live. I didn't leave the teahouse until that night. Once again, I had overheard a plot against you. I escaped through the window, using a rope that broke. You're safe now, let me go; it's not right for you to stay any longer in the company of a woman like me.
- Shut up," cried Fide-Yori, "what you've just told me has broken my heart; but do you think I've stopped loving you? How! It's because of me that you were reduced to servitude, it's because of me that you suffered, you saved my life twice and you want me to abandon you, to despise you!
You are losing your mind. I love you more than ever. You will be queen, do you hear? How many women in your position have been bought and married by lords? You are here, you will never leave.
- O master!" cried Omiti, "I implore you, think of your rank, of what you owe to yourself, don't let yourself be carried away by a passing desire.
- Shut up! Cruel child," said the king, "I swear that if you make me despair like this, I'll kill myself at your feet.
Fidé-Yori had reached for her sword.
- Oh no, no!" cried the young girl, who turned very pale.
I'm your slave, you can have me.
- My beloved queen!" cried Fidé-Yori, wrapping her arms around her, "you are my equal, my companion, not my slave; it's not just out of obedience that you give in, is it?
- I love you!" murmured Omiti, raising her beautiful eyes wet with tears to the king.
XXVIII
FROM NOW ON MY HOUSE WILL BE QUIET
The ringleaders of the conspiracy had been arrested in the inn of the Rising Sun; but the soldiers of Hiéyas, warned in time, had not disembarked, so that, although it was certain that Hiéyas was the secret leader of the conspiracy, no evidence could be adduced against him. However, it was clear that the civil war was about to start again. General Yoké-Moura was of the opinion that it was necessary to take the initiative and bring the war to the enemy province. The other generals, on the other hand, wanted to gather all their forces around Osaka and wait.
Discord broke out between the chiefs.
- You are reckless," they said to Yoké-Moura.
- You are madmen," replied the general.
Nothing was decided. Fidé-Yori, completely content, did not want to hear about war.
- Let my generals do their job," he said.
However, at the request of the Prince of Nagato, he sent an old chief named Kiomassa, known for his prudence and devotion, to Hiéyas.
- Let him go to Mikava under the guise of peace," said the prince, "and let him try to find out if Hiéyas really wants to start the war again. The Mikado has ordered us to remain at peace; the first person to break his order will incur his wrath. If war is inevitable, let our enemy be the first to blame. Kiomassa has a castle in the vicinity of Mikava; he can visit Hiéyas from his estate without arousing suspicion.
General Kiomassa had left, taking three thousand men with him.
- I've come to see you as a neighbour," he said to Hiéyas when he reached Mikava castle.
Hiéyas greeted him with a mocking smile.
- I have always held you in high esteem," he said, "and it is a pleasure for me that chance has brought you to this side. This morning, on hearing of your arrival on my lands, I told the lords of my house that, apart from three things, I saw nothing to admire in you.
- And what are those three things?" said Kiomassa.
- Firstly, you are travelling with an army, which is unusual in times of peace; secondly, you have a fortress that seems to threaten my provinces; thirdly and finally, contrary to fashion, you are letting your beard grow under your chin.
Kiomassa replied without appearing angry:
- I travel with an army to protect myself from all danger, because I believe the roads to be unsafe; I have a fortress naturally to house this army. As for my beard, it's very useful: when I tie the cords of my helmet, it makes a little cushion under my chin and protects it from rubbing.
- Fine, keep your beard, but shave your castle," said Hiéyas, smiling; "your soldiers can help you with that.
- If you're that keen, I'll ask Fide-Yori if he'll allow me to give you the castle. I'm going back to my master. Don't you have anything to tell him?
- You can tell him I'm angry with him," says Hiéyas.
- And why is that?
- Because he had the characters that make up my name engraved on the bronze bell that he dedicated to Buddha's temple, and they are tapped night and morning.
- How!" cried Kiomassa, "Fide-Yori had these words engraved on the bell: From now on my house will be quiet.
- I'm telling you that all the characters in my name make up this phrase, and that it is on my name, cursing it, that the bronze mallet is struck.
- I'll let the siogun know that this coincidence hurts you," said Kiomassa, without losing any of his placidity.
- He returned to Osaka and recounted how he had been received by Hiéyas. The mocking insolence and futile quarrel imagined by the former regent sufficiently indicated the latter's hostile intentions, which he did not even try to disguise.
- This behaviour is tantamount to a declaration of war," said Fidé-Yori, "and we must consider it as such. However, let us not attack, let Hiéyas advance; he will not do so immediately.
We will no doubt have time to dig the ditches around the fortress; let's get to work straight away.
Some time later Fide-Yori repudiated his wife, the granddaughter of
daughter, and sent her back to her grandfather. At the same time he announced his forthcoming marriage to Omiti, to whom he had given the title of Princess of Yamato.
The two fiancés forgot the rest of the world; their joy blinded them to the dangers that threatened them. For them, the only possible misfortune was to be separated, and they were certain that if disaster struck, they could die together.
They had been to see the lemon grove again. Faint buds were beginning to dent the bushes, for spring comes quickly in this climate; as soon as the last snow has melted, the trees are already turning green. They wandered through the misty alleys of the gardens, hand in hand, enjoying the happiness of being close to each other, of seeing each other in ways other than in their thoughts or dreams, for they adored each other but did not know each other. They had only seen each other for a moment, and the image they had kept of each other in their memory was incomplete and a little different from reality. Every minute brought a new surprise.
- I thought you weren't so tall," said Fidé-Yori.
- Your eyes had seemed proud and contemptuous to me," said Omiti; "on the contrary, an infinite tenderness fills them.
- How sweet your voice is, my beloved!" said the king; "my memory has distorted its divine music.
Sometimes they got into a little boat and rowed out to the middle of the pond. On the shore, a tall willow tree let the long folds of its green draperies hang down towards the water, irises pierced the liquid mirror with their rough leaves, water lilies spread out on the surface. The two fiancés cast a line. The hook sank in a series of circles on the water. But no matter how hard the fish bit, no matter how wildly the light buoy on the surface of the pond danced, they took no notice; from one end of the boat to the other, they stared at each other passionately. Sometimes, however, they realised that the fish were taunting them, and their clear laughter broke out, mingling with the birdsong.
He was twenty-three, she eighteen.
But it was Omiti who sometimes worried about the war.
- Don't forget your royal duties towards me," she would say.
Don't forget that war threatens us.
- Your heart is at peace with mine," said Fidé-Yori; "why are you talking about war?
Besides, the siogun could safely absorb himself in his love. The Prince of Nagato took his place. He had organised the defence and had tried to bring the generals, who hated each other and thought only of antagonising each other, into agreement. Harounaga in particular gave him a thousand worries. He had forbidden his soldiers to work on digging the moat around the castle.
- That's slave labour," he said, "and you are warriors.
The soldiers of the other cohorts, not wanting to be less susceptible than their companions, refused to work in their turn. So after a month and a half, the children could still easily climb up and down the ditch.
Nagato was forced to impose severe penalties. Little by little, order was established.
Signénari set up camp on the plain to the north of the town.
Yoké-Moura set up camp on the hill called Yoka-Yama; Harounaga, on the hill called Tchaousi-Yama. The rest of the troops guarded the beach or were massed in the fortresses. Nagato had also instructed Raïden and his companions to enlist anyone who wanted to fight. The brave sailors had gathered ten thousand volunteers.
Defended in this way, the city was difficult to surprise. Nagato had his eye on everything, and fortified the two bastions at the entrance to Osaka on either side of the river. Using the canals that criss-cross the city and by demolishing a number of bridges, he had managed to form a moat and isolate the district that contained the fortress. The prince seemed indefatigable. With such a leader, who thought of everything and fired up the soldiers with his words and his example, the city could defend itself and still have hope. But suddenly Nagato left Osaka.
One evening a horseman stopped at the gate of his palace.
Nagato recognised Farou-So-Chan, one of the lords specially attached to the service of the Kisaki. It was never without a deep throbbing of the heart that Ivakura saw anyone coming from the Dairi. This time, his emotion was even more violent: Farou-So-Chan had been entrusted with a special, secret mission.
- Here is a letter that the Kisaki has asked me to give to you," he said with a sad gravity that struck Nagato.
He unfolded the letter with a tremor in his fingers; it exhaled the subtle perfume he loved so much.
It read as follows:
"On the tenth day of the fifth moon, go to the province of Issé, to the temple of Ten-Sio-Daï-Tsin, when evening comes, kneel at the threshold of the temple and remain in prayer until a young priest approaches you and touches your shoulder, then rise and follow him, he will lead you to me."
Nagato was lost in conjecture. What was the meaning of this strange meeting on the threshold of the temple of the Sun Goddess, in the province of Issé? Was it a trap? No, since Farou-So-
Chan was the messenger. But then he was going to see her again, and all anxiety disappeared in the face of this joy.
The tenth day of the fifth moon was the day after tomorrow.
The prince had only time to arrive at the appointed hour; he left in a hurry.
XXIX
THE HIGH PRIESTESS OF THE SUN
It is at Naikou, in the province of Issé, bathed by the waves of the Pacific Ocean, that the primitive temple of Ten-Sio-
Daï-Tsin. According to divine legends, the Sun Goddess was born on the very site of this temple.
Here, the ancient traditions, the confused legends of the first ages, are religiously preserved by the priests, who meditate on the profound meaning of the symbols.
In the mysterious times when the world was not yet a reality, the confused elements floated in space. What was once earth and what was once heaven were mixed together, just as the yolk and the white are mixed together in the embryonic egg.
But then three immaterial gods appeared: the Supreme God, the Creator of Souls, the Creator of Matter, and the chaotic state ceased. The heavy, opaque parts came together and formed the earth; the light, subtle parts rose and became the sky.
Soon, from the soft, silty mass that was the earth, a hairy, half-open flower rose from the floating mists, its corolla bearing the God of the Germinating Reeds. He watched over the newborn world for countless years. The Genie of the Waters came after him and reigned for a thousand million years.
During these immeasurable periods, the gods succeeded one another in the heavens. The seventh of the divine dynasties reigned in the ether.
One day, from the top of a bridge thrown between the clouds, the god Iza-
Na-Gui and his companion Iza-Na-Mi looked down on the earth.
- Everywhere I see only the immensity of water," said the god.
With the tip of his jewelled spear, he shook the surface of the sea, and the silt rose up, spread out over the water and came to rest there. This is how the first island of Japan was formed.
Soon it was covered with vegetation, populated by quadrupeds and birds and became so charming that Iza-Na-Gui and her companion descended from the sky and came to live there. The birds taught them love, and the Sun goddess was born. The divine couple went on to give birth to the genies of the wind, the rain, the metallic mountains, the Moon god who "looks through the night", and finally to the first men, whose descendants populated the island.
Then the creators of Japan ascended to heaven, entrusting the government of the world to their beloved daughter, the Sun goddess.
All the subjects of this luminous divinity must, at least once in their lives, make a pilgrimage to her temple at Naikou, in order to purify their souls. This is why the town is constantly crowded with pilgrims arriving and departing, some on horseback or by norimono, others - and these are the most deserving - on foot, carrying a straw mat to use as a bed and a long wooden spoon to draw water from the streams along the way.
The temple is of great simplicity, a small building, open on one side, topped with a broad thatched roof, surrounded by hundred-year-old cedars and preceded, some twenty paces away, by a Torié, the sacred portico, which consists of two high beams leaning slightly towards each other and joined at their ends by two crossbeams, the highest of which has its ends curved towards the sky. The temple houses only one large round mirror, made of polished metal, a symbol of clairvoyance and purity.
It was in front of this mirror, on the few wooden steps leading up to the temple, that the Prince of Nagato came to kneel, at the moment indicated by the Kisaki. It was already dark, the moon had risen, and its light, broken by the screen of high branches and foliage, fell to the ground. The priests had returned to the sumptuous pagodas that surround the rustic monument of the early ages; the pilgrims had gone away, and all that could be heard was the vague rustling of the cedars in the wind.
The prince listened attentively. Impressed despite himself by the sanctity of the place, he found the night strangely solemn. There was something threatening about the silence, the shadows of the cedars were hostile, the moon-blue gaze seemed to weep over him.
Why did an invincible anguish oppress his heart so? What was he going to learn? Why was the sovereign in Naikou instead of in her palace? A hundred times he asked himself the same question, which he could not answer.
At last, he felt a slight pain in his shoulder and stood up; a young monk was standing next to him and started walking without saying a word.
Nagato followed him.
They passed through groves of bamboo and avenues of cedar, and came to a wide stone staircase rising between two embankments, on which the moon cast a snowy whiteness. They climbed this staircase, which led to the terrace of a tall pagoda, whose roof, flared like an upside-down lily, ended in a slender spire, turned in a spiral.
The young monk stopped and motioned to Nagato to stay where he was, then walked away. The prince then saw a white shape emerge from the pagoda and step out of the gloom cast by the roof. The moonlight struck it. He recognised the Kisaki. She was dressed in a long sleeveless tunic of white silk, falling over a robe of golden cloth. It was the costume of the High Priestess of the Sun.
- Queen!" exclaimed the prince, rushing towards her, "am I the prey of a dream? this costume...
- It's mine now, Ivakoura," she said. I have laid down my crown and come closer to heaven. However, out of a final weakness, I wanted to see you once more, to bid you farewell forever.
- Ah, perjurer!" exclaimed the prince, "that's how you keep your promises!
- Come," said the queen, "the night is mild, let us leave this open space.
They walked down a long alley lined with bushes and filled with a silvery mist.
- Listen," she said, "don't condemn me without hearing what I have to say. A lot has happened since you left Kioto. I want you to know, my friend, that on the day you saved me, the day we talked for a long time, sitting at the foot of a bush, a man was watching us.
- That's impossible!" exclaimed the prince in horror.
- It certainly is. Instead of fleeing, the man who kidnapped me came back and listened to us. He was a spy for Hiéyas. This treacherous man took advantage of the secret his servant had discovered and revealed it to the Mikado. At first, the son of the gods didn't believe it; he was still full of anger against the infamous man who was bloodying the country. But by skilful manoeuvres, Hiéyas managed to change the mikado's mind and win his trust. Your devotion and heroic conduct during the attack on Kioto were given as proof of our criminal agreement. One day the son of the gods asked for me, and when I was in his presence he handed me a document in which our conversation was reported, but distorted and rendered infamous. Lies have never stained my lips. I confessed proudly that I had given you my soul, but that as long as I lived I would not be ashamed of my actions. But after this confession I could no longer stay at the Dairi.
The high priestess of Ten-Sio-Daï-Tsin had been dead for some time. She was my husband's sister. I asked to fulfil her priesthood, wishing to end my life in retirement. The mikado immediately sent me the title I wanted, and a few days later he married Hiéyas's granddaughter, a child of fifteen.
- You have left the palace of your ancestors to kneel, solitary and grave, in the shadow of a temple, you the smiling goddess whom a whole people adored.
- I'll love this solitude, Ivakoura," she said. Here I am free, at least, free from the tenderness of a husband I did not love, even though he was a god. My thoughts will be entirely yours.
- Why won't you run away with me? Haven't we suffered enough? You love me and I breathe only because you are here on earth. What's the point of torturing ourselves like this? Let's go into exile!
You are our homeland; the world is where you set foot! What do we care what men say! The heavenly music of our love will drown out their despicable voices.
What does it matter to the bird that flees drunk with light to hear the murmurs of reptiles tied to the marshes?
- Shut up, friend," she said; "don't make me repent for wanting to see you again.
- Why don't you want to hear me? Why are you so implacably cruel? Since your husband has taken another wife, you are free now.
- No, Prince, I have not fallen that far; the Mikado has added another woman to his list of wives, but he has not elevated her to the position I occupied. If I were truly free, despite the blame I would incur, I would empty the wedding cup with you and go live wherever you wanted.
- Ah! I'll kill the man who's keeping us apart!" exclaimed the prince, whose mind was wandering.
- Silence, Ivakoura!" said the queen in a serious voice. Look at the garment I am wearing. Think of what I am. From now on I no longer belong to the world; its fevers and follies can no longer touch me. Purified by the divine flame of the Sun, I must meditate on its mysterious and creative essence, absorb myself in its splendour, let myself be penetrated by its rays, identify with the light and become as pure as itself, until the day my soul flies away and receives its deserved reward.
- Forgive me," said the prince; "what do you care about a man's despair? See, I am calm now, as calm as the dead in their graves. Forgive
me for hurting your ears with words too human.
- I have the power to forgive now," she said, "and I absolve you with all my soul.
They retraced their steps.
At the end of this alley, bathed in a diffuse light, everything would be over for them; they would leave each other, never to see each other again.
In spite of herself, the high priestess slowed her pace. The prince's sudden calm frightened her; she could feel that it was the result of an irrevocable resolution. He was silent and looked at her with an expression of appeasement.
- He wants to die, she thought.
But she felt that nothing she could say would shake his decision.
They had reached the end of the driveway and were walking out onto the terrace.
- Farewell," she said.
As she said this, her heart seemed to break in her chest; she was about to fall into the Prince's arms and cry out to him:
- Take me away, let's run away wherever you want!
- Farewell," he murmured, "don't forget that you gave me a rendezvous on the threshold of the other life.
She fled with a sob.
Near the pagoda, she turned round one last time.
She looked supernatural in the moonlight, her golden dress shining beneath her silk tunic, as white as her face.
Ivakoura stretched out her arms towards her, but the high priestess of the Sun sank into the shadows, which enveloped her and hid her forever.
XXX
BATTLES
Hiéyas was at the gates of Osaka with an army of three hundred thousand men. Coming from the northern provinces he had crossed the great island of Nipon, crushing in his path the detachments charged with guarding the country. The soldiers of Fidé-Yori had died heroically, not a single one had retreated; the troops of the princes had put up a feeble resistance, on the contrary. Moreover, the army of Hiéyas, as powerful as a river swollen by the rains, could not be hindered in its march. It arrived near Osaka and enveloped the city. Without resting, they attacked it from all sides at once.
Fidé-Yori had divided his army into three corps of fifty thousand men each: Signénari and Moritzka commanded the first, Harounaga, Moto-Tsoumou, Aroufza the second, Yoké-
Moura the third. The soldiers were intrepid, the leaders determined to die if they could not win.
The first clash between the armies was terrible. They fought with unparalleled fierceness and fury. If their numbers had been equal, Fidé-Yori's troops would have won, but they were so determined to let themselves be killed rather than retreat that they were unshakeable. General Yoké-Moura was attacked by twenty thousand men armed with rifles; he had only ten thousand soldiers around him, established on the hill called Yoka-Yama; Yoké-Moura's men also had rifles. One discharge followed another until the ammunition ran out. Yoké-Moura had been waiting for this moment. He had noticed that his attackers were armed only with rifles and sabres and carried no spears. So he stormed down the hill. His soldiers, spears in fist, threw themselves on the attackers who, almost defenceless, retreated in disorder.
Signénari, too, after a fierce battle, had managed to drive back his attackers, but on all the other points the generals, crushed by numbers, had been beaten and had retreated, with what remained of their soldiers, into the interior of the city.
Evening came and the fighting stopped. The exhausted soldiers lay down in the city streets, on the bridges and along the canals. Only Signénari and Yoké-Moura were still outside Osaka, one on the plain, the other on the hill.
When night had fallen, a man came up to the foot of Yoka hill and asked to speak to General Sanada- Sayemon-Yoké-Moura.
Sayemon-Yoké-Moura, on behalf of Hiéyas.
He was ushered into the warrior's tent.
Yoké-Moura recognised one of his old comrades-in-arms.
- You've come on behalf of Hiéyas," cried the general reproachfully.
- Yes, friend, I believe in the powerful genius of this man; I know how useful his triumph would be for the country, and yet, now that I am in your presence, I hardly dare express the proposal I have been asked to make to you.
- Is it shameful then?
- You shall be the judge: Hieras holds you in high esteem, and he thinks that to triumph over you would be a defeat for him, because your death would deprive the country of its best warrior. He offers you to join him. Your conditions would be his.
- If Hiéyas really holds me in high esteem," replied Yoké-
Moura, why does he pretend to believe that I'm capable of selling myself? You can tell him that if he gave me half of Japan, I wouldn't even think about his proposal, and that my glory lies in remaining faithful to the master I have always served, and in dying for him.
- I expected this answer, and if I accepted this mission that was proposed to me, it was because I gave in to the desire to see my old companion again.
- Weren't you afraid of the just reproaches I might make of you?
- No, because I didn't feel guilty. Now I don't know what remorse torments me in the face of your quiet and heroic devotion. I feel that my actions, dictated by wisdom, are not worth the folly of your blind loyalty.
- Well, there is still time to repent, stay with us.
- I will, my friend. Hiéyas will understand, when he doesn't see me coming back, that the man who came to buy you has given himself to you.
The same proposal was made to General Signénari.
- Hiéyas offers to give me anything I want," cried the young general, "so let him send me his head!
The next day, considerable forces had gathered in front of Signénari. The young warrior realised that the battle ahead was his last. He went round his camp, exhorting his soldiers. Grave, gentle and as beautiful as a woman, he went through the ranks, demonstrating to the attentive men the little value of life, not hiding from them that the outcome of the day was death or dishonour. He added that a glorious death was enviable and that the life of a coward was not worth that of a dog.
Then he returned to his tent and sent a messenger to his mother, telling her that he was going to die and sending her a rich dagger in memory of him. Then he approached a mirror and poured perfumes over his hair, and placed his black horn helmet on his head, topped above the forehead by a copper blade cut into the shape of a crescent. He tied it under his chin and cut off the floating ends of the silk cords. This meant that he would never untie them again, that he was dooming himself to death. If his head was carried to the victor, the latter would understand that he had killed himself voluntarily.
The battle began, and it was Signénari who attacked; he rushed to the head of his soldiers with impetuosity. They broke through the enemy ranks and massacred many of them. Signenari's army, decimated the day before, reduced to a small number of men, penetrated the enemy army like a ship in the sea, but the waves had closed behind it, it was enveloped, captive, yet more fiery than ever; the soldiers of Hiéyas thought they had imprisoned a storm. The desperate are terrible, the carnage was frightening; the wounded were still fighting, the earth flooded with blood was softening, people were trampling in the mud, you could have thought it had rained. However, ten thousand men against a hundred thousand could not hold out for very long. The heroes surrounding the young leader were not defeated, however; they did not retreat, they let themselves be killed in the square they had conquered. But their numbers were dwindling fast: soon there was nothing at the centre of the army but a huge heap of corpses. Signenari, covered in wounds, formidable, was still fighting. He was alone, the enemy hesitated in front of him, he was admired, but someone shot an arrow at him and he fell.
Hiéyas, lying in a litter, was on the battlefield.
The grave and charming young head of General Signénari was brought to him; he saw the cords of the helmet cut; he breathed in the perfumes with which the hair was flooded.
- He would rather have died than join my cause," he said with a sigh. Today's victory saddens me as if it were a defeat.
That same day, Fidé-Yori sent for Yoké-Moura and asked him what remained to be done.
- Tomorrow we must attempt a general sortie," he replied.
All the remnants of the armies gathered in the city form a total of around sixty thousand men, to which we must add the garrison of the fortress, the ten thousand men I have left, and the ten thousand volunteers you have gathered: we can begin the fight.
- Will you enter the city?" asked the Siogun.
- I think it would be better for me to keep my advanced position on the hill. When the army moves off, I'll attack from another point, so that he'll be forced to divide his forces.
The chiefs were brought together to consult with them. The seriousness of the situation silenced the discord that usually divided them: all submitted to Yoké-Moura.
- The enemy has spread out all around the town," said the general, "so that at the point you attack, you will encounter forces at most equal to your own. The exit should be to the south, so that, if possible, you can drive the enemy into the sea. Let the leaders inflame the soldiers by their example and their words, and we can triumph.
- I myself will lead the army," cried Fidé-Yori. The insignia that preceded my illustrious father in battle, the golden gourds with the red staffs, which have always been a signal of victory wherever they have appeared, will be drawn from their velvet scabbards; this memory of Taïko-Sama will enthrall the soldiers; it will remind them of ancient triumphs, of glorious battles won under his shadow. This talisman will protect us and fill the perjurer Hiéyas with dread, evoking before his eyes the image of the man whose trust he has betrayed.
The chiefs returned to their soldiers to prepare them for the decisive battle of the following day. Fidé-Yori ran back to his fiancée.
- This could be the last day we spend together," he said, "I don't want to waste a second.
- What are you saying, my lord?" said Omiti, "If you die, I'll die too, and we'll be reunited never to leave each other again.
- Anyway," said the king with a sad smile, "I would have liked our happiness on this earth to have been longer. I have been unhappy for so long, happy for so few days; and you, so devoted, so gentle, have suffered all kinds of hardship because of me, and as your reward, when I would like to shower you with riches, honours and joy, I can only give you the spectacle of the horrors of war and the prospect of imminent death.
- You gave me your love," replied Omiti.
- Oh yes," cried the king, "and that love, which was the first, would have been the last; it would have filled my whole life. What can I do to take you away from here, to flee this battle, this carnage! Power doesn't matter to me! It hasn't given me happiness. To live close to you, in a deep retreat, oblivious to men and their criminal ambitions, that would be true happiness.
- Let's not think about that," said Omiti, "it's an impossible dream; to die close to each other, that's a joy too, and it won't be denied us.
- Alas!" exclaimed the siogoun, "my youth revolts at the thought of death. Since I have found you, my beloved, I have forgotten the disdain I was taught for this fleeting life; I love it and I would not like to leave it.
Under the cover of darkness, Harounaga managed to regain the heights of Tchaousi that he had lost. General Yoké-Moura had advised him to make this attempt, the success of which would protect the exit from the Siogoun.
Everything was ready for the last effort, the soldiers were full of ardour, the chiefs were hopeful, Fidé-Yori was regaining confidence and he believed in victory. One thing saddened him, however, in this supreme situation, was the absence of his most faithful friend, his wisest adviser, the Prince of Nagato: what had become of him? what had happened to him? In the twenty days or so since he had abruptly left Osaka, there had been no news of him.
- He is dead because he is not near me in the hour of danger, the siogun said to himself, sighing deeply.
From daybreak, the inhabitants of Osaka crowded the area around the fortress; they wanted to see the Siogun emerge from the castle in full battle dress, surrounded by his richly dressed warriors.
In the meantime, they chatted with the soldiers camped in the streets, pouring them glassfuls of sake. The city looked cheerful: despite everything, the light-hearted nature of its inhabitants was back in evidence. They were going to see a show, they were happy.
Around the eighth hour, the gates of the second wall of the fortified castle opened wide, revealing a confusion of banners floating among the luminous stripes formed by the high lances.
The first corps of spearmen from the Siogun advanced, breast-armoured, wearing visored helmets flared around the nape of the neck and decorated above the forehead with a sort of copper crescent, spears in fist, a small flag slung behind the left shoulder; then came the archers, their foreheads girded with a white cloth headband whose ends floated back, their backs bristling with long arrows, holding the great lacquered bow in their hands. After them came a number of strange figures who looked more like large insects or fantastic crustaceans than men. Some wore large helmets adorned with copper antennae above their grimacing black masks; others had their headdresses adorned with enormous horns curved towards each other, and their masks bristled with moustaches and red or white eyebrows; or else a bonnet of chain mail pulled back over the face, leaving only the eyes visible, enveloped their heads. The pieces of armour, made of black horn, were square, heavy and strangely arranged, but under the stitches of silk of various colours that joined the blades of horn together, they produced a beautiful effect. These warriors, dressed as their ancestors had been, were armed with halberds, huge bows and two-handed swords. They marched for a long time, to the great admiration of the people. Finally, Fidé-Yori appeared on a horse with a plaited mane. They carried before him the golden gourds which, since the last victories of Taïko-
victories, had not left the castle. They were greeted with shouts of enthusiasm.
- I entrust them to you," cried Fidé-Yori, pointing out the glorious insignia to his army.
He said nothing more and, drawing his sabre, galloped off.
With a heroic dash, the whole army rode out of the city. The people followed them as far as the outskirts.
From the top of the hill, Yoké-Moura watched Fidé-Yori and his troops advance out of Osaka and spread out across the plain, waiting for the first offensive movement of the siogoun to attack Hiéyas' men from his side.
- Signénari, who died so nobly yesterday, and his heroic soldiers have done a great deal of harm to the enemy; I myself drove back the detachment attacking my position, causing them considerable losses. We can cut to pieces the part of the army that the Siogoun will attack. Then the two enemy forces will be more or less on an equal footing, and with equal strength we will triumph.
Fidé-Yori's army had stopped on the plain and was occupying the site
where the Signenari camp had stood the day before.
- What are they waiting for?" wondered Yoké-Moura; "why have they stopped moving forward?
The leaders were running up and down the flanks of the battalions. There was a strange restlessness in the ranks. Evidently something new had happened, people were hesitating, consulting each other. Suddenly, the army was shaken to its core, turned around and retraced its steps back into the city.
- What does this mean?" exclaimed Yoké-Moura, stunned and pale with anger. What madness has suddenly seized them? Are they cowards?
The soldiers of Hiéyas then advanced, crossing the plain abandoned by Fidé-Yori. At the same time, Yoké-Moura's men raised the alarm. They were being attacked from two sides at once.
- It's all right," said Yoké-Moura, "all is lost now.
- He summoned his young son Daïské.
- My son," he told him, "go back to the town, join the Siogoun and tell him that all I have to do now is die gloriously for him, which I intend to do before tonight. Stay with the master as long as he lives and die with him.
- Father," said Daïské, giving the general a pleading look, "I would prefer to die by your side.
- Do as I command, my son," said Yoké-Moura, his voice trembling slightly.
A tear rolled down the boy's cheek, but he said nothing and went away.
The general followed him with his eyes for a moment as he descended the hill. He sighed, then suddenly threw himself into the battle.
Without having fought, without having exchanged an arrow with the enemy, the Siogoun army had returned to the town in disarray. The people didn't want to believe it. What had happened?
How had the rout preceded the battle?
Here's what had happened: Harounaga, suddenly abandoning his position on the hill, had run towards Fidé-Yori, accompanied by a man who had come from the camp of Hiéyas. This man, who was a relative of Harounaga, claimed that most of the army had been sold to Hiéyas and that, at the moment of battle, Fidé-Yori would be surrounded by his own soldiers and taken prisoner. He said that he had overheard this secret and had run to warn the Siogun and prevent him from falling into an odious trap.
- Go back to the fortress," he told Fidé-Yori, "in the shelter of its ramparts, you can still defend yourself and die nobly, whereas here you are at the mercy of the victor.
After some hesitation, we returned to the city. This story of betrayal was completely inaccurate: it was a perfidy on the part of Hiéyas who, although he was strong, did not disdain to use trickery. But the people did not accept this reason, and the return of the soldiers had the most deplorable effect.
- They don't know how to behave, they said.
- They're lost, it's all over now.
- After all, it's none of our business.
Half the population was beginning to wish for the advent of Hiéyas.
The siogoun had barely returned to the castle when the enemy army attacked the outskirts of the town. The inhabitants shut themselves up in their houses. A terrible battle ensued, the ground was defended foot to foot. However, the enemy advanced.
They were fighting in the narrow streets, on the banks of the canals, whose blood-reddened waters were littered with corpses, and every bridge was swept away after a fierce struggle. Little by little, the soldiers of Fidé-
Yori's soldiers were pushed back towards the fortress.
There was great confusion in the castle, no one thought of defending the first wall, the bastions no longer existed and the moat had not been dug back more than two feet. They locked themselves in the second enclosure, but there they were too far away to be of any service to those fighting. After three hours of fighting, they were driven back to the castle walls; they invaded the first enclosure and shouted for the second to be opened. They were about to be crushed against it.
Yodogimi shouted to open up. All the doors opened at once, and the soldiers rushed in. But the enemy was hot on their heels, and when they had passed through, the doors could not be closed and Hiéyas's soldiers entered behind them.
Fidé-Yori had locked himself in with a thousand soldiers in the third enclosure of the castle, which surrounded the great tower of the Golden Fish, the residence of the siogun and some of the palaces of the noblest princes. He was not thinking of defending himself, but only of not letting himself or any member of his family be taken alive. In one of the rooms of his flat, sword in hand, between his mother and his fiancée, he looked out of the open window and, brow lowered, listened to the tremendous clamour of the soldiers fighting behind the second wall. Many of his people were surrendering. The man in charge of guarding Taïko-Sama's golden gourds - his name was Tsou-Gava - was burning them in front of the palace, in front of Fidé-Yori.
- It's all over!" murmured Fide-Yori. O you who are the dearest thing in the world, you are going to die because of me and with me! We're going to have to take your lives, so that you don't fall alive into the hands of the victors.
He looked down at his naked sword, then up at his mother and the gentle Omiti, with an expression of bewilderment.
- Is it not possible to save them?" he cried, "to let them live? I don't care who wins, as long as I die!
- To live without you!" said Omiti reproachfully. They were both pale, but calm.
- I don't want to see their blood spilt, I don't want to see them dead; it is I who will die first!
XXXI
THE BURNER
- No one will die!" suddenly cried a voice, just as Fidé-Yori turned his weapon on himself.
The Prince of Nagato appeared at the threshold of the room.
Loo was beside him.
- O my brother!" cried the Siogun, rushing towards him, "I never hoped to see you again.
- I knew that victory was impossible," said Nagato, "and I was busy preparing the means for you to escape from the enemy when your last effort to repel him failed. You are the only scion of your race, you are defeated today, but later your dynasty may flourish again.
- Is it really in your power to save us? said the Siogun.
- Yes, master," said Ivakoura. A boat is waiting for you near the bank of the Yodo-Gava. Raïden, a brave sailor whose devotion is well known to me, will take it up. He will take you out to sea and out to sea.
There, a large junk belonging to the Prince of Satsouma is at anchor ready to receive you. As soon as you set foot on it, it will sail to the island of Kiou-Siou; the lord of Satsouma, the most powerful prince in your kingdom, the most loyal of your subjects, will open his province and his castle to you; you can live there happily, close to the wife of your choice, until the day of vengeance.
- I'm very grateful for your tireless devotion," said the siogun, whose eyes were wet with tears. But how do you get out of the castle? How do you cross it without being massacred by the furious horde that surrounds it?
- You will go out as I came in," said the prince, "without being worried by anyone. If you will follow me to my palace," he continued, bowing to the two princesses, "I will show you the way out of the fortress.
- Prince," said Yodogimi, "your greatness of spirit fills me with confusion; I, who have so often tried to harm you, now see how unjust and blind I have been; tell me that you forgive me for my past mistakes, otherwise I will not accept to be saved by you.
- I have nothing to forgive you for, Princess," said Nagato, "it is I who am guilty of having had the incomparable blunder of displeasing you.
- Come on, let's get out of here," said the siogun, "you can explain later.
They left the room, with Loo in the lead.
In the first courtyard of the palace, the insignia of Taïko-Sama were still burning, forming a heap of embers. As they passed, Fidé-Yori turned her head away. They reached the pavilion of the Prince of Nagato and entered his room. The trapdoor that closed the underground pathway, through which brave Sado used to enter and leave the palace, was open. No one knew of the existence of this underground passage. The Prince of Nagato had had it secretly dug to facilitate Sado's comings and goings and to escape the surveillance of spies.
- This is the way," he said, "it leads to a fisherman's house on the banks of the Yodo-Gava. That's where Raïden is waiting for you with the boat; set off, Loo will guide you along this underground route.
- What!" exclaimed Fidé-Yori, "aren't you coming with us?
- No, Master, I'm staying here, I've still got something to do.
- Are you mad? Staying in this palace, which will soon be completely overrun; what else do you have to do? you won't be able to escape.
Don't worry about me," said the prince with a strange smile.
- Ivakoura!" exclaimed the Siogun, looking at his friend with fear, "you want to die! I understand you, but I won't accept salvation at that price. I'm still the master, aren't I? Well, I order you to follow me.
My beloved lord," said Nagato in a firm voice, "if it is true that I have served you with devotion, do not refuse me the first grace I ask of you, do not order me to leave this palace.
- I no longer order, friend, I beg you not to deprive me of a companion like you, I beg you to flee with us.
- I add my supplications to those of my son," said Yodogimi; "do not let us leave with pain in our souls.
- Illustrious Prince," said Omiti in his soft, timid voice, "this is the first time I have spoken to you, but I dare to beg you, in my turn, not to persist in your cruel resolution.
Loo threw himself on his knees.
- My master!" he cried.
But he could say no more and began to weep.
- I commend this child to you," said Nagato to Fide-Yori.
- Are you deaf to our voices?" said the siogun, "do our prayers have no power over your heart?
- If she were lost to you," said the prince, pointing to Omiti, "would you consent to live? O you to whom I have entrusted the terrible secret of my life, do you not understand how painful existence is for me? Can't you see the joy that shines in my eyes now that I am nearing the end of my suffering? It was to serve you that I did not free myself for a long time from the torment of living. You are not as victorious as I would have liked, but I see you in a retreat full of flowers, joy and love; you will be happy, if not powerful: you no longer need me, I am free, I can die.
- Ah, cruel friend," said Fidé-Yori, "I can see that your will is irrevocable.
- Make haste," said the prince, "you have delayed too long.
Get to the boat. Raïden will hide you under the folds of the sail thrown into the bottom of the boat, then he will take the oars, Loo will hold the rudder.
- No! no!" cried the child, clinging to his master's robe, "I don't want to go, I want to die with you.
- Obedience is the first virtue of a good servant, Loo," said the prince gently, "I order you to obey our master from now on, and to serve him until death.
Loo rushed, sobbing, down the dark underground stairs, the two women followed him, and then the siogoun descended in his turn.
- Farewell! farewell! my friend, my brother, you, the most beautiful, the most noble, the most devoted of my subjects! he cried, letting his tears flow.
- Farewell, illustrious friend," said the prince, "may your happiness last as long as your life!
He closed the entrance to the underground passage. He was alone at last. Then he returned to the courtyard of the palace and, taking a piece of burning wood from the brazier that was still burning, he set fire to all the princely pavilions and to the palace of Fidé-Yori, through all the rooms of which he passed, then he went to the Tower of the Golden Fish and, from floor to floor, lit the fire. When he reached the last terrace, he threw down his burning firebrand and leaned against the red lacquer balustrade of the platform, which was crowned by a vast roof, raised at the edges and supported by four heavy pillars.
The prince looked out to sea. The little boat was already at the mouth of the Yodo-Gava. Alone on the water, it seemed to attract the attention of the victorious soldiers camped along the beach; but Raïden, the fisherman, cast his net, and the reassured soldiers let the boat pass. Out to sea, the Prince of Satsouma's junk appeared as a small brown spot against the crimson of the setting sun. The atmosphere was incomparably transparent. The sea looked like a great turquoise.
The shouts of the soldiers could be heard around the castle.
- Fidé-Yori had set fire to the palace: he would perish in the flames, they said.
Those still sheltered by the third wall opened the gates and rushed out, surrendering.
Moreover, the battle had ceased; the usurper was at the gate of the fortress; people knelt down as he passed by, acclaiming him and proclaiming him the only legitimate Siogun. It was the second day of the sixth moon of the first year of Nengo-Gen-Va
[1] .
From the top of the tower, the Prince of Nagato could see the litter in which Hiéyas lay. He could hear the triumphant cheers that greeted him.
- Glory and royal power are nothing compared to happy love," he murmured, turning his eyes back to the boat carrying his friends.
It was now out at sea, beyond the reach of the soldiers; it was unfurling its sail and fleeing rapidly.
- They are saved," said the prince.
He could see the beginning of the road leading to the sacred city, which he had travelled so many times; he could see the coastline, silhouetted against the blue of the sea, stretching away into the distance, towards the province where the ancient temple of Ten-Sio-Dai-Tsin stands. He seemed to be trying to make out, across the distance, the woman he was never to see again.
The sun disappeared and the light of the fire began to overcome the brightness of the day. The siogun's palace, at the foot of the tower, was a vast furnace which, seen from above, looked like a lake of fire, agitated by a storm. The flames criss-crossed, swirled and formed volutes like waves in a storm. Every now and then, a cloud of red smoke passed in front of the Prince's eyes, obscuring the horizon. Every floor of the tower was on fire, and a tremendous roar, mingled with perpetual crackling, filled its sides. The last platform, however
The last platform, however, had not yet been reached, but already the floor was cracking and swaying. A flame rose and touched the edge of the upper roof.
- Come, then, liberating fire," cried the prince, "come and soothe the devouring burn of my soul, strive to extinguish the unquenchable flame of my love!
He took a crumpled piece of paper from his chest and unfolded it. He lifted it to his lips and read it one last time in the glow of the fire.
"One day the flowers will bow down to die, they will let their luminous souls fall like diamonds, and then the two drops of water will join and merge.
The heat was intolerable. The paper suddenly burned between the prince's fingers. He was gasping for air and felt himself dying.
My beloved!" he cried, "I'm leaving first, don't make me wait too long for the rendezvous.
Like the enormous petals of a fiery flower, the flames enclosed the last terrace and spread across the roof; the two gigantic golden fish twisted on the crest of the roof as if they were alive, then flowed in two incandescent streams. Soon the whole building collapsed, with a terrible crash, sending an immense spray of flames and sparks skywards.
